Fruit of Labor:The Banana Business IsRotten, So Why DoPeople Fight Over It?---It Has Set Off Riots, CoupsAnd Now a Trade War,Despite Weak Pr...
Fruit of Labor: The Banana Business Is Rotten, So Why Do People Fight Over It? --- It Has Set Off
Riots, Coups And Now a Trade War, Despite Weak Profits --- `Dance of Two Big Elephants'
By Gordon Fairclough and Darren McDermott
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
2,140 words
9 August 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Bananas. For more than a century, they have provoked riots and coups. Troops have been dispatched to
protect them. Now they are at the center of a bitter trade war between the U.S. and Europe.
Any business worth fighting for so fiercely must be great, right? Guess again.
Banana farming is a brutal business, from the sprawling plantations of Latin America to the struggling plots of
the Caribbean islands. In the past 10 years, retail U.S. banana prices have fallen roughly 15%, adjusted for
inflation. Global sales are weak; the Russians and Chinese can't afford to buy many bananas this year.
The world's three largest banana companies, Dole Food Co., Chiquita Brands International Inc. and Fresh
Del Monte Produce Inc. -- all based in the U.S. -- have been diversifying as banana profits have slipped.
Hurricanes and bugs are constant menaces. A single misfiring refrigeration unit can convert a multiton
shipment into gooey brownish sludge -- as happened not long ago to a $1.4 million boatload of Fresh Del
Monte bananas.
All of which raises the question: Are U.S. and European trade warriors crazy? In April, the World Trade
Organization, ruling in a longstanding complaint filed by the U.S. and others, held that Europe's elaborate
regime of quotas and tariffs on banana imports violates global trade laws. It was the latest of numerous
rulings over the years by various panels against Europe's banana rules. The WTO also allowed $191.4 million
of retaliatory tariffs by the U.S. on some European products, infuriating the Europeans and driving trade
tensions to heights seldom seen.
The European Union's executive branch is supposed to propose a response to the banana ruling next month.
The E.U. says it will comply, but many doubt it will go far enough to put an end to the banana wars. Because
when it comes down to it, the banana wars are about a lot more than bananas. And, as with most wars, there
are ways to make money, the banana business's many pitfalls notwithstanding.
Many Europeans see bananas as a vestige of colonial glory. Some European countries say they're obligated
by treaty to protect their former colonies' banana industries from foreign competition. The U.S., meanwhile, is
using bananas to set a precedent for agricultural goods nearer and dearer to American farmers' hearts, such
as genetically modified soybeans and corn. And developing countries have a soft spot for the industry
because it is a huge employer. The lone Intel Corp. computer-chip plant in Costa Rica generates more export
income than all of the country's banana farms, yet 50,000 work on the farms, while Intel employs about 2,000.
The banana business's problem isn't product popularity. About 65% of U.S. households buy bananas at least
once a week, compared with about 31% for apples. The average American eats about 28 pounds of bananas
a year -- more than any other fresh fruit. But bananas are growing even faster than we can eat them. World
banana production has been climbing steadily, reaching 58.6 million tons last year, according to the United
Nations's Food and Agriculture Organization. That's up 24% from the start of the decade. Even Hurricane
Mitch, which wiped out production in much of Honduras and parts of Guatemala last year, hasn't put much of
a dent in the global banana glut.
Commodity businesses aren't known for consistent profits, of course, but few are as competitive as bananas.
Margins are razor-thin: Fresh Del Monte, for instance, gets the majority of its profit from pineapples, even
though bananas account for 56% of sales and pineapples just 19%. "All the money is made by the
supermarket -- we only get the bones," complains farmer Edgar Quiros Gonzalez, president of Cooperativa
Difrusa in Costa Rica. He says he gets about $5.80 for a 40-pound box under his long-term contract with
Fresh Del Monte. He says he has to pay out $5.20 a box in costs.

--- Page 6 ---
Page 6 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In a sense, the banana business contains the seeds of its own troubles. Bananas are prolific. Think rabbits.
Bananas don't grow on trees; technically the banana plant is the world's largest herb. But they do grow all
year long, so every season is banana season. An average acre of banana plants today produces about seven
tons of fruit a year, nearly double the output of an acre of apple trees and six times that of an acre of wheat.
"You need an outlet for all that fruit. That's why people fight so hard to protect their markets," says Mario A.
Broutin, Chiquita's general manager in Costa Rica.
Fighting has been part of the banana business almost from the start. Largescale commercial production
began after the Costa Rican government hired Minor C. Keith, a 23-year-old engineer from Brooklyn, N.Y., to
build a rail line in 1871. To finance the project, Mr. Keith grew bananas along the right-of-way and sold them
in the U.S. Eventually, his enterprise grew into United Fruit Co., predecessor of Chiquita, and spanned much
of Central America. United Fruit's role there gave rise to the term "banana republic."
United Fruit came to symbolize America's growing commercial and political might, at the expense of
European colonial powers. Britain fought back, subsidizing the shipping of Jamaican bananas to the United
Kingdom. But that plan backfired when the British firms ran into financial trouble and were acquired by none
other than United Fruit. Britain tried again after World War II, sponsoring development of a banana industry in
the Caribbean's Windward Islands.
The U.S. and some Latin American governments didn't shrink from backing the interests of American banana
companies. When a left-leaning president of Guatemala expropriated United Fruit land in the 1950s, he was
overthrown in a now-infamous coup backed by the Central Intelligence Agency. But as the years passed,
various Latin American revolutions, an antitrust action by the U.S. against United Fruit and other
developments took their toll on profits.
By the late 1980s, the banana business looked more appealing. Demand surged after the fall of the Berlin
Wall. Eastern Europeans gobbled up bananas, the kind of cheap luxury they associated with the good life in
the West. And the formation of a single, unified market in Europe in 1993 was expected to turn Western
Europe, at the time the fastest-growing market for bananas, into a gold rush. Banana growers started
ramping up production in the early 1990s.
It was a bust. Rather than embrace free trade in bananas, the European Union agreed on a convoluted
system of tariffs and quotas designed to support banana growing in former colonies, including parts of the
Caribbean, Africa and Asia. They also effectively limited the number of Latin American bananas that could be
sold in the EU and divided the banana world into haves and have-nots -- those who have licenses allowing
them to import bananas into the protected European market, and those who don't.
Because the amount of bananas Europe allows in is far less than the amount consumers want to buy, the
price of bananas in Europe is inflated to about twice the U.S. level. A pound of bananas, about 50 cents in the
U.S., goes for roughly a dollar in Europe.
Here we arrive at the central paradox of bananas: Though a murderous business in many ways, bananas can
be lucrative for holders of the licenses that allow selling under Europe's higher prices. Indeed, many growers
railing against the European system call not so much for its dismantling, but for better access to its higher
prices.
Chiquita was hit the hardest when Europe created its quota system. Until the system was created, Chiquita
says, Europe was its most profitable market. It says it was frozen out of more than half its European market
share when the Europeans imposed their licensing and other requirements. Moreover, Dole has had more
success than Chiquita in forging alliances with European companies with import licenses. Chiquita's bitter
complaints helped get the attention of the U.S. government; it and a group of Latin American governments
took the case to the WTO, leading to the April ruling against Europe's banana quotas.
Brent Borrell, a former World Bank economist now working at the Center for International Economics in
Canberra, Australia, calls the European system "economic lunacy." Even viewed as an aid program for
former colonies, the scheme is highly inefficient, he says. Of the $2 billion Dr. Borrell estimates the
arrangement costs European consumers, only about $150 million finds its way into the hands of banana
farmers in former colonies. More than $1 billion ends up in the pockets of the companies that import the
bananas, according to Dr. Borrell.
The European system has also managed to make the economies of some former colonies heavily dependent
on bananas. Farms in the Windward Islands -- which include St. Lucia and four other island states -- are
alternately baked by the Caribbean sun and lashed by storms. They yield just eight tons an acre, while Latin
American plantations can produce 25 or 30 tons an acre, says Theresa Alexander Louis, an economist at the
Windward Island Banana Development & Exporting Co. "We are totally uneconomical," says Tony Smith,
manager of the St. Lucia Banana Co. "We couldn't survive more than two days" without European aid.

--- Page 7 ---
Page 7 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Yet bananas made up 56% of St. Lucia's domestic exports in 1997, and 35% of the exports for all the
Windward Islands. The industry employed about a third of the region's work force that year. Kenny D.
Anthony, St. Lucia's prime minister, summed it up in a speech last fall: "There is no future without bananas."
The centrality of the banana is viewed similarly in Latin America, and growers there are worried, too. "If we
don't get access to those higher prices, a good percentage of our growers would be in a critical situation,"
says Victor E. Herrera, president of Costa Rica's Corporacion Bananera Nacional in San Jose. "This is a
dance of two big elephants. And we are like an ant" about to get trampled.
Yet the bananas keep on coming. On tiny St. Lucia, Cecilia Blanchard totes an aluminum ladder around her 7
1/2-acre farm, picking the flowers off her banana bunches and spraying the plants and fruit with pesticides.
Once a week, she brings in extra help to pick the bananas and pack them in boxes in time to meet the weekly
banana boat bound for England and J. Sainsbury PLC, a British supermarket chain.
But even with subsidized prices, cut-rate loans and other aid, Ms. Blanchard, considered one of the best
growers in St. Lucia, struggles. She gets the equivalent of only about $6.80 to $7.55 for a 34-pound box of
bananas. After costs, Ms. Blanchard says, she averages roughly 38 cents to 72 cents in profit a box. She
ships as much as 100 boxes a week.
Meanwhile, on Fresh Del Monte's Carmen farm in Santo Domingo, Costa Rica, more than 100 workers toil as
long as 12 hours a day planting, pruning, weeding and spraying. To protect the fruit's thin skin from insects,
bruising and sunburn, insecticide-impregnated plastic bags are draped over the bananas while they grow.
Three-man crews drip with sweat in the tropical heat, harvesting tons of fruit every day. In the packing
stations, men and women in rubber aprons and gloves cut bunches apart, sort bananas by size and quality,
clean them, spray them with fungicide and pack them.
By afternoon, this day's fruit will be loaded onto a ship at the nearby port of Limon, bound for Wal-Mart Stores
Inc.'s Sam's Club stores. Harvested while they are green, bananas must be shipped and stored between 55.9
and 57.9 degrees. The fragile fruit can't even withstand bouncing. Chiquita's Costa Rican operations use
three road graders to keep the byways between its farms and the port smooth.
Given such costly headaches, few in the business want the European banana quotas -- and the higher prices
they bring -- to disappear. A truly free market "would mean very cheap bananas," says Jack Loeb, a
banana-industry veteran in Costa Rica. "And nobody wants that."
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Oppression of Workers" -- WSJ Aug. 26, 1999)

Forbes BetsFarm onBashing Fed
Potomac Watch
Forbes Bets Farm on Bashing Fed
By Paul A. Gigot
852 words
6 August 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A10
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
A great thing about presidential campaigns is that they always surprise. Take the fact that Fed Chairman Alan
Greenspan is being attacked in Iowa -- from the populist right.
Yesterday the supposed candidate of the angry affluent, Steve Forbes, began running a radio ad that all but
blames Ayn Rand's most famous admirer for the low commodity prices roiling the Farm Belt.
"Interest rates are too high," says Mr. Forbes in the 60-second spot, "and while Alan Greenspan and the
Federal Reserve raise rates, commodity prices have plummeted." He lumps the Fed chairman in with "deeply
flawed government policies" causing the farm "crisis."
That's mild compared to Dan Quayle, who's been lambasting Mr. Greenspan for causing "deflation" and
"ignoring the real-world evidence right before your eyes." And even that's tepid next to Jack Kemp, who sent
his old friend a "Dear Alan" letter assailing his preference for reducing debt over cutting taxes: "I was really
astounded . . ."
This wasn't supposed to happen in the fat and happy 1990s, but the farm economy is an exception to U.S.
prosperity. Fed chairmen are also usually attacked from the populist left, but the Forbes salvo is symbolic of
the political transformation Mr. Greenspan has made in the Clinton years.
From a suspect Reagan holdover in the early 1990s, he has become more important than Tipper to Al Gore's
election. Down in the polls and burdened by his boss, Mr. Gore knows his best campaign theme will be
don't-risk-the-boom, so continuity at the Fed is crucial.
That's why the veep recently all but endorsed Mr. Greenspan's reappointment, a full year before his third
four-year term expires. Even Bill Bradley, who voted against Mr. Greenspan's original nomination, now feels
obliged to praise him.
But that's left Mr. Greenspan's old friends on the right feeling estranged. Their complaints are both fiscal and
monetary, with the former more persuasive than the latter. Mr. Kemp, in particular, got mad when the Fed
boss recently threw cold water on tax cutting, at least right now.
As usual, Mr. Greenspan's remarks had more nuances than a Hillary Clinton psychoanalysis of her husband:
He endorsed tax cuts, for example, if the alternative were spending the surplus, as it surely is. But Democrats
and the media leapt on his comments anyway, inflicting real political harm on the tax-cutting cause. Fairly or
not, many Republicans suspect the Fed chairman was angling for Clinton-Gore reappointment.
The other dispute on the right is over Mr. Greenspan's recent move to tighten monetary policy. Supply-side
Republicans have watched the price of gold and farm commodities fall dramatically, and some say the Fed
chairman is being too tight.
They worry he's fighting the last war -- inflation -- when he ought to be worrying that soybean prices are
down by 40% since 1996. An editorial in the influential Farm Journal also painted a bull's-eye on Mr.
Greenspan.
This is known to make the Fed chairman a tad angry. He thinks that if the Fed were too tight, the
interest-sensitive housing and auto markets would be slowing down. But they've been booming.
As for gold, the falling price may reflect the kind of victory over inflation that Messrs. Kemp and Forbes
themselves fought so long for. Gold may be falling relative to the dollar and other currencies precisely
because investors have more faith in those currencies and so less need now to hold gold as an inflation
hedge.

--- Page 9 ---
Page 9 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Regarding farm prices, Mr. Greenspan told Congress the culprits are farm productivity and foreign export
markets. Farmers are raising output so rapidly that unit prices are falling, especially with Asian markets
depressed. In any event, Mr. Greenspan probably deserves some leeway when he makes a quarter-point
interest-rate call.
Politics isn't about leeway, however, which is why Mr. Forbes is running out his new ad. "Greenspan scores
for us big time," says Forbes campaign manager Bill Dal Col. "Even the non-farmers get it." It helps Mr.
Forbes in particular because he has credibility on economics that other candidates don't.
The Forbes camp also hopes the issue will cut against George W. Bush, who has already urged Mr.
Greenspan's reappointment. Mr. Bush's lead economic adviser is former Fed governor Larry Lindsey.
There's some irony to this, since Mr. Bush's father reappointed Mr. Greenspan only to blame him later for
raising rates and causing the recession that doomed him in 1992. (This was easier than blaming his own tax
increase.) But the George W. camp seems unconcerned. "Let's see how much money they can raise in New
York by attacking Alan Greenspan," quips one source close to the campaign.
All of which suggests a good intra-GOP debate to come. Maybe Mr. Greenspan can mediate by proposing a
compromise to his conservative critics: If they'll give him a break on monetary policy, he'll shut up about
taxes.

The Americas:Canadians Wonder Whether the Loonie Is for the Birds
The Americas: Canadians Wonder Whether the Loonie Is for the Birds
By Peter Holle
1,131 words
6 August 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A11
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The gnomes at the Bank of Canada are puzzled. The economy is thriving, unemployment has fallen, inflation
is a distant memory and the federal budget is moving toward a hefty surplus. Yet the Canadian dollar remains
in the doldrums.
In July, it gave up recent gains and slipped 4% against the U.S. dollar, to close the month at 66 U.S.cents.
This is a long way from the 75-cent value that some market watchers had predicted earlier this year.
The contradiction between a healthy macroeconomic picture and the sagging dollar has sparked a search for
new ideas in Canada. In June, a Toronto-based think tank, the C.D. Howe Institute, released a study called
"From Fixing to Monetary Union: Options for North American Currency Integration," which suggests merging
the two dollars into a common currency.
The initial public reaction to this idea was visceral and negative, offering proof that a Canadian identity does
exist, however elusive its precise definition. Still, Canadians have a practical side. A north-south economic
integration is at once growing and yet limited by the weaker northern currency. Even for identity-hungry
Canadians, North American monetary union now seems a proposal worth examining. This week an Angus
Reid opinion poll published in the Canadian newspaper the Globe and Mail, revealed that 37% of Canadians
believe that abolishing the national currency would help Canada. About 77% of respondents predict a
common dollar within 20 years; half see it happening within a decade.
The Canadian dollar's value has traditionally been tied to world commodity markets. But commodities now
account for a dwindling percentage of exports, as Canada's manufacturing and service sectors boom in the
integrated North American economy. A more likely reason for last month's weakness is that Prime Minister
Jean Chretien recently poured cold water on the idea of cutting taxes, particularly those on high-income
earners. Canada taxes capital gains twice as heavily as the U.S., and the top marginal income tax rates of
45.6% to 54% kick in at only 65,000 Canadian dollars. Higher costs induced by higher taxes have had a
depreciating effect on the dollar because they lower returns to Canada investors.
On the spending side, according to the most recent Organization for Economic Cooperation and
Development figures, government expenditures now represent 42.6% of gross domestic product in Canada,
compared to 31.6% in the U.S. Ottawa didn't help when it floated a proposal in late July for a $C12 billion
universal day-care program.
With the government so reluctant to tackle uncompetitive taxation and spending policies, the provocative
suggestion to restore purchasing power to Canadians through currency union stirred the country. Why not
merge the currencies of Canada and the U.S.?
Support for one currency rests on the fact that the two economies are increasingly integrated. Since the North
American Free Trade Agreement was signed in 1988, Canada's trade with the U. S. has almost tripled.
Interprovincial trade has also increased, but at a much slower pace. Nafta has allowed the U.S. and
Canadian economies to integrate along natural lines that reflect real trading interests rather than artificial
political constructs like the 49th parallel. Canada now ships 85% of its exports to the American market.
According to the C.D. Howe Institute study's authors, economists Thomas Courchene and Richard Harris,
increased integration of the U.S., Canadian and other Western Hemisphere economies as a consequence of
freer trade is bringing "market dollarization" closer. When this happens, businesses will no longer conduct
affairs in their national currencies; instead, they will sign contracts, operate bank accounts, set prices and
even pay their employees in U.S. dollars. If this scenario developed, Canada would start down a "slippery
slope." Floating exchange rates would become even more volatile as demand for the Canadian dollar slips in
favor of the greenback and the central bank loses its ability to control monetary policy. An official currency
union would allow Canada to manage this slide to the U.S. dollar and ameliorate the subsequent erosion of
its financial and regulatory institutions.

--- Page 11 ---
Page 11 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Canadian dollar is down by a third since exchange rates started floating in 1971. Its value is based
mostly on the judgments made by millions of traders whenever they buy and sell. High tax rates and intrusive
regulatory regimes all play a part in how the market values the currency. Canada has little real power left to
influence it; the gold supply, which just 18 years ago made up 80% of foreign reserves, now comprises only
5%.
This has forced Ottawa to face reality and Canada has balanced its budgets. Yet the political leadership is
still unable or unwilling to do the things that would create demand for holding Canadian dollars. The loss of
sovereignty represented by a currency union would merely be a de jure acknowledgement of the continuation
of this powerful trend. The transfer of power to markets composed of billions of buyers and sellers further
constricts and restrains the behavior of the traditional political classes.
A currency union with the U.S. would, as critics claim, erode Canada's power to intervene in markets.
Although taxation, spending and regulatory policies would remain within Canada's preserve, it would force the
government to be competitive with the U.S. in these policies or suffer the consequences. It would also
consolidate and extend the productivity gains Canadians have achieved under Nafta and allow the private
sector to become more competitive.
Under the institute's proposal for a North American monetary union, the U.S. Federal Reserve would have to
add a Canadian seat to its board of governors and this is certainly a problem. Federal Reserve Chairman
Alan Greenspan has recently told Congress that the U.S. will never share control of its central bank.
However, new ideas have a way of taking on a life of their own. A decade before Nafta, the notion of free
trade with the U.S. had almost no political support in Canada. Now everyone except a handful of die-hard
nationalists sees its value. A currency union has already been embraced by some members of Parliament
from the two largest opposition groups, the right-wing Reform Party and the left-wing, separatist Bloc
Quebecois.
The dollar coin with a loon on the front remains a symbol of Canada's uniqueness. As the Angus Reid survey
shows, however, Canadians may be developing a new sense of secure nationalism. One that says they can
compete on the level playing field of a single currency.
---
Mr. Holle is president of the Frontier Centre for Public Policy, a think tank in Winnipeg, Manitoba.

Gold-Fund Manager Ponders Weak Prices
Fund Track
Gold-Fund Manager Ponders Weak Prices
By Margaret Boitano
Dow Jones Newswires
1,193 words
3 August 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C27
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Mutual-fund manager Jean-Marie Eveillard isn't as confident as he used to be that gold will regain its luster.
If gold prices keep falling, says Mr. Eveillard, he may reconsider whether to keep the SoGen Gold Fund he
manages afloat. The fund has assets of $15.8 million, down from $70 million in its heyday three years ago.
Last December, Mr. Eveillard considered liquidating the fund and giving shareholders their money back. By
then, the prolonged slump in gold prices had stretched for over two years and was wreaking havoc on
returns. SoGen Gold fell 18.44% in 1998, after having dropped 29.79% the year before, according to
fundtracker Morningstar Inc. Heartened by a fleeting rebound in gold prices at the end of 1998, Mr. Eveillard
decided to keep the fund going.
Today, with gold prices hovering near 20-year lows and central banks all over the world eager to unload their
gold reserves, Mr. Eveillard wonders if he made the right decision. "We decided that we would go on, but
within six to 12 to 18 months, we'll have to think about it again," he says.
Comex gold closed Monday at $256.20 an ounce, near the record low of $253.20 set on July 20. At its peak
in February 1990, gold fetched $417.50 an ounce.
Mr. Eveillard began the fund in August 1993 on the premise that demand for gold would continue to outstrip
supply, thus keeping prices high and boosting prices of related gold stocks. Mr. Eveillard is known for making
bold, contrarian bets as a fund manager. He didn't anticipate that gold sales by some central banks would
depress prices so much.
Gold bugs who got into the SoGen fund when it opened have seen their investments fall 7.6% annually, on
average, according to Lipper Inc. Other funds in the sector are in the same boat: The average gold fund lost
6.24% over the past year and racked up five-year negative annualized returns of 13.05%, according to
Lipper.
Concern that gold is no longer the haven of last resort in times of trouble brought on the slump. In years past,
investors typically turned to gold as a hedge against inflation or a safe place to park cash when world
financial markets were in turmoil. But lately, central bankers are more concerned about worldwide deflation
than widespread inflation, and U.S. Treasury bills and bonds are the shelter of choice when markets hiccup.
Many financial planners have written off gold funds. "You'd be hard pressed to find a planner out there that's
using gold now," says Louis Altfest, a fee-only financial planner in New York who's telling his clients to buy
mutual funds that invest in real estate investment trusts, known as REITs, instead. REIT funds are a better
hedge against inflation than gold, Mr. Altfest says, because they perform well when interest rates go up.
"Black gold," or energy, funds are the place to be for Marshall Schield, a markettiming financial adviser in
Denver. "I'd rather hedge against inflation with a commodity where there's a limited supply and strong
demand," he says.
Ed Higgins, a financial adviser in Darien, Conn., won't go near gold funds for another reason: He can't trust
investors to stay put. "The problem with gold-oriented funds is that people pull out at the wrong time," he
says. That hurts investors who opt to stay in long-term because they can get saddled with taxable
distributions if managers are forced to sell winners to cash other investors out.
When the Bank of England announced plans in May to auction 400 tons of its gold reserves, investors
yanked $196.2 million out of gold funds, according to Financial Research Corp., a Boston research firm that
tracks flows of 29 gold funds. Some investors reversed course a month later, putting $74.8 million into gold
funds in June, according to FRC. There is $2.35 billion in gold funds now.

--- Page 13 ---
Page 13 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Clearly, some investors still think of gold funds as an inflation hedge. Recent worries that the Federal
Reserve may raise interest rates again lifted gold funds 1.3% on average last week, according to Lipper.
SoGen Gold led the pack with a 5.81% gain, but the fund is still down 5.38% for the year.
Last week's move into positive territory heartened Caesar Bryan, manager of Gabelli Gold Fund. Mr. Bryan
wasn't happy when the first British gold-reserve auction, held in July, sent the price of gold tumbling.
However, he stayed true to form, keeping the bulk of his portfolio in gold stocks and only 6% in cash. But that
cost him dearly. Gabelli Gold is down 10.95% this year -- much worse than the average 7.2% negative return
of its peers. "The temptation is to diversify the portfolio to base metals, diamonds or even Microsoft Corp., but
I haven't," admits Mr. Bryan. "Anybody who invests in this fund will get gold equities."
Mr. Bryan still thinks there's a role for gold in central-bank reserves. Once the banks decide on an
appropriate level, he expects conditions to improve.
Relief may already be on the way. Two weeks ago, Treasury Secretary Lawrence Summers said the U.S. is
now seeking alternatives to a proposal to sell 10 million ounces of International Monetary Fund gold reserves
to provide debt relief to the world's poorest countries.

Devalue the Yuan?
REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Devalue the Yuan?
643 words
3 August 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A22
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Just as China's leadership was packing for its annual policy retreat at seaside Beidaihe, World Bank Chief
Economist Joseph Stiglitz toured Beijing to stir the waters on devaluing the yuan. While of course proclaiming
that the Bank would give no advice, he said such things as "What is clear is that one of the advantages of a
depreciation, namely stimulation of aggregate demand, may be particularly germane today."
The elders now gathered at Beidaihe may have their hands full discussing the difficulties with Taiwan and the
crackdown on the Falun Dafa sect, but economic policy will be on the agenda as well. And while the official
line remains that China will not devalue, there have been hints out of Beijing that this could change. Mr.
Stiglitz coupled his view that "it is better for countries to have flexible, market-driven exchange rates" with
praise for the thoughtfulness of Chinese leaders with whom he'd met.
The fundamental objection to devaluing any currency at any time is obvious, but it's worth saying again: You
can't get something for nothing simply by altering the unit of exchange. There may be a short-term gain in
export competitiveness, but this is mostly offset by the increased cost of imported inputs. It also creates an
expectation of further devaluations, which discourages foreigners from investing and encourages domestic
savers to stash their wealth abroad. Since the rules of the game are no longer predictable, companies must
invest in expensive hedges against exchange-rate risk.
In the case of the yuan, further, a devaluation would have a major impact on other Asian economies, which in
turn could rebound on China. If China's neighbors felt forced to follow its lead and devalue, the benefits in
terms of export growth could turn out to be nil, while the costs to the Chinese consumer would be
considerable. That wouldn't be much of a stimulus to a slowing economy.
There would be a case for delinking from the dollar if the Federal Reserve, instead of preserving price
stability, were leading the world into deflation. Mr. Stiglitz of course said no such thing. And while China itself
is suffering from falling prices, there are plenty of internal explanations. It is in the middle of a very sensitive
transition to a market economy. State-owned enterprises are being restructured, and unemployment is rising.
So consumers are not spending readily, but putting about 80% of their savings straight into the bank. This
would be fine if China's banking system worked, but some analysts believe that China's biggest banks are
technically insolvent.
Should depositors ever lose confidence in the currency and start withdrawing that money, there could be a
very nasty chain reaction. This may seem very unlikely, but consider that when China last devalued in 1994,
there was a small run on gold jewelry as a hedge in case inflation spiraled out of control. Now consumers
are a lot more jittery and a lot more savvy, and they may try to find ways around China's capital controls to
get their money out of the country.
While Mr. Stiglitz also offered the view that a yuan devaluation would have less impact on the world economy
than it would have a year ago, it's hard to believe that Hong Kong would not be forced to follow suit. Clearly
this could trigger a new round of competitive devaluations in the region, reigniting the whole vicious cycle of
evaporating confidence and capital flight that decimated economies in 1997. That crisis was triggered by
devaluation of the Thai baht, after repeated urging from IMF Director Michel Camdessus. Whatever the
intellectual arguments over exchange rates, clearly devaluation sometimes goes wrong big time. We keep
wondering why international financial officials feel some compulsion to share the blame.

EUROPE
TotalFina Chairman
Proposes Elf Talks
Apart From Chiefs
The chairman of TotalFina, Thierry Desmarest, whose offer of talks to turn a French oil-takeover battle into a
friendly merger with rival Elf Aquitaine has been repeatedly rebuffed by Elf's chief, Philippe Jaffre, is now
proposing that such discussions be held by the companies' senior managers or even board members. Mr.
Desmarest said that TotalFina would attach no preconditions for the talks -- except that it might be better for
the two sides to concentrate on narrowing the differences between their offers for each other rather than
begin by discussing either bid. Mr. Desmarest said he had sent several notes to Mr. Jaffre proposing talks
between them, but received no response. Mr. Jaffre was in the U.S. last week trying to gather shareholder
support for his 50 billion euro ($53.6 billion) counteroffer for TotalFina, following the latter's 42 billion euro,
all-share offer for Elf on July 5. The companies have in common between 40% and 80% of their
shareholders.
Mr. Desmarest said he believes TotalFina shareholders "are backing us by 90%." He said he didn't know
"how shareholders who are common to us speak with Elf management. But I can imagine if they are common
shareholders they don't have two different messages for the two managements."
Pechiney's Sales, Operating Profit Fall
French aluminum company Pechiney said its first-half revenue fell 7.5% from a year earlier to 4.84 billion
euros ($5.19 billion), hit by lower aluminum prices, and operating profit dropped 20% to 279 million euros.
Revenue for the second quarter fell 4.8% to 2.60 billion euros. The company said it anticipates higher
aluminum prices in the second half of 1999 and hopes to post full-year operating profit roughly equivalent to
pro forma operating profit of 631 million euros in 1998.
Viag Confirms Talks With Veba, Others
German conglomerate Viag confirmed it is in informal talks with Veba and other German and international
companies about cooperation in the energy sector. But a Viag official on Friday denied the talks were of a
binding nature or involved a merger. The company was responding to a report Friday in the German business
daily Handelsblatt that Viag and Veba were planning a merger.
Trinity, Mirror to Merge in Britain
The plans of British regional newspaper group Trinity to merge with national newspaper company Mirror
Group shows how much pressure regional publishers face to link up with national brands. The new entity,
Trinity Mirror, will become Britain's biggest newspaper publisher by weekly circulation, in a deal valued at
GBP 1.24 billion ($2.01 billion). The deal, announced Friday, combines Mirror Group's national tabloids, such
as the Mirror, Sunday Mirror and the Scottish Daily Record, with Trinity's regional publications such as the
Liverpool Daily Post and Newcastle Evening Chronicle.
Ukraine Currency Is Under Pressure
Pressure on Ukraine's currency, the hryvnia, has put the country's central bank in a quandary just three
months before Ukraine presidential elections on Oct. 31. The hryvnia has fallen steeply, closing at around

--- Page 16 ---
Page 16 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
4.50 to the dollar in interbank trading Friday, about 15% weaker than 3.92 to the dollar on July 16, and is in
danger of crashing out of its exchange-rate band.
British Airways Considers Boeing 717
British Airways is looking to replace its fleet of short-haul jets with smaller planes, and is considering Boeing's
new 717 jetliner. A British Air spokesman said it is in preliminary talks with Boeing and other plane makers.
Short-haul aircraft in the 100-seat range will probably replace some of the airline's 47 Boeing 757s, which
seat about twice that number.
BRIEFLY:
-- The British Post Office wants to deliver big changes to its two pension funds, mainly by merging them into a
single GBP 16 billion ($25.9 billion) fund by next April. The Post Office says the plan will bestow economies of
scale, better management and cost savings. The funds' recent performance has been disappointing.
-- Lufthansa of Germany, Swissair and British Airways made Poland's short-list for a key investor in
debt-ridden state carrier LOT Polish Airlines, the Polish government said.
-- The European Commission is expected to clear the planned acquisition by Spain's Banco Santander
Central Hispano of a 40% stake in a Portuguese holding company that controls insurer Mundial Confianca
and Banco Pinto & Sotto Mayor, despite Portugal's veto of the deal.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Dai-Ichi Kangyo Bank to Waive Debt
Dai-Ichi Kangyo Bank of Japan said it has decided to waive 22 billion yen ($190.6 million) in loans to ailing
Japanese trading company Kanematsu. The move doesn't alter Dai-Ichi Kangyo's previously announced
earning estimates for the year ending next March, the bank said. Dai-Ichi Kangyo Bank joins Bank of
Tokyo-Mitsubishi and Norinchukin Bank, both of which said last week that they will forgive loans to the trading
company.
Oriental Brewery Wins Jinro-Coors
Oriental Brewery, a joint venture of Belgian brewing giant Interbrew and South Korea's Doosan Group, won
the court auction of ailing Jinro-Coors Brewery for $378.4 million. The auction Friday ended a battle between
Oriental Brewery's owners and Adolph Coors of the U.S. to take over Jinro-Coors, which controls 20% of
Korea's beer market of about $2 billion in annual sales. Oriental Brewery now will begin discussing details of
the takeover with Jinro-Coors's creditor banks and likely will sign an agreement this week, court officials said.
Adolph Coors this month filed for a court injunction against Friday's auction, claiming Oriental Brewery had
violated auction rules, but the court rejected the injunction request. Oriental Brewery was the sole bidder.
Jinro-Coors began in 1992 as a joint venture between Adolph Coors and Jinro, a South Korean
conglomerate. After Jinro went bankrupt in 1997, Adolph Coors sold its stake to Jinro and then tried to take
over the whole brewery, which now runs under court receivership.
Japan Lifts Import Ban on Some Fruit
Responding to a U.S. complaint, Japan lifted restrictions on imports of certain varieties of cherries and
apples. The decision follows a World Trade Organization ruling this year that Japan acted illegally by blocking
the imports. Japan lifted the ban on Lapin and Sweet Heart cherries and on Braeburn, Fuji, Gala, Granny
Smith and Jona Gold apples after a review showed no danger of infestation, a government ministry said
Friday.
BRIEFLY:
-- Diversified miners Broken Hill Proprietary of Australia and Rio Tinto, a resource company listed in London
and Australia, said they have abandoned iron-ore joint-venture talks that would have created the world's
largest iron-ore exporter.
-- The Indonesian government has reached an agreement with Parliament to end state-owned oil and gas
company Pertamina's monopoly, paving the way for an accord on a new oil and gas law.
-- Japan's Finance Ministry said it will suspend Credit Suisse First Boston Securities (Japan) from
underwriting and participating in auctions of new government bonds between Aug. 5 and Sept. 4. Credit
Suisse Group was previously sanctioned by Japan's regulatory agencies for obstructing bank examiners
when they launched a surprise but routine inspection of the bank's Tokyo operations.

Brazil, Argentina Mend Trade Dispute
The presidents of Argentina and Brazil agreed to restore trade relations, ending a dispute that threatened the
stability of South America's Mercosur trade bloc. After a meeting between Argentine President Carlos Menem
and Brazilian President Fernando Henrique Cardoso, the countries' foreign ministers said Friday that
Argentina had agreed to repeal restrictions announced July 23 on imports of certain Brazilian products to
protect its industries. Brazil had responded to the restrictions by breaking off all trade talks with Argentina.
The Argentine restrictions were aimed at reducing a flood of imports from Brazil that were suddenly made
cheaper by the January devaluation of Brazil's currency.
Thomson to Sell Unit to U.K.'s Pearson
Canada's Thomson Corp. said it agreed to sell its Thomson Financial Securities Management unit for $150
million to British publishing and media company Pearson. Thomson Financial is a provider of financial
information and other services. Pearson said the deal will enhance its provision of securities pricing and other
specialist information.
Antigua Court Won't Extradite Banker
An Antiguan court has determined it won't extradite U.S. offshore banker William Cooper to face federal
money-laundering charges in Florida. An Antiguan judge ruled last week that there was no extradition treaty
between Antigua and the U.S. when Mr. Cooper was arrested May 18. The two countries signed an
agreement on July 1. Mr. Cooper was head of American International Bank in the Caribbean island.
BRIEFLY:
-- Mexican construction concern Grupo Tribasa said it has agreed to sell 81.25% of a road project in Chile to
Groupe GTM of France. The company said the transaction will reduce its debt by $50 million. No other
financial details were disclosed.

CORPORATE PERFORMANCE 1999 REVIEW: 2ND QUARTER---Profits BeatForecastsIn Rebound
CORPORATE PERFORMANCE 1999 REVIEW: 2ND QUARTER --- Profits Beat Forecasts In Rebound
By Darren McDermott
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
7,667 words
2 August 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Corporate earnings have come roaring back so fast that it would seem the business environment can't get
much betteryet some signs suggest profits won't weaken anytime soon.
Second-quarter earnings of major U.S. companies blew past analysts' estimates. Setting aside the effect of
cable-TV provider MediaOne Group's extraordinary gain last year of about $24.5 billion, income at the 654
companies that make up the U.S. part of the Dow Jones Global Indexes rose 37% in the second quarter from
a year earlier. MediaOne's gain, from its separation from U S West, skews the year-to-year comparison;
including the gain, net income was up 5%.
Other one-time events, such as writeoffs and gains from the sale of affiliates, and easy comparisons against
year-earlier results weakened by restructuring charges boosted the companies' net income. But even
operating income, which excludes such items, rose 15% from the second quarter of last year, according to
calculations done by First Call/Thomson Financial for The Wall Street Journal.
In the 1999 first quarter, net income was down 6%, while operating income rose 9.6%. Analysts who
wondered early this year if the rebound in first-quarter operating profits was a fluke now know for sure that it
wasn't; business is better for a broad swath of corporate America.
"The bottom line is that business is good and we expect it to continue for a while," says Chuck Hill, director of
research at First Call, which tracks corporate earnings. "We're in a period of accelerating earnings growth that
will peak out in the third quarter, but we still expect earnings to stay strong."
That's because the earnings rebound is tied to the economy: a strong economy at home and a healing
economy abroad. "The sectors that were tied to the domestic economy were strong last year, and they're still
strong this year," adds Brian Rauscher, a strategist at Morgan Stanley Dean Witter.
He and many other analysts see room for more improvement ahead. The sectors that were clobbered last
year by Asian recession and Latin American instability have bottomed out and are starting to recover, Mr.
Rauscher says. Companies that were already surfing the wave of consumer spending, meanwhile, are finding
the ride is just getting better.
Comparisons with last year should prove especially easy in the quarter ahead. Last year's General Motors
strike badly dented earnings in the second and third quarters, making this year stellar by contrast. But the
worst of the fallout from 1998's global financial turmoil hit companies from banks to commodity producers in
the third quarter; that also will make comparisons easier next quarter.
Still, a golden second half isn't a sure bet. For one thing, the economy's direction is uncertain. The Commerce
Department said last week that gross domestic product slowed to growth of 2.3% for the second quarter,
fueling claims by some that a slowdown is imminent. Another possibility, however, is that a fast-growing
economy could be a hindrance to earnings. If the economy grows too fast and inflationary pressures are
reignited, the Federal Reserve Board may repeat its June increase in interest rates. Last week, economic
data showed that labor costs were rising faster than expected, spooking the stock market amid fears that the
Fed will act again at its next policy makers' meeting this month. "There's a trade-off" says Richard Bernstein,
chief quantitative strategist at Merrill Lynch. "You don't get very strong earnings without risking inflationary
pressure."
Investors looking for further gains in the stock market this year had better hope that the economy and
earnings tread a middle path between too-fast and too-slow growth, as Mr. Bernstein believes they will. Stock
prices rose sharply in early July on expectations of stronger earnings reports, but since then have given back
some of those gains as investors have shifted their focus toward interest rates.

--- Page 19 ---
Page 19 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Corporate earnings, which the Journal calculates based on net income, were boosted further by nonrecurring
gains and by comparisons to last year's difficult business environment. There was the General Motors
business surge from last year's strike-induced collapse, and Xerox and Motorola had easy comparisons to
their year-earlier quarters, when they took respective restructuring charges of $1.11 billion and $1.98 billion.
And, in the latest quarter, R.J. Reynolds Tobacco Holdings had a one-time gain of $2.67 billion from the sale
of its international tobacco business to Japan Tobacco.
But most Wall Street analysts track operating income, which they say cuts to the heart of companies'
underlying performance, and the effect of the one-time gains was just icing on an already good earnings
cake. The results for the quarter exceeded most analysts' expectations. Overall operating income for the Dow
Jones Global Index companies beat analysts' expectations by 3.9%. Companies that were struggling with the
effects of global turmoil are seeing a reversal of their fortunes. "The best growth industries are primarily
rebounding from poor year-earlier results," says Joe Abbott, an analyst at IBES International. And the
worst-performing industries produced some of the biggest positive earnings surprises; analysts missed how
quickly things would start to improve for hard-hit industries like energy and basic materials.
Energy companies provided the biggest upside surprise, beating expectations by 6.5%. But a near-doubling
in crude-oil prices since early this year came too late to reverse the woes of oil companies, especially since
they were hit by weaker chemicals prices as well. Net income at energy companies still fell 21% from a year
ago, while operating income fell 25%. Analysts are expecting a strong turnaround in the third quarter as the
full effect of higher oil prices is felt.
Basic-materials companies such as steel makers and paper producers continued to struggle as well, but
earnings fell less than last quarter as global demand began to revive. Basic-materials companies saw net
income fall 12% in the second quarter from a year earlier, a great improvement over the first quarter's 34%
year-on-year drop. Operating income for the sector fell 6.3%, still beating analysts' estimates.
Meanwhile, the forest-products industry posted strong results on robust demand for building products, falling
lumber prices and a recovery in pulp and paper markets. The sector turned in one of its strongest
performances in recent years, amid accelerated housing activity in the U.S. and a modest resurgence of
demand in depressed Asian markets. Weyerhaeuser's profit more than doubled, topping analysts'
expectations, while Boise Cascade's earnings surged more than 28-fold from a year earlier, and Willamette
Industries' profit nearly tripled.
Analysts say the outlook remains strong for building products through the current quarter, perhaps tapering
back afterward as housing construction enters a seasonal slowdown. However, they say, pulp-and-paper
business is poised to begin a stronger rebound next. That market has been largely constrained by the Asian
slowdown.
Many industrial companies saw heavy demand from the strong economy; companies involved in
transportation, factory equipment, building materials and industrial services all performed strongly in the
quarter. Only the heavy-equipment makers, which include companies like Case and Deere that are highly
dependent on agriculture, have seen little relief from their suffering. Prices for commodity grains remain low,
hurting farmers' ability to buy new machines. And while Asia is showing signs of renewed growth, Latin
America's troubles have deepened since the year-ago quarter. Last month, Case noted that the market for
farm equipment is still getting worse.
Industries tied to the booming domestic economy, however -- particularly to the quarter's free-spending
consumer -- fared well. Many makers of goods whose sales are closely tied to the economy, called consumer
cyclicals, saw double-digit growth over last year's profits, which in turn represented double-digit gains over
the previous year. The effect of MediaOne's extraordinary gain last year did throw off the net-income figure
for the consumer-cyclicals sector, but operating income for the sector surged 28%, the second-best
performance after technology. And net income for nearly every industry in the sector charged ahead. Homes
and everything that goes in them, from furnishings and clothes to the car in the driveway, were snapped up.
Home builders delivered superb earnings growth, with many companies reporting net-income growth of more
than 50%. The housing industry, boosted by low interest rates and strong consumer spending, has
exceeded nearly every analyst's expectations.
Consumers bought more cars to park outside their new homes. Among auto companies, the highlight was
General Motors' rebound from last year's paltry strike-affected profits; net income jumped 393% to $1.9 billion
($184 million of that was attributable to the sale of the company's share of Delphi Automotive). Auto makers
may struggle to keep profit margins up on key vehicles as competition heats up, particularly for popular
pickup trucks and sport-utility vehicles.

--- Page 20 ---
Page 20 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Companies in retail, of all industries perhaps the most closely tied to the consumer, are celebrating as well.
Most retailers won't report second-quarter earnings until later this month, but positive comments from several
have prompted some analysts to raise their forecasts.
Technology companies, too, benefited from the stronger-than-expected economy, although other factors --
including an easy comparison to last year, when the industry was hit by a slowdown in personal-computer
sales and inventory buildup -- also had an effect. Overall, net income at technology-sector companies was up
a strong 166% from a year ago. Some of that was from one-time gains at a handful of companies.
But underlying growth was strong as well. Operating income rose 35%, the best of any sector and above
analysts' expectations. Home users and businesses alike continued to invest heavily in technology ranging
from personal computers to software to communications equipment. The Internet remained a driving force in
the accelerating cycle of equipment upgrading, as producers and consumers of online content and
technology push to make sending and receiving data a speedier process. Overwhelming demand for cellular
phones and data services drove strong results at the nation's phone companies.

Concerns over a slowdown in technology investment as companies completed preparations for the year-2000
computer glitch proved overblown, at least so far. Big computer makers had a banner quarter, racking up
large gains in revenue and profit virtually across the board, as cheaper PCs and the popularity of the Internet
drove sales of computer hardware. International Business Machines forged ahead with a 65% increase in net
income and a 16% rise in revenue, fueled largely by sales of server computers and Internet-related services.
Sun Microsystems, a dominant presence in the Internet-server market, followed suit with a 22% increase in
revenue and a 45% rise in net income. The world's two largest direct sellers of PCs -- Dell Computer and
Gateway Computer -- likewise saw sales and profits boom. Two turnaround stories helped round out the
quarter, as both Apple Computer and Silicon Graphics reported robust profits.
The main exception to the trend was Compaq, which reported a loss of $184 million, or 10 cents a share, and
laid out plans to slash operating expenses as it struggles with lower-than-expected computer sales. The
world's largest personal-computer maker also said it will record a $700 million to $900 million restructuring
charge in the third quarter to cover plant closings and the elimination of as many as 11% of the company's
employees.
Sales of semiconductors, critical in all those PCs and in countless other gadgets, soared as well, though a
ruthless pricing war, particularly in the low end of the market, cut into profits for many companies. Intel
missed its earnings target slightly because of price cutting in the PC-microprocessor market, but managed to
take back several points of market share in the low-end PC-microprocessor business. The chip giant came
out the victor since its profit was only slightly lower than expectations and sales of high-end server and
workstation chips offset the price-cutting on the low end. The company also lifted spirits when it predicted that
it expects a strong second half as healthy seasonal consumer purchasing kicks in.
Advanced Micro Devices posted a record operating loss as its average chip prices sank precipitously in the
second quarter and the company failed to win back customers it lost in the first. A special one-time gain from
the sale of its Vantis programmable-logic-chip division helped the company report a net gain in the quarter.
Other chip makers fared well, thanks to an ongoing recovery in the chip industry after a three-year downturn.
Strong growth in cellular phones and video games helped companies such as LSI Logic, which is heavily
dependent on communications and consumer chip markets.
The exception to the general recovery was memory chips, with Micron Technology posting a
wider-than-expected loss on price-cutting in dynamic-access memory, which is used to store data in personal
computers.

--- Page 21 ---
Page 21 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The largest sector by income of both the Dow Jones and S&P 500 universes, financial companies, was also
one of the best-performing. The economic picture at home and abroad has been ideal for banks and
brokerages alike. On Wall Street, activity in trading, stock and bond underwritings, and mergers and
acquisitions has been going full-bore. And the international marketplace for trading as well as for loans is
looking more hospitable. Financial companies will benefit further next quarter, when their results will be
compared with the huge losses and write-offs many were forced to take a year ago as international financial
turmoil descended.

The steel industry saw glimmerings of an upturn, such as stronger pricing for some products, but it has a long
way to go to bounce back from the onslaught of cheap imports that has cut deeply into profits during the past
year. Most steelmakers posted losses for the second quarter, though much narrower than the first quarter's.
During the past year, the industry has been hurt by bloated inventories and falling prices. In the first six
months of this year, prices for all steel products were down 7% from a year earlier. But the imported-steel
level is dropping, and steelmakers believe the worst of the present crisis is behind them.
U.S. aluminum producers are also weathering tough times as they continue to be stung by excess capacity
and sluggish demand in some parts of the world. Aluminum inventories hit a two-year high at the end of
March, and prices of aluminum ingots on the London Metal Exchange, an important profitability determinant,
have remained low. Industry leader Alcoa has fared better than most because of continued cost-cutting and
acquisitions.
A continued glut of copper and gold kept prices low and profits meager for mining companies. Stillwater
Mining and Freeport-McMoRan Copper & Gold, as well as Asarco and Cyprus Amax Minerals, which recently
agreed to merge, reported lower-than-expected earnings because of low prices.
Analysts are hopeful that prospects for copper-mining companies will improve, on recent or planned mine and
smelter closures. Phelps Dodge, Australia's Broken Hill Proprietary, Cominco and Rio Algom all cut
production, helping copper prices climb slightly in recent weeks. However, gold prices are expected to
continue to suffer.
Chemical-company earnings continued to bear the scars of an industry downturn and weakening prices, with
the plunge in global economies, slowing demand and oversupplies. A spike in oil prices led to higher
feedstock costs and even slimmer profit margins. Union Carbide and Eastman Chemical profits declined
sharply from a year earlier. Lyondell Chemical reported a worse-than-expected loss. Rohm & Haas's loss
included $125 million in charges in its acquisition of Morton International. Dow Chemical was a rare bright
spot, beating analyst estimates with a profit decline of only 3%.
The outlook for the rest of the year is slightly more positive. While analysts expect the industry to linger at the
bottom well into 2000, rising prices should give short-term relief. Most companies are reporting improving
Asian sales. Year-earlier comparisons should turn positive in the third quarter as rising profits contrast with
the beginning of the downturn last year.

A jump in crude-oil prices and unusually high gasoline prices on the West Coast gave a bit of a boost to what
would have been a disappointing second quarter for some of the nation's biggest energy companies. But the
real improvement should show up in the third quarter.
The price of crude oil jumped to nearly $20 a barrel by the end of the second quarter, from a low near $10
early this year. But the increase came too late to make a big difference.

--- Page 22 ---
Page 22 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Exxon and Mobil felt the impact of low chemicals prices. Skinny refining margins in Europe also hurt
downstream results at Exxon, Mobil and Texaco. However, the companies that sell gasoline in California got
a boost from a sharp increase in prices following refinery fires and other outages that briefly led to tight
supplies. Atlantic Richfield, in particular, saw its refining profits more than double from a year ago.
Higher natural-gas prices and regulated gas-transmission margins helped pipeline companies sustain
relatively strong earnings. But the real improvement should show up in the second half, aided by price spikes
caused by hot July weather.
Pipeline companies have been focusing on opportunities at the end of the pipeline rather than the beginning.
Many in the industry are expanding their power-generation assets to make better use of their pipelines, while
some are reducing their spending on exploration and production of new natural-gas reserves. Enron
announced during the quarter that it will reduce its stake in its oil and gas unit to 10% from 54%. El Paso
Energy announced it would forgo significant new investment in exploration and production. But Coastal,
following a different strategy, said it raised its exploration and production spending budget by about 49% for
1999.
El Paso recorded a $30 million pretax gain from a rate settlement and $126 million in pretax charges related
to its acquisition of Sonat, which has been approved by shareholders but is still awaiting regulatory clearance.

Railroads posted mixed results in the second quarter. Union Pacific, the nation's largest railroad, which began
rebounding a year ago from its service crisis, said it continued to progress in boosting revenue and controlling
costs. But Burlington Northern Santa Fe was hurt by declines in coal and other freight shipments. Norfolk
Southern and CSX suffered from start-up glitches and freight congestion in their carve-up of Conrail, leading
to extra costs. CSX reported continuing weakness in coal shipments and revenue.
Truck companies benefited from the strong economy, stable freight rates and tight cost controls. Roadway
Express said tonnage improved and new expedited truck services added to revenue. Landstar credited its
continuing safety program for reduced insurance and claims costs. For CNF Transportation's air-freight unit,
international revenue grew for the first time in a year; growth was particularly strong in Latin America and
Europe. Werner Enterprises added 275 trucks to keep up with freight growth and said it expects solid freight
demand for the rest of the year. Earnings growth for truck companies, as a group, is expected to continue in
the third quarter. Although rising fuel prices may slow gains at some companies, the recent closure of two
regional truck concerns may tighten capacity in some segments of the industry and help support increased
freight rates, trucking executives said.
Air-freight couriers were hurt by slowing growth at home, some of it attributable to increased use of electronic
mail. Net income at Airborne Freight fell 20%, while the number of shipments it delivered in the U.S. declined
0.7%. Growth in standard overnight letters and deferred two-day deliveries was almost flat for FDX's FedEx
unit. But United Parcel Service of America posted a 3.5% increase in domestic volume, triggering a healthy
17.5% increase in domestic profit.
Overseas, there were increasing signs of strength. International operating profit nearly tripled at UPS, fueled
by a 29% increase in express volume in Asia. FedEx saw robust 10% growth in its most expensive
international priority-delivery service, pushing overseas profit up strongly to $58 million.
The trucking industry's boom helped drive earnings higher for several transportation-equipment companies.
How long the cyclical industry can stay hot is unclear, but truck and engine makers have been running so fast
for so long that some suppliers are hard-pressed to provide enough components. Paccar, maker of Kenworth
and Peterbilt trucks, had its most profitable quarter ever. Navistar's earnings remained strong, despite heavy
capital outlays to bolster the engine maker's production capabilities. Cummins, hurt by weak Asian demand,
had been expected to post a slight earnings drop, but the diesel-engine maker reported a profit upturn on cost
cutting and continued strength in sales to truck makers.
At railcar manufacturer Trinity, cost-cutting efforts contributed to higher operating profit, but the company
reduced production schedules on a drop in orders from a year earlier.

--- Page 23 ---
Page 23 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Heavy-equipment makers experienced a punishing earnings decline. The market for agricultural equipment
has plunged because farm profits have been badly hurt by low commodity-grain prices. And while Asia's
economy appears to be tentatively rebounding, Latin America's economic travails have deepened. Case,
which agreed in mid-May to a $4.2 billion buyout bid from Europe's New Holland, said the farm-equipment
market "continues to decline," and said it will make more production cuts.
Deere, the nation's leading ag-equipment maker, also suffers from order drought and gives a gloomy outlook
for the rest of the year. For Caterpillar, North America's building boom has kept domestic demand for
construction gear high, but weaker prices for commodities such as copper, petroleum and grains softened
sales in the mining, drilling and agricultural sectors.

The two major U.S. auto makers are looking to extend their ride on the red-hot North American market into
the third quarter after reporting record results for the second. But rising competition already is beginning to
put downward pressure on prices for Detroit's most profitable models, pickup trucks and sport-utility vehicles.
Preserving the North American profit engine is critical to Ford and General Motors, because results in the rest
of the world have been disappointing.
For both auto makers, the second quarter brought milestones. GM completed the spinoff of its Delphi
Automotive Systems parts unit in May, ending a decades-long tradition of making many of its components
in-house. Ford, meanwhile, acquired Volvo Cars of Sweden, extending its quest into the luxury-car market.
DaimlerChrysler reported lackluster earnings that came in well below U.S. analysts' consensus estimate. The
company also delivered an unexpectedly tepid profit forecast for the rest of the year. Though revenue for the
quarter rose 10.2%, to $38.48 billion from $34.92 billion a year earlier, profits were hurt by higher incentive
spending designed to boost sales of the former Chrysler's vehicles in an increasingly competitive North
American market.
Home builders nailed another quarter of solid earnings growth. The demand for dwellings continued to
outstrip the nation's housing supply, filling order books beyond capacity and raising competition for
construction materials and labor.
Perhaps more than any other sector, home builders are pushed and pulled by the fortunes of the broader
economy. But their biggest conundrum may be their valuation on Wall Street. Every home builder surveyed
for the second quarter posted record net income that was at least 50% higher than the year-earlier period, but
the sector's stocks remain banished to the basement of investor indifference.
Higher prices and lower supplies of gypsum wallboard and fiberglass insulation have dogged some builders,
as did a short supply of manual labor, though in most cases any additional costs were covered by rising
real-estate prices.
Overflowing order books indicate current market conditions will support the sector at least through the end of
the year, sending balance sheets for many builders into record territory. Again.
Though many merchants have yet to report earnings, retailers are continuing to ring up strong sales for the
fiscal second quarter, analysts say. Wall Street expected retailers to report higher earnings than they did a
year earlier because of the robust economy. The combination of stronger-than-expected sales in recent
months and improved operating margins led some analysts to raise their earnings forecasts for companies
such as Federated Department Stores, May Department Stores and Dayton Hudson.
Some economic indicators are showing signs of slowing, though, suggesting to analysts that the industry may
be starting to lose steam. But, some analysts say, the second half of the calendar year is likely to get an
unusual boost. Retailers expect a spurt in spending, due both to consumers who want to ring in the New Year
in style and to those who plan to hoard goods in preparation for any possible year-2000 problems.
Casual-dining restaurant-chain operators posted hearty results, as patronage reflected the ongoing economic
boom. Food and other commodity costs remained stable, although labor costs crept up. Darden Restaurants,
capping its best year yet as an independent company, said its Olive Garden chain posted a 9.1% same-store
sales gain for the quarter. Outback Steakhouse's sales at units open at least a year jumped 6%.

--- Page 24 ---
Page 24 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Fast-food leader McDonald's got a big domestic boost from its toy come-ons, but Tricon Global Restaurants
admitted that its reliance on the "Star Wars" prequel was a mistake, as same-store sales stagnated at its KFC
and Taco Bell units. Hardee's operator CKE Restaurants was hurt by interest costs from recent acquisitions.
Industry analyst Paul Westra of Credit Suisse First Boston predicts a continuing "bull cycle" domestically as
profit margins remain favorable, labor inflation continues to be moderate and fewer new restaurants are built,
leading to a better supply/demand ratio for operators.
Casinos reported strong earnings, reflecting a booming gambling business, while hotel results softened
slightly. Casinos such as Harrah's Entertainment, Memphis, Tenn., and Station Casinos in Las Vegas
reported improved revenues and earnings around the country. In Las Vegas, where competition has been
fierce amid new megacasino openings, most operators reported increases in occupancy and room prices.
Analysts speculated the new casinos drew new and younger visitors. One exception to the good news was
Mirage Resorts, which warned investors it won't meet expectations. It said its new Beau Rivage casino in
Biloxi, Miss., isn't getting the business expected.
Nationwide, as supply rises slightly faster than demand, many hotels report softening room rates after years
of record increases. Nevertheless, analysts from PricewaterhouseCoopers in New York predicted continued
profit increases for the rest of the year in most U.S. markets.
Toy and video-game makers posted mixed results for the quarter, reflecting consumer shifts in the toy
industry and weak foreign markets.
The changing consumer landscape and an inventory-reduction program by retailers prompted Mattel to
reduce staff by 13% and restructure the firm. The company's results were also weakened by a $345 million
pretax charge primarily related to its acquisition of Learning Co., a children's software firm. But toys tied to the
new Star Wars film helped Hasbro post better-than-expected profit and sales.
Both toy and video-game makers expect conditions to improve in the second half of the year, and most
expect to meet their full-year forecasts. A number of new video and personal-computer game introductions
are expected to help boost European sales.
Cable-television companies are expected to continue to show good gains in the second quarter, buoyed by
strong customer demand for their ever-expanding packages of services. Most cable operators haven't yet
reported second-quarter numbers. But analysts are looking for strong cash flow, a key indicator of a cable
company's health, and aggressive additions of customers for digital services.
During the quarter, AT&T set plans to buy MediaOne Group, further heating up the merger-mad industry. The
prices that buyers are willing to pay for cable subscribers, meantime, are continuing to skyrocket. Cox
recently agreed to pay an eye-popping $5,100 a subscriber, more than three times the $1,500 a head that
was commonplace just a few years ago.
Meanwhile, broadcasting-company profits are expected to remain robust in the second quarter, thanks
primarily to gains in the radio business, which continues to perform strongly. Analysts say radio-company
profits could be up nearly as much as in the first quarter, when the sector -- led by Chancellor Media, Infinity
Broadcasting and Heftel Broadcasting -- was up 35% in after-tax cash flow. The television side of the industry
will be less robust, with the television-station business, including Sinclair Broadcasting and Young
Broadcasting, expected to be flat.
The nation's top publishers posted better-than-expected results, aided by strength in newspaper advertising
and low newsprint prices. Going into the third quarter, publishers expect profit to continue to rise from a year
earlier, despite a possible increase in newsprint prices this fall and continued softness in advertising for the
companies' broadcast operations.
Gannett benefited from strong advertising demand at its newspapers, as did Dow Jones, publisher of The
Wall Street Journal. E.W. Scripps reported growth in its cable division and strong demand for newspaper
advertising. New York Times net was buoyed by strong newspaper advertising and lower newsprint prices.
Times Mirror, publisher of the Los Angeles Times, benefited from improvements at its flagship newspaper,
where after five consecutive quarters of year-over-year declines in operating profit it posted an increase.
Strong cable performance and lower newsprint costs helped offset increased Internet spending at
Washington Post.
Airlines had some growing pains. After boosting capacity in the U.S. about 5%, and flights to Europe even
more, airlines suffered weaker pricing as they scrambled to fill empty seats. Earnings for the 10 major carriers
were down a collective 14% compared with last year. Revenue for the industry as a whole grew by less than
4%.

--- Page 25 ---
Page 25 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Still, the industry fared a bit better than the predictions of analysts, who had forecast an earnings decline of
about 16% for the major carriers. Analysts pointed to several carriers with operations on the West Coast as
those that rose above the clouds. Southwest Airlines posted the biggest increase in profit for the industry,
with an 18% gain in net income. West Coast rivals Alaska Airlines and America West also posted earnings
gains.
