Ongoing restructuring in an industry beset by slow top-line growth hurt several large food manufacturers in
the second quarter. H.J. Heinz incurred a loss because of more restructuring charges, and said pricing
pressures pinched results. ConAgra's earnings were whacked by the first of several restructuring charges.
But its meat business showed a recovery on higher retail prices. Sales of higher-margin branded meats
boosted Hormel Foods' results.
Quaker Oats' profits soared, partly because of restructuring charges a year ago. General Mills posted a 3%
sales increase, among the best in the industry. Nabisco Holdings, which incurred a big restructuring charge a
year ago, reported lower revenue in its latest period, reflecting the sale of several businesses.
Still, many results exceeded expectations. "There was finally a glimmer of light among the packaged-food
companies after more than a year" of bleak reports, said Prudential Securities analyst John McMillin, who
looks for continued improvement in the third quarter.
Major soft-drink companies posted mixed earnings, with Coca-Cola hurt by continuing economic softness in
some overseas markets and a huge product recall in Europe. Though PepsiCo reported strong earnings, they
were mostly fueled by robust results from its snack businesses and one-time gains from the sale of bottling
interests; its soft-drink business reported only modest operating-profit growth.
In the U.S., consumers continued to balk at price increases resulting from a campaign by soft-drink bottlers to
boost profitability. Coke posted a 1% drop in case volume. PepsiCo, helped by a popular "Star Wars"
promotional campaign, recorded a respectable 3% volume increase. Soft-drink prices have grown 2.7% since
the beginning of the year in U.S. supermarkets, discount chains and drugstores, according to analysts. Sales
were sluggish internationally, with Coke's world-wide case volume declining 2%.
Analysts expect earnings for both Coke and Pepsi to benefit from easier comparisons in the third quarter.
Coke's recovery will depend partly on how quickly it rebuilds its brands in Europe after the product scare,
when people in Belgium and France blamed Coke products for symptoms such as headache and nausea.
Managed-care companies, in turmoil for years, achieved a modest rebound after ending a protracted price
war and raising employers' premiums 6% to 9%. But the industry still faces big challenges to keeping pace
with galloping medical costs. Aetna posted a 15% decline in net income, but operating earnings rose 12%,
slightly ahead of analysts' estimates. WellPoint Health Networks' net income rose 15% on membership
growth. Oxford Health Plans reported a narrower-than-expected loss and reiterated projections that it would
return to profitability in the second half. But HMOs must contend with price increases from newly aggressive
hospitals and physicians and with the continuing problem of rising drug costs.
In hospitals, many big players continued to suffer from Medicare cuts, low admissions and billing problems
with managed-care companies. Tenet Healthcare reported a fiscal fourth-quarter loss of $137 million, which
included $363 million in pretax charges. Columbia/HCA Healthcare posted a 36% increase in net income,
partly on improved hospital performance.
Drug-company earnings rose, generally meeting or slightly exceeding analysts' expectations. The gains were
due to healthy sales of relatively new drugs for ailments including arthritis, allergies and diabetes. Most drug
companies reported double-digit net-income gains, with Warner-Lambert profit soaring on sales gains for its
cholesterol-lowering drug Lipitor. American Home Products was a significant exception, with a 24% decline in
profit, reflecting in part a one-time charge of $53 million related to a suspension of shipments of rotavirus
vaccine RotaShield as well as poor performance of the company's agricultural unit.
Analysts expect most drug companies to continue to post relatively strong earnings and sales gains for the
next few quarters, until a series of patent expirations starts cutting into sales in 2000 and 2001. Investors will
be particularly watching Merck, which has several drugs that will lose patent protection in this period.

--- Page 26 ---
Page 26 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Excluding numerous one-time items, the cigarette makers' underlying profits were once again hurt by weak
earnings abroad and settlement-driven cigarette-price increases in the U.S. Philip Morris reported a 17%
increase in second-quarter earnings. But excluding one-time items taken this and last year for litigation
settlements and subsequent downsizing efforts, earnings would have risen just 3% to $2.06 billion. While
operating income in the U.S. tobacco unit fell nearly 4% to $1.2 billion, analysts pointed out that the company
managed to increase market share and predicted improved earnings growth in the year ahead.
R.J. Reynolds Tobacco Holdings reported a large profit, reflecting a $2.67 billion gain on the sale of its
international tobacco business. But excluding that gain and various spinoff- and settlement-related charges,
Reynolds's earnings would have dropped 35% to $98 million. Reynolds far surpassed analysts' expectations.

The continuing expansion in the personal-computer market in both homes and businesses was sufficient to
generate healthy sales and profit at most software companies in the second quarter. The results confounded
the predictions of some skeptics, who had said the industry would tank owing to a year-2000-related
slowdown.
Microsoft enjoyed booming sales in all its product areas as well as profit that was in front of analysts'
expectations. The quarter also saw a rebound for Oracle, the database-software maker whose slowing sales
in the first quarter spooked investors. Another winner in the quarter was Novell, whose high-powered
directory products are finding buyers among business users. Adobe Systems, too, saw a profit increase
based on strong sales of its graphics products.
Aerospace and defense companies turned in mixed results, with Boeing's recovery resulting in sharply
increased earnings while Lockheed Martin's problems led to a $41 million loss. Boeing exceeded analysts'
expectations for the quarter and boldly projected higher profit for this year and next. Meanwhile, Lockheed
turned in the previously disclosed loss as it struggled to combat problems in its military-aircraft and
space-related businesses. Even so, Lockheed is outshining Boeing in one area -- fighter jets. Lockheed's
F-16 twice beat out Boeing's F-15 in recent months, winning large international orders from Greece and
Israel. Those losses led Boeing to eliminate 2,000 jobs at its St. Louis facility, where its F-15s are built, and
will cause a minimum one-year break in production for that aircraft.
Biotechnology companies had an exceptionally strong quarter. Most companies beat the Street's
expectations, as sales of established pharmaceuticals continued to grow and several new products got off to
a running start. Amgen, benefiting from robust growth in its Epogen and Neupogen products, beat estimates.
Biogen also pleasantly surprised analysts with earnings bolstered by a 68% increase in sales of Avonex, its
multiple-sclerosis drug. Genentech, now marketing two new breakthrough cancer drugs, was an upside
surprise as well. Immunex, with a brand-new arthritis drug, met expectations.
Earnings are expected to continue strong at most companies for the rest of the year, but the bottom line may
be affected at some by the high cost of developing new drugs. Immunex, for example, disappointed some
investors by announcing it plans to increase its research budget.

The benign economic and trading environment drove profit higher at many big banks, but the level of
bad-loan write-offs jumped at some smaller, regional banks in a worrying sign that the overall quality of bank
loans could be slipping. Citigroup and Bank of America, the nation's two largest banks, showed signs of
overcoming merger-related problems. Citigroup posted a 9.3% rise in net income; Bank of America's net fell
because of special charges, but its operating profit grew 1.9% and revenue held steady despite
merger-related staff cutbacks.

--- Page 27 ---
Page 27 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
U.S. Bancorp and Wachovia were among those that saw jumps in their write-offs for bad loans compared with
a year earlier. And, for the first time, banks began to caution that profit toward the end of the year could be
hurt by anxiety over the conversion of computers to handle the date change to the year 2000 and the
transition's attendant complications.
A continuing soft market, a pickup in weather-related losses and other woes made the quarter a challenge for
companies involved in property-casualty insurance, causing many to fall short of Wall Street's expectations.
Price wars continued among companies offering standard commercial insurance, with many retreating from
the most-competitive lines. American International Group and others said they refused to renew hundreds of
millions of dollars of underpriced business, sometimes at the expense of premium growth. Some opted to
focus on more-profitable specialty-commercial lines such as environmental cleanup, liability insurance for
executives and casualty losses exceeding certain high limits.
After a lengthy stretch of fairly robust profit growth, auto insurers also had a tough quarter, thanks to
nationwide auto-insurance rate declines and bad weather. Progressive, in the midst of an advertising push,
showed strong premium growth but somewhat eroding profitability, while Allstate showed sluggish premium
growth and slightly higher claims costs. Meanwhile, life insurers such as Hartford Life continued to benefit
from the popularity of variable annuities and other investment-related products, even as many saw traditional
life-insurance sales drop off. Annuity-related assets at many companies hit record levels, buoyed by the
robust stock market. Many concerns, flush with extra cash and a dearth of affordable high-quality companies
to buy, have been buying back shares.
Wall Street brokerage houses posted better-than-expected earnings, thanks in large part to continued
strength in stock and bond underwritings, as well as merger-and-acquisition activity. Joan Solotar, an analyst
at Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette, says she expects the trend to continue into the third quarter, thanks to a
relatively stable economy and brokerage firms' recent successes in expanding business in Europe. One
caveat: potential investor unease because of year-2000 fears.
Several firms posted record earnings, among them Merrill Lynch, Lehman Brothers Holdings and Bear
Stearns. At Merrill, new money flowing into the firm's U.S. private-client brokerage system exceeded $30
billion, a significant increase from the $9 billion of net inflows during the first quarter, Ms. Solotar says.
Lehman and Bear Stearns showed strength in investment-banking and trading units.
UTILITIES

The second quarter is often slow for electric utilities, and this year mild weather further damped earnings at a
number of companies, including American Electric Power and Edison International. But recent soaring
temperatures in much of the East and Midwest are putting many utilities into overdrive to meet demand in the
third quarter. At the same time, many utilities have been building up the unregulated portion of their
businesses, including big power-trading operations in the nascent wholesale market for electricity. Increased
volatility in this market proved a boon to companies with well-established trading operations, including Enron.
Nuclear power-plant operations continued to buoy several companies, such as Unicom, whose earnings
nearly doubled.
Most local telecommunications carriers met or exceeded Wall Street expectations as demand for the
industry's fastest-growing segments -- cellular phones and high-speed-data services -- skyrocketed. All of the
Baby Bells saw double-digit revenue gains from their data businesses. Telecom giants AT&T and MCI
WorldCom also reported healthy gains in revenue. AT&T beat analysts' expectations for growth in its booming
wireless business as the company expands its test of offering local-phone service over cable lines.
Meanwhile, MCI WorldCom enjoyed continuing high growth in data and Internet traffic and said it would meet
cost-cut targets from its acquisition of the former MCI Communications.
Sprint, meanwhile, saw losses related to its troubled global joint venture with Deutsche Telekom and France
Telecom widen. Still, the third-largest long-distance company benefited from higher revenue from data
services and its cell-phone unit, Sprint PCS.

General Electric can thank the strong U.S. economy, continued globalization and cost cutting for its 15%
increase in net income. Most of GE's long-cycle industrial businesses, including aircraft engines and power
systems, showed strong growth. Businesses with shorter cycles, such as GE's plastics unit, have lagged
slightly, while the appliance unit was the only one to see profit drop, suffering in part from shaky markets in
South America.
GE Capital, the financial-services arm, which contributes about 40% of earnings, said profit rose 17% to
$1.09 billion, on continued growth in Europe and Asia. Across all GE units, revenue from Asia rose 26%,
while revenue from Europe increased 11%. Most analysts expect GE to continue gains of at least 15% for the
rest of the year.

Strained Peace:Gerber Baby Food,Grilled by Greenpeace,Plans Swift Overhaul---Gene-Modified Corn and SoyWill Go, Although FirmFeels Sure Th...
Strained Peace: Gerber Baby Food, Grilled by Greenpeace, Plans Swift Overhaul --- Gene-Modified
Corn and Soy Will Go, Although Firm Feels Sure They Are Safe --- Heinz Takes Action, Too
By Lucette Lagnado
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
2,572 words
30 July 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The letter scrolling out of a fax machine at the Gerber baby-food company in Michigan May 28 was just one
of many arriving that day and didn't even name the person it was meant for, but it sure got attention. Within
days, it had found its way to Gerber's parent company in Switzerland, Novartis AG, and come to the attention
of its CEO. There, executives soon were taking steps to overhaul a decades-old product that generates $1
billion in annual sales.
The letter came from Charles Margulis, a New York man who addressed it simply "to the CEO" because he
didn't know the chief executive's name. The return address was his small apartment on the Upper West Side
of Manhattan. But the letter also carried the logo of his employer, Greenpeace, the activist European
environmental group.
"As you know, there is growing concern around the world about genetically engineered food," it said.
Greenpeace is "concerned that the release of genetically engineered organisms into the environment and
food supply may have irreversible consequences." Does Gerber use genetically engineered products in its
baby food, the letter wanted to know. If so, which products? And "what steps have you taken (if any) to
ensure you are not using" genetically modified ingredients?
Mr. Margulis asked for a reply within five business days.
These are tense times for U.S. food and agricultural industries: European opposition to importing corn and
soybeans grown from genetically modified seeds, or beef from hormone-fed cattle, has led to an ugly trade
dispute and taken a big bite out of U.S. agricultural exports. American consumers, however, have so far
greeted the hubbub roiling Europe with a big yawn. The Food and Drug Administration says genetically
modified foods "are as safe as other foods in the grocery store."
But the concern at Novartis is that all this could turn on a dime -- that because of Greenpeace's efforts,
European worries about the safety of genetically modified crops could leap across the Atlantic and damage
Gerber, the largest baby-food producer in the U.S. Novartis officials had been hoping Gerber was immune to
the anti-bioengineered-food passions that have whipped through Europe in recent years. The letter from Mr.
Margulis made them stop hoping and start planning.
The company firmly believes that the bioengineered foods are safe to consume. Indeed, another unit of
Novartis sells genetically altered crop seeds with which to produce such foods. But this summer, Novartis is
moving rapidly to make preemptive changes at Gerber: At some cost and considerable inconvenience,
Gerber is dropping some of its existing corn and soybean suppliers in favor of ones that can produce crops
that aren't genetically altered. That is to say, it will no longer buy corn grown from seeds modified so that the
plants are resistant to corn borers, or soybeans from seeds altered so the crop can tolerate being sprayed
with a potent weedkiller.
It is an issue that is suddenly confronting all the U.S. baby-food producers. H.J. Heinz Co., the maker of the
Earth's Best line, says it has just decided that this line and all other baby food it produces will be
manufactured without using genetically modified crops. A private manufacturer in Poway, Calif., called
Healthy Times Natural Foods has switched from Canola oil, which sometimes is genetically altered, to
safflower oil after facing questions from Greenpeace. As for BeechNut Nutrition Corp., it says it probably
doesn't have much of a problem with this because it uses no soy and little corn.
Gerber, going even further than what Greenpeace demands, plans to use corn flour and soy flour that are
"organic" -- that is, the crops not only aren't genetically altered, but they also were grown without the use of
any insecticides or herbicides. Then, if the technicalities can be worked out, it plans to change ingredient
labels on certain baby-food boxes and jars to include the word "organic." The company will still use some

--- Page 44 ---
Page 44 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
corn that isn't organic, but it will be corn that hasn't been genetically altered. Gerber, however, is cautious
about offering any guarantees that its products can be made totally free of such ingredients.
"I want our mothers to be comfortable," says Al Piergallini, president and chief executive officer of Novartis's
U.S. consumer health operation, which oversees Gerber. "I have got to listen to my customers. So, if there is
an issue, or even an inkling of an issue, I am going to make amends. We have to act pre-emptively."
The label issue is trickier, first because of the difficulty of avoiding trace amounts of genetically modified foods
in the U.S., and second because the FDA has strict rules about what a food producer can claim about its
product on its label. Yet one of Greenpeace's central demands is that Gerber and other baby-food makers
label all their products to say whether they contain genetically modified ingredients. To sort out this ticklish
issue, Gerber is assembling an advisory panel of outside experts from several U.S. environmental and
consumer groups.
The whole undertaking is a dicey matter, because by shunning ingredients it has used for years, Gerber risks
confusing or frightening its core customers, as well as appearing to endorse food fears that the company itself
proclaims to be invalid. And who is to say more Greenpeace demands won't follow if this one is met? In
Europe, the organization has recently started demanding that even dog food be free of genetically modified
organisms. But Gerber officials want to get out in front of the competition on the issue.
And they can't take a chance, says Gerber's vice president for research, Jan Relford. "The parents trust us; if
they don't trust us, we are out of business," he says. So he supports the supplier changes even though he
says the scientific evidence so far supporting the safety of genetically altered crops is "2,000-to-nothing."
Although the FDA says it "has no information of any health effects with foods derived from genetic
engineering," Greenpeace's point is that their long-term effects -- on health and the environment -- simply
aren't known. The organization cites various scientists and institutions, including the British Medical
Association, which has publicly expressed concern about bioengineered foods. "Some of the effects may be
subtle," says Martha Herbert, a pediatric neurologist in Boston who supports Greenpeace's campaign. "The
problem with studying this is that the effects may be long-term."
In the U.S., Greenpeace is by no means the potent force it is in Europe. There, armies of staffers and
volunteers can target large corporations in a flash, hooking up with numerous well-connected political allies to
form what some regard as an environmental fear brigade.
In the U.S., Greenpeace has only several dozen staffers and is typically on the fringe of political discourse; its
reputation was grist for an episode of the TV comedy "Seinfeld" a few years ago. Greenpeace made little
headway in a previous effort to call attention to genetically modified crops, when activists went to Iowa in
1996, donned biohazard suits and spray-painted pink a field of soybeans.
Mr. Margulis has had better luck, by shrewdly homing in on the potentially emotional issue of baby-food
safety, as his Greenpeace colleagues did earlier in Europe. "I am not going to be disingenuous," says Mr.
Margulis, who recently moved his base of operations to Baltimore. Baby food "pressed so many buttons. We
picked it because people are going to be concerned about what they are feeding their kids." Figuring the time
is ripe to import Europe's concerns to the U.S, he says, "I have no qualms in saying, yes, I hope people do
get very upset."
In Europe, Greenpeace has had considerable success on this front. Last summer, it confronted executives of
Novartis's Swiss baby-food line, Galactina, asking them point blank if it contained any genetically modified
ingredients. Within 24 hours, Novartis blinked, saying it would yank certain products off Swiss grocery-store
shelves. In recent months, it has made Greenpeace a firm promise that new Galactina products would be free
of genetically altered ingredients.
But Galactina is tiny. It sells only three million jars of baby food a year. Gerber produces 5 1/2 million per day
in the U.S. and has U.S. sales of $700 million annually, plus $300 millon abroad.
Buoyed by this success, Greenpeace launched a similar campaign in the U.S. late last year. To run it, the
group hired Mr. Margulis, a peace-studies graduate of the University of California at Berkeley. Unlike some
more-radical Greenpeace veterans, he has had only two arrests growing out of environmental protests. His
most flamboyant act to date: In Colombia, to protest against strawberries modified with the gene of a flounder
(for its cold tolerance), he dressed up as a berry with fishy eyes.
The campaign started slowly. The owner of Savoy, a restaurant in the SoHo section of New York, hosted a
lunchtime news conference to denounce genetically modified food, attended by a group of star chefs, some of
them French. The event was largely unnoticed, but Mr. Margulis, a former pastry chef himself, formed a
useful bond with the chefs.

--- Page 45 ---
Page 45 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Then one day this spring, Mr. Margulis went to New York-area supermarkets and bought $5 to $10 worth of
baby foods, from Gerber, Beech-Nut and Heinz's Earth's Best. Elsewhere he picked up two kinds of liquid
feeding formula for the elderly and disabled, one of them made by a different unit of Novartis. He shipped
some of the samples to Britain for testing by RHM Technology, a laboratory experienced at detecting minute
amounts of genetically modified material.
Within a couple of weeks, the results came back. The lab found no genetically modified ingredients in the jars
of baby food. But Gerber's dry cereal for babies contained modified corn and soy, and the formulas for the
elderly also had bioengineered soy, Greenpeace said.
Rather than confronting companies right away, Mr. Margulis faxed letters to several U.S. baby-food makers
posing questions and seeking quick responses. He later called Gerber's Mr. Relford in Fremont, Mich., where
Gerber is based, and left a voice message.
Although Gerber appeared to be ignoring Mr. Margulis, it wasn't. His "To the CEO" letter was making its way
up the corporate hierarchy, ultimately sparking debate at the highest levels of Novartis, a $21 billion
corporation whose largest business is pharmaceuticals. For its CEO, Daniel Vasella, who is a veteran
observer of Greenpeace tactics in Europe as well as a physician, the letter was a sign that Greenpeace would
try to strike in his lucrative U.S. market.
It did. On June 18, Mr. Margulis disclosed the British lab report. Venerable Gerber was guilty of using
genetically engineered food in its dry baby cereal, he told reporters. The news conference took place at
another trendy New York restaurant, Avenue, whose owners had begun marketing their own brand of organic
baby food -- at $2.95 a jar, five times Gerber's price. Some star chefs were again in attendance.
Greenpeace's report accused Gerber of using "altered corn" in its cereal, which it said "produces a bacterial
toxin, Bacillus thuringiensis (Bt)." The report said that when "genetically altered Bt plants grow, they produce
the toxin, which is an insecticide farmers use to ward off certain pests." It also said that soybeans in Gerber
baby food were grown with "a toxic weed killer."
Gerber doesn't dispute Greenpeace's essential finding about the presence of ingredients from genetically
modified crops in some of its products. But Novartis's Mr. Piergallini, a former boss of Gerber, angrily
dismisses the notion that genetically modified corn produces dangerous toxins, calling this "ludicrous and
inflammatory."
While maintaining a cool outward stance, Novartis and Gerber soon were abuzz with debate. Mr. Relford, a
scientist and Midwesterner, believed customers wouldn't be swayed by Greenpeace -- that sound science
and Gerber's reputation would prevail. At the same time, he wanted the company to be ready to act in case
circumstances suddenly changed. Mr. Piergallini also doubted that European food attitudes would catch on in
the U.S., but he thought that to be safe, Gerber should quickly take pre-emptive action.
Dr. Vasella, the Novartis CEO -- having been through the Galactina affair and having witnessed
Greenpeace's publicity-grabbing tactics for years in Europe -- pushed to find an immediate solution at Gerber.
And three weeks ago, the board of Novartis's U.S. arm agreed that Gerber should move aggressively to deal
with the problem. One key thing on the table: possibly relabeling products to indicate when any of them may
still contain tiny amounts of genetically modified crops. Some ingredients come from suppliers that handle
both organic and genetically modified crops, so that minute traces of the bioengineered stuff may show up in
even the "purest" foods.
But there are questions as to what Gerber's labels can say about genetically modified ingredients -- either
their presence or their absence. That's because the FDA doesn't see any health risk in these ingredients.
James Maryanski, the FDA's biotechnology coordinator, says that companies are free to include optional
information if it is truthful and doesn't mislead the consumer. But "if someone wants to say that a product was
or was not developed by genetic engineering, and if that information implies to consumers that a competing
product . . . wasn't as safe, that would be a misleading label." He adds that the FDA hasn't developed specific
guidelines regarding label mentions of genetically modified foods.
To figure out what to do, Gerber will consult a pool of non-Greenpeace environmentalists who are concerned
about the long-term effects of bioengineered foods, and of consumer groups concerned about food safety.
In the past two weeks, meanwhile, Gerber has moved to rid its products of genetically modified corn used in
various cereals and other foods. Mr. Piergallini says it is will use mostly organic corn. While the new supply
will probably cost twice as much -- raising corn without weed killer or insecticide is labor intensive and tends
to produce lower per-acre yields -- corn is used in relatively small quantities, so the move will add only
several hundred thousand dollars a year of expense. Once the switch is made, the labels will probably say
"organic corn" or "organic corn flour."

--- Page 46 ---
Page 46 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The company is also switching soybean suppliers. Soon, its baby products will use mostly organic soy, even
though these aren't easy to find in large quantities. Novartis's objective is to take the lead in the baby-food
industry in moving away from genetically modified ingredients and set the "gold standard" for measuring and
labeling these ingredients, Mr. Piergallini says.
Will the bioengineered-food issue take hold in the U.S. as it has in Europe? "I can't bet -- I plan," Gerber's Mr.
Relford says. "If this becomes an issue, we will be ready."

Gasoline Market Fuels Burst In Crude-Oil Price
Commodities
Gasoline Market Fuels Burst In Crude-Oil Price
By Matthew F. Gallagher
Dow Jones Newswires
790 words
30 July 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C17
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- A runaway gasoline market pushed crude-oil futures above $21 a barrel at the New York
Mercantile Exchange for the first time since November 1997.
After a three-day correction last week, the nearby August gasoline contract roared back, gaining more than
five cents a gallon, or 9%, in just six trading sessions. More than three cents of that jump came this week, as
refinery outages and data showing fast-dwindling supplies of gasoline injected some profound supply concern
into the market.
The August contract gained 1.90 cents yesterday to 64.98 cents, the highest close for front-month gasoline
futures since August 1997. Thin trading ahead of the contract's expiration today helped to extend gains,
traders said. The September contract rose 1.41 cents to 63.20 cents.
September crude followed higher, adding 43 cents a barrel to $20.97. The contract struck $21.12 during the
session, the highest point for front-month crude futures since November 1997.
An American Petroleum Institute inventory report released Tuesday evening showed a sharp
3.744-million-barrel decline in U.S. gasoline stocks to 208.159 million barrels in the week ended July 23.
Department of Energy data released Wednesday morning showed a decline of 3.5 million barrels to 205.4
million barrels during the period.
It was the seventh straight weekly gasoline decline in the U.S., which left stocks around 12 million barrels
under inventory reported a year ago, and closer to the lean, just-in-time stock levels the industry operated at
prior to 1998, when oil overproduction and Asian economic problems led to world-wide gluts in all petroleum
categories.
At the same time, demand for the product in the API report was 8.85 million barrels a day, bringing the
four-week demand average to 8.97 million barrels a day, which compared bullishly with the year-earlier period
when demand was 8.46 million barrels a day. Year-to-date demand ran at 8.382 million barrels a day,
compared with 8.183 for the 1998 period, a rise of more than 2%.
If the data weren't enough to generate supply fears, refinery outages did the trick. Mobil Corp. said
Wednesday that a fire at its 160,000-barrel-a-day refinery in Torrance, Calif., forced it to reduce output to an
already-tight California market. The price of gasoline on that coast soared, opening up an uncommon
opportunity to ship gasoline from the Gulf Coast to California for profit.
"The West Coast is screaming, 'Send us all the barrels you can!' " said John Kilduff, senior vice president,
energy risk management, at Fimat USA Inc. in New York.
More Gulf supply heading to the West Coast means less supply for the New York Harbor, the market
underlying the Nymex contract, during the peak-demand summer season.
At the same time, Exxon Corp.'s huge Baytown, Texas, refinery was experiencing problems at its
193,000-barrels-a-day catalytic cracker, which produces gasoline, according to market sources. And rising
prices in the Chicago gasoline market led to speculation that a Midwest refinery may be having problems.
Looking ahead, some energy analysts worry supply is going to get only tighter, as the Organization of
Petroleum Exporting Countries continues to ratchet back production to keep compliant with output-reduction
pledges, and demand jumps in the second half of the year.

--- Page 48 ---
Page 48 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"As long as OPEC compliance is good, this market is going to head up to between $22 and $25 a barrel,"
said Nizam Sharief, director of energy research at the Houston energy trading firm Hornsby & Co., who sees
sharp drops in world oil supply in the third quarter accelerating in the fourth quarter.
In other commodity markets:
GOLD: Futures rose on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange as U.S. employment-cost
data sparked investor concern about rising inflation, analysts said. The December contract gained $2.90 to
$259.70 a troy ounce. News that U.S. employment costs rose 1.1% in the second quarter prompted a sell-off
in Treasury bonds and stocks.
LUMBER: Futures fell by their limit on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange on fears of higher interest rates and
a stumbling cash market, analysts and traders said. The September contract dropped $10 to $362.90 a
thousand board feet. Brian Leonard, broker and lumber analyst at Iowa Grain Co. in Chicago, said the risk of
higher interest rates, combined with uncertainty about just how far cash prices have eroded this week,
convinced speculative commodity funds that it was time to sell.
---
David E. Parkinson contributed to this article.

Investors Accept Break in Link of Gold, Platinum
Investors Accept Break in Link of Gold, Platinum
By Andi Spicer
Dow Jones Newswires
598 words
26 July 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
B8C
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
JOHANNESBURG, South Africa -- South Africa's platinum producers are sang-froid about sister-metal gold's
fall from grace.
While once they shuddered at any drop in the gold price as the two precious metals tended to rise or fall in
tandem, they now say the relationship is finally severed in investors' minds.
"The fundamentals of the two metals have asserted themselves in the price, and we believe platinum's
fundamentals are quite solid and we see relative price stability. We're quite confident," said Dorian Emmett,
commercial director at Anglo American Platinum Corp., the world's premier producer, based here.
This is in sharp contrast to gold, which has tumbled to a series of fresh 20-year lows since the Bank of
England announced it was going to dispose of more than half of its official bullion reserves.
Gold is trading at around $253 a troy ounce on the European spot market while platinum is trading at around
$353 an ounce.
Analysts say gold also won't get any help from a rally in platinum prices due to the uncoupling.
Derek Engelbrecht, marketing manager at South Africa's Impala Platinum Holdings Ltd., the globe's No. 2
producer, says, "the fall in the gold price has cemented the idea that the link is broken, even though it had
been broken well before bullion's fall."
The reason for this parting of paths is that the metals' fundamentals are now so different. "Platinum's market
is so much smaller than gold's. It's just 6% the size in trading and handling volumes," Mr. Emmett says.
Another industry observer comments that gold just isn't that rare anymore, with plenty of it coming out of
central-bank vaults. Central banks across the world hold more than 30,000 tons. This can be mobilized
cheaply and fast.
"Platinum doesn't have the above-ground-surface stocks that gold has -- there's very little," Mr. Emmett says.
This means that supply and demand are much better matched, leading to price stability.
Demand for platinum comes from its use in automotive catalytic converters, jewelry, chemicals and glass
manufacturing, petrochemicals, electrical and computer components and investment bars and coins. In 1998,
total demand for the metal rose 4% to 5.35 million ounces. Mine and supply from stocks gained 8.9% to 5.4
million ounces during the same period, says the metal's main marketeer, Johnson Matthey PLC.
But it is this industry demand that sets the white metal apart from gold, which has few practical uses except
as a rather poor store of value and for use in jewelry.
Platinum is a metal of the future, and every year, many more high-technology applications are discovered,
from computer hard disks to new fuel-cell vehicles where platinum is used in the proton exchange membrane
technology for electric cars.
Gold is mined in many countries, from Latin American to Asia, with South Africa as the largest producer. But
platinum is mined almost exclusively in southern Africa, and South Africa in particular. Only Russia poses any
competition to South Africa's overriding dominance in the metal's production. Last year, South Africa
produced 3.68 million ounces.
While South Africa has been historically linked to gold, there has been a quiet revolution with platinum
increasing in importance. As employment in gold mines shrinks, the platinum industry is investing in

--- Page 50 ---
Page 50 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
cutting-edge methods of mining and extraction. Very soon, platinum's value to the country may eclipse
gold's.

How to Keep Growth Alive:Repeal Humphrey-Hawkins
How to Keep Growth Alive: Repeal Humphrey-Hawkins
By Brian S. Wesbury
645 words
22 July 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A26
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Today Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan visits Capitol Hill to give his semiannual
Humphrey-Hawkins report. The 1978 Humphrey-Hawkins Act requires this report and empowers the Fed to
target any economic or market indicator that it wishes in order to justify its monetary-policy stance.
Lately the Fed has argued that rising stock prices are causing an overheating economy, so it has tightened
monetary policy. But by doing so the Fed has caused a large decline in commodity prices. Farmers are
getting prices for their products more than 30% below year-ago levels. Scrap steel prices are down 23% from
last year.
This would not happen if the Fed followed a price rule for money. If the Fed targeted gold, a basket of
commodities or other current real-time price indices, it would be lowering interest rates now. It is time to
change the Fed's operating law before more damage is done.
As in the past, Mr. Greenspan will tell Congress today that the Fed's models have been wrong. They have
predicted that the economy could not grow as fast as it has without creating inflation. Despite this admission,
the Fed refuses to change its models or strategies.
The Fed pays lip service to "new era" high-tech productivity growth, but underestimates its power. Thus it
argues that there are too many jobs, that higher wages will cause inflation, and that rising stock prices are
bad for the economy. Yet the best thermometers of inflation -- commodity prices and gold -- are telling us
exactly the opposite. The New Era is creating wealth at an unprecedented pace. Manufacturing productivity
has grown at a 4% annual rate during the eight years of this recovery -- the strongest sustained productivity
growth in U.S history.
The result of this strong growth is lower inflation. More real goods soak up excess liquidity and drive prices
down, not up. Durable-good prices in the Consumer Price Index -- computers, autos and household
appliances -- have fallen for three years in a row and are down 2.3% at an annual rate so far in 1999. Even
service-price inflation, which the data horribly overstates, rose at just 2.47% during the second quarter, a
34-year low.
While there is nothing wrong with slight deflation caused by rising productivity, deflation induced by monetary
policy can be very dangerous, as the Great Depression showed. The signs of tight money are everywhere.
Every country that pegs its currency to the U.S. dollar but does not have a currency board has had problems
holding its peg. Moreover, since June 30 gold prices are down 2.8%, soybean prices 0.5%, wheat 4%, corn
5% and lean hogs 7.8%. Oil and copper prices are up, but because of OPEC production cuts and mine
closures, not inflationary pressures.
In the late 1920s, the Fed ignored falling commodity prices and continually raised rates to pop what it
perceived as a bubble in financial markets. Eventually, falling commodity prices forced the passage of the
Smoot-Hawley Tariff Act. The combination of tight money and protectionism caused the Great Depression.
Today falling farm prices are creating pressures for trade protection, restored subsidies and a government
bailout for farmers. The only way to avoid such measures is to stop focusing monetary policy on stock
prices. The Fed should listen to commodity prices and let the economy run rather than assume that failed
models will begin to work again.
Congress should pass Sen. Connie Mack's legislation to repeal Humphrey-Hawkins and mandate the Fed to
target prices. Let's hope it does before misguided monetary policy brings the new era to an end.
---
Mr. Wesbury is chief economist at Griffin, Kubik, Stephens & Thompson in Chicago.

Cable & Wireless
Gives NTL a Lead
In U.K. Cable Talks
NTL, Britain's No. 3 cable operator, gained the upper hand in the battle for Cable & Wireless
Communications' residential cable assets, but rival Telewest Communications still has a chance. Cable and
Wireless, which owns 53% of Cable & Wireless Communications, said it had entered into exclusive talks with
NTL aimed at "concluding an agreement for a possible merger" of C&W Communications' cable and
consumer-telephone businesses. Analysts said NTL, which is believed to be offering a mix of cash and
shares valued at GBP 8 billion ($12.5 billion), was now ahead of Telewest -- which has been negotiating with
C&W Communications since April to acquire the assets -- because of the exclusive Cable & Wireless talks.
Cable & Wireless, however, said a transaction with NTL isn't guaranteed.
An NTL representative wasn't available to comment. Telewest said it will "monitor the situation carefully."
Pernod Ricard Won't Back BNP's Bid
French liquor group Pernod Ricard said it won't tender the 2% stake it owns in banking group Societe
Generale to hostile bidder Banque Nationale de Paris. Pernod Ricard's decision came as French regulatory
authorities, responding to a request by Societe Generale, made clear that BNP will have to seek renewed
authorization to retain its stakes in twin targets Societe Generale and Paribas if it fails to win outright control
of either bank. BNP is bidding for both banks in an effort to thwart an agreed merger between Societe
Generale and Paribas. The final timetable for the competing bids is expected to be announced today by the
Financial Markets Council, following the publication of Societe Generale's and Paribas's official rebuttals of
BNP's sweetened bids. The offers are likely to close early next month.
IMF Approval Could Trigger Russia Aid
Russia could receive as much as $3.8 billion from foreign lenders this year if the International Monetary Fund
approves a new $4.5 billion loan program, said Mikhail Zadornov, Russia's top envoy to international lending
institutions. He said the IMF loan would trigger more lending from the World Bank and Japan, and would
enable the government to stop borrowing from the central bank to meet foreign-debt payments this year.
BCP Revises Bid for Mundial Confianca
Banco Comercial Portugues of Portugal revised the terms of its bid for Cia. de Seguros Mundial Confianca
and made an additional offer for three of the Portuguese insurer's financial units. But it remained unclear how
the struggle for control of Mundial Confianca will finally play out, following the European Commission's
announcement that it plans today to suspend the Portuguese government's veto of a sale that would have
given management control of Mundial Confianca to Spain's Banco Santander Central Hispano. Under the
new terms of its bid, valued at about 4.5 billion euros ($4.6 billion), BCP "reserves the right to acquire all
Mundial Confianca shares tendered in the offer provided it acquires at least the majority" interest in the
insurer's units, Banco Pinto & Sotto Mayor and Credito Predial Portugues. BCP's initial offer, which was
contingent on acquiring 51% of Mundial Confianca, was doomed to failure as long as financier Antonio
Champalimaud controlled 52% of the insurer and refused to sell out, analysts said.

Mexico Bank Rescue May Cost More
Mexico's rescue of its banking system will cost taxpayers 633.3 billion pesos ($68 billion), according to
estimates of an independent audit. The team, led by Canadian auditor Michael Mackey, also found that
irregular or "reportable" transactions in the capitalization and loan-purchase program sponsored by the
government totaled 72.7 billion pesos. Of that, six billion pesos were related to illegal bank operations.
Mexico's Congress last year approved legislation to consolidate a pile of nonperforming bank loans as public
debt, with the proviso that only transactions of legal origin were to be assumed. The report said the cost of
the rescue at June 30, 1998, was above the government's estimate of 576.6 billion pesos.

Mexican financial-services company Grupo Financiero Bital is conducting talks with ING Group of the
Netherlands over selling a stake to raise fresh funds for a capital injection, a Bital executive said. Bital, which
has Mexico's largest branch-banking network, plans to inject $300 million in capital when its merger with
Banco del Atlantico, which the government took over after the 1994 peso-devaluation crisis, is completed this
year. A spokesman for ING in Amsterdam had no comment.
Placer Dome Stops Venezuela Project
Placer Dome, citing weak gold prices, said it suspended construction of its Las Cristinas gold mine in
Venezuela. The Vancouver, British Columbia, mining company owns 70% of the mine, while the Venezuelan
government owns 30%. Placer Dome said the suspension defers about $500 million in capital expenditures
and postpones production of 530,000 ounces of gold annually. It said it will resume construction when prices
indicate "an appropriate return on the project."

has taken control of 81 supermarkets in Brazil since December.

the government's failure to help them weather slack crop prices and rising debts.

Dollar Moves Lower Against Yen, EuroAs Volatility in U.S. Currency Continues
Foreign Exchange
Dollar Moves Lower Against Yen, Euro As Volatility in U.S. Currency Continues
By Stephanie Hoo
Dow Jones Newswires
656 words
20 July 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C23
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- The dollar fell against the yen and euro in volatile trading as the U.S. currency's decline to
five-week lows against the yen prompted nervous traders to cover short positions in the euro.
"When the dollar fell so far vs. the yen, it created imbalances on the crosses," said Ram Bhagavatula, chief
economist at NatWest Global Financial Markets.
The dollar traded as low as about 117.65 yen, more than three yen below late Friday, as yen-buyers were
emboldened by the Bank of Japan's failure to defend against the yen's strength.
The Bank of Japan intervened repeatedly during June and early July, but appears to have been absent from
the market for the past two weeks.
The selling of dollars for the yen also led to dollar-selling against the euro, the British pound and the Swiss
franc as well, traders said.
The euro, which traded as low as $1.0120 Monday, spiked as high as $1.0340 when traders rushed to cover
their short positions in a rising market. The speed of the euro's upward move prompted talk that central banks
in Europe had intervened to prop up the currency -- talk that further fueled euro strength.
But several veteran traders said the price action didn't suggest central bank action. The European Central
Bank, for its part, declined to comment on the intervention rumors.
All in all, the volatility revealed the market's heightened nervousness against making new lows in the euro,
which has fallen about 12% since its Jan. 1 launch. As the euro heads toward one-to-one parity with the
dollar, the market's downward leaning makes upswings more violent.
Fundamentally, sporadic signs of strength in the European economies and the narrowing gap between U.S.
and European bond yields suggest that the euro has room to recover, analysts said.
"The euro is weak enough if you look at the underlying data," NatWest's Mr. Bhagavatula said. "Someday it is
bound to turn, but it's still difficult to tell if the turn has happened yet."
"A lot of people think it has fallen enough," said Jeffrey Yu, chief dealer at Sanwa Bank in New York, adding
that traders have become more reluctant about pushing toward parity.
The euro fell against the resurgent yen, however, trading at a new low of about 121.15 yen. Investors
continue to buy yen to plow into stocks included in Japan's Nikkei index, which is up more than 30% so far
this year and which finished yesterday above the 18500 level for the first time since September 1997.
But Japanese officials have said they don't want the yen to appreciate too much ahead of an economic
recovery, and the Bank of Japan's two-week absence from the foreign-exchange market has puzzled many
traders.
Yen-buyers have been particularly bold during North American trading hours, since U.S. officials have
complained publicly about Japan's manipulation of its exchange rate. Over the past two weeks, U.S. Treasury
Secretary Lawrence Summers has reiterated that Japan should focus on demand-led growth.
Late Monday in New York, the dollar had rebounded a bit and was trading at 118.30 yen, but was still down
sharply from 120.95 yen late Friday.

--- Page 57 ---
Page 57 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Elsewhere, the Canadian and Australian dollars weakened against the U.S. dollar, pressured by continuing
bearishness in the metals markets.
"There's just no apparent reason to be long gold," said James McGroarty, senior vice president at Orbitex
Capital Strategies in New York. Global inflation pressures are subdued, and central banks appear ready to
sell more gold, he said.
Tensions between China and Taiwan continued to weigh on the new Taiwan dollar, but heavy intervention by
Taiwan's central bank kept the currency aloft.

Global Markets Need Golden Rule
Global Markets Need Golden Rule
By Judy Shelton
1,119 words
16 July 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
"Manipulating currencies is not the approach that produces long-run prosperity." So said Lawrence Summers
last week during his first television interview as Treasury secretary. More than a few mouths undoubtedly fell
open.
Not that the idea behind the statement is so shocking. Why shouldn't we expect government authorities to
recognize that a stable monetary regime provides the best foundation for profitable enterprise and sustained
economic growth? It is just that intellectual fashion and official practice have moved so far away from that
concept during the final few decades of this century.
Skeptics can be forgiven for questioning whether Mr. Summers was confessing to a genuine change in his
own monetary philosophy or merely taking a dig at Japan for its current policy of cheapening the yen to
maintain high exports. Mr. Summers has long been an advocate of currency depreciation as a remedy for
economic stagnation. But perhaps we are witnessing the dawning of a new Summers doctrine born of
personal observation, analysis and reconsideration. As John Maynard Keynes famously retorted after being
accused of inconsistency in his economic prescriptions: "When my information changes, I alter my
conclusions. What do you do, sir?"
Hints that conclusions were being altered at Treasury in light of recent global experience have been apparent
since April, when Mr. Summers' predecessor, Robert Rubin, gave a major speech outlining U.S. proposals to
reform the international financial architecture.
The overall goal, Mr. Rubin explained, was to promote global growth while reducing the likelihood of crisis.
This required special attention to exchange-rate regimes. "The costs of failed regimes," said Mr. Rubin, "can
be significant not only for the countries involved but also for other countries and for the system as a whole."
While some countries might choose fixed exchange rates, others might choose to have floating rates. Bottom
line: "The right exchange rate regime is a choice for the individual country." The only wrong monetary regime
would be one based on an exchange rate "peg" that was unrealistic and unsustainable -- in which case, Mr.
Rubin warned, there should be no expectation of large-scale official financial assistance from the international
community. Mr. Summers will no doubt continue in this same policy vein.
The good news is that the message has now been broadcast that burning up billions in foreign reserves in an
effort to intimidate currency markets often proves futile. Speculative plays can quickly overwhelm
central-bank tweaking. The bad news, however, is that the U.S. seems to be suggesting a sort of moral
equivalence between fixed and floating rates. By effectively allowing emerging-market nations to choose their
own exchange-rate regime, we are sidestepping the question of how best to facilitate their solid integration
into the global marketplace and expand their access to global capital markets. In a world dominated by three
major currencies -- the dollar, the euro and the yen -- emerging-market countries with floating rates, cast
adrift by a seemingly benign U.S. policy, have no anchor to secure their position in the roiling seas of global
finance.
Meanwhile, it is distressing to see a nation like Argentina, which has stalwartly maintained a currency-board
system that backs every issued peso with U.S. dollar reserves, get pummeled by political uncertainties and
rogue financial waves. Thus, even though the collapse of the Brazilian real constituted a monetary failure for
Brazil, it has also imposed costs on Argentine exporters who are now paying a heavy price to maintain dollar
parity. In short, it is not just illogical but irresponsible to suggest that every nation should feel free to choose
its own "right" exchange-rate regime as if it were selecting new drapes.
Sound money and accurate price signals are the ultimate form of financial transparency. International
monetary order and full economic integration will be achieved only if all nations agree to abide by the same
rules for establishing the value of their currencies. Money, after all, is meant to serve as the unit of account. It
enables us to measure the value of competing goods, services and investment opportunities throughout the
world. The value of money itself should not be subject to flexible interpretations and varying criteria. In the

--- Page 59 ---
Page 59 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
absence of an international monetary system, currencies do not effectively convey useful information about
opportunities available in the international marketplace, but rather become an investment gamble in
themselves. Witness the explosive growth in foreign-exchange market activity, which now exceeds $1.6
trillion in daily turnover.
Nations are not liberated by the right to choose an exchange-rate regime; they are alienated. If the goal is to
have a global common market -- and what else can we mean when we talk about the global economy,
especially with the explosive growth of electronic commerce -- how can we ignore the parallel need for a
common unit of account, a global form of money?
This need not be a single currency; the virtue of the gold standard was that it allowed each nation to issue its
own currency while ensuring stable rates, since all currencies were convertible at a fixed rate into gold. The
critical issue is whether there exists any kind of monetary order or set of rules by which the concept of a
meaningful unit of account can be preserved. Under the system established in 1944 at Bretton Woods, N.H.,
nations maintained their own currencies at fixed rates relative to the dollar, while the U.S. agreed to convert
the dollar into gold at a fixed rate. But the drift toward chronic U.S. budget deficits in the 1960s compromised
the integrity of the dollar and doomed the system.
Today the U.S. has put its own financial house in admirable order. We are in a position to help other nations
-- particularly the less developed ones -- become integrated into the global economy. The same cannot be
said for the other big players, Europe and Japan, which are too preoccupied with their own financial woes to
extend a helping hand to less wealthy nations. And so it falls to the U.S. to provide meaningful direction
toward the establishment of a new global monetary system.
One thing, at least, should be clear: So long as developing countries are stuck with boutique currencies that
are easily shredded by speculators, there is little chance they will be able to establish themselves firmly in the
international financial community. Mr. Summers, who has finally seen the light on the pitfalls of currency
manipulation, is now well-positioned to prescribe the remedy. Let's hope recent experience has taught him to
pick the right one.
---
Ms. Shelton, an economist and author, serves on the board of Empower America.

--- Page 60 ---
Page 60 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Gold's Fall Vexes Investors, Consumers---Jewelry Shops GetHeirloom Queries
Commodities
Gold's Fall Vexes Investors, Consumers --- Jewelry Shops Get Heirloom Queries
By Peter A. McKay
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,319 words
16 July 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
A concerned customer called Chicago jeweler Tobina Kahn last week to sell a Tiffany necklace inherited from
her late mother and made from six ounces of gold, plus two carats of diamonds. Her worry: Had the
50-year-old heirloom's worth diminished literally overnight?
Ms. Kahn said no -- that just doesn't happen to vintage designer jewelry. But Ms. Kahn received several such
calls last week after the Bank of England's auction of 25 tons of gold slashed the metal's price on world
commodity markets.
Flash to San Antonio: Portfolio manager Gil Atzmon is scratching his head. He has followed precious metals
for 15 years, and can't understand the concern over the auction either, considering that 25 tons isn't much
compared with the total volume of gold traded daily.
"What's been going on with gold leading up to, and since, the auction just goes against the fundamental
principles of what makes markets," says Mr. Atzmon, chief investment strategist for U.S. Global Investors Inc.
"But maybe the problem with me is that I've just been at this for too long. I might understand things better if I
had no experience to influence the way I see things."
Put mildly, gold has an image problem right now. But it is not just because of the notoriously bearish outlook
for the metal's asset value. Rather, investors and consumers alike are struggling to make sense of the
economics surrounding gold, which some say have deviated from the trend lines the metal has
conventionally followed.
World gold demand is increasing; but its commodity value continues to plummet. Shoppers expect bargains
on products made from gold, or they flock to sell the pieces they already own. Yet there hasn't been any
change in those objects' worth.
Gold futures have hit several new 20-year lows in recent weeks, and the August contract closed at $254.80
an ounce on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange yesterday.
The metal, which traded above $600 early in the 1980s, is way past those heady days and has been
declining for several years now. But the latest $34 slide began just after the Bank of England announced on
May 7 its plans to eventually sell 415 tons of gold, beginning with the recent auction as its first installment.
Low inflation, gold's declining role as a monetary standard, and central-bank sales such as the British
auction have generally been blamed for the falling value. However, nuggets of good news are to be found in
the landslide of gold selling.
Asian gold demand, which fell sharply during that continent's financial crisis, has begun to improve over the
past month or so, analysts say. American jewelry-manufacturers' demand, which had increased 13% last
year, is measuring strong gains again, as is consumer buying of gold coins. In the first half of the year, the
U.S. Mint sold almost 39 tons of gold American Eagle bullion coins -- one of the more convenient ways for
small investors to own gold -- more than twice the total for the year-earlier half.
Because of those demand trends, plus the fact that the British announcement had all of June to finish
pummeling gold prices, market watchers' expectations going into last week's auction were split. Ultimately,
the sale was interpreted as a bearish development as much because of what it symbolized as its fundamental
effect on the gold market, said analyst Philip Klapwijk of Gold Fields Mineral Services Ltd., a London
metals-research firm.

--- Page 61 ---
Page 61 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"I think it's the signaling effect that's most important here, as much as anything," Mr. Klapwijk said. "The 25
tons certainly isn't a huge amount, but the fact that someone's decided to go from having 17% of their
reserves to 7% is significant. This isn't a Mickey Mouse bank, after all. It's the Bank of England."
But retailing analysts say those falling commodity prices have not yet resulted in similar cuts for jewelry
buyers, and most likely won't for at least three months. Frequently, they say, manufacturers simply substitute
higher-quality gold -- for example 18 karats instead of 14 karats -- when commodity prices fall, thereby
justifying similar prices for their jewelry.
Ms. Kahn says the effect of gold's commodity price is even more negligible on the type of designer items that
her small estate-jewelry company, the House of Kahn, purchases. Nevertheless, she says she gets three to
four calls from concerned sellers on any day there's significant bearish gold-commodity news, such as that of
the British auction.
One of her recent customers, retired accountant Claire Fischer, said many older Americans view jewelry as
an investment asset, a philosophy Ms. Khan discourages, since jewelry pieces don't appreciate much in value
over time. Craftsmanship and collectible value mostly determines the worth of such jewelry.
"People my age lived through a time when money wasn't worth anything and banks were no good. For them
gold -- even their jewelry -- was always something they could sell for value if they needed to," said Ms.
Fischer, 75 years old, who says she has tripled the money gained from her jewelry sales on the stock market.
"This is a different time now, though. Money is better than gold," she continued. "The times have changed.
Unfortunately, some people haven't."
Indeed, a recent poll taken by the mining-industry trade group World Gold Council showed that citizens in
most industrialized countries still view gold as a valuable asset, even if their governments are busy selling it
off. In the survey, 67% of British respondents and 76% of Americans said gold reserves are important to
national economic strength.
Similar percentages in both countries said gold reserves are important to maintaining a strong currency.
Gold Council research has also shown public sentiment about gold to be largely uninformed, however. A
1997 poll by the organization, for example, showed that only about 7% of Americans can correctly quote the
price of gold on any given day.
---
In other commodities markets yesterday:
COPPER: Cyprus Amax Minerals Co. and Asarco Inc. confirmed they will merge, forming the world's largest
publicly traded copper company. Although the merger announcement came after the close of commodities
trading, copper futures rose throughout the day because of expectations of a deal. The front-month copper
futures contract rose 2.25 cents to 79.35 cents a pound on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile
Exchange.
GASOLINE: Gasoline futures drove the New York Mercantile Exchange's energy complex higher, as traders
worried supply problems on the West Coast could divert gasoline from other regional markets. Refinery
problems in California have lent support to the energy complex throughout 1999, as the major consuming
state has been in a near-constant struggle to meet demand. The latest glitch came over the weekend, when
Chevron Corp.'s 240,000-barrel-a-day Richmond, Calif., refinery had to reduce gasoline production by 70%
because of damage to a 65,000-barrel-a-day catalytic-cracking unit. The Nymex August gasoline contract
closed up 2.18 cents, or 4%, at 61.70 cents a gallon, the highest point for gasoline futures since October
1997. With the Chevron unit down, and Tosco Corp.'s 150,000-barrel-a-day Avon, Calif., refinery struggling to
return to normal after a February explosion, the California market is undersupplied by about 100,000 barrels a
day, according to market sources. The August crude contract fell as low as $19.58, a 34-cent drop, but
reversed course and rallied late in the session to a high of $20.25 a barrel. The contract settled 24 cents
higher at $20.16 a barrel.

Is It Goodbye to Gold, or Just `See Ya Later?'
Global View
Is It Goodbye to Gold, or Just `See Ya Later?'
By George Melloan
1,162 words
13 July 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A23
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Winston Churchill's brave 1925 revival of the British gold standard came to a sorry end in 1931 when London
couldn't cover demand for the precious metal. By 1936, what economist John Maynard Keynes had dubbed a
"barbarous relic" was pretty much finished as the guarantor of industrial world currency values. It enjoyed a
second coming of sorts from 1944 to 1971 as the base for the Bretton Woods monetary system, but Bretton
Woods was essentially a global U.S. dollar standard, one that eventually broke down as a result of U.S. fiscal
and monetary excesses.
This little historical excursion provides a backdrop for a new monetary stir in London last week. The fuss this
time was over the Bank of England's difficulties in getting rid of gold it doesn't want. Its plan to sell 415 metric
tons has driven the world market price down 10% since it was announced in May, and buyers bidding for the
first tranche of 25 tons last Tuesday were hoping to push the price down even further. The price did in fact hit
a 20-year low, closing at $257.30 an ounce in London on Friday.
Belgium and the Netherlands, among others, had preceded Britain, although a great deal more quietly, and
Switzerland has a big sell-off scheduled. There seems to be something of a herd instinct developing among
the central bankers. Additionally, the International Monetary Fund wants to sell off 10 million ounces to raise
money for the debt relief its G-7 leading members voted last week for 41 poor nations.
It hasn't been so long ago that this rush by monetary authorities to dump gold would have caused an uproar
among "gold bugs," those people who have never trusted the fiat currencies that have now become the
medium of exchange in most corners of the world. There are not a few corners, like Russia, where barter or
dollars are preferred over the national currencies, and that is why the gold bugs have a point. But by and
large, money that has value only because a sovereign state has declared it legal tender has supplanted
money that is universally regarded as having intrinsic worth independent of any state decree. Gold and silver
have been the favorites in that category over the centuries.
Today's world is very different from the world of 1925 or 1931 or 1944 or even 1971. Money is electronic, with
billions moved over circuitry every day. Money is credit cards, or smart cards, or food stamps, or
grocery-store coupons. Central banks and treasuries are monopoly issuers of currencies, but money has a
life of its own as well, responding to the forces of supply and demand and public trust. It is a scary world to
traditionalists who cling to the concept of intrinsic value, but it seems to work, only occasionally offering up
hiccups like the Asian meltdown or the ruble collapse. Inflation, that great destroyer of economies, is in
check almost everywhere. Quite likely, the ephemeral money of electrons and megabytes is here to stay.
Which is why central banks are unloading gold. With inflation low, gold is not a profitable investment. Its
market price has been slowly eroding in recent years and there are few people who think it will go soaring
again as it did during the dollar inflation of the 1970s, finally peaking briefly at $970 an ounce in January
1980. The Bank of England says its return on loans of gold is only 1.8% annually, which means that this part
of its reserves is hardly a cherished asset at a time when the bank could be using the money from gold sales
to buy foreign bonds.
Central banks and multilateral banking institutions such as the IMF are said to have about 30,000 metric tons
of gold, a holdover from the days when gold was indeed a cherished asset, an indicator of a country's ability
to protect itself from adversities such as war or inflation. Annual world-wide production doesn't measure up
to demand, so there is a brisk market for existing gold. But it should be no surprise that the price has fallen
on the prospect of large sales over the next few years.
There is a compensating factor, however. Gold is expensive to mine and refine, which is partly why it is a
scarce commodity that tends to have a stable real value and why it has been a useful money over the
centuries. Many tons of earth have to be brought up from deep underground in the South African mines to get
a little bit of gold ore. The refining process adds a further cost. The price of gold has already fallen below the

--- Page 67 ---
Page 67 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
cost of production in some South African mines, so mines are being closed and miners are being laid off. The
government of newly elected President Thabo Mbeki sees this as a crisis and is pleading with the IMF and
the central banks to stop their gold sales. He is getting a sympathetic ear in some quarters, including the
Black Caucus of the U.S. Congress, which will try to block the IMF offering. It is ironic that the gold sales with
which the IMF hopes to aid the black countries of Africa are disturbing a more prosperous part of Africa.
The cutbacks in South Africa, however, tend to soften the market effects of the central bank actions. By
reducing the supply of new gold, they will limit the extent to which the gold sales drive prices down. That is
some protection for the central banks, since every drop in prices diminishes the potential value of their
reserves. But a 10% price drop since May is significant. Declines in reserve values are themselves
deflationary, contributing to other deflationary pressures abroad in the world.
Another element in the London controversy is the dubious claim by British Euroskeptics that Chancellor of the
Exchequer Gordon Brown is selling off the gold to make it easier for the United Kingdom to discard the
pound sterling and adopt the European single currency, the euro, at some future time. These nationalists are
nostalgic about sterling's distinguished past, when the empire and the gold standard were flourishing.
Sterling was hardly a paragon in the 1960s and 1970s, however, when inflation was rampant.
Yet maybe there is something more palpable in the unease of the Euroskeptics. The rise of the fiat money
and the decline of gold requires a lot of faith in the states that issue fiat money. The best hopes for the future
may rest in the possibility that we are moving now beyond state control of money, to yet another plane where
money supply is controlled by demand. But the history of money is a history of change and don't bet that gold
is gone forever.

Page 68 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.

Intervention Rumors, Later Denied, Take Dollar Off Highs Against Euro
