Reappoint Greenspan Now
Reappoint Greenspan Now
By Lawrence B. Lindsey
1,260 words
7 July 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A22
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
A week ago the Federal Open Market Committee raised interest rates for the first time in 2 1/2 years. Under
different circumstances, this would probably have had a dampening effect, yet the markets rallied sharply,
taking comfort in the perception that the Federal Reserve would not raise rates again soon. Commodity
prices, led by gold, copper and oil, all jumped significantly, as did the Dow Jones Industrial Average, which
closed Friday at a record high before falling 4.12 points yesterday.
Whether the Fed was right to raise rates is a matter of legitimate debate. But the reaction of the markets
points to the delicate position in which the Fed now finds itself: What it says (or seems to say) is as important
to the economy as what it does. This is not a new phenomenon, but two factors have combined to accentuate
its significance. First, the surprising duration of the current economic expansion is causing anxiety about how
much longer it can last. Second, Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan has come to be seen as the economy's
indispensable man, and the prospect that he might depart when his term expires next June is creating
uncertainty about the future course of monetary policy. Taken together, this is a potent recipe for economic
trouble -- the most effective remedy for which is for President Clinton to reappoint Mr. Greenspan at once to
another term.
By most indications, the U.S. economy is the picture of health. But there are disquieting signs of danger. The
American consumer has become the consumer of first and last resort for the world. With wage and salary
income soaring at 6% annually and consumer prices rising at only about 2.5%, the consumer is flush. But
even this ample cash flow has not been enough to satisfy consumer spending habits. Households are
increasing their spending faster than their incomes are rising, liquidating a portion of their wealth, or going
into debt, to finance the difference.
In addition, corporate cash flow is no longer covering business investment. The borrowing needs of the
private sector have far outstripped both available private-sector saving and the federal budget surplus. The
result is an unprecedented private-sector reliance on foreign inflows of capital. Without such inflows, the
American private sector could no longer finance its current level of economic activity.
This borrowing need is not only large but growing. Last year our net indebtedness to foreigners rose by more
than $200 billion. By the end of this year we may see that number approach a $400 billion annual rate. By the
end of next year, if the economy does not slow, we will be relying on foreign financing for nearly $600 billion a
year. As our borrowing needs grow, so does the price we pay for the money. Even before the Fed acted, the
cost to the Treasury of 30-year money had risen 140 basis points from its low last fall.
This is not necessarily bad, but it is imprudent. It means that the fate of the private economy is being held
hostage to foreign developments. Heightened credit demands in Asia or Europe, or a perceived weakening in
the dollar, would have immediate and profound effects on our economy. The Fed must have the full freedom
of movement to preempt such a situation.
The Fed must also find its way through a variety of confusing crosscurrents in our so-called New Economy.
One of the most important is the use of options as a form of employee compensation. Many employers now
give employees throughout the company stock options in lieu of significant pay increases. This has the
positive effect of increasing the employee's stake in the firm's performance. Employees have no complaints
given the recent performance of the equity markets.
But the widespread use of options sends a number of confusing signals to decision makers. Options are not
counted as "compensation" either in the national income accounts or on the firm's profit-and-loss statement.
As such, wage data can suggest that employment costs are contained, when in fact they are growing quite
rapidly. Furthermore, corporate profitability can be easily overstated since compensation costs can be paid in
"free" money -- promises of corporate stock that appear not to dilute shareholder value. The combined effect
is to create the appearance of a benign economic environment underneath which assets and liabilities are
becoming increasingly mismatched.

--- Page 70 ---
Page 70 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Fed also faces confusing signals on productivity. There are many reasons to believe that we may be
understating productivity in some high-technology areas. But we are also not correctly measuring the decline
in quality of customer service resulting from labor shortages, downsizing and the increasing use of workers
with subpar language, math and social skills. The addition of these new workers to the labor force, while
socially very beneficial, imposes a cost that is not counted in the traditional productivity calculation.
But the Fed's problems are not only economic. During his most recent congressional testimony, Mr.
Greenspan was assailed for daring to raise rates just as the American worker was starting to benefit from the
current expansion. Politicians of varied political stripes will increasingly find the Fed an easy target as the
campaign season heats up. Should the bond market come to feel that Fed policy is being dictated by political
considerations, the economic consequences would be severe.
Complicating this still further is the prospect of Mr. Greenspan's departure. In this regard, Mr. Clinton has
three options. First, he could choose to nominate someone else. That would send a clear vote of no
confidence and is sure to upset the markets. Then too, the chances are slim to none that the Senate would
confirm an entirely new nominee of a lame-duck president from the opposite party. Thus, nominating
someone other than Mr. Greenspan is both dangerous for the economy and politically futile.
Second, Mr. Clinton could announce that the next president should choose the next chairman, leaving Mr.
Greenspan to serve as a lame duck. There is some political merit in this, especially since the term as
chairman would no longer come up during a presidential election year. But these considerations are far
outweighed by the economic risks. Leaving the choice of chairman to the next president would politicize the
issue of monetary policy while creating dangerous uncertainties about its future. This is not a recipe for
stability.
The third option, of course, is to renominate Mr. Greenspan. The only question is when. Waiting until his
current term expires would place his confirmation hearings at the height of the political season. In the
meantime the markets would be free to speculate about behind the scenes deals between the Fed and the
White House, thus eroding the perception of the Fed's independence. It is exactly what the markets, the
economy, and the country cannot afford.
There is, then, only one thing that Mr. Clinton can do that would both remove monetary policy from politics
and alleviate the current uncertainty in markets: renominate Mr. Greenspan now. The combined effect of his
reappointment by a Democratic president and his confirmation by a Republican Senate would signal to global
markets that American monetary policy has a stable bipartisan basis. It would allow the Fed to concentrate
on the difficult task at hand, free of political distraction. And it would leave a legacy worthy of any president.
---
Mr. Lindsey, a former governor of the Federal Reserve, is a resident scholar at the American Enterprise
Institute.

Gold Falls to $257.80 on Bank of England Auction
Commodities
Gold Falls to $257.80 on Bank of England Auction
By Peter A. Mckay
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
895 words
7 July 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C19
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Gold prices' most recent protracted slide lasted for more than a month, beginning when the Bank of England
announced plans to sell 400 tons of its reserves of the metal. When the central bank's sell-off actually
started yesterday, the shellacking began anew.
The bank sold 25 tons of the metal at $261.20 an ounce in an auction in which participants had submitted
bids to buy five times the tonnage of gold being offered.
Mining companies and bullish investors had hoped that the oversubscribed bidding would demonstrate strong
demand and bolster gold prices. But those expectations were quickly dashed by Bank of England statistics
showing that the vast majority of the offers were below gold's market price.
Following that bearish sentiment, gold futures made their biggest move of the year on the Comex division of
the New York Mercantile Exchange, falling $6.80 to a 20-year low of $257.80 an ounce. With more British
gold auctions planned throughout the year and possible sales by the International Monetary Fund and
Switzerland's central bank pending, market watchers worried that gold's value could continue to wallow until
it hits the price of production -- or even lower.
"Clearly, the market was divided on what would happen" yesterday, said analyst Philip Klapwijk of Gold
Fields Mineral Services Ltd. "But overall, the bearish sentiment has certainly existed for some time now. I
certainly don't think it's going to be changing anytime soon."
Mr. Klapwijk said the telling statistic following yesterday's auction was the "scaling factor" of 91.8%, which
was the rate at which the bank could fill orders for given amounts of gold at its cutoff price after selling as
much metal as possible above that bid. A lower scaling factor would have meant that there were more orders
at or above $261.20, which would have demonstrated the demand gold bugs were hoping for.
Instead, the $261.20 was below even Friday's close on Comex, where gold had risen almost $5 in the past
two weeks. Some analysts and traders had even wondered aloud last week whether the negative effect of the
May 7 announcement of the British auction had already run its course.
After yesterday, they knew it hadn't. Some market watchers even said prices could fall to between $230 and
$240 per ounce -- generally considered the cost just to mine gold from the ground.
"A lot of mining companies can't even operate at that price level, particularly in South Africa and other parts of
the continent," said one veteran bullion trader. "What this is going to mean is a father going home to a village
in Ghana and saying, 'I don't have a job anymore.'"
The trader saw little hope for gold's recovering its losses by the end of the year, because the Bank of
England is planning a series of auctions until it has sold 400 tons of the metal.
Several dealers and the World Gold Council, a producer lobby, were highly critical of the U.K.'s gold sales.
They complain that the May announcement unnecessarily caused the price to fall from $281 an ounce,
devaluing the nation's gold reserves of 715 metric tons. The bank wants to reduce its holding to 300 metric
tons over the next few years, an official there says.
Clifford Smout, head of the foreign-exchange division at the Bank of England, says the auctions are the most
effective way to proceed. "It's better to sell the gold in an open and transparent way," he says.
The auctions are timely because they are taking place as the year 2000 approaches, when many people are
buying coins and jewelry as mementos, and amid the removal of the value-added tax on gold, he adds.

--- Page 74 ---
Page 74 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Wholesale bidders such as investment banks should in turn be able to easily place the metal with investors,
he says.
In other commodities trading yesterday:
GRAINS: Chicago Board of Trade grains and oilseeds slumped amid weather forecasts calling for ideal
growing conditions in the Midwestern U.S. The nearby July soybean contract fell to a 26-year low, dropping
16.75 cents to $4.1375 a bushel. July corn futures fell 8.75 cents to settle at $1.8775 a bushel, while July
wheat lost 4.50 cents to $2.3575 a bushel. Analysts said the selling was prompted by forecasts for cooler,
wetter weather in the Midwest, an about-face from Friday's six-to-10-day forecast of above-normal
temperatures. The new forecast "appears excellent for crop development," said Steve Bruce, a broker at
trading house ED&F Man in Chicago.
COFFEE: Warm weather in Brazil, the world's biggest coffee producer, sent coffee futures downward on the
Coffee, Sugar & Cocoa Exchange in New York. The nearby July contract fell to 3 1/2-year lows, closing down
5.90 cents at 92.05 cents a pound. Frost is typically a threat in Brazil at this time of year, but traders said
unusually mild temperatures have defused any immediate threat of crop damage, damping the
weather-related support that had helped keep prices above $1 a pound.

Fight Inflation, Not the Bond Market
Fight Inflation, Not the Bond Market
By Lincoln Anderson
1,253 words
1 July 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A22
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The Federal Reserve Open Market Committee, worried that strong growth and low unemployment may be
creating inflation, voted yesterday to raise the federal-funds rate by a quarter point. Concern over inflation is
justified. Inflation is never dead when a central bank has control over money creation. But we have learned
too much over the past 30 years and across different monetary-policy regimes to continue to believe that
real growth causes inflation. The Fed needs to turn its full attention to the requirements for cementing price
stability. Surely hand wringing over the low unemployment rate and sneaky efforts to put people out of work
and destabilize financial markets do not contribute to that process. It is time to review Fed tactics.
Back in 1987 when Alan Greenspan took over as Fed chairman, the benefits of price stability were not widely
appreciated in political, financial and corporate circles. Nor did individual investors or workers fully understand
the benefits that are now obvious. The lack of support led the Fed to avoid a direct assault on inflation using
the tool at their disposal -- the federal-funds rate. Rather, the Fed turned to using the markets to help with the
dirty work. By generating uncertainty in the bond market, risk premiums and bond yields can be pushed
higher. But why do that?
Phillips Curve
The answer lies in the Fed's general acceptance of the Phillips-curve model as the principal source of
inflationary pressure. This model postulates that rapid real growth leads to low unemployment, which pushes
up wages, forcing companies to raise prices. Given this model, the prescription for reducing inflation is to
slow the real economy. Raising the fed-funds rate -- the rate on overnight lending between banks -- is not
sufficient for that purpose. Interest-sensitive sectors in the real economy -- housing and business investment
-- are driven by mortgage rates and other long-term interest rates. Also, the Fed worries that a "bubble" in
equity valuations may be promoting excessive consumer spending. Long-term interest rates are used to
discount future earnings estimates and can drive equity prices.
So the Fed has embarked on a campaign aimed at forcing bond yields up and, if everything goes according
to plan, the economy is supposed to slow. To get yields moving up, the Fed has to inject risk into the bond
market. These days that is easy to do. Bond traders have learned the drill. As soon as the saber-rattling starts
at the Fed, the smart thing to do is sell. Traders know better than to stand in the way of coming capital losses.
This time around, long-bond prices have fallen 15% from their high in October 1998.
Traders and other bond holders are also genuinely unsure of the situation. When the Fed embarks on a
program to slow the real economy, nobody knows where it will stop. Does the funds rate have to go up 25 or
50 or 200 basis points? Nobody knows. Does real gross domestic product growth have to slow to 2% or the
unemployment rate rise to 5%? Nobody knows. In the resulting confusion, bond markets lose sight of the
anchors of fundamental value -- long-run inflation expectations, long-run real growth, the level of current and
expected real interest rates and long-run fiscal policy and debt supply.
All this would be endurable if the "real growth causes inflation" model made sense. However, there is ample
evidence that it does not. Over the last seven years the U.S. has enjoyed strong real growth while inflation
fell sharply. In the 1970s we had high inflation and high unemployment simultaneously. In fact, unless
preceded by surges in money-supply growth, real GDP growth and inflation are generally inversely
correlated. Across time and in many different countries, again and again, it has been shown that inflation is a
monetary phenomenon.
Other highly successful central bankers, like Hans Tietmeyer in Germany or Jean-Claude Trichet in France,
have stoutly resisted attempts to get them to endorse the "real growth causes inflation" model. They know it
will divert public and political attention away from the primary focus on controlling money-supply growth to
stop inflation. And they know that reliance on this model injects unnecessary risk into bond markets. Bond
yields in France, Germany, Italy and Spain, to name a few, are more that a percentage point below U.S.
yields.

--- Page 76 ---
Page 76 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Why, you might ask, if we have such a successful Fed policy, does the world's sole superpower, running
huge budget surpluses at all levels of government, with falling gold and commodity prices and a strong dollar
exchange rate, have much higher long-bond yields than the other G-7 nations? On June 11, with fear of Fed
tightening at a fever pitch, Wall Street bond traders were startled to see Greek government long-bond yields
below equivalent U.S. Treasury yields. Yesterday Greek 10-year yields were only 33 basis points above U.S.
yields. That only makes sense in a situation where investment horizons have collapsed to next week.
And, lest we forget, there is a pretty good chance that the focus on real growth causing inflation will divert
our attention from the real risk -- too much money chasing too few goods. For the past two years, money
growth, especially the broad aggregates M2 and M3, has been rapid. There are two sources of money growth
-- high-powered money (reserves and currency) and multiple expansion of that high-powered money through
bank lending. With two sources of money growth come two levers to control it. First, increase the
federal-funds rate -- short-term rates, not long-bond yields, control the growth of high-powered money.
Second, the Fed can discipline overly aggressive lending by banks. Time and again we have seen a "race for
the bottom" in the latter stages of an economic expansion as deposit-insured banks push the risk envelope to
keep loan growth high. The Fed should focus on its bank oversight responsibilities, not the unemployment
rate.
The easing last fall was probably a mistake. It sent the wrong message to the financial markets and to the
banking system. It smacked of fine-tuning, bank coddling and clearly underestimated the strength of the U.S.
economy. Now the Fed has raised the funds rate. But the Fed should emphasize that changes in the funds
rate are meant to control money and thereby inflation, not target the real economy.
Greece's Example
This shift in tactics would allow the Fed to move the funds rate, in a timely fashion, to control money without
introducing wide swings in bond yields. A central bank with true credibility should be able to move short-term
rates up and down without significant moves in long-bond yields. Greece has inverted its yield curve; why
can't we?
Emphasizing long-term inflation control rather than short-run real economy control would refocus bond
traders on long-run value. Stable long-bond yields would reduce risk premiums in financial markets and allow
U.S. bond yields to move to levels consistent with price stability and stay there. The U.S. bond market could
move from last to first place in the G-7. That would achieve Mr. Greenspan's stated goal of minimizing the risk
premium to help maximize our future prosperity.
Mr. Anderson, formerly director of economic research at Fidelity Investments, is chief investment officer at
LPL Financial Services in Boston.

Page 77 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Give It Back Now
REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Give It Back Now
1,255 words
30 June 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A26
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The news this week is that even President Clinton can no longer deny that Washington has been taking
excess taxes from the American people. But he still doesn't want you to know that he's fighting ferociously to
hold onto as much of that excess as possible, for spending programs that make taxpayers dependent on
Washington.
This is the big political picture behind Mr. Clinton's plan this week to deal with the "surprise" federal budget
surplus of some $3 trillion over 10 years, a mere $1 trillion over five. For some of us, this is no surprise at all.
Supply-side economists, notably Steve Moore and Larry Kudlow, have been predicting this revenue cascade
for two years. The White House denied the flood as long as possible, in our view to fend off calls for tax cuts.
But now that the facts are obvious, Mr. Clinton has joined the game with proposals to do everything but cut
taxes. Indeed, he is proposing a new entitlement binge unseen since the 1960s. What makes it so Clintonian
-- that is, slyly dishonest -- is that he wraps it all in the political disguise of "fiscal discipline" and "freeing our
nation from the shackles of debt." In fact, he is proposing to do the opposite.
Start with the poll-derived promise that the feds will somehow "reserve" most of the surplus for Social
Security. Unless Americans are given personal accounts with property rights to the FICA taxes they pay, this
is a fiscal fiction. There is no Social Security "lock-box" that somehow can't be raided by politicians.
All tax revenue -- income, FICA, other -- flows into the general Treasury and is either a) spent this year, or b)
used to pay off federal debt as it comes due. The pattern so far in this new surplus era is that politicians in
both parties find ample ways to do the first, so that each year's surplus turns out at the end to be smaller than
advertised.
Last year, Rep. Bud Shuster (R., Pa.) laid claim to hundreds of billions for highways, and this year he's back
at the trough for aviation. Senators Pete Domenici and Bobby Byrd recently staged a mini-raid on the surplus
to subsidize steel and oil and gas companies -- only hours after the Senate voted for its version of the
"lock-box." So much for the White House talking points that boast of "tough protections, including maintaining
the pay/go rules" that require 60 Senate votes to overrule.
But even the surplus that does survive to pay down debt does nothing for Social Security. Repaying debt
doesn't reduce federal pension liabilities by one penny. The Baby Boom's retirement is still going to have to
be paid out of federal revenues collected in the years when they retire.
It's true that a smaller national debt reduces federal interest costs, as if that money would be spent on better
purposes. But the national debt is already falling so fast as a share of the U.S. economy that it is
economically irrelevant. It has almost zero effect on interest rates, contrary to Mr. Clinton's claims. The only
virtue of debt reduction is that at least the money isn't being spent by Bud Shuster.
In any event, Mr. Clinton is proposing new entitlements that will absorb any surplus long after he's left office.
There's "a new $156 billion children's and education trust fund." Then there's $540 billion for "USA Accounts,"
which the White House advertises as a tax cut but are really another way to redistribute income to politically
favored constituents. They let politicians keep tax rates higher than they need to be so pols can take credit for
doling out annual cash grants to swing-vote soccer moms.
But the really Big Bribe is Mr. Clinton's promise of free prescription drug coverage for the elderly. We are
talking here about the largest entitlement expansion since Medicare itself in 1965. Yet White House
economics aide Gene Sperling actually said Monday that this "will not have a large cost." And the President
did not have sex with. . . .
The entire history of entitlements is that their spending explodes with what economists call "moral hazard."
This is especially true for drug therapies that are increasingly available for "life-style" choices, such as Viagra.
The tab is going to be hundreds of billions of dollars by the time the Baby Boomers retire.

--- Page 78 ---
Page 78 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The real political outrage here is that seniors would already have drug coverage if Medicare weren't a
Soviet-style bureaucracy. The private insurance market for younger Americans has adapted to the changes in
medicine since the 1960s to cover advances in pharmaceuticals. But Medicare responds only to political (not
market) incentives. And instead of reforming Medicare to change with the times, liberal Democrats have
made it more bureaucratic by imposing price controls on the services it does cover.
Despite denials, Mr. Clinton is now proposing to turn drug coverage over to the same Health Care Financing
Administration bureaucracy. That means, inevitably, price controls on drugs -- another of the liberals' longtime
holy grails. And that means turning drug companies into utilities, with regulated profits that will reduce the
incentives for R&D and thus for new, miracle drugs. Republicans should kill drug coverage that doesn't
include Medicare reform as a threat to modern health, the same way they did HillaryCare.
Which brings us to the only real political response likely to prevent this Beltway money grab -- a really big tax
cut. Republicans should stop tinkering around the edges and return the U.S. tax burden to normal peacetime
levels. The best economic bang for the buck would come from reducing marginal income tax rates by 20% at
least across-the-board. This also has the huge moral advantage of returning the money to those who earn it,
rather than those most successful at Washington deal-making. Happily, potential Republican presidents on
the stump are gravitating to this ideal.
Given the current Congressional realities, though, there remains the immediate imperative of keeping
politicians from grabbing the surpluses. In this spirit we'd support any bona fide tax cuts. We like the plan of
Senators Paul Coverdell and Bob Torricelli to raise the income threshold for the 15% rate, for one. A huge
expansion of the Roth IRA, including the repeal of income limits, would open a backdoor route to a
consumption tax. Death taxes help to ruin small businesses and deserve to be slashed, and do ultimately
impose high marginal rates.
We'd also encourage Senators Joe Lieberman and Orrin Hatch to return to the capital-gains gold mine.
According to a new study by David Wyss of Standard & Poor's DRI, their 1997 capital-gains cut to 20% was a
big economic winner. Mr. Wyss, who is no conservative, estimates that the cut will raise net business
investment by 1.5% a year. He also figures it's responsible for about one-fourth of the 30% increase in
stock-market prices since the law passed.
The broader point here is that it's time for tax cutters to throw deep. If they don't, the surplus will stay in
Washington forever and the main achievement of the Republican Congress will have been to make the era of
big government permanent.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Forget Tax Reform -- Reform Spending" -- WSJ July 8, 1999)

The Greenspan Rule
REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
The Greenspan Rule
872 words
29 June 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
In a country with inflation so low it's almost gone, it is intriguing to see the Federal Reserve on red alert, with
Chairman Greenspan madly signaling that the Fed in its meetings today and tomorrow may tighten money to
ward off inflation. Leading inflation indicators have been mixed, with May's flat consumer price report
canceling the April alarm. The best argument for a boost, maybe the only one, is that the Chairman wants it.
In his 12 years as Chairman, Mr. Greenspan has logged a record solid enough that we're prone to bet he
knows what he's doing. The question is, does the rest of the world know what Mr. Greenspan is doing? How,
precisely, does he decide when and how much to ease or tighten money?
Having spent much time ourselves deciphering the speeches, acts and runic utterances of one of this nation's
most successful Fed Chairmen, we figure Mr. Greenspan's technique goes roughly as follows: He fuels up on
a few tons of data, checks such vital readings as the consumer price index, the price of gold, the stock and
bond markets and the extent to which his countrymen are sounding irrational, exuberant, off on an
unsustainable track, or whatever.
Then he flies by the seat of his pants.
This approach has worked for the economy, and is of course politically handy for Mr. Greenspan. It lets him
make his own calculations, and the explanations of them let him throw bones to various constituencies. In
particular, it spares him having to actually shut down such nonsense as the apparently endless discussion in
some dusty quarters of the Fed over the Phillips Curve -- which wrongly preaches that high growth brings
inflation. Growth is good for an economy. It creates wealth, not inflation. The job of the Fed is not to decide
how much wealth the country should be allowed to create, but to keep the price level steady enough so it's
easy to create all we can.
Mr. Greenspan himself made clear in testimony to the Congressional Joint Economic Committee last week
that he doesn't think much of the Phillips Curve, or its sidekick notion, known as the non-accelerating
inflation rate of unemployment, or NAIRU -- which in the spirit of further nonsense argues that it's bad for
everyone who wants a job to actually have one (See related article: "The View From the Fed" -- WSJ June
29, 1999). The Phillips curve has "significant flaws," warned Mr. Greenspan, who also said that growth due to
normal rises in employment and productivity should not be seen as "anything other than a plus." Yet the
absence of a Phillips curve doesn't mean there are "no limits" to how fast employment can grow without
raising a danger of inflation. The Phillips curve doesn't generally apply, that is, but does right now.
Well, OK, we guess. Our worry is that once the 73-year-old Mr. Greenspan moves on, whoever succeeds him
may prove less skilled at off-the-cuff navigation. Word is that Mr. Greenspan might agree to stay, if offered
reappointment when his term expires in June 2000, with a Presidential election campaign in progress. But
there's no guarantee he won't at some point, say in a Gore or Bradley Presidency, be replaced by a Fed
Chairman without such sure instincts. Someone trying to imitate his methods might either actually unleash
inflation or, alternatively, crash the economy by picking the wrong time to follow the Phillips curve.
With Mr. Greenspan still in the Fed's top chair, this would be a good time for the Fed to try to codify his
technique. Some form of some clear rule for Fed decisions might not be more reliable than Mr. Greenspan's
pantseat, but would have the virtue of letting the rest of us understand what the Fed is doing, and giving us a
standard against which to measure future action. Most immediately, such a rule could provide enough
certainty to help the bond markets avoid the convulsions that come with trying endlessly to second-guess the
Chairman.
We know it's a tall order. The Fed's job is to keep the price level stable, and the challenge there starts with
simply figuring how to measure whatever it is we call the price level. Any rule amounts to a grab for certainty,
which can never be completely achieved in a fast-changing world. There is even a danger that the best of Mr.
Greenspan may become outdated by changes in technology and the economy that no one can predict right

--- Page 80 ---
Page 80 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
now. But these dangers pale next to the worry that Mr. Greenspan's departure, whenever it comes, might
leave us adrift.
In the long run a Greenspan rule could transform the talents of one man into a useful legacy for the future.
Mr. Greenspan has presided over an era of bounty, in which the Fed looked mainly to steadying the money,
and left the economy free to grow. That's a great way to navigate, and unless Mr. Greenspan plans to remain
Fed Chairman forever, it's time he tried to share his map.

Livestock Prices Decline on Supply Data by U.S.
Commodities
Livestock Prices Decline on Supply Data by U.S.
By Peter A. McKay
Dow Jones Newswires
868 words
29 June 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C15
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Livestock prices fell following a report showing there will be more animals to slaughter during the next six
months than market watchers had anticipated.
The bearish news battered one of the few commodities complexes that has performed well so far this year as
it has recovered steadily from a sharp decline in 1998. The lower prices left some analysts wondering
whether livestock prices might again begin to flatten under a long-term crush of supply.
On the Chicago Mercantile Exchange, front-month feeder cattle futures fell 1.5 cents to 75.92 cents a pound,
lean hog futures fell two cents to 48.875 cents a pound and pork bellies fell three cents to 41.72 cents a
pound. The two pork contracts fell by their exchange-imposed limits.
"There are really a lot more hogs out there than we thought, particularly for next fall and winter," said analyst
Dan Vaught of A.G. Edwards & Sons. "Really, the selling that we saw today didn't even capture the full
bearishness of the report."
Quarterly Agriculture Department statistics released Friday afternoon showed that the nation's hog inventory
so far this year is down about 3% from 1998 to about 60.5 million animals. However, that decline was a
disappointing one, since analysts had been expecting one at least 50% larger, Mr. Vaught said.
That means there are about 800,000 more hogs on U.S. farms than analysts expected. The average number
of pigs per litter also rose slightly to 8.8, signaling there could be an increased supply for at least six months
-- the amount of time it takes for a hog to mature to slaughtering age.
The bearish pork news dragged down cattle prices as well, since consumers' demand for the two meats is
often linked, analysts said.
"We've known for about a month that we have more than we need. The question has been whether that's built
into the price," analyst Scott Meyers of Pioneer Futures Inc. said of the pork prices. "Right now, the market is
saying no."
In a generally sluggish commodities market, livestock had been one of the few bright spots until yesterday.
Although the Bridge/CRB Index of 17 commodities futures is down 2.32 points for the year to 191.22 so far,
its livestock component is up 21.88 points to 208.58, said Robert Hafer, the index's managing director.
He said much of the livestock run-up can be characterized as a recovery from record-low prices that began
with an oversupply about a year ago. By January 1999, the price of live hogs had fallen to about nine cents a
pound, its lowest point since the 1940s. "The number of hogs going to market in the winter last year
overwhelmed the industry's ability to slaughter them," Mr. Vaught said. "Instead of negotiating prices, farmers
were negotiating with the packers for when they could deliver to the plants."
Yesterday's sagging prices left some analysts worrying that the market may again be headed in a similar
direction. Mr. Vaught said he remains bearish about pork prices, although beef prices could recover by year
end, particularly as the Labor Day barbecuing season approaches.
Nevertheless, the price drop couldn't have come at a worse time for Jim Odle, general manager of Superior
Livestock Auction Inc., Fort Worth, Texas. His firm is planning a four-day auction next week, in which more
than 1,000 farmers will sell 191,000 cattle via satellite.
"It's going to be hard on our customers, and the problem is they market one time a year," Mr. Odle said. "You
figure, if a farmer gets 1 1/2 cents per pound less than he would've gotten before on an 800-pound steer,
that's $12. That could be his profit right there."

GOLD: Futures closed at $261.50 an ounce, up $1.10, on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile

spurred by the announcement by Britain's central bank last month that it would sell a portion of its gold
reserves.
With the actual British gold auction scheduled to begin only eight days from now, yesterday's improved gold
price may mean that the ill effects of the sale have run their course, said analyst Vanessa L. Motto of CPM
Group.
"Really, as long as the price remains above $260, I think there's room for improvement," she said. "It's really
difficult to justify going further short at these price levels."
WHEAT: Futures closed slightly higher on both the Chicago Board of Trade and the Kansas City Board of
Trade following a week in which the contracts were battered by bearish forecasts of hot weather.
The front-month wheat contract on the CBOT closed 1.25 cents higher at $2.525 a bushel, and the KCBOT
front-month contract for a slightly different variety of the grain closed one cent higher at $2.825 a bushel.

The Fed's Inflated Expectations
The Fed's Inflated Expectations
By Michael J. Boskin
1,183 words
28 June 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A26
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
With the sort of attention usually reserved for royal weddings, the Federal Open Market Committee of the
Federal Reserve convenes today and tomorrow to determine whether or not to leave the current federal funds
rate unchanged. In the course of their deliberations, which closely resemble an academic seminar, the Fed
governors will take into account everything from the unexpectedly large increase in the April consumer price
index to its benign follow-up in May to Japan's first-quarter growth. The probable result of all this activity: a
rate hike of one-quarter of one percentage point.
Why so much attention to an event once known only to Fed watchers at investment banks? The reason has
little to do with whether and by how much the Fed raises the rate, though this will of course have some
bearing on the immediate direction of the markets. What really matters, however, is what the Fed's action
suggests about its view of the future of inflation.
After decades in which mainstream economists held that the costs of inflation were negligible, and that an
inflationary monetary policy could stimulate economic growth, it is now widely appreciated (thanks to the
work of Milton Friedman, Robert Lucas, Martin Feldstein and others) that stable, low inflation is essential to
longer expansions and stronger growth. Thus the intense speculation: Will tomorrow be the start of another
"long march," a 300 basis point increase such as began in 1988 and 1994 to ward off inflation? Is the Fed
going to reverse the 75 basis point decrease in interest rates in response to the last year's global turmoil and
the prospect of deflation?
The change in forecasts of what the Fed will and should do has been rapid. As recently as late February, the
Economist magazine ran a cover story worrying about the risk of global deflation; a few weeks later it
hawkishly editorialized that the Fed was late in raising interest rates. Behind these large swings in opinion lie
some unresolved questions about the economy, among them:
-- Why has inflation continued to decelerate? Is it due to a string of one-time favorable "shocks," such as low
oil prices, a strong dollar, and a steeper than anticipated decline in computer prices? Has that string run out?
-- Has the economy changed fundamentally? If so, is its growth potential being seriously underestimated?
-- Is the (alleged) short-term relationship between a tight labor market and inflation even more ephemeral
than previously thought?
-- Have measurement difficulties overstated inflation and underestimated real growth and productivity gains?
-- Is the American economy slowing down or likely to slow down on its own?
-- Are we in a financial bubble? If so, what if anything should monetary policy do about it?
-- Is the world economy finally recovering?
-- Where is inflation now, and where is it heading? What would tighter monetary policy accomplish?
The answers to these questions -- the last set in particular -- are far from obvious, not least because the
statistics that policy makers rely upon to measure inflation are not perfectly accurate. Thus, even after some
valuable improvements by the Bureau of Labor Statistics, the Consumer Price Index still overstates the
change in the cost of living, probably by about 0.75% per year. This bias is very much of a piece with the
rapid change in the economy due to technological change -- new products, quality and variety improvements
and changing buying patterns of consumers that outstrip the current measurement techniques.
The flip side is that real growth and productivity, even the recent strong gains, are underestimated. Core
inflation is thus running closer to 1% than 2%. Given the remaining bias in the price statistics, we are close
to a practical definition of the long-standing Fed goal of price stability.

--- Page 84 ---
Page 84 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Still, indicators of future inflation are mixed. Long-term interest rates, even after the recent retreat, do
suggest a modest upward tick. The best conceptual measure is the yield difference between regular and
CPI-indexed bonds, which has increased by over 100 basis points since its recent low last fall. But more
traditional measures, such as gold and commodity prices (other than oil), point in the opposite direction. In
any event, fundamental changes in the structure of the global economy, most importantly increased global
sourcing of everything from raw materials to production facilities, suggests the one-time favorable "shocks"
mentioned above contributed to the current low inflation but were far from the whole story.
What, then, should the Fed do? The goal of monetary policy should be to contribute what it can to
non-inflationary economic growth by keeping inflation low and stable. But monetary policy works with a
sizeable and variable lag; today's decisions will affect output and inflation several quarters down the road.
With inflation currently benign and indicators of the future mixed, there is no obvious course. A rate hike, two
at most, may be justified as a useful precaution; a more extended series of hikes, however, should not be
undertaken unless a major change in the inflation outlook unfolds.
But beyond the immediate question of a rate hike is the larger question of growth. Here, however, the Fed
must take a back seat. Stable, low inflation is a key input to long expansions and stronger growth, but it is
not the only input. Furthermore, the Fed's ability to control the rate of inflation does not mean it can also
dictate the pace of growth. Indeed, earlier Fed efforts to finesse the rate of growth on the theory that there
was a simple tradeoff between inflation and unemployment (the notorious Phillips Curve) led to the
disastrous stagflation of the 1970s.
If we are going to raise the nation's long-run growth potential, we must seek policy changes in the areas
where they are likely to strengthen non-inflationary growth: a simpler, broader, flatter rate tax system that is
less inimical to saving, investment, and entrepreneurship; slower growth of non-defense government
spending; entitlements reform; fewer and more flexible regulations; expanded open rules-based trade;
incentives in the civil justice system to substantially reduce the prevalence of frivolous lawsuits; private
market-style reform incentives to strengthen and improve elementary and secondary education and job
training. Even a rise of a few tenths of a percentage point a year in annual growth will make an enormous
difference in the long-term to improvements in the living standard and the affordability of entitlements.
Is it the Clinton administration's lack of leadership or outright opposition to most of these measures that tilts
public focus almost exclusively to the Fed? Whatever the case, I am confident that the Fed can and will be
able to adjust monetary policy to accommodate the higher rate of growth. What remains to be seen is
whether policy makers in the other branches of government will do their part to ensure future prosperity.
---
Mr. Boskin, a former chairman of the President's Council of Economic Advisers, is a professor of economics
at Stanford University and senior fellow at the Hoover Institution.

IBM, Hewlett-Packard ReboundAs Sluggish Market Looks to Fed
Abreast of the Market
IBM, Hewlett-Packard Rebound As Sluggish Market Looks to Fed
By Thomas Granahan
Dow Jones Newswires
1,015 words
28 June 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- The stock market is once again waiting for Greenspan.
And it is a good thing the wait will be over this week, because stock volume threatens to dry up. Friday was
the slowest day for Wall Street so far this year.
Blue-chip stocks did manage to reverse a four-day slide, as investors awaited the completion of this week's
meeting of Federal Reserve policy makers. A modest hike in interest rates is expected at the meeting on
Tuesday and Wednesday.
Big technology names and financial issues, two groups that got hit hard Thursday, showed some resiliency in
Friday's session. Shares of International Business Machines rose 9/16 to 123 1/8, Hewlett-Packard increased
1 3/4 to 92 5/8, American Express added 2 7/16 to 122 7/16 and J.P. Morgan gained 1 1/16 to 128 9/16.
"The sell-off [of early last week] does give the market a little room to rally, but the dialogue from the Fed
meeting will be the big concern," said Barry Berman, managing director of equity trading at R.W. Baird & Co.
It is that dialogue that has a big chunk of investors sitting out recent trading sessions. There is increasing
concern on Wall Street that even strong second-quarter earnings won't be enough to offset a potential series
of interest-rate increases. If it is evident the Federal Reserve is prepared to pull back all three rate cuts that
resulted from the global economic crisis last year, then stocks may have further downward adjustment.
"Unless [the Fed] says something real negative -- which would probably only happen if they raised rates [a
half percentage point] -- then I think the market will go back to focusing on economic data," Mr. Berman said.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, up more than 100 points intraday on Friday, gained 17.73 points, or 0.2%,
to 10552.56. Volume on the New York Stock Exchange was a weak 609 million shares, no doubt affected by
a hot summer Friday on the East Coast. Advancing stocks and declining issues were about even.
For the week, the Dow industrials dropped an even 303 points, or 2.7%, and the Standard & Poor's 500-stock
index lost 27.53, or 2%.
Big Board volume on Friday totaled just 609,753,900 shares, compared with 688,181,000 the day earlier.
In Friday's notable movers, Campbell Soup shares eased 5/16 to 42 1/8. The company said fiscal
fourth-quarter earnings will fall short of expectations. Mark Leckie, who was brought on about a year ago to
improve Campbell's U.S. soup business, resigned, signaling further uncertainty at the Camden, N.J.,
company's most important operation, according to analysts.
Shares of Lockheed Martin improved 11/16 to 35 7/8. The big defense company in Bethesda, Md., plans to
sell its militaryaircraft electronics businesses, parts of its information-services unit and an energy and
environmental services business, assets valued at about $1 billion.
Williams-Sonoma jumped five, or 17.2%, to 34. The San Francisco retailer reportedly turned down a takeover
offer earlier this month. The suitor was said to be coffee-house chain Starbucks, but analysts said
Williams-Sonoma has indicated it wants to remain independent. Shares of Starbucks lost 1 1/4 to 35 1/2 in
Nasdaq Stock Market trading.
Lucent Technologies said it will buy Nexabit Networks, a maker of high-speed Internet switches, for about
$900 million in stock. Lucent, a Murray Hill, N.J., communications company, will issue 14 million shares in the
buy. The stock closed off 5/16 at 63 11/16.

--- Page 86 ---
Page 86 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
American Electric Power fell 2 3/4 to 38 7/8. The Columbus, Ohio, company said it will fall short of earnings
goals because of costs from restarting an idled nuclear power plant in Michigan. American Electric Power
said it will spend $574 million to restart the Cook nuclear plant in Bridgman, Mich. That will reduce earnings
by 27 cents a share for the first half, 37 cents a share in the second half and $1.15 a share in 2000. The
company said its dividend won't be affected.
The highly anticipated initial public offering from Juniper Networks didn't let Wall Street down. The Mountain
View, Calif., company, which has developed a device to speed up Internet communications, priced 4.8 million
shares at $34 each, and the stock opened at $105. The shares closed at 98 7/8.
America Online shed 3 15/16 to 102 13/16. Merrill Lynch analyst Henry Blodget said the Dulles, Va.,
company is on track to meet or exceed analysts' earning expectations for its fiscal fourth quarter, but AOL
probably won't beat those views by as much as it has in past quarters. Mr. Blodget is concerned about the top
Internet-access provider's ability to continue to beat analysts' expectations by a wide margin, citing the
proliferation of alternative Internet-access models.
Copper stocks moved higher on word that an Australian conglomerate is shutting down just about all of its
North American copper operations. The industry hopes the move may relieve the oversupply of copper
around the world and boost prices. Shares of Phelps Dodge rose 3 5/16 to 61 11/16, and Freeport-McMoran
Copper & Gold rose 1 5/16 to 16 3/8 .
Shares of AirTouch Communications, San Francisco, rose 3 1/16 to 111. Vodafone Group plans to complete
its merger with AirTouch on June 30. In January, Vodafone signed an agreement to acquire AirTouch for $56
billion in cash and stock. The merger would create the world's largest telecommunications company.
On the New York Stock Exchange, there were 1,499 issues advancing, 1,448 declining and 593 unchanged
on Friday.
The New York Stock Exchange Composite Index was 623.34, down 0.63. The average price per share fell
five cents.

THE AMERICAS
Franco-Nevada,
Euro-Nevada Form
Canada Gold Giant
Canadian gold-mine-royalty companies Franco-Nevada Mining and Euro-Nevada Mining agreed to merge in
a share exchange valued at 1.78 billion Canadian dollars (US$1.21 billion), creating the world's fifth-largest
gold company ranked by market capitalization. Each Franco-Nevada shareholder will receive one share in
the merged company for each share held, while Euro-Nevada shareholders will receive 0.77 share for each
Euro-Nevada share. The two companies earn royalties on some of the world's biggest mines and other
natural-resource projects, the development of which they helped finance.
Euro-Nevada's chief executive said the merged company, to be called Franco-Nevada Mining, will provide
greater liquidity for investors, as well as more flexibility for future transactions. The companies, which have a
combined C$1 billion in cash, have a combined market value of about C$3.6 billion.
Repsol Clinches Tender Offer for YPF
Spain's Repsol joined the ranks of the world's top 10 oil companies as it clinched a $13.16 billion takeover bid
for Argentina's YPF. Repsol said a preliminary tally of its all-cash tender offer, which expired Wednesday after
being launched a month ago, showed 83.24% of shareholders sold their shares. That means Repsol will pay
out a total of $13.16 billion to take control of YPF. Repsol in January bought an initial 14.99% stake in YPF for
$2.01 billion from the Argentine government, and it now controls 98.2% of YPF, Argentina's largest company.
Colombia to Help Nonfinancial Firms
Colombia's finance minister, Juan Camilo Restrepo, said a new government-financed credit line for 1.6 trillion
Colombian pesos ($936 million) will be set up to help troubled nonfinancial firms restructure their debts. He
said 1.1 trillion pesos of the facility would be administered through the state Institute of Industrial
Development and the remainder through official export bank Bancoldex. Mr. Restrepo said the new funds
aimed "to help . . . in the restructuring of the debt of the nonfinancial sector so that [firms] benefit from longer
terms, so that those who are behind can put themselves up to date, so that they can reduce interest rates."
He told a bankers' association meeting that the arrangement would enable companies with past-due debts to
reach single agreements with all their creditors, under which interest rates would be cut, overdue interest
penalties pardoned and payment terms extended to a maximum of seven years.
Endesa Chile Holders Approve Shares
Shareholders of Chilean electricity generator Empresa Nacional de Electricidad approved, at an extraordinary
meeting, a $600 million share issue to be carried out within the next three years. The company's general
manager told reporters the issue would likely be launched this year. Board Chairman Pablo Yrarrazaval also
predicted the utility will post losses in the first half of 1999, primarily because of electricity purchases made on
the market to fulfill contract obligations.
BRIEFLY:
-- The International Monetary Fund approved a $512 million credit to Peru, which has pledged to use the
funds only during times of financial distress. An IMF spokeswoman said the Extended Fund Facility will
support the Peruvian government's economic reform program for 1999-2001.

EUROPE
British Aerospace Plans Restructure
British Aerospace will restructure its military-aviation operations, closing one plant and shedding 2,200 jobs
as it winds down production of older planes and begins full production of the Eurofighter. The moves come on
top of other cuts announced recently and as British Aerospace prepares to make final its acquisition of
General Electric Co. PLC's Marconi defense-electronics unit. The cuts will result in a one-time after-tax
charge of GBP 190 million ($300 million) and will increase earnings starting next year, the company said.
Allied Irish to Buy 80% of Polish Bank
Poland agreed to sell 80% of Bank Zachodni to Allied Irish Banks of Dublin, ending an era of Polish
commercial bank privatization and opening the way for coming consolidation. AIB, Ireland's largest bank by
market capitalization, agreed to pay Poland's State Treasury Ministry $582 million for Bank Zachodni, or 102
zlotys ($26.02) a share, pending regulatory approvals. AIB further committed to invest 850 million zlotys in the
bank, which is ranked ninth in Poland by deposits, over the next 10 years. The Zachodni sale, and
Wednesday's agreement to sell 52.09% of Bank Pekao, Poland's largest commercial lender, to a consortium
led by UniCredito Italiano of Italy, ends a wave of sell-offs. Only two Polish bank networks unlikely to be sold
remain in state control.
Poland Plans Telecom Privatization
The Polish government's planned sale of a strategic stake in telecommunications company Telekomunikacja
Polska SA could raise between $4 billion and $5 billion, or a premium of at least 60% over the company's
current market value, Communications Minister Maciej Srebro said. That would make TPSA's privatization the
country's biggest ever. Mr. Srebro said his assessment of TPSA's value reflects strong international interest
in Poland's telecommunications sector. The government plans to sell between 25% and 35% of TPSA to a
strategic investor this year, he said. A tender for bids for TPSA will open in late July or early August. He said
SBC Communications of the U.S. and France Telecom are "most interested" in buying the stake.
KBC Takes 66% Stake in Czech Bank
KBC Bankassurance Holding of Belgium, as expected, signed a contract with the Czech government to take
a 66% stake in Czech bank Ceskoslovenska Obchodni Banka for 40 billion koruna ($1.12 billion). At the
same time, the International Finance Corp., the World Bank's private-sector financing arm, said it acquired a
4.3% stake in CSOB for $75 million. KBC Chairman Remi Vermeiren, at a Prague signing ceremony, said
KBC expects to complete the financial portion of the transaction by mid-July.
Telia May See Cable as Merger Key
The proposed merger between state telephone companies Telia of Sweden and Telenor of Norway could be
severely hampered if the European Union insists that Telia sell its cable assets, people close to the Swedish
company warned. The merged company couldn't realize its Internet growth ambitions without its cable
operations, these people said. The European Commission last week launched an antitrust investigation into
the planned merger, and commission officials said divestiture of cable operations would help resolve
concerns about competition in Nordic markets. A commission spokesman yesterday declined to comment on
suggestions the deal would be endangered if Telia had to shed cable operations to secure merger approval.
BRIEFLY:
-- British computer-services company Logica said it has agreed to buy Team 121 of Britain for GBP 74.5
million ($118 million) in cash and shares, and plans a GBP 113.7 million share placement to fund recent
acquisitions. Team 121 is a computer-services company.

Goldman Sachs Group's Asian unit failed in its battle to take over Rajadamri Hotel of Thailand, raising its

EUROPE
British Airways
Chosen to Manage
Olympic Airways
British Airways' Speedwing unit has been selected to manage Greece's state-owned Olympic Airways until
the end of 2001, Greek Transport Minister Tasos Mantelis said. He told Greece's parliament the agreement is
for "a short-term management contract of up to 30 months under which [Olympic Airways] must turn profitable
and viable and join one of the leading airline alliances." He spoke after parliament approved legislation
allowing a foreign company to manage Olympic. He also confirmed that British Airways will have the option to
buy as much as 20% of Olympic's shares within a year of signing the contract. Speedwing's fee has been
agreed at $8.85 million, with further performance bonuses and the option to exchange the bonuses for a 1.5%
stake in Olympic.
Separately, the president of Polish airline Polskie Linie Lotnicze LOT said British Airways is interested in
taking a stake in the carrier. Poland will sell a 10% stake in LOT this year. Bidders have until July 23 to
submit firm offers.
Three French Bank Chiefs Hold Talks
The heads of Banque Nationale de Paris, Societe Generale and Paribas met for over three hours yesterday
under the aegis of French central bank governor Jean-Claude Trichet, but it was unclear whether they made
any headway in resolving the country's banking-takeover war. Chairmen Michel Pebereau of BNP and Daniel
Bouton of Societe Generale and Paribas Chief Executive Andre Levy-Lang made no statement, while the
Bank of France said only that "contacts will continue." Mr. Trichet called the meeting in an attempt to find a
"consensual solution" to the lengthy standoff between the banks. Societe Generale and Paribas were in the
process of completing an agreed $17 billion merger when BNP launched twin unsolicited bids for both banks
valued at $37 billion.
Pohjola, Skandia, Storebrand to Tie Up
Finnish insurance company Pohjola agreed to merge with insurers Storebrand of Norway and Skandia of
Sweden to create the largest nonlife insurance group in the region, further consolidating the once-fragmented
Nordic insurance market. The three-way merger would create a property-and-casualty insurance company
with assets of 51 billion Swedish kronor ($6.05 billion). Skandia will hold a 42% stake in the new company,
which hasn't yet been named, while Storebrand will hold 33% and Pohjola 25%. The three companies will
have equal voting rights, an important issue for Pohjola, which was reluctant to give up its independent status
in Finland. The combined company will control roughly 20% of the Nordic nonlife-insurance market.
Spain's GDP Rose 3.6% in 1st Quarter
Spain's gross domestic product grew 3.6% in the first quarter from a year earlier, the national statistics office
INE said. It also said GDP grew by a revised 3.7% in the fourth quarter, and was up a revised 4% for all of
1998; the INE previously reported fourth-quarter growth of 3.6% and a full-year GDP rise of 3.8%. An INE
spokeswoman said the revisions were partly because of changes in accounting standards aimed at
harmonization with the rest of the European Union.
Portugal Pressured on Its BSCH Veto

THE AMERICAS
Brazil Court Delays Monsanto's Seeds
U.S. agribusiness company Monsanto will contest a Brazilian court ruling last week suspending planting and
commercial distribution of its genetically modified Roundup Ready soybean seeds, the company said. The
Brazilian Institute of Consumers' Defense, a consumer group, won the suit. The court required that Monsanto
present an environmental impact study before proceeding with planting or distribution. Monsanto said it
"assures that all necessary legal procedures for approval of the Roundup Ready seeds were, are being, and
will be undertaken." Monsanto had expected to start planting the beans in September for harvesting early in
2000, following the registration of the seed species two weeks ago with Brazil's Agriculture Ministry.
Placer Dome to Cut Budget and Jobs
Canadian gold miner Placer Dome, reacting to weak gold prices, said it will reduce its exploration budget
and cut about 200 jobs, or 3% of its total staff. Placer Dome said it will cut exploration spending to $56 million
this year from $89 million last year, and corporate overhead will be reduced by about $10 million to about $35
million. The company will take a restructuring charge of about $15 million for the second quarter, it said. "We
have decided that in this price environment we will spend less on looking for new mines, and consequently
we are reducing our exploration and project development staff to a nucleus of key people," said John Willson,
president and chief executive officer.
Bacardi Files Rum-Brand Suit in Spain
Bermuda-based rum maker Bacardi said it has joined a suit by the original owners of the "Havana Club" rum
trademark to recover the brand name in Spain from a joint venture whose partners are the Cuban
government and French liquor company Pernod Ricard. According to a news release from the rum brand's
original owners, the Arechabala family, and Bacardi, the Havana Club trademark has been registered in
Spain since 1935. The trademark and the family distillery were confiscated without compensation by the
Cuban government of Fidel Castro in 1960. In 1993, Pernod Ricard and the Cuban government entered into a
joint venture to market Cuban rum under the Havana Club label in Spain and other countries around the
world. Bacardi's legal action marks an escalation in the war between the two companies over the right to the
trademark. Pernod Ricard couldn't immediately be reached for comment.
Chile's Stimulus May Take Some Time
An economic-stimulus and job-boosting package announced this week by Chilean President Eduardo Frei
isn't expected to severely upset the government's fiscal position. But analysts said its immediate impact will
be somewhat limited. Economists also point out that the 0.75 percentage-point cut in the interbank lending
rate to 5% that went hand in hand with the fiscal package won't accelerate economic growth until the end of
the year.

Discovering Inflation-Indexed Bond Funds
Fund Track
Discovering Inflation-Indexed Bond Funds
By Karen Damato
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,108 words
21 June 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C25
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Mutual funds that specialize in inflation-indexed bonds have a curious distinction these days: They are
probably the best-performing set of bond funds that investors have never heard of.
A tiny trio of such funds -- with combined assets under $100 million -- have delivered returns ranging from
1.52% to 3.92% so far this year. That's a lot in a period when rising interest rates knocked down the prices
of most bonds, producing a negative 0.51% average return for taxable bond funds tracked by Morningstar
Inc.
Even investors who know nothing about inflation-indexed bonds, however, may already have a stake in such
securities. Some well-known bond-fund managers, including Bill Gross of the $27-billion-inassets Pimco Total
Return Fund and Robert Rodriguez of FPA New Income Fund, have recently included these bonds in their
diversified portfolios. A spokesman for Pacific Investment Management Co., or Pimco, says Mr. Gross
expects to be an active participant in the next government auction of 10-year inflation-indexed Treasury
notes, scheduled for July 7.
Many individual investors, understandably, feel no great pressure to hedge their portfolios against inflation.
Inflation has declined over the course of the 1990s, and, after a blip upward in April, the government's
consumer-price index was unchanged in May. Prices in May were up an average 2.1% from the year before.
But even without a huge inflation threat right now, fans of inflation-indexed bonds say these securities make
lots of sense for individual investors. Inflation-indexed Treasurys are "the asset of choice" to combine with
stocks in a long-term portfolio, writes Jeremy Siegel, a finance professor at the Wharton School of the
University of Pennsylvania, in his book, "Stocks for the Long Run." The indexed Treasurys are "the safe
alternative to stocks," he adds, while uncertain inflation makes standard bonds risky for the long term.
The predominant assets in people's investment portfolios -- stocks and traditional bonds -- both tend to suffer
in periods of high inflation. That's a clear argument for including an inflation hedge. Meanwhile, compared
to some traditional hedges, such as gold, indexed bonds offer both inflation protection and decent returns.
Inflation-indexed Treasurys offer an effective yield of about 6% including the inflation adjustment. The
Treasury began selling inflation-adjusted securities in 1997, prompting some agencies and corporations to
follow suit.
Over time, Mr. Rodriguez of FPA New Income says, indexed Treasurys should deliver higher returns than
money-market instruments. Returns should be competitive with longer-term bonds, he says. Indexed
Treasurys do fluctuate somewhat in price but have significantly less volatility than long-term bonds.
Mr. Rodriguez says he loaded his $560-million-in-assets bond fund with indexed Treasurys last year to
"protect our principal value in the event that rates did rise" -- and to get a reasonable return besides. FPA
New Income's 3% return so far this year partly reflects the 37% of fund assets invested in inflation-indexed
securities, he says. Pimco Total Return, the nation's largest bond fund, has roughly 5% of its assets in
inflation-indexed bonds.
Inflation-indexed securities are simple in concept but rather complex in the details. The basic idea is that
investors can lock in a "real" -- that is, after-inflation-yield. On a 10-year inflation-indexed Treasury, for
instance, that real yield is now just under 4%.
The interest on indexed Treasurys is fixed in percentage terms. Now here's how the inflation adjustment
comes in: The bonds' maturity value rises in step with the CPI. And as the interest is calculated on the
growing principal value, holders get larger and larger interest checks. (A further complication: Investors
collect the increase in principal at maturity or upon sale, but owe income tax on it each year.)

--- Page 94 ---
Page 94 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Among the three mutual funds specializing in inflation-indexed bonds, Pimco Real Return Bond Fund has
been the best performer over the course of 1998 and this year.
Investors can also buy individual indexed Treasurys from brokerage firms and through the government's
Treasury Direct program. "I would just buy them directly from the Treasury," says Kent Newmark, portfolio
manager of Loomis Sayles U.S. Government Securities Fund, which has about 8% of its assets in indexed
Treasurys.
In weighing the attractiveness of inflation-indexed Treasurys, bond-fund managers typically compare the
yield on the indexed bonds (before the inflation adjustment) to the yield on ordinary Treasury securities of
similar maturities. Then the fund managers weigh that yield spread against their own expectations for
inflation. The yield difference, which individuals can track in this newspaper's bond listings, is currently
around two percentage points. Over time, "should inflation come in at a faster-than-2% clip, you would do
better . . . with the indexed bonds," explains David Schroeder, manager of American Century
Inflation-Adjusted Treasury Fund.

Inflation's Death Is Greatly Exaggerated
Inflation's Death Is Greatly Exaggerated
By David L. Littmann
988 words
21 June 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A26
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Testifying before Congress last Thursday, Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan strongly hinted that
the Fed will raise short-term interest rates when it meets a week from tomorrow -- despite the news
Wednesday that May's Consumer Price Index had remained unchanged from the previous month. It's the
right thing to do. To sustain America's economic expansion, the Fed will have to move pre-emptively against
the threat of inflation.
This view runs counter to that of many economists, who argue that in light of the good news on inflation,
there is no need for a rate hike and might even be reason to lower the rate from its current target of 4.75%.
These economists fall into three camps. First are the "new era" advocates, who say that productivity gains
spawned by the introduction of new technologies, the ever-greater mobility of labor, the remarkable leanness
of modern corporations and the globalization of the economy have offset the traditional inflationary
implications of tight labor markets. A second group believes that burdensome borrowing costs will
automatically correct growth rates that currently threaten price stability. Finally there are the gold bugs, who
are convinced that the falling price of gold fails to justify current inflationary jitters.
The new-era economists call to mind Irving Fisher, who more than 70 years ago wrote that the stock market
had reached a permanently high plateau. He, too, underscored the "new era" of restructuring, globalization,
technological change, better labor relations and Fed control over inflation. The 1929 stock-market crash
soon gave the lie to his argument.
The other camps are no less myopic. Those convinced of the self-correcting nature of onerous lending rates
fail to take into account that it can take several years before real interest rates on borrowed funds can
outpace inflation, by which time the correction is likely to be severe. And as for gold, prices at this time are
poor leading indicators of inflation. Gold prices are artificially low thanks to actual and anticipated sales by
international agencies and foreign central banks.
To understand why the Fed must remove the punchbowl before the party gets out of control, it is necessary
to understand the essence of monetary action. As the supply of money grows vigorously over a prolonged
period of time, households find themselves unusually liquid -- with more cash than usual relative to other
assets. This leads them to spend more. Strong retail sales, vibrant housing markets, better-than-expected
new-car sales and an exuberant stock market all testify to earlier growth in the money stock.
The same process works in the business sector. With greater consumer spending, firms discover that they
have a higher cash-to-inventory position. They, too, begin spending more, converting cash into hard goods,
thereby sparking an increase in flows.
In short, an increase in money stock, by throwing balance sheets out of kilter, will set off a series of increased
flows -- often with variable and disconcerting lags. Today's money stock is considerably greater than it was in
1996, and its growth is not abating. Indeed, the indications of a revival of inflation have never been more
widespread, even if they are somewhat disguised.
Thus, for more than two years, the Fed has exceeded nearly every target range it had previously set for
acceptable monetary growth. Annual growth of the Adjusted Monetary Base has risen to 9% this year from
5% in 1997. Upper range targets of 6%, set for M2 and M3 aggregates, have also been exceeded,
contributing to higher inflationary expectations, especially in the mortgage and government-securities
markets. Thirty-year mortgage rates are now above 7.5%, and 30-year Treasuries moved above 6%, the
highest levels in two years.
Still more worrisome is the CPI's year-over-year acceleration to 2.1% in May from 1.5% in January. And this
is not the only cause for concern. The ratio of people voluntarily quitting their jobs to total unemployment has
reached an eight-year high, suggesting that compensation packages will rise; retail sales continue to surge;
and, despite a stronger dollar, prices for imported goods rose for the third month in a row.

--- Page 96 ---
Page 96 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
With money growth outpacing economic growth by wide margins, why hasn't inflation already moved higher?
The answer lies in the wretched state of the world economy. By running a merchandise trade deficit
equivalent to 5% of GDP, the U.S. is purchasing goods and services from underused capacity in foreign
nations, rather than straining its own factories and work force. The willingness of American firms to move
overseas to tap cheaper sources of labor and materials has served as a daunting threat to labor and has
undercut the ability of retailers to set prices. Thus, despite superb auto sales, new-car prices are at their most
affordable levels in nearly two decades.
Fed governors must pray each day that the world economy won't improve dramatically anytime soon. But the
world economy is recovering. And while there is no immediate danger of inflation, it would be unwise to wait
until one appears. The economy is certainly strong enough to absorb a quarter-point hike; the last time the
Fed acted similarly, in March 1994, inflation was kept at bay and borrowing costs fell, making the U.S.
economy the powerhouse it is today.
Yesterday's monetary policy has consequences for the future. When the Fed cut rates sharply last fall, it
was applying, in somewhat panicky fashion, yet another layer of stimulus upon two successive years of
stimulative monetary policy. Reversing one-third of that added stimulus would not sink the economy.
Instead, by calming inflation fears in the mortgage and bond markets, such a move could avert yet higher
rates and extend the expansion. Why wait?
---
Mr. Littmann is a senior vice president and chief economist at Comerica Inc.
