These Ads' Missing Element? Solid Gold Timing
Commodities
These Ads' Missing Element? Solid Gold Timing
By Peter A. McKay
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,043 words
21 June 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The gold market just can't seem to get its timing right.
Smiling cruise ship passengers tip their top hats and wave through a shower of confetti and streamers in a
1929 photo being used in the World Gold Council's latest advertising campaign. Under the photo is the
headline "party crasher," referring to the stock-market crash later that year that ended such care-free
opulence.
But to a certain extent, the gold industry's campaign celebrating gold as a sound investment has also been
crashed lately, especially by the British government's announcement of sales of the metal that has driven
gold prices even lower.
Gold council officials shrug off the 9.6% drop in gold prices since the $3 million ad campaign debuted on
May 3 as not reflective of gold's long-term value as a hedge against inflation.
"Our ads are not so much about the value of gold as they are about the value of gold in your portfolio as a
portion of it. The council had been planning these ads since at least the fourth quarter of last year, and their
message really hasn't changed since then," says Pat Shaine, the account director handling the ads for
DellaFemina/Jeary & Partners Advertising Inc.
Still, the organization of gold mining firms has scrambled to develop new "tactical" ads responding to the
recent drop in gold prices. That decline apparently was just as unforeseen to the group when it started its
gold "image" campaign as any of the financial collapses in their ads were to investors of those eras.
Each installment of the trade group's image campaign pokes fun at some investing fad or points to a financial
calamity that struck when it was least expected. "It's a message that we think is timeless, regardless of what
else is going on," says Michael C. Barlerin, the gold council's chief executive for the Americas and Europe.
So far, the first three of five ads in the series have run, starting with the one on Tulip Mania, a 17th-century
investment craze in which tulip bulbs soared in value.
Gold council officials say they began brainstorming ideas for the campaign when year-end statistics showed
increased U.S. gold sales and strong demand for gold coins and bullion in 1998. Only print versions of the
ads have run so far, but related broadcast commercials are being developed as well, adds Mr. Barlerin. The
print ads' text concludes by comparing gold to investing manias, saying the precious metal lasts "forever."
The ads conclude: "In a volatile world, many smart investors keep a portion of their portfolios in the asset that
retains its value over time: gold."
However, gold hasn't even retained its value over the course of the ad campaign itself. Through last Friday,
the price of the nearby gold futures contract on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange
had fallen to $260.30 an ounce from $287.70 on April 30. A key development was the May 7 announcement
by the British Treasury that it would sell 125 metric tons of gold from reserves. Following news of the planned
sale, the gold council commissioned a public opinion poll in Britain and ran an ad saying it found that 54% of
the public disapproved of the sale.
But gold sales by governments appear to be a fait accompli. In the past three years, gold has lost more than
25% of its value, mainly because European central-bank sales have flooded the market, analysts say.
Investors have feared for months that the International Monetary Fund and the Swiss government may also
sell off some of their reserves.
John Ryding, senior economist at Bear, Stearns & Co., points out that gold prices have been in an "abysmal"
decline for almost 20 years. The front-month futures contract for gold has declined 70% since reaching its

--- Page 98 ---
Page 98 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
all-time high of $873 an ounce on Jan. 21, 1980, a plateau it has never approached since, notes Robert
Hafer, managing director of Bridge/Commodity Research Bureau.
"Gold is a great hedge to prevent you from becoming rich," scoffs Mr. Ryder when told of the gold council
ads.
In other commodities trading Friday:
SOYBEANS: Futures rallied to onemonth highs at the Chicago Board of Trade on concern that Midwest
weather could turn dry and hot.
For the second straight day, U.S. weather models showed a high-pressure ridge of heat blowing into Midwest
growing areas by late next week. On Thursday, soybean futures jumped to a higher open based on the
forecast, then fizzled. But on Friday, with the forecast unchanged and the weekend ahead, futures held onto
early gains.
Nearby July soybean futures climbed 11.75 cents to $4.7475 a bushel, the highest settlement for a
front-month contract since May 6. The rise was more than one-third the daily 30-cent trading limit.
Despite the hot-weather forecasts, Randy Fortenberry, an analyst with Frontier Risk Management in Chicago,
said he has to see similar forecasts for another eight to 10 days before he will worry about a more prolonged
drought.
"This needs to be sustained for a while," he said. "Unless forecasts look really drastic over the weekend, we'll
give some of these gains back on Monday."
By late Friday, Mr. Fortenberry said, some forecasters were already backing away from their earlier
predictions, saying that western corn belt temperatures late next week would reach only the low-tomid 90s,
and not approach 100 degrees.
SUGAR: World raw sugar futures rose to a 14-week high on the Coffee, Sugar and Cocoa Exchange,
reflecting buying by speculators, strength in the London market and Russian purchases, according to traders
and analysts.
The July contract rose 0.33 cent to 5.85 cents a pound. Much of the impetus for the rise in raw sugar came
from white sugar futures at the London International Financial Futures and Options Exchange, which have
risen recently on seasonal tightness in high-quality white sugar, according to Boyd Cruel, analyst at Alaron
Trading Corp. in Chicago.
-- Daniel Rosenberg and Brendan Coffey

How the `Masters of the Universe' LostTheir Glitter and Approached Nerdiness
Credit Markets
How the `Masters of the Universe' Lost Their Glitter and Approached Nerdiness
By Gregory Zuckerman
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,774 words
18 June 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
James Cusser, a mutual-fund manager, used to be a hit on the social circuit. So when he was asked for a hot
tip at a recent cocktail party for parents at a Kansas City, Mo., prep school, he wasn't shy. His pick: "I like
Coca-Cola `zeros.' "
Oh, Mr. Cusser is a bond-fund manager, not a stock-fund manager. An uncomfortable silence ensued. "All
eyes glazed over," Mr. Cusser sighs later. "At parties I'm going from the center of attention to the bond nerd."
So it goes in the bond world. Bond guys ruled Wall Street for much of the past two decades, the subject of
best-selling books "Liar's Poker" and "Bonfire of the Vanities." Now the bond business is troubled, and bond
professionals are getting less respect. Trading revenues have been flat in this decade, even as stock-trading
revenues have soared. Profits from bond underwriting fell in the first quarter, even as more deals got done.
And with every jump in stock prices-blue-chip stocks in the 1990s are up more than 420%, bonds
100%-inferiority complexes multiply.
The bond business continues to set the tone for stocks and the entire economy, including rates for mortgages
and auto loans. Indeed, on many days, bonds are the big story on Wall Street-such as yesterday when a rally
sent the benchmark 30-year Treasury yield back under the closely watched 6% level, for the first time in two
weeks.
But that doesn't mask the fact that the bond business has, like Austin Powers, lost its mojo. Bond trading and
underwriting have become commodity products in mature markets, and Wall Street's bond expertise is no
longer as valuable as it once was. As a result, profit margins on junkbond sales have sunk to 1.9% of
transactions from 2.8% in 1991. And some firms are fleeing the bond business: There are now 30 "primary
dealers," or firms bidding at auctions of Treasurys, down from 46 in 1988, albeit some of the attrition is due to
mergers.
"There's massive pricing and margin pressures [in the bond business] and just as soon as you think can't get
worse, it does," says Sallie Krawcheck, an analyst at Sanford C. Bernstein & Co.
A string of bond blowups in recent years, capped off by last summer's market crisis and the near collapse of
hedge fund Long-Term Capital Management LP, have Wall Street firms devoting fewer resources to the area
and keeping bond guys on a shorter leash. Salomon Brothers, now known as Salomon Smith Barney and a
unit of Citigroup Inc., even closed down its storied bond-arbitrage group last year. The unit, once led by John
Meriwether, founder of Long-Term Capital, at times raked in more profit than the rest of the firm combined
through big bond bets. But the firm no longer has the same appetite for risk, and doesn't see big profits in
gutsy bond bets. Robert Stavis, the former cohead of the unit, who earned $35 million in 1994, now runs
trendy Manhattan restaurants.
To be sure, fortunes are still made everyday in the $13 trillion bond market. And Salomon still has a growing
business buying and selling bonds. But bond profits are often coming from a market-share grab from smaller,
less-nimble bond players, and drastic cost cutting. Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc.'s Lehman Brothers, long a
bond kingpin, says it has cut its bond staff by 30% since 1994, when the company was part of American
Express Co. An industry conference for traders at the legendary Sleepy Hollow Country Club in Westchester,
N.Y., musters 150 attendees, down from more than 1,000 in 1990. Now, the traders are looking for a smaller
place to meet.
There are more-fundamental problems. Bond-broker clients like Jeff Welter are bigger than ever, and
demanding lower commissions, and more service, from Wall Street. Last month, Mr. Welter, who controls
$100 billion in bond investments at American Express's American Express Financial Services, warmly
greeted a visiting bond broker, one of many that come to his Minneapolis office each week hoping to grab a

--- Page 100 ---
Page 100 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
piece of his business. The broker was unhappy. Mr. Welter was sending fewer trades his way, and the broker
wanted to know why. Sitting across from the broker in his office, off American Express's trading floor, Mr.
Welter was blunt.
"Listen, when you give me a price it better be your best shot," he said. Mr. Welter told the broker he pays
60% less on commissions than he did a decade ago, because of more competition and more information
about pricing widely available. " 'I can't believe that,' " he says the broker replied. " 'You can't be serious.' "
Mr. Welter's retort: "It's true." In fact, profit margins are likely to keep shrinking as the bond market begins to
embrace online bond trading.
Meanwhile, few lucrative new bond markets have developed in recent years, other than debt derivatives.
While the advent of the junk-bond and asset-backed securities markets in the 1980s helped bond profits
surge, most bond markets are now mature. Bankers speak of potential profits from the developing European
capital markets, but so far the profits haven't been huge.
How have things changed? Michael Milken, the former Drexel Burnham Lambert Inc. junk-bond king, made
$550 million in salary and bonuses in 1987. Last year -- a brutal period in the bond business-Lehman
Brothers had net income of less than $400 million from its entire bond business, according to analysts. The
firm declined to comment. Headhunters say no one came anywhere close to the $100 million salary mark last
year.
In the process, the business has become less glamorous. Salomon pampered its bond guys in the 1980s and
early 1990s, delivering catered lunches to senior executives, who in turn ordered vats of free food for traders.
Today, Salomon no longer pampers its bond team. Instead of a catered meal, a visitor recently was offered a
box of Krispy Kreme doughnuts.
David Horner, a senior bond economist Merrill Lynch & Co., knows firsthand how prestige has plummeted.
His 29-year-old daughter, Katya, a paralegal in a Houston law firm, recently began trading stocks online.
Earlier this year she turned in a 30% gain in a month trading highflying Internet stocks. Now she phones her
father, a 30-year veteran of the bond market, and playfully teases him about his choice of careers.
"My dad doesn't listen to me, but he needs to get into the stock market and give up this bond stuff," she says
with a laugh.
Some investors go to unusual lengths to avoid bonds. Richard Fichtel, a Rutherford, N.J., investor, coaches
his eightyear-old son on the merits of individual stocks, mutual funds-and fixed-income investing. At a recent
gathering with friends, Mr. Fichtel asked his son: "Christopher, what are bonds for?" His quick response:
"Bonds are for LOSERS!"
More troubling, the best and brightest, like Richard Courtemanche, are beginning to turn up their noses at the
bond market. After recently receiving his MBA from the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, Mr.
Courtemanche, 29, planned to jump into the bond-trading fray.
But an eye-opening summer on Goldman Sachs Group Inc.'s bond-trading floor last year changed his mind.
Sitting next to a senior trader, counting down to the release of key monthly employment data early one July
morning, Mr. Courtemanche braced himself for some gutsy wheeling and dealing. The data came out and the
trader swung into action, reaching for his phone to buy bonds from a client. "This is what I've been waiting
for," Mr. Courtemanche recalls thinking, as the trading floor erupted with noise.
After buying the bonds, the trader immediately rose from his chair and screamed: "I've got bonds for sale!"
The trader, skittish about holding on to the bonds for too long, was scrambling to get rid of the bonds. Mr.
Courtemanche's heart sank.
"I had a vision of market wizards taking big positions, but they were hitting singles instead of swinging for
homers," he says. "They were just taking orders and hedging risk-it was much less exciting than I expected."
(Mr. Courtemanche accepted a job trading stocks for Dresdner Kleinwort Benson in London.)
Not to worry, some say. Profits will rise in the bond world as firms get out of the business, leaving less
competition for everyone else, some analysts say. And the outlook for bond issuance from Europe and Asia is
good. Meanwhile, stocks won't go up forever, bond professionals point out.
"This business always ebbs and flows," says Lewis Ranieri, the father of the mortgage-backed bond market
and now a private investor. "It's not like bond {pros} are selling their homes in the Hamptons and buying used
cars."
At least not yet.
---

--- Page 101 ---
Page 101 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Treasury Securities
Treasurys rocketed higher on soothing words from Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan that traders
interpreted to mean a series of interest-rate increases is unlikely.
In late trading, the bellwether 30-year Treasury bond was up 1 9/32, or $12.8125 for a bond with a $1,000
face value, at 90 2/32. The bond's yield, which moves inversely to the price, fell to 5.958%, the first time the
long bond's yield dropped below the key 6% level since June 8.
In testimony before the Joint Economic Committee of Congress, Mr. Greenspan said that when possible, Fed
policy makers should be "pre-emptive" in fighting inflation. Traders said Mr. Greenspan's comments
indicated that the Fed will raise rates at its June 29-30 meeting.
But the Fed chairman hinted that rate increases could be limited, easing market concerns that policy makers
would reverse all three rate cuts made last year. Specifically, Mr. Greenspan said "modest preemptive actions
can obviate the need of more drastic actions at a later date."
Mortgage Securities
Mortgage-backed securities had one of their best performances ever yesterday, compared with the 10-year
Treasury, traders said.
Prices of mortgage securities soared and yield spreads narrowed in relation to those of Treasurys. Among
active issues, Freddie Mac Gold 7% securities were at 99 12/32, a gain of 24/32.
The bond rally on Mr.Greenspan's testimony also benefited mortgage issues. Traders said transactions by
several large funds helped lift mortgage securities prices substantially, as did dealer repositioning.

Page 102 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Fed's Curio Closet
REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
The Fed's Curio Closet
977 words
15 June 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The U.S. economy is thriving. Creativity and new technologies abound, people are working in record numbers
and even the battered world economy shows signs of pulling out of crisis. This is all good news. But the mood
now could hardly be called exuberant. Everyone is worrying about inflation.
Or, would it be more accurate to say that everyone is worrying about the Fed worrying about inflation?
This distinction matters, because there are signs the Federal Reserve is worrying about inflation for the
wrong reasons. While modern markets keep trying to move on to the 21st century, the Fed won't let go of
such hand-cranked notions as the Phillips curve, or NAIRU, the non-accelerating inflation rate of
unemployment -- a concept as dense as its name.
These models ply the idea that there is a tradeoff between unemployment and inflation: that you can have
fast growth, or you can have low inflation, but you can't have both at once. So to fight inflation, stamp out
growth. Or at least stamp out "overheating" -- whatever that may mean.
This is nonsense. Good growth with low inflation is exactly what this country has been enjoying for years
now. During the late 1970s and early 1980s, this country had high inflation and low growth -- the very
opposite of what the Phillips curve would imagine. Over the past 20 years, the Phillips curve has failed
completely to serve as a model with any predictive power.
Phillips-curve economics never made much sense, even in the days when it seemed to coincide somewhat
better with reality. Inflation is a matter of price level, and depends on the supply of money (and the demand
for it, which confounds simple-minded monetarism). So, you might think the Fed would shelve the Phillips
curve in some library of quaint but useless curiosities, maybe next to the flat-earth exhibit. Nope. As one
senior Wall Street banker notes, some of these folks grew up with the Phillips curve implanted in their brains.
Fear of too much growth was the basic reason for the Fed's warning last month that its policy had "tilted"
toward fear of inflation. Tighter money might soon be called for, said a Fed press release: "against the
background of already tight domestic labor markets and ongoing strength in demand in excess of productivity
gains." (Translation: Lots of Americans are employed, and we'd better soon put a stop to that.)
It makes sense, of course, to be alert to real signs of inflation -- which disrupts commerce and costs the
economy. Certainly the Fed needs guideposts in deciding how fast to create money. The place to look,
however, is not the Fed's curio closet, but the market itself. Folks with real money on the line every day have
lots of incentive to pay attention not to Phillips-whatevers, but to real life, and their judgments will be reflected
in the prices they pay to clear the market.
There, the signals on inflation are mixed. As the nearby charts show, the Consumer Price Index rose
noticeably last month. Much of that change appears to have been a one-time rise in the price of oil. And as a
measure of price level, the CPI itself falls well short of telling us what's actually happening in the way of
quality of life and productivity in this country, where the hard-to-quantify service industries increasingly
dominate. Certainly the May CPI number, due out Wednesday, bears watching. But nothing here so far
warrants panic.
On the contrary, the price of gold has been hitting record lows, suggesting not inflation, but deflation. With
the exception of oil, where the price has been driven up by OPEC production curbs, other commodity prices
have also been weak. And the dollar, despite some bad days recently, remains high relative to other major
currencies this decade. Falling commodities and high foreign exchange rates are signs of tight money, not
easy money.
In quest of other inflation indicators that start to steer clear of Phillips-thinking, one intriguing measure
involves some of the relatively new inflation-indexed bonds that Treasury began issuing last year. As Fed
Chairman Alan Greenspan has repeatedly observed, the spread between yields of these inflation-indexed

--- Page 103 ---
Page 103 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
bonds and fixed-rate Treasurys offers a theoretical measure of what the market expects in the way of
inflation. To the extent this is a valid indicator, it does suggest an inflation uptick; see nearby chart, where
the shaded area indicates the spread. Back in early October of 1998, the difference in yield between
inflation-indexed and fixed-rate, 30-year bonds reached a low of only about 1.29% -- suggesting expected
inflation of 1.29%. Since then, this spread showing expected inflation has widened to 2.38%.
As indicators, though, even these bonds come with caveats, as Chairman Greenspan keeps noting. For one,
the flexible-rate bonds are indexed to the CPI, itself a faulty measure. Also, given limited experience with this
measure, we're not sure to what extent the spread may include market expectations of what the Fed might do
next. It was also at about this level a year ago, when there was a lot of talk about a NAIRU-induced rate
increase. When the Fed began signaling it would instead cut rates, and then did so because of the
international economic crisis, the spread fell.
The rationale for Fed policy matters a great deal. If it seems justified by tomorrow's price announcement and
related to actual market prices, an interest rate boost would not reverse economic growth or roil markets. But
if the Fed is getting on the NAIRU bandwagon, making growth itself the enemy, we would be in for a series of
tightenings until we get the recession this perverse ideology demands.

A Heroine, but Not by Accident
A Heroine, but Not by Accident
By Douglas Brinkley
874 words
15 June 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
For all the controversy she sparked 44 years ago, these days everybody seems to love 86-year-old Rosa
Parks, who made history on Dec. 1, 1955, when she was arrested in Montgomery, Ala., for refusing to give up
her seat on the Cleveland Avenue bus to a white man. Her courageous act touched off a 381-day bus boycott
led by the Rev. Martin Luther King Jr. and helped spark the civil-rights revolution that followed.
Since then Mrs. Parks has become an unimpeachable national hero in a cynical age. She received a standing
ovation at this year's State of the Union address and was blessed by Pope John Paul II when he celebrated a
mass in St. Louis last year. Dozens of streets and public schools across the country have been renamed in
her honor; next year, the city of Montgomery will open a Rosa Parks Museum and Library on the very corner
where she was arrested. Topping even that, today she will receive the nation's highest civilian honor: a
Congressional Gold Medal in recognition of the "uncommon courage" she showed in standing up to racial
segregation.
But what do we really know about this enigmatic crusader? Like so many cultural icons, Rosa Parks is
shrouded in myth, hers that of a tired seamstress who, like so many others, one day just said no.
The truth, of course, is more complex -- and more inspiring. "It was not that I was fed up in 1955," she
explained recently. "I had been fed up my whole life as far back as I can remember with being treated [as]
less than a free person." In fact, the "tired seamstress" was a veteran civil-rights activist by the time she took
her bold stance on the bus.
She had become secretary of the Alabama chapter of the National Association for the Advancement of
Colored People in 1943, the same year she was thrown off a Montgomery bus for the first time, in that case
for boarding through the front door rather than at the back (and, as it happens, by the same bus driver who
would have her arrested 12 years later). While serving as secretary, she had tried to register to vote on three
different occasions, and was turned away each time. Her political awareness only grew and hardened after
World War II, as she saw how her brother Sylvester, still in uniform, was treated by bigots when he returned
home after serving with honor in both the European and Pacific theaters.
That experience set her to monitoring cases of violence against veterans and other blacks in Montgomery,
and in 1949 she also began advising the NAACP's Youth Council on maintaining one's dignity in the face of
white-supremacist attitudes. Then, two months before her renowned arrest, Mrs. Parks learned that an
all-white jury had freed the white men who for kicks had murdered Emmett Till, a black teenager from
Chicago unfortunate enough to have tried to pass through their Mississippi town. It was another blow upon
the bruise left earlier that year when three other black women were arrested for refusing to give up their bus
seats. E.D. Nixon, a Pullman porter and president of the Montgomery NAACP, had decided that these
women's civil disobedience would not make for a good enough court challenge. Soon, however, he found a
perfect one right in his own office: that of his associate, Rosa Parks.
When Mrs. Parks refused to give up her own bus seat, she knew it was very likely she would be fired from her
job, as perhaps would her husband. But by then she could no longer help but be blinded to her personal
interests by anger at the white citizens who continued to perpetuate violence against blacks and their white
sympathizers.
By 1957 a long series of death threats forced her and her husband to leave Montgomery for Detroit, where
Mrs. Parks, a widow since 1977, has resided ever since. But she hardly shunned politics in the aftermath of
her bold act, participating in the 1963 March on Washington and in the 1965 civil-rights march from Selma,
Ala., to Montgomery. That same year she began working as a congressional assistant to Democratic Rep.
John Conyers of Michigan, with whom she remained until her retirement in 1988.
In "Parting the Waters," Pulitzer Prize winner Taylor Branch wrote that "Rosa Parks was one of those rare
people of whom everyone agreed that she gave more than she got. Her character represented one of the

--- Page 105 ---
Page 105 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
isolated high blips on the graph of human nature, offsetting a dozen or so sociopaths." Now that the 106th
Congress has awarded her a richly deserved gold medal for her contributions to the nation, it is important to
recall precisely what such a character is made of, and where it can lead.
---
Mr. Brinkley is director of the Eisenhower Center for American Studies and a professor of history at the
University of New Orleans. He is writing a biography of Rosa Parks for the Penguin Lives series.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: The Training Of a Heroine" -- WSJ June 30, 1999)

Art & Money:All That Glitters Isn't Expensive
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Art & Money: All That Glitters Isn't Expensive
By Eileen kinsella
787 words
11 June 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
W14
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The recent sharp drop in gold prices may mean very good buys on jewelry this fall and holiday season.
To date, retailers have been reluctant to slash prices, despite gold's steep slide this year. But now that prices
are hovering at historic lows -- gold prices slipped to their lowest point in 20 years on the New York
Mercantile Exchange last week -- some retailers concede that consumers may see some bargains at the
jewelry counter in the months ahead.
"The consumer will eventually see a reduction in prices," says Paolo Novembri, president of Gori & Zucchi
Inc., an American subsidiary of Italy's Unoaerre, the largest goldsmith in the world.
That could mean a lot more glitter come the new millennium. Beryl Raff, president and chief operating officer
of Irving, Tex.-based Zale Corp., one of the nation's largest jewelry retailers with 1,100 stores, says that,
assuming the gold market doesn't stage a recovery, "we might look at an item where the ticket is a little high,
such as $399, and all a sudden we are able to offer it for $299." The company, she says, will "be in a position
to offer the consumer a terrific value this holiday season."
Some industry experts say shoppers may see other benefits too, including improved selection and
better-quality gold in the jewelry they buy. "It's not an uncommon tactic in retail to change from 14-karat gold
to 18-karat gold," to give the consumer more value when metal prices soften, says Michael Exstein, a retail
analyst with Credit Suisse First Boston.
It may be worth it to postpone gold-jewelry purchases. "In a sense, it's the worst time to buy [jewelry right
now] because the price of the actual gold has gone down, but you're still paying the same for the object,"
says Paul Walker, director at Gold Fields Mineral Services Ltd., a London-based precious-metals research
firm.
Says Mr. Novembri, "There is always a lapse of time between the change of the gold price and the actual
reflection in the retail jewelry market." His best guess for when retail prices should start to drop: "at least three
months" from now.
David Rocha, deputy executive director of Jewelers of America, a New York trade group, agrees. "It's
probably going to take at least a season," for lower prices to kick in.
Where will the best deals be? Most likely on simple pieces -- chains, stud earrings, unadorned cuff links.
Jewelry with high gold content and relatively low labor costs will probably see the biggest price cuts, says Mr.
Novembri of Gori & Zucchi.
But don't look for bargains on ornate pieces such as pave brooches. Ornate jewelry or pieces that include
gemstones probably won't be affected as much. That means some of today's most fashionable styles are
likely to retain their high prices. (Gold with "pave" diamonds -- lots of tiny diamonds set close together -- is
especially popular now, says New York-based jeweler David Yurman, whose pieces are sold at Saks Fifth
Avenue.)
A combination of factors, including the Asian financial crisis, has pushed down the price of gold, analysts
say. But the main cause is that gold, long regarded as a hedge against inflation, has seen its price fall along
with the inflation rate. And, one month ago, worries about an oversupply of gold were magnified when the
United Kingdom announced it was selling a large part of its gold reserves.
Gold peaked at about $875 an ounce in January 1980, traded slightly under $400 an ounce for much of this
decade, and hasn't broken above $300 this year.

--- Page 107 ---
Page 107 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
With such a drastic decline in gold prices, it seems like a cut in jewelry prices would be a no-brainer. But
Richard Wheeler, owner of Panache Jewelers in Los Gatos, Calif., says the fall in gold prices has been
"somewhat slow to show up" for consumers. He notes: "It always seems that jewelry prices go up a lot faster
as gold rises, then they come back down."
Labor costs add a big markup to the finished product. Even significant drops in the pure-gold price can
translate into a mere $2 difference for some pieces of jewelry, especially if it has diamonds or other
gemstones, says Mike Paolercio, treasurer of Michael Anthony Jewelers in Mount Vernon, N.Y.
He dismisses the idea of letting jewelry purchases be influenced by moves in the precious metal.
"The price differential is not that much and most people buy jewelry for fashion reasons," says Mr. Paolercio.
"Don't wait. Buy it now, enjoy it now."

Yield on U.S. Treasury Bond Closes Above 6%Amid Concerns Fed Might Raise Interest Rates

Yield on U.S. Treasury Bond Closes Above 6% Amid Concerns Fed Might Raise Interest Rates
By Gregory Zuckerman
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
897 words
10 June 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C20
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Investors continued to shy away from Treasurys, pushing the yield on the benchmark 30-year
Treasury bond above the key 6% level for the first time in 13 months amid a surge of corporate bond sales
and continuing worries that the Federal Reserve will raise interest rates later this month.
In late trading, the 30-year Treasury bond fell 13/32, or $4.063 for a bond with $1,000 face value, at 89 1/4.
The bond's yield, which moves in the opposite direction of its price, rose to 6.022%.
Yesterday was the first time the long bond finished the day above the key 6% level since May of last year.
The yield on the 30-year bond briefly hit 6% on Tuesday before finishing the day at 5.99%.
Shorter-term securities also fell. The five-year note, for example, fell 9/32 to yield 5.787%.
The long bond's yield has climbed from 5.08% since February, hurting all types of bond investors, as worries
about rising inflation have sparked concern that the Federal Reserve will soon boost short-term interest
rates by at least one-quarter of a point.
Although yields rose, few investors showed willingness to buy bonds ahead of major economic reports, and
amid worrisome comments from Fed officials. Recently, various Fed officials have suggested they are
leaning toward raising rates when the Federal Open Market Committee meets June 29 and 30.
"The new open-mouth policy means they almost have to follow through [and raise interest rates], or they
might lose credibility," said Scott Grannis, chief economist at Western Asset Management Co. in Pasadena,
Calif.
Tomorrow's release of the producerprice index and retail-sales figures and next week's release of May's
consumerprice index, will likely weigh on the Fed's decision, but economists said only benign data will stave
off a rate increase.
"If retail sales are weaker than expected and the CPI is way down from last month, they would be hard
pressed to tighten, but it would require both numbers to come in on the good side and that's asking a lot," Mr.
Grannis said. "I don't think they should raise rates -- gold has had a monster decline in price, the dollar has
been very strong, and the last nine years have demonstrated that growth doesn't bring inflation-but they're
likely to move."
Some investors are gearing up to buy Treasurys if the Fed in fact raises rates later this month. "If they wait
until late summer or the fall to raise rates, they may lose the advantage on inflation, but if they move in June
they may only have to raise rates by one-quarter of a percentage point, and that could spark a rally in
short-term and intermediate securities," said Jim Somers who manages $500 million at Martindale Andres &
Co. in West Conshohocken, Pa.
Corporate Bonds
Corporate issuers priced nearly $3 billion in new debt, and another $2 billion expected to hit the market today.
The supply deluge pressured the secondary market, traders said.
Yesterday's deals included a $1 billion two-part offering from Marsh & McLennan.
A $600 million, five-year portion priced at a yield margin of 0.93 percentage point above Treasurys, while a
$400 million, 10-year part priced at a yield margin of 1.25 percentage points above.

--- Page 109 ---
Page 109 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The deal was rejiggered, with the fiveyear part boosted by $100 million and the 10-year cut by the same
amount.
"There was significant demand in the fives, enough to get us to rebalance the trade a little bit," said John

Also Bank of America priced $1.5 billion of five-year global notes (rated double-A2 and single-A-plus) Aa2/A+

The deal, which was increased from $1 billion, was quoted unchanged in secondary dealings.

Treasurys.
Separately, Seagram is planning to issue $1 billion of 7% to 7.5% convertibles known as adjustable

which is expected next week.

convertible at any time prior to the maturity.
Seagram will issue 18.5 million shares, according to the officials.

NEW YORK -- The dollar fell against the yen and euro in line with early declines in stocks and bonds, after a
stronger-than-expected manufacturing report increased speculation Federal Reserve will raise interest rates
to cool the economy.
While the Dow Jones Industrials Average ended up, it spent most of the day in negative territory. As U.S.
assets show weakness, "there are certain concerns by market participants about whether the U.S. is the
place to put money," said Jeffrey Yu, vice president at Sanwa Bank in New York. "The idea is, the U.S. can't

The dollar's recent declines put it more than four yen below its 1999 high of 124.75 yen reached less than two
weeks ago.
But in the view of Alex Beuzelin, market analyst at Ruesch International in Washington, it is still too early to
tell whether sentiment on the dollar has shifted. "The U.S. economy continues to grow. Inflation pressures so
far remain benign," he said.
"With the implication of tighter Fed policy, the dollar tends to track stocks and bonds. But over the medium
term and longer term, higher interest rates are dollar-supportive," he added.
In late afternoon New York trading, the dollar was at 120.63 yen, down from 121.70 yen Monday in Frankfurt
(New York and London markets were closed Monday). The euro was trading at $1.0451, up from $1.0437.
The pound rose to $1.6118 from $1.6028.
Near midday Wednesday in Tokyo, the dollar was trading at 120.59 yen. The euro was at $1.0451 and
sterling was quoted at $1.6108.

higher-than-expected 55.2 in May from 52.8 in April and the highest reading since October 1997. The
prices-paid index rose to 52.2 in May from 49.9 in April, its first reading above 50 since December 1997. The
numbers added fuel to inflation fears.
The euro continued to rise from its low of $1.0396 hit Friday. Bearish sentiment continues to dog the
currency, though it found some near-term support as players took profits off the euro's fall last week and
squared weak short positions ahead of the European Union summit later this week.
The dollar's decline against the yen started even before the purchasing managers' numbers were released,
on a report -- later denied -- that Japanese Prime Minister Keizo Obuchi might suspend the nationwide sales
tax. Despite the government's quick denial, the report in the little-known Nikkan Toshi Shimbun was enough
to roil a jittery market on the thinking that suspending the 5% tax might help boost Japan's ailing economy.
The pound rallied on data showing the United Kingdom purchasing managers' index for manufacturing set a
13-month high of 49.4 in May, from 48.1 in April. A reading below 50 shows contraction, a reading above 50
shows expansion.
The Australian dollar fell on concern about the nation's widening current-account deficit and as gold prices hit
new 20-year lows. The Australian dollar fell to 64.38 U.S cents from 65.24.
The South African rand strengthened slightly despite the fall in gold, buoyed by the calm leading up to
today's national elections. The dollar was valued at 6.2050 rand, down from 6.21 rand a day earlier.

The euro has declined more than 10% since its launch in January. Gloomy eurozone growth prospects, the
conflict in Kosovo and the U.S. yield advantage all have weighed on the common currency.
Tim Fox, currency strategist at Standard Chartered Bank in New York, said European Central Bank
President Wim Duisenberg's admission that "developments [in the euro] up to now haven't caused any
sleepless nights" also was a factor in yesterday's weakening of the single currency.
Mr. Fox called that comment "ill timed" and said it increased the negative sentiment toward the euro.
Antonio de Sousa, Bank of Portugal governor and a European Central Bank board member, said the bank
has no specific target for the euro and suggested central-bank intervention wasn't a longterm solution for
weakness in a major currency.
With so much attention on the euro, worries about emerging markets took a back seat. Fears that Argentina
would abandon its fixed-exchange rate system subsided on trading floors amid gains in U.S., Brazilian and
Mexican markets.

commodity-linked currencies such as Canada's dollar. Yesterday, the U.S. dollar ended at 1.4697 Canadian
dollars, up from C$1.4675 Tuesday.
The Australian dollar was sharply weaker, trading at 64.83 U.S. cents, from 65.01 U.S. cents Tuesday. The
currency's recent slide has partly reversed its turnaround from a 62.28 U.S. cents low in early April to a
thirteen-month peak at 67.46 U.S. cents reached in mid-May.
The Australian dollar had been one of the important plays in what some investors have called the "reflation"
trade that was characterized by the move back into emerging and commodity-linked markets amid
expectations of increasing global growth and demand.
Meanwhile, the Colombian peso weakened considerably in "next day" trading because of political uncertainty
following the resignation of the defense minister and senior military officials, traders said.
At 4 p.m. local time, traders said, the exchange rate was quoted at 1,730.00 pesos to the dollar, above the
ceiling of the central bank's fixed-exchange-rate band of 1,720.93 pesos to the dollar for Thursday.
Wednesday's official market close was 1,690.00 pesos to the dollar.
The resignation of Rodrigo Lloreda, one of the most experienced and respected cabinet members, is
considered a serious political blow to President Andres Pastrana and his quest to make peace with left-wing
guerrilla groups.
"The peso has gone outside the band, which shouldn't be possible. It looks like the central bank will have to
intervene tomorrow," a trader said.

Warm Replaces Wet in Iowa, Boosting Corn Prices
By Peter A. McKay
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
582 words
26 May 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C17
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Forecasts for warmer weather in the Midwest boosted corn prices on the Chicago Board of Trade yesterday,
following several days of rain that hindered late-season planting in the region.
CBOT's front-month corn contract rose three cents to close at $2.14 a bushel. The increase meant that corn
recovered half of a loss it posted Monday that analysts attributed to oversupply and the recent precipitation.
"At this price level, it's kind of dangerous to be short with the risk of adverse weather coming up," said Steve
DeCook, president of Fundamental Futures Inc., a Des Moines, Iowa, brokerage. "People saw today that that
shouldn't be too much of a worry for now, but every day from now until the rest of summer prices will be
dependent on what the forecasts are."
The nation's largest corn-producing state, Iowa, was particularly hard hit by rains last week that caused
severe flooding in the northeastern part of the state, according to the National Weather Service.
Forecasts yesterday predicted drier weather for Iowa, Illinois, Indiana and other corn states this week and
temperatures in the 80s.
The prime time for planting corn -- roughly the last two weeks of April and the first two of May -- passed with
88% of the crop planted, Mr. DeCook said. Although that represents a slightly stepped-up pace from past
years, that didn't prevent a sell-off on corn futures on CBOT Monday.
Bryce Stremming, a broker for Grain Service Corp. in Atlanta, noted that dry, hot weather forecasts are
usually bearish news for corn, although they were welcome news this week given the recent Midwestern
weather.
However, he was skeptical that weather was the only reason for yesterday's corn recovery. He called the
initial sell-off "overdone" and said the real problem is that oversupply has developed in the U.S. corn market
in recent years.
He pointed out that China, which had been importing U.S. corn as recently as 1996, has now become a net
exporter of the commodity.
"That's the real story in the world market, although I guess people will use weather as an excuse for
anything," Mr. Stremming said. "That's definitely what the consensus was today."
In other commodity markets:
GOLD: The front-month gold futures contract fell $2.10 to $270.80 an ounce on the Comex division of the
New York Mercantile Exchange. The metal's value has continued to slip in recent days despite inflationary
worries that would normally be bullish news for gold.
But analyst Bernard Savaiko of PaineWebber said Friday reports that the Fed may increase interest rates
have counterbalanced any possibility that inflation would strengthen gold prices.
"As long as investors think interest rates will outstrip inflation, it really doesn't matter what inflation does,"
said Mr. Savaiko. "That's why, quite frankly, gold flunked the inflation test Friday."
PALLADIUM: Palladium futures closed higher on the New York Mercantile Exchange yesterday amid strong
demand by auto makers and electronics manufacturers, analysts said. The front-month futures contract rose
$4.95 to close at $338 an ounce.

"Deliveries of palladium are still lagging, so demand is high," said Jeffrey Christian, managing director for the
metals research-firm CPM Group. "There have been rumors of big shipments from Russia in recent weeks,
but those still haven't materialized into finished products."

1991---Crunch Time:For small businesses, the hardest thing to get early in the decade wascredit; That may be hard to imagine these days
Small Business (A Special Report)
1991 --- Crunch Time: For small businesses, the hardest thing to get early in the decade was credit;
That may be hard to imagine these days
By Joshua Harris Prager
2,816 words
24 May 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
R6
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Bremerton, Wash. -- Driving through downtown here, in a Chevrolet Suburban, Jim Newell Jr. slowly passes
a Westsound Bank. "It used to be U.S. Bank," he says. "They turned me down." A few blocks later, he comes
upon a Seafirst Bank. "They said no, too."
Mr. Newell, a 50-year-old Midas muffler franchisee, is never far from reminders of past financial woes. Back
in 1991, he needed financing to jump-start his second Midas shop and applied for loans at more than a dozen
banks in Kitsap County, a ship-repair community surrounded by Puget Sound. "I took the phone book out and
hit every one of them," he recalls. "Every one said no."
But in 1996 and 1998, many of those same banks said yes when Mr. Newell applied for loans to acquire four
more Midas shops. And now, as he looks to expand yet again, institutions are so eager to lend to him that
some have approached unsolicited, aggressively courting his business with the promise of very low interest
rates.
For Mr. Newell and many entrepreneurs, the 1990s were the worst of times, then the best of times. The
decade that began with a crippling credit crunch is nearing a close amid a bonanza of easy money. Indeed,
according to Kitsap Bank Vice President Rodney B. Near, who personally turned down Mr. Newell's loan
application in 1991, if Mr. Newell came to the bank today with the same application he had back then, "we
probably would look at it a lot harder. Everybody's hungry for loans."
Mr. Newell, meanwhile, sitting in his office peering out a large window at several cars suspended on hydraulic
lifts, reclines in a black-leather rolling chair. Wearing a brushed-cotton shirt, Dockers pants and gold-rimmed
glasses, he looks comfortable. But he is forever chewing gum, suggesting an underlying nervousness. He
appears the prototypical small-business man who overcame the vicissitudes of the 1990s: comfortable but
scarred.
"I think I'm permanently wounded from having gone through" so many loan rejections, he says. "You can't go
through that much stress and be the same."
Mr. Newell, whose interest in cars began in high school when his 1955 white-and-turquoise Oldsmobile
Holiday 88 kept breaking down, opened his first Midas shop in 1977. He financed the enterprise with some
money from siblings and a $60,000 loan from Seafirst Bank -- establishing a credit history that would later do
him no good when the institution turned down his request for more money during the heart of the credit
crunch in 1991.
For 10 years, he ran the shop, serving as technician, manager and owner of the franchise, part of the chain
created by Chicago-based Midas Inc. Soon he was amassing about $500,000 in annual sales. As his staff
and business grew, so, too, did his ambition: In 1987 he took out a seven-year, $200,000 loan from Kitsap
Bank and bought a second Midas franchise. With the new shop in nearby Silverdale, Mr. Newell quadrupled
to 20 both his payroll and the number of automobile bays he had.
But like King Midas, who in Greek mythology grew to lament his wishes, Mr. Newell soon saw that a large
second shop was too much to handle. That first year, he says, while total revenue grew, the combined results
of his two businesses amounted to a loss of $150,000. He didn't have enough inventory -- mainly mufflers,
brake linings and shock absorbers -- to meet demand at both stores, he explains. And as his costs grew, his
customers started heading elsewhere.
He would have bought more inventory, he says, if he'd had the capital. But Mr. Newell decided to wait until
both businesses turned a profit before approaching a bank for another loan. "We felt we couldn't go to the
bank with a losing track record," says Mr. Newell, who once worked as a second-mortgage lender.

--- Page 119 ---
Page 119 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
And so he waited -- for four years. Finally, in 1991, after cutting his staff 20% and staying open seven days a
week -- with him and his wife working every day, he says -- his two shops posted a profit of $1,580. "We had
demonstrated and documented we're healthy now," says Mr. Newell. He figured "that ought to make [the
banks] feel very good."
Mr. Newell had paid off half of the seven-year loan he had taken out in 1987 and now sought $100,000 more.
"All we were really doing was adding three years onto the loan," he says.
But his timing couldn't have been worse.
In 1991, Congress, spurred by a greatly depressed real-estate market and widespread failures of
savings-and-loans institutions, enacted the FDIC Improvement Act, requiring banks to maintain greater
amounts of capital for each loan made. "Congress said to the banks, `Make no mistakes in lending,'" says
James Chessen, chief economist at the American Bankers Association in Washington, D.C. "Banks were
under a much shorter leash in their ability to lend."
Still, banking on his previous experience as a lender, Mr. Newell prepared a meticulous loan application and
approached Kitsap Bank. "When I asked for $100,000, one guy at the bank told me, `The bank is very
comfortable where they're at,'" remembers Mr. Newell. "He said, `You got to know when to hold 'em and
when to fold 'em.'"
For 10 months, the routine was the same. "I bought a new tie every time," says Mr. Newell. "I tried to iron a
shirt real professionally." He never took his wife with him to the loan meetings because, he says, "I didn't want
her to see her husband get beat up."
A loan officer at North Sound Bank, impressed with Mr. Newell's presentation (and convinced he wouldn't get
a loan from anyone), offered him a job as a loan officer. But the bank wouldn't offer him a loan.
"It didn't seem to matter that I was depositing $1 million a year, that a second mortgage was available, that I
never missed a payment," says Mr. Newell. "It seems ironic to me that they'd give me $200,000 with no
historical performance basis and then, a few years later, they wouldn't give it to me though we had a proven
track record."
The problem was, that track record was only one year long -- consisting of one good year, preceded by three
years of losses.
"Back then, for most banks, one year [of profitability] wasn't enough," says Scott D. Harvey, manager of the
Small Business Administration loans department at Kitsap Bank. "Now banks are moving much faster." Adds
Mr. Near, the vice president and manager at Kitsap: "We were definitely more conservative then. There was
the credit crunch, the regulators."
As a result, many small businesses with less access to capital than big business were shuttered. But for the
Newells, the idea of leaving the muffler business bordered on the blasphemous.
"[My wife] felt, `If I can't be Mrs. Midas anymore, who am I?'" recalls Mr. Newell. "She would have had an
identity problem. At 40 years old, that's a tough thing. I realized our identities were wrapped up in what we
did. I couldn't take that away from her. Then I kind of realized, I felt the same way."
"It's hard," adds Lorie Newell. "When your identity is Mr. and Mrs. Midas -- we didn't want it to stop."
Indeed, the Newells' enthusiasm for Midas is palpable. Mr. Newell could tell you that Nate Sherman founded
Midas, that the first Midas shop was erected in 1956, in Macon, Ga., that Midas is an acronym for "muffler
installation dealers association service."
For 14 years, Mrs. Newell had worked beside her husband, staffing the service counter, keeping the books.
She nursed their daughter, Kerri, in the back office of their Bremerton store and packed up for their son, Jim,
when he drove off with Dad on overnight trips to pick up auto parts at a warehouse in Sacramento, Calif. She
and her husband had filled the Newell home with glassware, jackets, sweatshirts and umbrellas all bearing
the Midas name. And the Newells had added a Midas touch to the Kitsap community, sponsoring several
youth sports teams with uniforms in signature Midas gold and black.
So, without a loan, the Newells set out to squeeze their pennies and make a success out of what was for
them more than a business. They sifted through their personal belongings with a simple rule of thumb:
"Anything that had a dollar in it, we took the dollar out." That included the family's three cars. "I turned the
Mercedes into mufflers," says Mr. Newell.
The Newells also began to delay payment of their Midas bills. "You become a slow pay," says Mr. Newell,
explaining his survival strategy. "We extended the kindness of our creditors."

--- Page 120 ---
Page 120 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
That policy prompted a letter from Midas, upset that Mr. Newell was four months and $65,000 behind on
paying his 10% sales commission and his bills for auto parts. "They said, `Pay up or we're taking your keys,'"
recalls Mr. Newell. But when Mr. Newell argued that his business was at its core healthy, that he had posted
revenue increases for several years and simply needed a few more years to jack up cash flow even further,
he and a regional Midas vice president worked out an extended repayment plan. His landlord similarly
consented to allow Mr. Newell to gradually repay the $35,000 in late rent he owed.
Even with their help, and money borrowed from friends, the Newells had to continue to cut financial corners
for years. The leak in the roof went ignored. They ate rice and beans. "We just gritted it out," Mr. Newell says
with a grimace.
With their revenue stretched to pay expenses -- including outstanding debt -- business growth was slow. In
1991, Mr. Newell had just over $1 million in sales. The next year revenue slipped to $991,000. By 1994 it had
crept back up to nearly $1.2 million. Then, in 1995, the Newells at long last made enough to start putting
money back into the business. They bought more inventory and invested in better equipment, such as
computers. Sales jumped to $1.7 million.
That sales balloon signaled to Mr. Newell that it was again time to expand. He hoped on this go-around to
have the benefit of a loan, and so once again prepared a loan application.
This time the economy would prove an ally to Mr. Newell. In July of that year, for instance, the Federal
Reserve made the first of three quarter-point interest-rate cuts, lowering the prime rate to 5.25% by the
following January. In July 1990, by comparison, the prime rate was 8%.
Those cuts helped the valuation of real estate and stocks rise, and in turn, investment. What's more, much of
the 1991 FDIC Improvement Act had since been repealed, and by 1996, banks had ample capital to back
new loans.
So, when Mr. Newell approached Kitsap for a loan in early 1996, the bank's response was starkly different
than it had been in 1991. "I said, `I need $80,000,'" recalls Mr. Newell. "And they said, `How about
$100,000?'"
"I was stunned," he says. "I thought it was some sort of trick question."
The responses at Seafirst and Keybank were equally encouraging. But brandishing a green Waterman pen
he bought just for the occasion, Mr. Newell signed on to receive a $100,000, five-year loan from Kitsap, five
years after the bank had denied him a loan of equal size.
It was a Small Business Administration loan, which seemed a good fit for both parties: Because SBA loans
are government-backed, Kitsap felt secure, and also was able to make the loan repayable over a long term,
which pleased Mr. Newell. Still, the interest rate was two percentage points over the prime rate, nothing
spectacular. "I didn't really play the game," says Mr. Newell. "My goal wasn't to get the best rate. It was to get
the money." Besides, he says, all three banks offered him the same deal. In the end, he stuck with Kitsap --
the issuer of his 1987 loan -- because of their longstanding relationship.
Mr. Newell's third store, in Port Orchard, hit the ground running, and two years later he was set to expand --
and borrow -- again. This time, fresh off the success of his most recent loan, Mr. Newell may have been able
to shop around for competitive interest rates. But, diffident ever since his experience in 1991, Mr. Newell
returned to Kitsap in 1998. He asked the bank for $330,000 to open three more Midas shops in Bellingham,
on the Washington-Canadian border. They again tendered him long-term SBA loans.
Reclining in his black leather chair, in the comfort of his office at his flagship Silverdale shop, Mr. Newell has
reason to be happy. His business is running on all cylinders. While the average Midas franchise generates
about $600,000 in annual sales, Mr. Newell expects his Silverdale store this year to hit $2 million. All told, Mr.
Newell's six shops will have sales of about $5 million this year, about 10% of which is profit.
Mr. Newell's success has led him to try to grow larger still. He is currently seeking a $1 million loan to acquire
four more Midas franchises, which would bring his total to 10. At his wife's urging, he says, this may be their
last expansion.
It couldn't have come at a better time. The economy, spurred by profligate investment, has banks awash in
capital. And the Newells figure at long last to cash in.
On his desk is a brochure he received in the mail from Heller Financial, a corporate lender in Chicago, and
among a smattering of institutions dangling loans in front of Mr. Newell with interest rates of 8.35% to 8.85%.
(Those rates easily best the 10.25% loans he has from Kitsap.) Near it is a yellow Post-It note with the name
and number of a loan officer at International Franchise Capital, a lender in San Diego.

--- Page 121 ---
Page 121 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"This was a phone call from one of these lenders. Wants to know when we're going to do business with him,"
says Mr. Newell, smiling. "Calls quite a bit."

Mr. Prager is a staff reporter in The Wall Street Journal's Atlanta bureau.

Refco AgreesTo SettlementIn CFTC Case
Commodities
Refco Agrees To Settlement In CFTC Case
By Andy Pasztor
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,798 words
24 May 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Refco Inc., one of the country's largest futures brokerage firms, has agreed to a multimillion-dollar settlement
of federal allegations of improper trading involving fallen Beverly Hills, Calif., financial adviser Jay Goldinger,
according to people familiar with the matter.
Slated to be announced as early as today, the civil settlement is expected to be one of the largest ever signed
with the Commodity Futures Trading Commission. Preliminary drafts called for payment of roughly between
$3 million and $5 million, these people said, and the final agreement is expected to have implications for
other brokerage houses.
Mr. Goldinger's flashy financial empire fell apart in 1995 after a wrong-way bet on interest rates and
allegations that he distributed false trading reports, leaving dozens of companies and well-heeled individuals
who had trusted him with losses amounting to about $100 million.
Refco, without admitting any wrongdoing, is expected to beef up internal controls, revamp certain reporting
procedures and take other steps to more closely police employees and floor brokers, these people familiar
with the situation said. Such moves, in turn, may set a precedent for tighter self-regulation by other major
industry entities as a way to prevent trading irregularities, according to these people.
Barring a last-minute glitch, the agreement also will be the first major victory for a multiagency federal
investigation delving into the once-highflying Mr. Goldinger's activities. Along with Refco, Pier 1 Imports Inc.
and PairGain Technologies Inc. are the other companies that have described themselves entirely as victims
but face government scrutiny for their dealings with Mr. Goldinger.
After Mr. Goldinger's fall, the flurry of inquiries initially centered on suspicions that his firm, Capital Insight
Brokerage, secretly shuffled funds among accounts to keep clients in the dark about huge losses. From the
beginning of the scandal, Chicago-based Refco, which handled many of Mr. Goldinger's high-risk trades,
maintained it acted properly, was merely executing his orders and wasn't responsible for his actions.
But today, with commodities regulators, the Securities and Exchange Commission, the Los Angeles U.S.
attorney's office and the Federal Bureau of Investigation seeking to end their long-running probes in this area,
Refco's top management is under pressure to resolve its part of the inquiries. Refco allegedly failed to keep a
close enough eye on Mr. Goldinger's practices. Dennis Klejna, Refco's general counsel, declined to comment
except to say he couldn't confirm or deny whether there was a settlement "until we're notified if some public
act has been taken" by the CFTC.
Pier 1 and PairGain, which together lost about $35 million invested with Mr. Goldinger, have maintained they
were hoodwinked by Mr. Goldinger, who courted publicity and reveled in running a racing stable. But last
summer, federal agents raided the offices of PairGain, a Tustin, Calif., maker of telecommunications
equipment, that everyone agrees was among the biggest casualties of Mr. Goldinger's collapse.
Now PairGain faces possible punishment for failing to promptly and fully disclose the results of its
investments with Capital Insight. Just last week, a PairGain filing with the SEC disclosed that the company,
Chairman Charles Strauch, and Chief Financial Officer Charles McBrayer have been formally notified they
are targets of a continuing federal-grand-jury investigation.
Government investigators and others familiar with the matter say Pier 1 faces potential civil claims by the
SEC for failing to promptly and fully report details of its investments with Capital Insight. Last year, Clark
Johnson, who was then Pier 1's chief executive, said in an interview that the company reported losses due to
Mr. Goldinger four days after he found out about them. Shortly after Mr. Goldinger's firm collapsed, the
home-furnishings retailer fired its chief financial officer and was forced to restate its results back to mid-1994.

--- Page 124 ---
Page 124 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Pier 1 officials have declined to return repeated telephone calls, and the company hasn't disclosed that it is
under SEC investigation. A spokesman for PairGain and its chairman says "there is no evidence of
wrongdoing" by the company or its officers. "It is ironic that PairGain, the victim, has been subjected to this
type of government investigation while [Mr.] Goldinger remains free and uncharged."
The unusual tale partly reflects federal efforts to crack down on publicly traded companies which fail to
adequately report investment risks and losses. More broadly, it illustrates not only how time-consuming and
difficult it is for law-enforcement agencies to unravel complex white-collar cases, but how unpredictable the
outcome sometimes can be.
Mr. Goldinger, who has been cooperating with the government, hasn't been charged with any wrongdoing.
But his lawyers have exchanged proposed settlement documents with the CFTC, and people close to the
case say they eventually expect him to settle with the SEC and the Justice Department. Lawyers for Mr.
Goldinger have declined to comment on the status of any of the inquiries, as have press officers for the
CFTC, SEC and Justice Department.
Refco, for its part, during the past few weeks has been discussing a potential settlement with the CFTC
calling for a payment roughly in the range of $3 million to $5 million, according to industry and government
officials. The size of the final settlement could fall slightly outside that range, one of these people said, but
details of the anticipated self-regulation aren't likely to change.
One reason for the size of the anticipated settlement may be Refco's repeated run-ins with the CFTC. Since
1983, the company has paid a total of more than $3.2 million to settle various allegations that it failed to
diligently supervise employees, properly segregate funds and comply with earlier commission orders. It didn't
admit wrongdoing in any of those earlier settlements.
Refco has paid well in excess of $10 million to settle various private lawsuits stemming from its dealings with
Mr. Goldinger. It has at least another $35 million in claims pending, including allegations of helping
perpetuate a "complete sham" on some investors because profitable trades were "moved" among accounts.
According to allegations in some of those suits, Refco earned between $45 million and more than $90 million
in commissions on trades it handled for Mr. Goldinger from the mid1980s to the mid-1990s. The company
declines to comment.
Indications of Refco's close ties to Mr. Goldinger, though, show up in various court cases and enforcement
documents. Mr. Goldinger's ex-wife Janet, for instance, gave an April 1997 deposition in one private lawsuit
alleging Capital Insight routinely used Refco to execute "block" or "bunched" trades for a number of clients,
but then failed to allocate losses or gains for specific accounts until the next day. She also testified Mr.
Goldinger's operation "most definitely" was set up to allegedly be able to "designate a profitable or
unprofitable trade" to specific accounts.
A turning point in the investigation came in January when an independent trader who had worked extensively
with Refco and handled much of Mr. Goldinger's business, agreed to a $1 million fine and a 10-year ban
imposed by the Chicago Board of Trade. Without admitting any violation, Constantine Mitsopoulos agreed to
settle allegations that he failed to maintain proper records and failed, as required by Board of Trade
regulations, to record account designations on customer orders upon receipt.
In the PairGain criminal inquiry, prosecutors want to determine, among other things, whether the company or
certain executives knowingly tried to mislead or purposely provided incorrect or incomplete information to
shareholders, government investigators and perhaps even some of their own lawyers or directors about the
status of investments with Mr. Goldinger, according to government investigators and others familiar with the
matter. In an unusual twist, some company officials have been summoned to appear before the grand jury
twice or three times, people familiar with the matter say.
The government also wants to find out if company records were altered. Prosecutors are asking questions
about why PairGain continued investing with Mr. Goldinger despite unauthorized trades and why the
company didn't publicly disclose the potential risks and losses months before Mr. Goldinger's company finally
went out of business. The inquiry repeatedly has gotten bogged down in acrimonious arguments over
attorney-client privilege as the government has demanded information from company lawyers.
PairGain has consistently complained to the U.S. attorney's office that the government is mistakenly pushing
for criminal prosecutions to resolve complex, often ambiguous disclosure issues that typically have been
handled as routine SEC civil matters. In the past, a spokesman for PairGain and Mr. Strauch, who lost a
personal investment of $1.8 million with Mr. Goldinger, said company officials were "absolutely outraged" and
felt "like Alice in Wonderland" because of the government's insistence on "putting innocent victims through
the wringer."
A lawyer for Mr. McBrayer has said neither his client "nor any other PairGain employees did anything wrong."

--- Page 125 ---
Page 125 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Meanwhile, in commodity trading Friday:
SOYBEANS: Futures fell to new contract lows at the Chicago Board of Trade as U.S. farmers made rapid
progress planting their crop. Soybean planting is ahead of schedule, and the weather appears ideal for more
seeding activity in the next week, traders and analysts said. A quick planting makes a bountiful harvest more
likely. Nearby July soybean futures sank 6.25 cents to $4.6175 a bushel. The contract set a new lifetime low
at midday just under the closing level. "I don't see any opportunity for a comeback unless we have a drought,"
said Dan Basse, vice president of Chicago consulting firm AgResource, Co. "Without a weather problem, we
could be putting a '3' in front of bean futures, something we haven't seen since the early 1970s." The last time
soybeans traded under $4 a bushel was in 1972. As recently as 1997, they traded at $9 a bushel.
GOLD: Prices fell on a combination of hedge-fund and producer selling, traders said. Spot gold equaled the
20-year low of $272.50 a troy ounce set Wednesday, while the June contract on the Comex division of the
New York Mercantile Exchange dropped $1.40 to $273.30 an ounce, just above the contract low of $272.40
set Wednesday. Participants continue to focus on looming gold sales from the United Kingdom, the
International Monetary Fund, and Switzerland, market observers said.
SILVER: Prices fell after Gold Fields Mineral Services said demand shrank last year. The Comex July
contract fell 10 cents to $5.21 a troy ounce. In a report, the U.K. research company showed that world silver
demand fell 2.2% in 1998.
-- Daniel Rosenberg contributed to this article.

1990---Before the Boom:The small-business outlook at the onset of the decade gave no hint ofthe good times to come
Small Business (A Special Report)
1990 --- Before the Boom: The small-business outlook at the onset of the decade gave no hint of the
good times to come
By Hilary Stout
1,020 words
24 May 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
R4
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
It was a bad year to be an entrepreneur: Business failures were up. Sales were down. The outlook was
dismal, and credit seemed impossible to come by.
That was 1990, and it looked like the 1980s party was over for a good long time.
As the economy slipped toward recession, small business owners retrenched. The loose lending that led to
the savings-and-loan disaster brought anxiety among bankers and eventually legislation tightening lending
standards and squeezing credit dry for some little firms. Small businesses, which had developed into great
job generators during the 1980s, slowed their hiring plans. Some even had to lay off workers. Some closed
their doors entirely.
"Small-business confidence is in a free fall," wrote William Dunkelberg, an economist at Temple University, in
an introduction to the National Federation of Independent Business's third-quarter 1990 survey of members.
"October's small-business data were bad and suggest that small-business owners will face rough times
ahead."
Who would ever have imagined how it would turn out? Yes, things were tough for a while. But nine years
later, as the decade draws to a close, the 1990s turned out to be one of the brightest periods in history for
entrepreneurs. "The '90s has been a tremendous decade," Mr. Dunkelberg, the NFIB's chief economist, says
today.
Several things were at work. The stock market, which began the decade with the Dow Jones Industrial
Average at 2810 on Jan. 2, 1990, closed above 10000 by early 1999 and kept right on climbing. The
extraordinary rise created vast individual and corporate wealth, making it much easier for entrepreneurs to
obtain seed capital to start their own firms.
At the same time, the downsizing of giant corporations pushed many senior managers onto the street and
gave them the impetus to break out on their own. "Lots of talented people who didn't expect to be fired were
fired," says Mr. Dunkelberg. Aided by fat severance packages, "they had lots of money," he says. "Those
people would never have started their own companies" without the push out the corporate door. Meanwhile,
the economy pulled itself out of a brief recession and went on to chug through the longest expansion in U.S.
history, boosted by low interest rates and low inflation and by a federal budget surplus unforeseen by
anyone in 1990.
Of all the decade's developments, though, none was as unimaginable as the rise of the Internet and its
transformation of the economy and the way people do business. Back then, the Internet was an obscure
research network overseen by the Defense Department. The World Wide Web, which made the Internet
accessible to the masses, didn't exist. Today, the Internet offers unprecedented opportunities to start
businesses with very little capital -- or experience -- and to reach markets world-wide.
This page offers a look at some of the small-business-related stories and events making news in the pages of
The Wall Street Journal in the first year of the decade. And the following report looks at the array of forces
that created one of the most dynamic and creative periods of entrepreneurship in history. To be sure, there
have been some rough spots. A rollback of affirmative-action laws hurt minority contractors. And one of the
most promising industries, biotechnology, hit the skids early on. But there have been some stunning
surprises, including the rise of a growing coterie of twentysomething "dot com" millionaires who are changing
a young generation's perceptions of work and success.
Looking back, Mr. Dunkelberg says the 1990s have helped breed an attitude that his organization has long
preached: "Being an entrepreneur is a great thing. Just go out and do it."
