Fed Fallout: `Boring' Stocks May Look Good,While Junk Bonds Could Beat Out Treasurys
Heard on the Street
Fed Fallout: `Boring' Stocks May Look Good, While Junk Bonds Could Beat Out Treasurys
By Robert McGough and Gregory Zuckerman
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
924 words
19 May 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Out with flashy and cutting-edge investments. In with the boring and tattered.
Though the Federal Open Market Committee didn't raise interest rates at its meeting yesterday, it did shift its
"bias," signaling that it is more likely to raise rates in the coming months.
With the possibility of higher future interest rates, and with the recent uptick in inflation that motivated the
Fed to change its stance, money managers are taking profits from companies that represent the "new
economy," and buying stocks that embody the old.
Stocks like Inco, a nickel producer, and Phelps Dodge, a copper producer, could thrive in a market that is
unfriendly to Internet stocks. And threadbare junk bonds could fare better than Treasurys.
After years of hibernation, fears of inflation are again rising on the heels of Friday's report showing that
consumer prices jumped an unexpected 0.7% in April. Even if inflation doesn't surge, a sustained rise could
hurt stock and bond prices. That's because most investors are expecting inflation of about 2% this year, so a
jump of even 2.5% would cause nervousness, and send some stocks tumbling.
High-priced stocks of fast-growing companies are especially susceptible, because higher inflation and
interest rates make investors less willing to pay high prices for future earnings.
"An inflation scare is what we're in, and that's very different from an outbreak of inflation, which is still
remote," says Stephen Roach, chief economist at Morgan Stanley. He reckons stocks are now overvalued by
about 37%. "Still, investors are getting a real shock because in many respects expectations are more
important than reality in financial markets."
Charles Ober, manager of T. Rowe Price New Era Fund, expects a mild inflation rise. "In general, it's
positive for the commodity cyclicals because they tend to get a little more of an inflation premium in their"
price-earnings ratios. He favors oil and natural-gas producers such as Noble Affiliates and Ocean Energy. But
he says he is steering away from refiners, such as Ultramar Diamond Shamrock and Sunoco, whose profit
margins already have been pinched by rising prices.
One factor behind worries about inflation is the budding recovery of the global economy, particularly in Asia.
Mr. Ober likes Inco because rising industrial activity in Japan would drive up demand for its nickel, used in
making stainless steel. Phelps Dodge, a copper producer, also would benefit from growth in Asia.
Food companies will also do well in an environment of mildly rising inflation, argues Dick Thorsell, of Thorsell
Parker Partners, in Westport, Conn.
"Supermarkets benefit from higher inflation because they can pass along price increases better than other
companies," says Mr. Thorsell. He recommends Richfood Holdings, a food retailer in Southeast that is
undergoing a turnaround. Other analysts like supermarket chain A&P (Great Atlantic & Pacific Tea) for
investors nervous about inflation.
If the Fed actually starts raising shortterm interest rates, investors would face another set of problems.
George Vanderheiden, manager of several Fidelity Investments stock funds, studied the past five times the
Fed raised rates, and found that some cyclicals do well, but others don't. He found that what he calls "late
cyclicals," such as basic-materials businesses, aluminum, gold, paper and commodity chemicals, outperform
when Fed tightening begins, as do energy companies. But if Fed tightening continues, these stocks lose their
luster because of fears of a recession.

--- Page 130 ---
Page 130 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Consumer-staple companies, on the other hand, continued to perform as demand for restaurants, beverages,
entertainment and tobacco remained strong. Groups that didn't do well, Mr. Vanderheiden says, include
financial stocks, which are hurt by rising short-term rates relative to long-term rates. High-priced stocks get
hurt, as do cyclical stocks tied to interest rates, such as autos, home builders, building materials and
furniture companies.
Junk bonds typically have fared far better in times of rising interest rates than Treasury issues, says Martin
Fridson, chief high-yield strategist at Merrill Lynch. This is counter-intuitive, because rising rates lead to fears
of defaults among junk-bond issuers, which have below-investment-grade credit ratings. But "most high-yield
companies have their borrowing costs largely fixed," so higher rates don't immediately translate into higher
interest costs, Mr. Fridson says.
Moreover, the higher yields of junk bonds help protect their prices from the debilitating effects of higher
interest rates. Since Dec. 31, yields on 10-year Treasury notes have risen to 5.72% from 4.65%, he says
(bond prices move in the opposite direction to yields). But the yield on the Merrill Lynch 175 high-yield bond
index has actually declined a tad, to 10.11% from 10.17%. Unless there was a real blowout of corporate
credit -- which Mr. Fridson doesn't expect -- he believes junk bonds will continue to outperform Treasurys as
interest rates rise.
Though some shifts could be appropriate, analysts say, there is no reason for wholesale portfolio changes
simply because of the discouraging inflation news. Friday's was just the first bad CPI report, and the recent
rise in interest rates -- on the bond market's own initiative -- could slow the economy enough to avoid a
troublesome rise in inflation.
"I still have a lot of confidence" in Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan," says Mr. Thorsell. "I wouldn't run for cover
just yet."

Dollar Rises to 10-Week High on YenAs Bond-Yield Gap Continues to Widen
Foreign Exchange
Dollar Rises to 10-Week High on Yen As Bond-Yield Gap Continues to Widen
By Stephanie Hoo
Dow Jones Newswires
731 words
18 May 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C20
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- The dollar rose to a 10-week high against the yen on the ever-widening gap between U.S. and
Japanese bond yields, a surprise fall in Japan's current-account surplus and dollar-bullish remarks from U.S.
Treasury Secretary-designate Lawrence Summers.
The dollar edged lower against the euro in lackluster trading, with the euro finding some support from news
that Russian President Boris Yeltsin had survived an impeachment effort.
The main play yesterday was the dollar-yen, which rallied above 123.50 yen for the first time since March 5
and finished New York trading near its highest levels of the global day.
"People are getting more comfortable being long dollar and short yen," said Mike Rosenberg, head of global
foreignexchange research at Deutsche Bank in New York, citing the "huge spread" between 10-year U.S. and
Japanese yields to about 4.45 percentage points Monday afternoon, compared with about 2.45 percentage
points in February.
Late Monday in New York, the dollar was trading at 123.55 yen, up from 122.75 yen late Friday. The euro
was trading at $1.0670, up from $1.0664. Sterling was at $1.6185, down from $1.6203.
Around midday Tuesday in Tokyo, the dollar was trading at 123.39 yen, while the euro was at $1.0681.
Sterling was trading at $1.6174.
Several traders said the dollar has room to climb against the yen, though they reported hesitation in taking
the dollar higher ahead of the U.S. Federal Open Market Committee meeting today. The announcement
Friday of a sharp rise in the consumer price index for April increased concern that the Fed might announce a
bias to tighten policy.
Some say the dollar might not be benefiting from U.S.-Japan yield spreads because bond prices are falling.
According to Henry Willmore, senior economist at Barclays Capital in New York, a currency finds support
against another once yield spreads are wide and stable. "Very few people think this process is finished," he
said.
But dollar bulls remain. "Exchange rates have a great deal of inertia," Mr. Rosenberg said. "The fundamentals
are lining up, the appetite for risk is increasing. . . . Everything is falling into place for a big move up in the
dollar."
The dollar rallied early in Asian trading on news that Japan's current-account surplus plunged 25.5% in March
from March 1998, to 1.073 trillion yen. Economists surveyed had expected an increase. Japan's trade surplus
grew 4.9% in March on the year -- but because an import decline outpaced an export decline.
Also helping the dollar: Mr. Summers said late Sunday that "a strong dollar is very much in America's national
interest" and the dollar shouldn't be used as a trade-policy tool. His statements went a long way in assuring
that his views are in line with those of outgoing Secretary Robert Rubin, several traders said.
In other action, the Australian dollar recovered slightly from Friday's sell-off, though further gains may be
capped in the near term by softer gold prices, a weakening yen and uncertainty whether the government's
planned tax package will pass. Australia's currency was at 66.53 U.S. cents late Monday in New York, up
from 66.43 cents late Friday but still down from 66.73 cents late Thursday.
The Russian ruble recovered some ground on market relief that Mr. Yeltsin dodged the impeachment bullet.
Still, the currency remains pressured by economic woes and an unsettled government. The dollar for
next-day delivery fell to 24.79 rubles on the Moscow Interbank Currency Exchange from 25.43 rubles Friday.

--- Page 132 ---
Page 132 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In east Asia, the Thai baht, Indonesian rupiah, Singapore dollar, Philippine peso and New Taiwan dollar all
weakened slightly against the U.S. dollar in line with the yen's decline. The South Korean won held firm,
finishing unchanged.
Israel's financial markers were closed for national elections; traders in New York said they expect the shekel
to recover some of its recent losses today on relief that the divisive campaign is over. The shekel fell more
than 2% in late April on pre-election jitters, and the dollar was at 4.1050 shekels Friday.

Raw Sugar Prices Hit by Heavy Producer Selling
Commodities
Raw Sugar Prices Hit by Heavy Producer Selling
By Brendan Coffey
Dow Jones Newswires
580 words
18 May 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C17
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- World raw sugar prices were walloped yesterday as heavy selling by Brazilian producers
stifled an early rally and sparked large-scale dumping by speculative traders, according to traders and
analysts.
The nearby July contract on New York's Coffee, Sugar & Cocoa Exchange lost almost 6% of its value, settling
at 4.63 cents a pound after trading as high as 5.05 cents in the session.
Prices jumped 0.15 cent early in the session as speculators looked to build on modest gains of late to break
over strong resistance of 5.05 cents a pound, said Michael McDougall, a broker at Fimat USA Inc. in New
York.
Brazilian producers looking to lock in prices over five cents a pound sold quickly and heavily into the market,
however. That generated selling by speculators, and triggered commodity-fund sell stops, which are orders to
sell at certain prices to cut losses, exacerbating the plunge, Mr. McDougall said.
The slide not only erased two weeks of hard-earned price gains, but a sense the sugar market had put the
14-year price lows seen in April behind it.
"This will be the trigger that pushes prices to the lows of July 1985," said Ann Prendergast, sugar analyst with
Refco Inc. in New York.
The summer of 1985 saw the lowest sugar prices in memory, trading as low as 2.30 cents a pound. By
comparison, the lowest price for the front-month sugar contract since August 1985 was 4.36 cents on May 3.
"It's no secret: there's oversupply and no one's buying," she said.
Sugar has been in the grips of a bear market for months mainly because of a world-wide glut of supply.
Adding further pressure is an expected bumper crop of as many as 16 million metric tons from Brazil to be
harvested through July, according to Prendergast.
The oversupply problem is expected to last through 2002, according to a World Bank study released earlier
this month.
James Cordier, a broker with Liberty Trading Group in St. Petersburg, Fla., agreed the market's outlook has
turned distinctly bearish after yesterday's drop.
"We really need to see a tender surface to curtail this tailspin, otherwise we can hit four cents again," he said.
Tenders, or orders to buy physical sugar, have been scarce of late, however.
World consumption is flat at best over previous years because of the economic hardships faced by many
economies over the past year, another bearish factor affecting the market, analysts said.
-- Daniel Rosenberg and Janet Whitman
In other commodity markets:
HOGS: Futures fell on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange yesterday because of weakness in cash and
product prices, analysts said. The June contract dropped 0.90 cent to 57.97 cents a pound. "The cash market
sold off, and that got futures traders taking profits," said Paul Georgy, an analyst with Allendale, Inc. in
McHenry, Ill.

Page 134 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
GOLD: Futures extended their recent fall at the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange. The
June contract fell $1.40 to $274.80 a troy ounce. The market remains burdened by looming sales by the
United Kingdom Treasury, the International Monetary Fund, and Switzerland, market observers said. "Gold
seems like it's heading lower still, all in spite of the fears of inflation," said Robin Alssid, senior dealer with
Mitsubishi International Corp. in New York.

It Isn't Time to Bury'New' Economy Just Yet
The Outlook
It Isn't Time to Bury 'New' Economy Just Yet
By Bernard Wysocki Jr.
929 words
17 May 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
WASHINGTON -- It's been a long time since economy watchers went through this kind of mood swing. Until
last Friday morning, the financial markets were taking their cue from companies like International Business
Machines Corp. IBM chief Louis Gerstner told analysts last week of nearly insatiable demand for the
company's electronic-commerce products. IBM's stock went up $20.50 on Thursday alone.
That was good for IBM, but there was something more: All this e-commerce buzz from IBM was a handy
barometer of that miraculous e-economy of new technology and its amazing productivity payoff: strong
economic growth without inflation.
Then, Friday morning, came the nastiest inflation report in nine years. Core prices jumped 0.4% in April, the
government said. Tossing in the energy and food numbers, the consumer price index rose 0.7% for the
month.
Suddenly, the technology miracle was yesterday's news, gone and very much forgotten. Remember when the
Internet would usher in the age of deflation? On Friday, nobody did.
In its place wasn't just the old economy, but Wall Street's reaction to an old economy overheating. Stocks and
bonds swooned. Everyone was trying to read the minds of Alan Greenspan and the Federal Reserve, whose
open market committee meets this week to review monetary policy. The consensus on Wall Street was that
there's some chance of an intellectual nod toward tightening monetary policy, but that any move to raise
short-term interest rates is unlikely. Of course, the consensus could be wrong.
Whatever the Fed decides to do, one thing remains clear: The information-technology revolution continues.
Companies continue to harness computers and networks to drive tremendous efficiencies in their businesses.
Mr. Greenspan himself has marveled at these astonishing transformations in his speeches recently.
It's worth considering how just a few days ago, Wall Street analysts were such believers in the new economy.
Several raised their price targets for IBM's stock after Mr. Gerstner said about a quarter of IBM's business
was electronic-commerce related. He also said that IBM's own sales via the Internet would be $10 billion to
$15 billion in 1999, and that these "drive customer retention and reduce our costs."
The larger point is that so much of U.S. business is trying to reap similar efficiencies by deploying IBM and
many other technologies. An airline ticket costs $8 to process in the traditional way, but only $1 via the
Internet, an 87% savings. Multiplied time and again through various channels of commerce, these savings
tend to take costs out of business, cut inventories, improve output per hour worked -- and often result in brutal
price wars among competitors. That isn't very inflationary.
At this juncture, Friday's report is one terrible number amid a slew of favorable ones. Wage increases are
moderate, producer prices are benign, and the price of gold, an inflation signal of sorts, is sinking. Also,
there were some unusual price spikes in the price index, such as cigarette prices. "It's full of one-timers," says
William Dunkelberg, chief economist at the National Federation of Independent Business. "Inflation is a fear,
not a fact."
Oil prices, of course, have been rising sharply for months. Analysts who saw crude oil falling to $10 a barrel
were premature, if not just plain wrong. Oil prices have topped $18 a barrel, though they have retreated in the
past couple weeks. If the Balkan war winds down and Middle East suppliers pump up production, some
further price drops could occur, a number of analysts say.
But is oil's rise a symptom of an inflationary global economy? That's a stretch. For one thing, many other
commodity prices aren't following oil's upward spiral. For another, financial markets are probably signaling a
big recovery in many troubled economies, yet the actual economic revival is pretty halting and fragile.

--- Page 136 ---
Page 136 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The hope is that the world is becoming a safer place economically, as the U.S. continues its strong growth,
Asia's crisis economies rebound, Europe holds steady and Japan bottoms out. All these developments could
absorb excess capacity. But they also could be the harbinger of rising demand and rising prices, i.e. inflation.
Chances are, all these developments are still embryonic. "Financial markets go overboard in both directions.
The real economy doesn't change that fast," says James Glassman, managing director at Chase Securities
Inc., New York. Mr. Glassman, too, thinks the latest bout of inflation fears about the U.S. are overblown.
All that business redesign is probably still working its disinflationary magic. And all the price cutting
represented by the rise of Internet commerce is alive and well, too.
To some economists, such as James Paulsen, chief investment officer at San Francisco-based Wells Capital
Management Inc., technology investment is one of those deep structural changes in the economy that tend to
keep a lid on prices, or are even deflationary. Another one is global competition, which for the U.S. means
cheaper imports, at least today. Still another is the work of the bond markets, whose very reaction -- raising
interest rates -- tends to quell growth and damp inflation.
Could inflation be a problem at some point? Certainly. Could monetary policy fail to see inflation coming?
It's possible. But judging from the way the world reacted to one bad number, probably the least likely mistake
by today's policy makers is to get blindsided by spiraling prices.

Wheat Tumbles on Rumors About Chinese Ban
Commodities
Wheat Tumbles on Rumors About Chinese Ban
By Robin K. Taylor
Dow Jones Newswires
567 words
17 May 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C15
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
CHICAGO -- Rumors that China was threatening to reinstate its ban on U.S. wheat, along with technical
selling, sent wheat futures sharply lower Friday at the Chicago Board of Trade, market watchers said.
The nearby May contract fell 10 cents to $2.53 a bushel. The July contract also fell 10 cents, to $2.605 a
bushel.
Rumors of a renewed wheat ban followed the accidental bombing of the Chinese embassy in Belgrade,
Yugoslavia, earlier this month by forces of the North Atlantic Treaty Organization. That provoked antiU.S.
protests in China and led to fears in the U.S. agricultural sector that trade relations had been damaged.
Last month, while bidding to gain entry to the World Trade Organization, China announced an end to its
27-year ban on U.S. wheat from the U.S. Pacific Northwest, put in place because of concerns about the plant
disease TCK smut. The dropping of the ban was one of several trade concessions made by China as part of
an agreement signed with the U.S.
However, U.S. Trade Representative Charlene Barshefsky Friday said China hadn't backed out of the
pledges made to secure membership to the WTO.
Shi Guangsheng, foreign trade minister at the Commercial Office of the Chinese Embassy in Washington,
said in an interview that there was no change in those pledges. "The agreement has been signed, and I don't
think that the Chinese side will make any changes," he said.
Brenda Tucker, analyst with consulting firm AgResource Co. in Chicago, played down the importance of the
issue. "Even if it were true, we don't see China as a major wheat importer during 1999-2000 anyway," she
said.
Wheat was also hit by technical selling, or selling related to what traders identified as key price levels.
Commodity funds and the locals, or traders, did a lot of selling in wheat, said Victor Lespinasse, analyst with
A.G. Edwards on the CBOT floor.
John Janney, vice president with Salomon Smith Barney on the CBOT floor, said technical selling was
triggered by sell stops, or sell orders placed below the market. "People were taking profits and getting out of
long positions," he said.
In other commodity markets:
GOLD: Futures prices on gold fell to the lowest settlement since 1979 on the Comex division of the New
York Mercantile Exchange, despite a rise in inflation expectations signaled by a sell-off in Treasury bonds.
The June contract dropped $1.90 to $276.20 a troy ounce. The consumer-price index for April "was up 0.7%,
the stock market fell, and the bonds got pummeled, and what did gold do? It fell to new contract lows," said
William O'Neill, chief futures strategist with Merrill Lynch in New York. "Once again, gold is showing its true
colors. It really is losing its function as a monetary commodity."
COCOA: Futures prices on cocoa fell on the Coffee, Sugar and Cocoa Exchange, a unit of the New York
Board of Trade, after a large commodity fund sold 2,500 lots, traders said. The July contract fell $27 to $961
a metric ton. "Friday is a strange day for commodity trading; people like to get squared away before the
weekend," one trader said.

Soybean-Oil Futures Sink to Two-Month Lows
Commodities
Soybean-Oil Futures Sink to Two-Month Lows
By Daniel Rosenberg
Dow Jones Newswires
744 words
14 May 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C17
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
CHICAGO -- Soybean-oil futures crumbled to two-month lows at the Chicago Board of Trade as traders
prepared for bearish news from Malaysia.
Tomorrow, Malaysia will release palm-oil production data for April, and expectations are for a huge rise from
the previous month. Palm oil competes with soybean oil in world export markets, so what hurts palm oil tends
to hurt soy oil as well.
Nearby May soybean-oil futures fell 0.5 cent to 17.75 cents a pound, the lowest settlement for a front-month
contract since early March. The drop was half of the exchange's one-cent daily limit.
"Expectations are for a big increase in Malaysian palm-oil stocks and production, which would confirm that
the El Nino episode, with its negative effect on production, is behind us," said Anne Frick, an analyst with
Prudential Securities in New York.
The El Nino weather phenomenon, which hit Southeast Asia nearly two years ago with a drought that crippled
palm-oil output, was one factor behind soy oil's rally in 1997 and early 1998. Just one year ago, the nearby
soy-oil contract traded above 28 cents a pound.
Traders in Malaysia expect tomorrow's report to show an 18% to 20% rise in April crude palm-oil production
from the previous month. Some private forecasters expect an even sharper increase.
"We have no reason to not suspect huge palm-oil production over the next couple of years," Ms. Frick said.
"And when palm-oil values decline, they force soy oil to decline in sympathy."
To make matters more bearish, the U.S. Department of Agriculture released a report this week predicting
record U.S. soybean production in the 1999/2000 crop year, which starts Sept. 1. Beans are crushed to
produce oil and meal, and a bountiful bean crop usually means ample oil output.
"It's not anything new, but projections for big production just don't go away," said Dan Cekander, head of
grain research for FIMAT Futures USA Inc. in Chicago.
Lately, demand for U.S. soy oil has been "stagnant," according to Professional Farmers of America, an Iowa
consulting firm. South America, which is harvesting record soy crops, garnered the majority of world business
recently.
Last week, Iran and Argentina restored trade ties, which had been broken off last year in a dispute over a
1994 bombing in Argentina. U.S. analysts expect Iran to resume its place as the largest importer of Argentine
edible oils.
The patching of relations came at an inauspicious time for U.S. farmers, who recently cheered the Clinton
administration's announcement that it would again allow food sales to Iran after a four-year halt. Iran bought
U.S. soy oil in the 1970s, but South America filled most of Iran's needs during the past 20 years. U.S.
soy-industry officials said it will be a challenge to re-enter the Iranian market.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Futures rallied at the New York Mercantile Exchange, recovering most of Wednesday's losses.
Confirming what U.S. crude-oil traders had expected since the March meeting of the Organization of
Petroleum Exporting Countries, Saudi Arabia has notified U.S. crude-oil customers that state oil company
Saudi Aramco will cut term-contracted volumes of crude delivered to them in June by 20% to 25%, market
sources said. Also, Saudi Arabia said it was in continued consultations with other world oil producers in an

--- Page 140 ---
Page 140 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
effort to boost cash crude prices to about $20 a barrel by year end, the Associated Press reported. The June
contract rose 46 cents to $18.03 a barrel yesterday. Prices had dropped the previous two sessions on
inventory data. "What we saw today was a correction of that move to the downside," one analyst said.
SILVER: Futures rose on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange because of
options-related buying and arbitrage against gold, traders said. The July contract added 18.7 cents to $5.57
a troy ounce. Dealers noted that silver doesn't face the pressure of central-bank sales -- unlike gold, which
last week tumbled after the United Kingdom said it would reduce its gold reserves. Thus, "there's not the
same kind of physical overhang in silver as there is in gold," a trader said.

Seoul Survivor:Korea's Fast RecoverySuggests That ReformIsn't the Only Answer---Unusual Openness HelpedWin Over Wall Street;People `Donate...
Seoul Survivor: Korea's Fast Recovery Suggests That Reform Isn't the Only Answer --- Unusual
Openness Helped Win Over Wall Street; People `Donated' Jewelry --- Biggest Problems Remain
By Michael Schuman
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
2,172 words
14 May 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
SEOUL -- Here's another kind of Asian miracle. From the depths of recession last year, South Korea has
risen to healthy growth predictions for 1999 and has restored one of the sorriest victims of Asia's financial
plague to the good graces of foreign investors.
Perhaps even more preternatural, Korea has achieved this while failing to pursue reform as far as many
outsiders have been urging.
That isn't to say that this nation of 45 million hasn't suffered from upheaval and austerity since the region's
financial crisis broke in mid-1997 and reached Korea a few months later. Much has been done to shore up
weak spots in the economy and revamp poor policy. But even Seoul's toughest reform efforts have fallen
short of what experts recommended. Many banks remain weak and burdened with bad loans, while the
unwieldy conglomerates on which the economy was built continue to balk at slimming down -- and in some
cases are even adding more bulk.
Nonetheless, half-measures have been handsomely rewarded. The currency is strong again, the stock
market -- despite falling sharply this week -- is still up 49% from its low this year in February,
foreign-exchange reserves are hefty after substantial draining, and economists predict that this year Korea
will return to growth -- around 4% by some estimates -- in what would be a sharp turnaround from last year's
5.8% contraction.
For other countries facing an economic crisis, the combination of recovery amid incomplete reform raises the
question of whether the experts are overstating the amount of reform needed for revival. A look at how Korea
came back so quickly provides a few answers. But while the comeback may be remarkable, the unfinished
business behind it raises doubts in some quarters about whether Korea's rapid recovery has legs. Some
believe the country's long-term health depends on a small number of free-market reformers determined to
revamp Korea Inc. altogether.
Korea's was a standard case of Asian nation-building, in which the government set developmental goals, told
the banks to fork out the necessary cash and let industry grow willy-nilly. The result was a feeble banking
sector, a slew of sprawling, family-run conglomerates known as chaebols and only ramshackle notions of
credit-worthiness and accounting. Rigid labor laws that virtually barred layoffs also blocked any serious
streamlining.
By the time the Asian contagion began hitting Korea hard in October 1997, making lending rates higher and
cash scarcer, domestic debt in Korea was well above $400 billion. Foreign-exchange reserves were drying
up, and the value of the currency, the won, was plummeting. By December, the international financial
community was worried that Korea might have to renege on external-debt payments. That month, though,
Korea got a new president and some breathing room through a $58 billion bailout led by the International
Monetary Fund.
Kim Dae Jung, the nation's perennial opposition leader, won the presidency on his fourth try and then set
about winning over foreign investors, union leaders and a populace terrified by the economic crisis engulfing
their country.
On a night in January 1998, President-elect Kim held a Clinton-style town-hall meeting on national television.
It was an unprecedented piece of public relations in a nation where aloof autocrats had been the postwar
norm. He took questions from ordinary citizens, pledged reforms that were once political poison and warned
workers accustomed to lifetime employment that "unemployment is going to be inevitable."

--- Page 142 ---
Page 142 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
That broadcast was a major step in shaping a consensus for reform -- not least within the government itself.
M.G. SriRam Aiyer, a director at the World Bank, says government officials resisted the reforms he requested
in negotiations that month, such as toughening accounting standards and changing labor laws to make
layoffs easier. "`No layoffs, no loans,' I said," Mr. Aiyer recalls. "They said: `You can't be serious.'"
By contrast, President Kim, the 73-year-old longtime dissident, became reform's champion. When he met
Lawrence Summers, deputy U.S. Treasury secretary, soon after winning the election, Mr. Kim gave him a
book he had written in 1996 whose ideas struck Mr. Summers as resembling an IMF reform program. "His
was not a reluctant conversion to economic reform," Mr. Summers says. And by contrast with other stricken
Asian nations, where leadership was collapsing or muddled, Mr. Kim remained a staunch constant throughout
the crisis.
The president-elect quickly set about getting powerful interest groups -- big business and the unions -- behind
stringent reforms. On Jan. 15, 1998, a month before his inauguration, his party formed a commission of
government, business and labor representatives to hammer out a labor agreement. Meeting resistance from
union leaders, the government appealed to their nationalism, saying that "unless Korea followed the IMF
conditions, then we cannot expect any more financial support," says Labor Minister Lee Ki Ho. The unions
accepted changing the law to allow easier layoffs after the government promised to monitor companies for
any abuses of the new law.
The government used the same message to win over the general populace. A state-run television network
launched a campaign to collect and export gold to raise hard currency. Nearly two million Koreans accepted
weak won for their gold jewelry, with which the government raised $1.3 billion. Egging them on was a
government-sponsored TV advertisement featuring Koreans winning a hard-fought tug-of-war. The message:
"Let us overcome the economic crisis."
At the same time, Mr. Kim pressured the chaebols. Much blame had been put on the conglomerates, all
debt-ridden and bloated, for contributing to the economic crisis, yet they had resisted reform efforts in the
past. But on Feb. 6, the leaders of the 30 top chaebols met with Mr. Kim and agreed to a five-point plan in
which they promised to improve financial disclosure, discard businesses and focus on core industries. The
framework for corporate reform was quickly in place.
In April, Korea tested the skepticism of the international financial community with its first-ever sovereign bond.
After some initial wariness, the issue was a smashing success. The government issued a total of $4 billion --
$1 billion more than originally planned -- at a rate lower than anticipated. Only four months after the IMF
agreement, foreign-investor confidence began to return -- even though the deepest part of the recession was
yet to come, the state of the banks was worsening and companies were falling into bankruptcy by the
thousands.
What sold the bond was a feeling among investors that Mr. Kim had chosen the right direction and was
sticking to it, and that differentiated Korea from other emerging markets like Russia and Indonesia. "People
were so desperate for something that looked good," says Kim Conroy, then an emerging-markets portfolio
manager at J.P. Morgan & Co. "The Korean government, more than most, was at least trying."
The growing confidence in Korea, combined with IMF funds and an agreement between Korea and foreign
banks to restructure short-term debt, helped stabilize the won. From a 1997 high of 843 won to the dollar, the
local currency's value had fallen to 1,962 won by December 1997. But by April 1998, the won had settled
down around the 1,400 level, and by this past January, it had reached a post crisis high of 1,149. It is
currently trading around 1,200. The calmer, stronger won allowed the government to lower interest rates,
which eased the pressure on Korea's debt-burdened corporations and in turn helped the country's troubled
banks. In December 1997, the benchmark three-year corporate bond yield hit 31.1%; by this past January, it
had fallen to 7.15%.
Once Korea regained confidence, it worked hard to keep it. Last May, the spread between the yield on
Korea's sovereign bond and on U.S. Treasurys widened sharply, meaning that investors considered the
bonds a riskier play. Byeon Yang Ho, a director at the Ministry of Finance and Economy, met about two
dozen bond investors in a New York hotel. Instead of delivering a long-winded lecture on the virtues of
Korean reform -- as the investors might have expected -- Mr. Byeon showed atypical openness. He asked the
assembly why they thought the spread had broadened and let them pepper him with suggestions -- speed up
bank reform, force the chaebols to change -- that he scribbled down in a notebook and carried back to his
ministry. The open attitude was a big hit with investors.
The government then began making good on some of its tougher reform promises. In June, regulators closed
five weak banks. By September, the government could report progress on recapitalizing banks and buying
their bad loans. By December, the five largest chaebols had agreed to reform programs with their creditors to
bring down debt. The chaebols in general agreed to mergers, acquisitions and asset swaps to consolidate

--- Page 143 ---
Page 143 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
major industries, such as automobiles and semiconductors, in the hope of cutting overcapacity and boosting
competitiveness.
Those that broke from the government-favored plans faced stern consequences. In December, LG Group
balked on the terms for merging its memory-chip maker with that of Hyundai Group. LG's banks, with the
government's public support, cut off fresh loans to LG's chip firm until it fell in line.
Yet all along the way, analysts have criticized Korea's reform efforts as insufficient. The government's plan to
support the banks wasn't enough to rescue them, the experts said. The chaebols' house-cleaning efforts have
been half-hearted at best, they argued.
To be sure, the Korean government sees its efforts as far from complete, and it continues to push reforms.
The more moderate approach has helped to preserve social peace -- a crucial factor in a country with a rich
postwar history of protest -- and to sell the public on the reforms undertaken. More important, if the growth
projections hold up, the government hopes it can let an improving economy help work problems out, rather
than, say, buying up every bad loan to support the banking sector.
"The government doesn't have to do everything," says Oh Jong Nam, an economic secretary to the president.
Yet some skeptics worry that the gaps in reform today will weigh down growth in the future. The current
revival, they argue, could be short-lived. Those who find the government's approach too optimistic believe
Korea's economic future may depend on a small group of adventurous free-market reformers. They are
fighting the old ways of doing business and have been given more power by Mr. Kim's program and the
economic crisis.
Nam Sang Koo, a 52-year-old business professor at Korea University in Seoul, is one of the first two
representatives of minority and foreign shareholders on the board of directors of SK Telecom Co., Korea's
largest cellular-phone service provider and a member of SK Group. Mr. Nam's assignment is to monitor
management to protect shareholders' interests -- a radical change in a country where families have run the
chaebols as personal fiefs.
Mr. Nam took up his post in March 1998 and quickly became concerned about rumors that SK Telecom was
providing financial assistance to affiliates that Mr. Nam considered weak. Chaebols often support their worst
companies with funds from their best. SK Telecom's management, Mr. Nam says, at first was uncooperative.
But he got information from supportive shareholders, started to piece the finances together and then
confronted management. Under this pressure, management revealed about $250 million in holdings of the
affiliates' commercial paper and other outstanding funds. Mr. Nam threatened to file a lawsuit if management
didn't clean them off the books. By the end of September, SK Telecom had gotten rid of the
commercial-paper holdings. A company spokesman declined to comment on the matter.
Another adventurer is 43-year-old Cho Dong Kil, vice chairman of paper maker Hansol Paper Co. and a
member of the family that controls the Hansol Group. Hansol suffered severe cash-flow problems last year,
and Mr. Cho worried about his ability to compete with major international paper companies.
His solution was another departure for Korea, where chaebol managers traditionally have been reluctant to
shed key assets or cede control to foreign firms. Hansol reached a preliminary agreement in June 1998 with
Norway's Norske Skog Industrier ASA and Canada's Abitibi-Consolidated Inc. to merge some of their Asian
operations into a three-way joint venture, and sealed the deal this year. About a third of Hansol's directors
protested that Mr. Cho was cutting the heart out of the company.
But Mr. Cho held firm: "What's the point of maintaining control if you don't make money?"

Dollar Ends Lower Against Euro, YenAfter Falling on Latest Kosovo News
Foreign Exchange
Dollar Ends Lower Against Euro, Yen After Falling on Latest Kosovo News
By Marianne Sullivan
Dow Jones Newswires
664 words
11 May 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C13
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- The dollar was unable to recover against the euro and yen after fresh, but fleeting, hopes of an
end to the conflict in Kosovo drove it lower.
Early in the New York day, news that the Yugoslav army had announced a partial withdrawal of military and
police forces in Kosovo sparked a broad dollar sell-off.
The euro spiked 3/4 cent to a new four-week high around $1.0836 and the dollar fell from about 121.20 yen to
120.60 yen on the news of the pullout.
Although the North Atlantic Treaty Organization and the U.S. called the Yugoslav move a mere
"half-measure," the dollar was unable to recover.
Clearly, the euro continues to bounce off events in Kosovo, said Alex Beuzelin, market analyst at Ruesch
International in Washington. Earlier yesterday, the euro sank as low as $1.0710 as markets in Asia got their
first chance to trade on news that NATO had inadvertently bombed the Chinese Embassy in Belgrade Friday.
"But there also seems to be a feeling in the market that we are moving toward a resolution and that is
contributing to the euro's underlying strength," Mr. Beuzelin added.
The euro, despite negative euro-zone economic fundamentals, has been moving higher since hitting its
record low of $1.0540 on April 30.
Late yesterday afternoon in New York, the dollar was trading at 120.63 yen, down from 120.80 yen Friday in
New York. The euro was trading at $1.0786, up from $1.0761. Sterling was trading at $1.6250, down from
$1.6305.
Near midday Tuesday in Tokyo, the dollar was trading at 120.33 yen, while the euro was at $1.0783.Sterling
was at $1.6264.
The Swiss franc also gained slightly yesterday against the dollar amid new signs of peace, shedding its
traditional role of a safe-harbor currency and surrendering to Switzerland's proximity to the conflict.
Sterling, however, tumbled as low as $1.6235 yesterday morning in Europe as markets continued to react to
Bank of England Governor Eddie George's comment Friday that the pound is "clearly too strong." Sterling
was doubly hit as speculative traders sold it on the back of HSBC Holdings PLC's confirmation of an
agreement to buy Republic New York Corp. and Safra Republic Holdings SA for $10.3 billion.
Commodity-linked currencies continued to come under pressure from news that the Bank of England plans to
sell more than half of its holdings in gold.
In Australia, where gold is a major export, the Australian dollar and Australian gold-mining stocks were hit
hard. Late in New York, the Australian dollar was trading at US$0.6664, sharply lower than US$0.6718 late
Friday.
The Bank of England said Friday it plans to cut its gold holdings to 300 tons from 715 tons, pushing gold's
price down to $278.40 per troy ounce yesterday in New York. Since the announcement, gold has fallen $11
per ounce.
In Canada, the slump in gold prices combined with concern about a divergence in U.S. and Canadian
interest rates to pressure the Canadian dollar. Late yesterday, the U.S. dollar was trading at C$1.4609, up
from C$1.4582 late Friday.

--- Page 145 ---
Page 145 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Bank of Canada cut rates on May 4 and market players are focused on signs of changes in U.S. and
Canadian rates.
The Bank of Canada is thought to be leaning toward another cut in short-term rates around the time of its
May 19 semiannual monetary-policy report. But there are fears the U.S. Federal Reserve will adopt a
tightening bias on monetary policy at next Tuesday's Federal Open Market Committee meeting. Such a bias
would pressure the Canadian dollar by increasing the yield differential in favor of U.S. assets.

Rising Rates Fan Fears Inflation Will Cool Bull Market
Abreast of the Market
Rising Rates Fan Fears Inflation Will Cool Bull Market
By Gregory Zuckerman
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,867 words
10 May 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Inflation isn't raging, but the fear of it is drawing blood on Wall Street.
Rising interest rates, caused by concerns that a strong economy might soon begin generating inflation,
have helped cool off the formerly highflying shares of technology and finance companies. And while the Dow
Jones Industrial Average managed to end last week at a record 11031.59, its gains have come largely from
rallies in shares of cyclical companies. Those rallies have been spurred by hopes that economically sensitive
companies can boost earnings by raising prices. And higher prices is what inflation is all about.
"Analysts have been saying since the end of last year that rising rates would stop the bull market, but the
market has plowed ahead," warns James Paulsen, chief investment officer at Norwest Investment
Management. "Now, for the first time, there's some evidence of inflation, in oil and some raw industrial
commodities, for example, so worries are rising."
Highflying Internet stocks have been among the hardest-hit. The Dow Jones Internet Index finished the week
at 253.76, down 20% from its high on April 13. Bellwether America Online has tumbled to 118 3/16 from its
record high of 175 1/2 on April 6, although Internet stocks overall still are up a remarkable 69% for the year.
The punishment that so many fastgrowing stocks are enduring is being dealt out in large part by a slumping
bond market, a direct reflection of inflation fears. The yield on the benchmark 30-year Treasury bond, which
moves in the opposite direction of its price, Friday stood at 5.808%, up from 5.08% on Feb. 1 and the highest
level since June 1998.
Higher rates for borrowing mean financing costs will rise for any number of companies. At the same time,
higher rates tend to curb consumer spending. Mortgage rates are at their highest levels since March,
contributing to recent weakness in financial stocks. And the higher that bond yields go, the more likely
investors will be to switch from stocks to the safety of bonds.
The higher a stock flies, at least in relation to its earnings, the more dangerous rising rates can be.
Internet-related stocks and other big stock-market winners have soared as investors bet that the companies
will generate big earnings -- at least some day. But as interest rates rise, investors tend to assign a lower
value to the promised future earnings of those companies. The lesson: A dollar of future earnings is worth a
lot less when interest rates and inflation rise.
So while cyclical stocks have bailed the market out so far, the higher interest rates go, the greater the
chance that key highflying technology, Internet and financial stocks will come back down to earth, taking the
overall stock market down with them.
"High rates hurt technology and finance stocks the most, and those are the leaders that we need," says
Richard Eakle, president of Eakle Associates in Fair Haven, N.J., an advisory firm that counts hedge funds,
including those of George Soros, among its clients. "If rates continue to push higher, they will act as a real
brake on the market."
High rates can certainly slow the market, but it isn't likely they alone will kill the bull. Only if inflation truly
rears its ugly head, driving long-term interest rates higher and spurring the Federal Reserve to raise interest
rates, will the lengthy bull market finally end, analysts say. And so far, the evidence of rising inflation is
mixed at best.
Many stock-market analysts have pointed to the recent impressive rally in cyclical stocks as one reason to
fear higher inflation. If these stocks are up, they figure, it must mean these companies are regaining "pricing
power," the ability to raise prices.

--- Page 147 ---
Page 147 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Some evidence of improved pricing power does exist. Last week, data from the National Association of
Purchasing Management showed that manufacturers are paying the highest prices for supplies since
December 1997, while nonmanufacturers saw prices rise for the second straight month after 12 months of
either a decline or no change.
And some companies, such as paper-producer Georgia Pacific and steelmaker Nucor, have been able to
raise prices lately.
But while the best news on inflation is clearly behind us, a troubling rise in prices may not be in the cards.
Friday's employment data showed that wages rose minimally, suggesting that companies may not feel
pressure to raise prices to offset rising wages.
And much of the price gain in the NAPM index was concentrated in petroleum-based products and doesn't
augur an overall rise in inflation. Economists doubt the Fed will raise rates until clear evidence of higher
consumer prices arises. Indeed, other than oil, most commodities are showing little sign of a sustained rise.
Meanwhile, some analysts argue that the jump in shares of cyclical companies simply reflects a search for
relative value on the part of investors, rather than a sign the companies are regaining the ability to raise
prices of products.
"People who think pricing power is returning are absolutely, 100% wrong -- if anything, prices of the
companies I follow are stabilizing at low levels," says Lisa Shalett, capital-goods analyst at Sanford C.
Bernstein. "There's tons of excess manufacturing capacity still, so we won't get pricing power for the next four
or five years."
Indeed, last week, farm-equipment maker Deere warned investors about "aggressive" price discounting in its
construction-equipment market. Yet Deere's stock nevertheless rose, suggesting that it really is relative value,
rather than incipient pricing power, that is spurring investor interest in at least some of the cyclical stocks.
"If you talk to the companies, and the dealers they're selling to, and look at the numbers, there's no evidence
pricing has improved, and that was the single message from Deere," says Ms. Shalett.
Deere isn't the only one having trouble raising prices. At US Steel Group, a unit of USX, "we've met with
some modest success since April 1 in getting price improvements, but it's just been that-modest-although
we're hopeful as the year goes on," says J. Paul Kadlic, USX's vice president of sales.
Some argue that the rise in interest rates may actually serve to reinvigorate, rather than kill off, the aging bull
market by slowing the U.S. economy to a sustainable, noninflationary pace.
"Rising rates are a good check on the economy and inflation, and we could look back on this period as a
refresher," says Mr. Paulsen. "If rates stop going up, financials will be the leaders again, and if the economy
slows, growth stocks will re-emerge."
---
Friday's Market Activity
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 84.77 points, or 0.77%, to end at 11031.59. That marked its best
finish ever, topping last Monday's high, and thus ending a fitful week with highs at the start and the finish. The
Dow industrials increased 242.55 for the week, a gain of 2.2%.
Shares of International Business Machines moved up eight points to 217 1/4, helped by some encouraging
comments about the computer maker's second-quarter performance from an analyst at SoundView
Technology.
Other growth-stock names also commanded higher prices Friday. Lucent Technologies improved 1 1/4 to 57
3/4. General Electric gained 1 11/16 to 109 15/16. Wal-Mart Stores advanced 1 7/8 to 44 3/4.
Banking stocks had an indifferent session. Mellon Bank advanced 1 3/16 to 73 11/16. Chase Manhattan
added 13/16 to 78 3/8. But those were among the biggest winners in the sector.
Brokerage-firm names enjoyed some success, amid hope that the bull market in equities would continue, as
well as on spillover from the Internet-brokerage sector. Morgan Stanley Dean Witter rose 5 13/16 to 100
11/16. Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette added 5 1/4 to finish at 65 7/8. Lehman Brothers Holdings gained 3 to 55
7/8.
Online-brokerage operation Ameritrade rose 5 1/4 to 113 3/4, while E*Trade added 5 5/16 to 110 3/8, both on
the Nasdaq Stock Market. Lehman started coverage of both stocks with "buy" ratings.

The broader technology group did well. Sun Microsystems (Nasdaq) moved up 2 7/16 to 58 1/16. Applied
Materials (Nasdaq), which lost ground Thursday, recovered Friday, adding 2 13/16 to 56 3/4. EMC moved up
1 13/16 to 97 5/16.
Bell Atlantic gained 2 5/16 to 58 3/16, while GTE added 2 3/4 to 67. The Justice Department resolved its
antitrust concerns regarding Bell Atlantic's $65 billion acquisition of GTE.
Texaco gained 5 1/16 to 67 1/4 in Big Board trading, then fell back to 67 1/4 in after-hours markets. The

7/8. Neither Chevron nor Texaco would comment, but people familiar with the situation said Chevron is in
talks to acquire Texaco for roughly $80 a share, or about $42 billion.
Barrick Gold lost 2 3/4 to 20 1/2, while Homestake Mining declined 1 3/16 to 9 7/16, and Placer Dome
dropped 2 7/16 to 13 3/16. The group fell among a broad decline in gold prices, after the United Kingdom

in the sector.
McDermott International moved up 2 5/16 to 30 1/4. The New Orleans energy equipment and services
concern said it will buy the 37% of J. Ray McDermott that it doesn't already own for $513 million. J. Ray
McDermott gained 4 3/4 to 35 1/4.
Ingram Micro moved up 2 1/2 to 27 1/8, while Tech Data (Nasdaq) added 3 1/4 to 29 1/8. The two companies
were among the technology-products distributors that Compaq Computer named as exclusive distributors of
its products, an analyst at Donaldson Lufkin & Jenrette said. Compaq shares moved 1/8 higher to 24 3/4.
Litton Industries fell 1 5/8 to 63 1/8. The Woodland Hills, Calif., electronics and defense systems maker made
separate, unsolicited bids to acquire Newport News Shipbuilding and its merger partner, Avondale Industries,
for a total of $1.8 billion in cash and stock. Newport News moved up 5 3/4 to 32 3/4, while Avondale
Industries (Nasdaq) added 4 5/16 to 36 1/16.
DuPont moved ahead 1 7/16 to 74 1/8. Company officials said they planned to create a tracking stock for
DuPont's lifesciences business as a means of providing currency for acquisitions.

would boost its U.S. presence with the purchase, but it is believed to be most interested in Republic's

Gold Futures Prices Drop on U.K. Auction Plan
Commodities
Gold Futures Prices Drop on U.K. Auction Plan
By Janet Whitman
Dow Jones Newswires
770 words
10 May 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C17
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Gold futures prices fell Friday on news that the United Kingdom Treasury plans to sell 125
metric tons of the metal from its reserves.
The gold will be sold in five auctions, one every other month, starting in July. It will be replaced with
foreign-currency assets. In the medium term, the U.K. Treasury said, it will reduce its gold holdings to around
300 tons from 715 tons now.
The nearby June gold contract on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange fell $7 to
$283.70 an ounce on the news, recovering from an early slide to $280.30 an ounce.
"The U.K. news just triggered massive [speculative] selling and long liquidation," said one bullion dealer in
New York. "With the specter of IMF and Swiss sales, this has broken gold's back for the time being."
Long liquidation is the selling of long positions, or bets that prices will rise.
Proposed sales by the IMF have been looming over the market for months, while Switzerland recently voted
to break the link between the Swiss franc and gold and plans to sell part of its gold reserves.
These prospective disposals have led to speculation that European central banks would try to get gold sales
in first.
While expectations of central-bank sales have been factored into prices to some degree, the specific plan
outlined by the U.K. Treasury took market participants by surprise.
"A lot of people were shocked," said Bridget Fraser, managing director with Bankers Trust in New York. "It's
the same old stuff, but at least it's not just jawboning."
"It's kind of weird the way the bank did it, but I guess they want to be more transparent," said another bullion
dealer. "In the grand scheme of things, they want to change their assets, and that's sending a message to
other central banks. The fear is that other banks won't be as transparent."
While agreeing that the news from the U.K. Treasury was "a very dramatic statement," Joseph Rosta,
research director with CPM Group in New York, said he expects the sales to have a minimal effect on prices.
"It was used as a vehicle to take profits. In terms of a real price effect, it's marginal."
Mr. Rosta and other market observers pointed to rising inflationary fears as a factor that could offset bearish
sentiment in gold.
Bullion dealers and analysts are hoping other central banks that decide to sell gold will follow Britain and hold
auctions. Such a policy would at least underpin quotes, they believe.
Dealers noted that gold prices have declined ahead of persistent rumors of central-bank sales, some true,
some false. If central banks decided at least to agree on organized auctions, the market could make an
adjustment for the extra known supplies and settle down, dealers say.
"Prices tumbled Friday because the market was caught on the hop," says Colin Griffith, head of precious
metals at Standard Bank London, a unit of Standard Bank Investment Corp. "For decades, the Bank of
England has been a strong supporter of gold and the London gold market, so the market wasn't expecting it
to sell."

--- Page 152 ---
Page 152 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Gold was above $400 at the beginning of 1996, but the price has collapsed, mainly because of a series of
unexpected European central-bank sales that flooded the market, dealers say.
In other commodity markets Friday:
SOYBEAN OIL: Futures fell on the Chicago Board of Trade, after a drop in palm-oil futures triggered by
projected output increases in Malaysia. The May soybean-oil contract dropped 0.37 cent to 18.85 cents a
pound. Palm Intelligence Services Sdn. estimated Malaysia's production to have risen 22% to 858,000 metric
tons in April. Additionally, the U.S. National Oilseed Processors Association reported a rise in the U.S. weekly
soybean crush to 29.6 million bushels, pointing to higher soybean-oil production and an addition to existing
large stockpiles.
HOGS: Futures rose to a 10-month high on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange amid concerns about declining
supplies. The June contract rose 1.55 cents to 60.47 cents a pound. Hog slaughter on Friday was 350,000
head, the U.S. Department of Agriculture reported. That was down 2,000 from a week ago and off 13,000
from a year earlier. "Packers are having to chase supplies," said Leo Burns, broker with Iowa Grain on the
CME floor.

Korea's Hyundai Kicks Off `Apron War'---New Fund Begs Housewives to Invest and Save the Economy
International
Korea's Hyundai Kicks Off `Apron War' --- New Fund Begs Housewives to Invest and Save the
Economy
By Jane L. Lee
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
632 words
6 May 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A22
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
SEOUL, South Korea -- Hyundai Corp., South Korea's largest business group, has hit the jackpot this year:
the purses of Korean housewives.
Just two months after its launch, Hyundai's "Buy Korea Fund" has vacuumed up about five trillion won ($4.18
billion) from investors, including thousands of housewives, making it the biggest investment fund South Korea
has ever seen. And that's just the start: It plans to collect an additional 30 trillion won to 60 trillion won this
year and reach 100 trillion won in three years.
Hyundai is funneling this money into local blue-chip stocks and bonds -- including some of its own affiliates --
and that's making some people nervous.
The country's main stock-market index has soared almost 45% since the fund started March 2, and some
brokers attribute that surge partly to the fund. Analysts say this kind of market-moving clout puts tremendous
power into the hands of Hyundai's finance affiliates, which manage and market the fund-Hyundai Securities
Co., Hyundai Investment Trust & Securities Co. and Hyundai Investment Trust Management Co. The
government has started investigating it, as well as funds run by the next four largest conglomerates.
Hyundai used a tried and true tactic to put the Buy Korea Fund on steroids: it appealed to the patriotism of
frugal Korean housewives who control their husbands' salaries. After the country's economic crisis began 18
months ago, citizens rushed to donate gold to shore up the central bank's reserves and stopped eating in
foreign restaurants to help the balance of payments, so Hyundai's strategy had success written all over it.
Hyundai kicked off the fund -- which is similar to a mutual fund -- with the slogan "Apron War 1999." In the
first Apron War in 1592, Korean housewives helped quash the Japanese enemy by carrying rocks for
ammunition in their aprons. Hyundai's message: Housewives could help save the country once again by
putting their money into the fund and boosting local companies.
Then last month, with stocks rallying, Hyundai decided to go a step further. It began supplementing the ad
campaign with public presentations for housewives touting the glories of the stock market. The case hasn't
been difficult to make: The benchmark Kospi index has almost tripled since its low last year. Lee Ik Chi, the
chairman and chief executive of Hyundai Securities, has conducted all the sessions.
"The only way you can become rich anymore is investing in the stock market," boomed Mr. Lee into a
microphone at a presentation last week. More than 300 women took notes and nodded in agreement. Mr. Lee
predicts that the Kospi will rise to 6000 by 2005 because he believes that investors have few other options for
investing; the index is about 771 points. Interest rates, which touched 30% last year, have fallen to about
7%, so bonds and time deposits at banks are unappealing. And to top it off, the government will guarantee up
to only 20 million won of a person's bank account beginning in 2001.
It's the fund's market-moving power, and the possible conflicts-of-interest if it invests in the shares of Hyundai
Group affiliates, that's caught the attention of regulators. No specific allegations of wrongdoing have been
made, but South Korea's Fair Trade Commission says it will investigate transactions between affiliates of the
top five chaebols, or conglomerates, including Hyundai.
Mr. Lee, at Hyundai Securities, hopes the investigation will give the Buy Korea Fund a clean bill. And his
advice to investors mulling whether to invest: "Tomorrow may be too late."
