Sitting Pretty:Strong Banking SystemHelps Australia ProsperAs Neighbors Struggle---Luck Plays Part, but SuccessOf Economic ReformsMay Off...
Sitting Pretty: Strong Banking System Helps Australia Prosper As Neighbors Struggle --- Luck Plays
Part, but Success Of Economic Reforms May Offer Lesson for Asia --- Zip Claims a Famous Client
By S. Karene Witcher And David Wessel
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
2,055 words
28 April 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
When Asia's economies hit the skids nearly two years ago, it looked like Down Under was soon to be down
and out.
After all, 60% of Australia's export goods were bound for Asia, many of them commodities such as copper,
nickel and aluminum, whose prices were tumbling. Asians also accounted for about half the nation's foreign
tourists, and hotels like the Radisson Resort, along the beachstrewn Gold Coast of Australia's eastern shore,
soon reeled from a decline in arrivals. Several private economists looked around and cut their growth
forecasts.
But Australia hasn't just avoided the Asian-Pacific downturn; it has roared ahead. While the economies of
most of its Asian trading partners contracted last year, Australia's expanded 5.1%, surpassing the U.S.'s
3.9% pace and making it one of the fastest-growing economies in the developed world. And 1999 is likely to
be its eighth consecutive year of growth.
After a decade of unflattering comparisons to Asia's once-booming economies, Australia now is basking in
praise from the most unlikely sources-including the proud Singaporeans who had looked down on Australians
as poor cousins.
During a visit to Australia last month, Singapore Prime Minister Goh Chok Tong recalled the fable of the
tortoise and the hare, likening Australia to the tortoise who surprises the Asian hares by winning the race.
"Australia has a good record over the past 15 years or so" of policies that "have given an underpinning to the
country," Mr. Goh said. "In many parts of Asia, we were concentrating on fast growth, quick infrastructure, but
forgetting the fundamentals."
What accounts for Australia's success? Equal parts good fortune and good management.
Australia's central bank had begun cutting interest rates for domestic reasons a year before the Asian crisis
began in July 1997, so there was a strong dose of stimulus in the country's economic pipeline. Moreover, the
nation had weathered an Asian-style banking crisis in the 1980s; by the mid-1990s, its banks had been rebuilt
and its regulators were battle-hardened.
"The one, two and three main reasons that Australia isn't in the contagion is because of the strength and
soundness of the banking system," says Robert Joss, an American banker recruited in 1993 to turn around
Westpac Banking Corp., one of Australia's biggest banks, after it nearly collapsed under bad debt.
On the management front, Australia let its dollar float freely back in 1983, so it had no rigid exchange rate to
defend, as did such countries as South Korea and Thailand, which tried unsuccessfully and expensively to tie
their currencies to the U.S. dollar. Once the Asian crisis was afoot, its central bank -- in contrast to that of
neighboring New Zealand -- read the situation correctly, and didn't tighten monetary policy to shore up its
currency.
Meanwhile, Australian exporters -- which deregulation and privatization had forced to become more nimble --
diverted their wares from sinking Asian economies to healthier ones elsewhere. When the South Korean
market went sour, for example, Qantas Airways redeployed aircraft on more-promising Indian routes. As
Indonesia's economic crash hammered sales of live cattle there, Australian producers began wooing buyers
in Mexico and Libya. All told, Australia's sales of goods to Asia, including Japan, slid 6% last year, in value
terms, from 1997, while exports to the U.S. and Europe climbed 34% and 42%, respectively. And its total
exports of goods and services rose in 1998, by a modest 2%, to 114.9 billion Australian dollars (US$74.56
billion).

--- Page 189 ---
Page 189 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Australia's floating dollar apparently has allowed it "to sail almost unscathed through the Asian crisis," says
Paul Krugman, an international economist at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology. In a new book, he
asks: "If Australia could so easily avoid getting caught up in its neighbors' economic catastrophe, why couldn't
Indonesia or South Korea do the same?"
His controversial answer: The financial markets have a double standard. When the currency of a country in
which they have confidence, say Australia, plunges, they see it as an excuse to buy; the country benefits, and
the market's good opinion is confirmed. When the same thing happens elsewhere -- in Indonesia, for example
-- investors flee, the country suffers, and the market's bad opinion is ratified.
But John Edwards, chief economist of HongkongBank of Australia Ltd., contends that the answer lies in the
structural changes Australia has made. "They weren't the reason we grew, but they were the reason we
weren't a victim of the Asian crisis, although we shared a number of characteristics of countries that were
victims." These include a heavy foreign-debt load and a relatively big deficit in the current account, a gauge of
trade in goods and services, plus certain fund transfers.
Though "you can't just take a template from somewhere else and slap it on," Australia is "an inspiration for
implementing tough economic reforms," because it has overcome "a number of challenges that Asian
economies are going to face," says Alex Erskine, who watched the Asian crisis unfold as Citibank's regional
market strategist in Singapore.
Of course, the story isn't over yet. Economic growth is likely to slow in the months ahead, though the IMF is
predicting better than 3% growth for 1999, and Australia's already large trade deficit is widening.
However, for all their differences in geography, natural resources, history and culture, Asia's economies might
have learned something by studying Australia's mistakes of the 1980s.
Long before Asia overdosed on easy credit, Australia had done much the same thing, though on a smaller
scale. It deregulated its financial sector and, in 1985, opened the doors to 16 foreign banks. Hungry for
market share, the new competitors from abroad lent readily, which spurred lending at the four big domestic
banks, including Westpac. Real-estate prices soared, and a crop of highflying entrepreneurs emerged,
including Perth businessman Alan Bond, with his flagship Bond Corp. Holdings Ltd. By the early 1990s, a
recession had pricked the asset bubbles. Mr. Bond's empire collapsed, owing creditors A$10 billion, and he
now is in jail for corporate misdeeds. Regulators say troubled debt now amounts to 1% of the outstanding
loans of all banks in Australia, down sharply from a 1992 peak of 10%.
Before the 1980s crisis, the science of assessing credit risk "just didn't exist" in Australian banking, says Les
Phelps, executive general manager of the nation's bank regulator. In its wake, banks such as Westpac moved
to implement a better risk-management system and provide greater disclosure, and regulators added staff,
increased the frequency of bank visits, and standardized and tightened definitions of such things as troubled
assets.
"After the disasters of the cowboy era, everybody got religion," says Mr. Joss, who recently left Westpac to
become dean of Stanford University's business school. "Corporate balance sheets are much healthier in
Australia today than they were six or eight years ago." As both equity and debt capital got scarcer, Australian
companies had to manage resources better, something that Asian companies must learn to do, he says. The
government, too, is in better financial shape today, having recorded a budget surplus, excluding asset sales,
last year and predicting another surplus for the fiscal year ending in June.
As a result, Australia was better prepared than some other economies when Thailand's 1997 devaluation set
off a chain reaction that turned growth in Asia into recession. Not surprisingly, the Australian dollar fell, losing
25% of its value as the crisis deepened. The currency, which had been at 80 U.S. cents in late 1996,
weakened to 74 cents after Thailand devalued, and touched bottom at 55 cents after Russia's default and
devaluation in August 1998.
Inside the Australian central bank, however, policy makers concluded that the country's dollar would have to
stay weak for at least six months before a resulting rise in import prices would stoke inflation. Betting
correctly that the currency would rebound, the bank, unlike many of its counterparts, didn't tighten monetary
policy, though it spent US$2.5 billion, 20% of its hard-currency hoard, to buy the Australian dollar in an effort
to stem selling that was deemed mostly "speculative." Its wager paid off; the Australian dollar has been
hovering around 63 U.S. cents, and inflation has been steady at around 1.6%.
Central bank chief Ian Macfarlane remains cautious, however. "We had been expecting a noticeable
slowdown, and we still are," he says. "But it will be a slowing off a much higher basis than we formerly
thought."
What happened in New Zealand, which also overhauled its economy in the 1980s, underscores the
importance of Mr. Macfarlane's policy decision. New Zealand's central bankers had been tightening

--- Page 190 ---
Page 190 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
monetary policy through the end of 1996 to cool inflationary pressures, and began easing in early 1997. But
partly out of fear that the weakening New Zealand dollar would stir up inflation, it didn't ease quickly enough
-- and a recession ensued.
"We'd probably have eased more if we'd actually had a realistic understanding" of the magnitude of both the
Asian crisis and a drought that hurt local agricultural production, says Donald Brash, New Zealand's central
banker. Still, Mr. Brash thinks that because of the time it takes for such changes to affect an economy,
monetary policy would have needed to be "much easier in 1996" to stop New Zealand -- whose economy
now is growing againfrom sliding into recession in early 1998.
But propelling a capitalist economy forward takes more than strong banks and central bankers who are
prepared to risk a weakening currency. It also takes businesses that can and do respond when the world
around them changes.
For years, Australian businesses and workers had struggled to cope with the dismantling of policies that, in
the government's view, were restraining the Australian economy. Tariff barriers protecting Australian
industries were stripped away. The rigid national wage-setting structure that had governed pay has moved
toward a productivity-based system of labor agreements reached at individual companies. Air travel,
electricity and telecommunications have been opened to competition.
The changes were painful and controversial, but the resulting flexibility now is yielding benefits.
Take, for example, Zip Heaters (Aust) Pty. Ltd., Sydney, which makes instant water heaters for hot drinks.
Like many other Australian manufacturers, Michael Crouch, chairman of the closely held company, which
employs about 200, decided in the mid-1980s to look outside Australia to build his business. Zip now exports
to about 20 countries, deriving 65% of its earnings from overseas. Indeed, Mr. Crouch boasts that several
world leaders, including British Prime Minister Tony Blair, use Zips; Mr. Blair's office wouldn't comment, citing
a standing policy.
Before the Asian crisis, Zip was exporting about 10% of its output to Asia. That figure has been halved, but
Zip has shifted its focus to the buoyant British market, where Mr. Crouch says sales have more than offset
the Asian slump.
Over the past five years, deregulation has cut his business
costs-helping trim 20% off Zip's energy bills, for example. But Mr.
Crouch credits Australia's low
interest rates-Australian companies can
borrow at about 5% to 6%-and its flexible exchange rate as the biggest
factors in his company's favor. "I can't emphasize enough how important
that has been to Australian manufacturers," he adds.

Rubin Outlines Plan to Curb World Fiscal Crises
Economy
Rubin Outlines Plan to Curb World Fiscal Crises
By Michael M. Phillips
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
625 words
22 April 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
WASHINGTON -- With the global economic crisis easing, the Clinton administration laid out a modest agenda
for remodeling the world financial system to prevent and shorten future emergencies.
Treasury Secretary Robert Rubin, in a speech at the Johns Hopkins School for Advanced International
Studies, mostly reiterated proposals that have been discussed for months, adding some detail in preparation
for next week's meetings of world economic officials. On Tuesday, President Clinton said these changes will
help ensure "a platform of prosperity" for the next century.
Though generally cautious, Mr. Rubin did take a step forward to address one of the main issues in the current
crisis: whether developing countries should tie their currencies to a major currency, such as the U.S. dollar, or
let them float freely.
"The right exchange-rate regime is a choice for the individual country," he told students and professors. "Yet
at the center of each recent crisis has been a rigid exchange-rate regime that proved ultimately
unsustainable."
In Brazil and East Asia, panic ensued when speculators and investors began to bet that fixed-currency rates
wouldn't last.
Going beyond previous statements on the subject, Mr. Rubin encouraged developing nations either to let their
currencies float or to establish legal constraints that make it extremely difficult to loosen a fixed rate. One
such system is a currency board, such as Argentina's, which requires the central bank to keep one U.S.
dollar in reserve for every peso in circulation.
To drive the point home, Mr. Rubin warned developing countries that the U.S. won't support huge
international rescues for countries that try to defend nonbinding-fixed-exchange rates, unless global financial
stability is at stake or there is a credible legal constraint in place.
Over the past two years, the U.S. has backed multibillion-dollar loans for Indonesia, Thailand, South Korea,
Russia and Brazil, all of which tried to defend particular exchange rates.
Mr. Rubin will take the administration's financial-reform priorities to Monday's meeting with top economic
officials from the Group of Seven major industrialized nations, with a view toward winning agreement at
June's G-7 summit in Cologne, Germany. The complex steps, however, are expected to be implemented only
gradually.
Among other items on the U.S. agenda for the G-7 next week: Mechanisms to ensure developing countries
don't get hooked on short-term foreign borrowing; tools to ensure that Western banks, hedge funds and
others correctly assess and disclose the risk of their developing-country investments; and bond contracts to
make it easier for countries that get into financial trouble to cut deals with private creditors. Mr. Rubin said
bondholders shouldn't always expect to recover their money when a country in which they invested is forced
to restructure debts.
"These things, which don't sound glamorous, have enormous influence on how markets work and on who's
able to borrow," said one senior Treasury official.
The G-7 also will discuss the economic situation in Russia, which has struggled with financial turmoil since
devaluing the ruble and defaulting on some debt last August. After months of talks, the Russians and the
International Monetary Fund are nearing a small-loan agreement that will help the Russians to repay their
existing IMF debts this year.

--- Page 192 ---
Page 192 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"The next few days will probably bring us good news" about a Russian deal, IMF Managing Director Michel
Camdessus said yesterday.
The G-7 also will consider proposals to increase debt relief for poor developing countries, financed partly by
the sale of as much as $2.8 billion from the IMF's gold holdings.

World Watch
International
World Watch
Compiled By Paul J. Deveney
1,523 words
19 April 1999
The Wall Street Journal

A15
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
EUROPE
EU Seems Unlikely
To File an Appeal
Of Banana Ruling
The dispute over banana imports that has caused so much friction between the U.S. and the European
Union, two of the world's biggest trading partners, may finally be coming to an end. EU diplomats say
Brussels doesn't plan to appeal the World Trade Organization's recent ruling that found that the EU
discriminates against imports of bananas from Latin America in favor of shipments from its former colonies in
Africa and the Caribbean. Apparently nearly all of the 15 member nations feel it's time to move on to more
pressing matters -- that is, except for France, which observers say isn't ready to give up the long-standing
battle just yet. But either way the ultimate decision on all this still rests with the European Commission, the
trade's bloc's executive body.
And if the commission -- which is serving in a caretaker capacity after recently resigning amid allegations of
financial mismanagement -- does decide against filing an appeal, it will have to scurry around and come up
with ways to modify the import regime once again. The unusual part about this saga is that the U.S. has no
banana exports of its own and has taken up the fight these past six years on behalf of big U.S. companies,
like Chiquita Brands International, which has vast plantations in Latin America.
Switzerland Moves Off Gold Standard
Switzerland could very well begin revaluing its vast gold reserves and start selling them on the open market
in the not too distant future. The nation's voters yesterday approved a new constitution that among other
things eliminates once and for all the traditional gold backing of the Swiss franc. That move will permit the
tiny country nestled comfortably between France, Germany, Austria and Italy to eventually start selling some
of its 1,300 tons of surplus gold. To keep nervous traders at bay, Switzerland, which has the highest
per-capita gold reserves in the world, is stressing that any such sales would be made in small increments
over several years. The World Gold Council apparently thinks that strategy will work as well; it recently said
the market could easily absorb all that gold if it were sold in equal installments over 10 years.
Spain Cuts Phone Rates, Other Tariffs
Reacting to the recent jump in consumer prices, Spain went ahead and approved cuts in electricity tariffs,
phone rates and even the amount that toll collectors reach out for along the nation's highways. For all the
fanfare, though, most economists weren't impressed by what they see as a Band-Aid approach to dealing
with an inflation rate that at 2.2% far outpaces the increases in prices elsewhere in the euro zone. They say
Madrid still isn't addressing the structural rigidities in Spain's service sector, where year-on-year inflation
reached 3.8% last month. Nonetheless, the tariff reductions sent utility shares tumbling; electricity companies
were particularly hard hit with Endesa down 5.6% and Iberdrola off 5.1%. And stock in Spain's largest phone
company, Telefonica, lost 4% of its value.
U.K. Asked to Propose Eurobond Plan
The European Union dominates the multitrillion dollar industry in Eurobonds -- paper that is issued by
borrowers outside their own country. Financial market associations have been warning for some time,
however, that a EU plan to slap a tax on savings instruments could drive that business to other shores. Now,
to try and cool the debate, EU finance ministers have agreed to consider excluding some Eurobonds from the
savings tax if Britain can come up with an acceptable way to do it. London is all stirred up about this issue

--- Page 194 ---
Page 194 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
because not only could a withholding tax drive the Eurobond market away from the EU but from the country
that is at the very center of Eurobond trading -- the U.K.

difficulties.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Overcapacity Spurs Taiwan Talks
Some of Taiwan's biggest companies are talking about mergers -- something there hasn't been that much of
on an island that has managed to skirt the worst of the Asian financial crisis. This time, though, the few
negotiations there are aren't being generated by distress sales by companies facing cash-flow problems.
Instead, firms in key industries are looking at such arrangements as a way to deal with overcapacity. For
example, the conglomerate Walsin Lihwa has begun negotiations with the struggling steelmaker Yieh Hsing
about spinning off each other's specialty steel businesses into a joint venture. And Fubon Securities, one of
Taiwan's largest securities companies, is contemplating marriage with local brokerage firm Universal
Securities -- though those talks are still only at the initial stages.
Daewoo Group Moves to Slash Its Debt
South Korea's second largest conglomerate, Daewoo Group, plans to make payment this year on about half
of its 59.88 trillion won ($49.2 billion) in debts. In a revised restructuring plan, it indicated that it plans to raise
the money to repay the loans by selling some of its assets, such as subsidiaries and real estate, and offering
rights issues and securing foreign investment. Daewoo is doing all this in the hopes of lowering its
debt-to-equity ratio -- a key condition stipulated by the International Monetary Fund when it arranged a record
$58.35 billion bailout package for the country at the height of its economic crisis.

-- is a whopping 45% higher than its previous offer, and nearly 11% above the price the U.S. investment bank
is now bidding for all of Rajadamri.
-- Winbond Electronic, Taiwan's No. 3 chip manufacturer, reported its earnings fell 21% for the first three

depreciation expenses. Yet Winbond, a supplier to Japan's Toshiba, also joined a wave of Taiwan
semiconductor producers that predict full-year earnings will improve on sales of new products.
-- Australian gambling concern Tabcorp Holdings launched an offer for Star City Holdings, Sydney's only
casino company, for 933 million Australian dollars (US$600.7 million).
THE AMERICAS
Brazil to Return to Bond Market Soon
Brazil's president says the country plans to return to the international financial markets with a bond issue of
up to $5 billion possibly as soon as the end of June. If so, President Henrique Cardoso said it would mean
that what had been planned for the second half of this year could be carried out in the first half. The leader of
South America's mightiest economy said that officials from his country are in contact with financial authorities
in the U.S. about a possible bond issue, though he added that the details of any such deal are in the hands of
Brazil's finance ministry and its central bank.

In Russia, Capital Flight Continues Unabated---Stemming Exodus Is Key to Achieving Growth, Paying Off Debt
International
In Russia, Capital Flight Continues Unabated --- Stemming Exodus Is Key to Achieving Growth,
Paying Off Debt
By Mark Whitehouse
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
854 words
19 April 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A19
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
MOSCOW -- At Moscow's Sheremetevo international airport, customs officers are cracking down on a threat
to national security. All passengers leaving the country must fill out a declaration, and a Soviet-era chill has
returned as locals and foreigners alike undergo careful questioning.
The enemy isn't spies or drug traffickers. It's capital flight, the estimated $1.5 billion that has been flooding out
of Russia every month since long before last summer's financial collapse. The loss comes to about $120 a
year a person, or about a fifth of the official average wage. As of April 2, it had depleted the central bank's
foreign-currency and gold reserves to a four-year low of $10.7 billion, about $6.7 billion of which was cash.
While Russian officials point to falling inflation and tight spending as signs of stability, nothing speaks more
eloquently about the true state of the economy than the vast amount of money that manages to escape it.
"This issue is absolutely key to Russia, because why should foreign money come in if Russian money won't
stay?" said James Fenkner, strategist at Troika Dialog, a Russian investment bank. "There's that golden rule:
If the insider is selling, maybe you should be, too."
As companies send their profits to offshore accounts and exporters ship out nonreplenishable resources like
natural gas, oil and metals, very little returns in the form of investment. The exodus undermines the rare
benefits of Russia's decision last August to devalue its currency, negating a trade surplus that has grown to
about $2 billion a month. Unless the government can stop the rot, economists say it has little chance of
achieving growth or ever paying off some $145 billion in foreign debts.
The problem cannot fail to worry the International Monetary Fund, which has a mission in Moscow negotiating
the details of a new loan package. If, as Russian officials have suggested, the IMF lends $4.8 billion, it will be
enough to finance just more than three months of capital flight at its present level. IMF officials in Moscow
couldn't be reached for comment. But Martin Gilman, the fund's chief representative in Moscow, has said the
IMF would be willing to support only a program that could persuade average Russians to keep their money in
the country and in rubles.
One gnawing question is whether Russia's ruling elite is genuinely interested in getting the money back. Yuri
Skuratov, the country's embattled prosecutor general, offered his answer earlier this month, when he said he
had sent President Boris Yeltsin a list of Swiss bank accounts belonging to senior officials. The Kremlin
denied receiving the list, and Mr. Skuratov didn't name names, but as Mr. Fenkner put it: "There's a group
that is benefiting, and it's not the group that is getting paid the minimum wage."
Russia's solutions have been flawed at best. Last year, Prime Minister Yevgeny Primakov promised amnesty
and numbered bank accounts for flight capital that returned to the country, though as with most of his
economic initiatives, nothing concrete has happened. Instead, Russian officials have imposed a series of
restrictive measures such as the new rules at Sheremetevo airport, which forbid individuals to take more than
$5,000 out of the country. The central bank, for its part, has issued a slew of regulations on hard-currency
trade: Since Jan. 1, exporters must repatriate their foreign revenues within three months instead of six
months and sell at least half for rubles on the Moscow currency exchange; as of last month, importers who
buy hard currency must make large security deposits that they can retrieve only after proving that the
purchased goods have entered Russia; and recently, the central bank forbade foreign banks to buy hard
currency with rubles on deposit in Russia.
Currency traders say the restrictions have rendered the ruble inconvertible, financially isolating Russia from
the rest of the world. The Interfax news agency, citing an unnamed government source, reported recently that
IMF officials had objected as well. The central bank didn't respond to written questions.

--- Page 197 ---
Page 197 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The most effective way to keep money in the country, economists say, is to create an attractive investment
climate with a stable currency and predictable laws.
Meanwhile, the capital flight stays one step ahead of the central bank's attempts to regulate it. According to
Jamison Firestone, a lawyer who specializes in offshore structures, there are still plenty of legal ways to get
money out of Russia. Many companies, for example, conclude management contracts with offshore firms, a
scheme that allows them to buy hard currency and send it abroad as payment.
Less legal routes abound. Exporters, for example, can sell goods to an offshore affiliate at artificially low
prices, effectively sending the profit abroad, or transfer the goods to a Russian shell company that exports
them and then folds, avoiding the three-month repatriation rule.

Executive Critical of `Managed' EarningsDoesn't Mind if the Street Criticizes Him
Heard on the Street
Executive Critical of `Managed' Earnings Doesn't Mind if the Street Criticizes Him
By Robert McGough
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,355 words
16 April 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Here is an exercise bound to give some CEOs a panic attack: Imagine what would happen if you didn't
"manage," or smooth out, your quarterly earnings.
Pretty frightening, no? This is a market, after all, where stocks that miss earnings by a penny get pounded.
Now add this: Imagine you didn't give analysts any "guidance" on your undoctored earnings.
Who in their right mind would purposely blow off Wall Street that way?
Meet Peter Lewis, chairman of Progressive, a fast-growing auto insurer in Cleveland. Mr. Lewis, 65 years old,
refuses to guide analysts about Progressive's quarterly earnings. He also won't lift a finger to smooth out
earnings zigs and zags.
Though this could earn Mr. Lewis a gold star from securities regulators, it has exposed him to criticism from
Wall Street. And the strategy has triggered unusual short-term gyrations in Progressive's stock. But he's not
about to change.
"It is not honest" to manage earnings, Mr. Lewis says. Besides, when companies "manage" their earnings, he
adds, they mar their own business intelligence: "The accounting stuff that's required to smooth things out
causes management to mislead itself."
This is more than idle grousing. Corporate America's commonplace practice of managing earnings is coming
under increasing scrutiny by regulators. Arthur Levitt, chairman of the Securities and Exchange Commission,
publicly has carped about earnings manipulation.
The SEC has begun taking action. The commission in December sued W.R. Grace in a Miami federal court,
alleging that the company fraudulently managed earnings when a unit was growing faster than executives felt
could be sustained. Grace denies wrongdoing.
But it isn't just Grace. Many companies are concerned because they for years have felt that managing
earnings -- as long as the tweaking remained "immaterial," in accounting parlance -- was perfectly proper.
The practice, they argued, benefited shareholders by protecting the stock from wild bounces over mild
events.
Mr. Lewis's stance has taken shape over his 34-year career at Progressive. At its root is an attitude that Wall
Street analysts are paid to analyze. You know, go out and kick the tires: Interview customers, suppliers and
competitors, and dig through company filings, that sort of stuff. If analysts are spoon-fed by companies, Mr.
Lewis believes, they just aren't doing their job.
That approach has caused aftershocks. Progressive's stock can be wildly volatile when it surprises Wall
Street with its earnings. On Oct. 16, 1998, the stock surged nearly 20 points when Progressive beat analysts'
consensus third-quarter operating earnings by 44 cents a share. Then on Jan. 26, the stock plunged more
than $30 when Progressive's fourth-quarter operating earnings of $1.38 a share fell 16 cents short of
expectations.
The fun is set to begin anew. Early next week, Progressive is expected to release first-quarter earnings. And
analysts already are getting antsy.
"I'm getting more nervous as the day gets closer," says Nancy Benacci, an insurance analyst at McDonald
Investments. She's even having trouble sleeping. "It's like taking a final exam," she says. "Did I pass or did I
fail?"

--- Page 199 ---
Page 199 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Weston Hicks, an insurance analyst at J.P. Morgan, has an air of annoyed resignation: "I can't predict the
quarter," he sighs. "I might as well flip a coin."
Over the long haul, it's difficult to make the case that Progressive's stock has been hurt by its indifference to
Wall Street convention. As the company's earnings have leapt, so has the stock. Progressive's share price
has outperformed the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index in the past five, 10 and 15 years. For instance,
Progressive's 15-year return through Wednesday was 4,438%, compared with 735% for the S&P 500. That
performance is highly unusual for any company, let alone an insurer.
Indeed, its leaping and loping make Progressive's stock particularly useful as a contrarian play. Morgan
Stanley equity strategist Leah Modigliani says Progressive's independent behavior has made it a valuable
stock in a portfolio: Not only has it produced high returns, but it often zigs when the market zags, smoothing
out the ride for the whole portfolio.
Don't expect other companies to follow Progressive's path anytime soon. "The pressure from analysts is
mind-boggling," Mr. Lewis says. There's even pressure inside the company to smooth out the stock's
gyrations: "Some of our people care a lot about the stock price."
How does Progressive resist that pressure? Well, like some other large companies that decline to smooth
earnings or give analysts guidance (Berkshire Hathaway and Loews come to mind), Progressive is run by its
biggest shareholder. Mr. Lewis owns a 13% stake, valued at more than $1.3 billion. "My job is secure," he
says. "It doesn't depend on the day-to-day stock price."
But his attitude seems to be contagious. Ask Progressive's controller and (part-time) investor-relations
executive, Tom King, why the company doesn't manage earnings, and he rails that it is "a slippery slope.
Somebody expects you to earn a dollar a share," but instead you earn 95 cents, he says. "You borrow five
cents to make it up. Next quarter, you do it again. Before you know it, you're in trouble."
For Progressive, it wasn't always this way. Some 15 years ago, Mr. Lewis recalls, the company did try to
manage its earnings -- and it had the opposite problem. It kept earning more than it wanted to report; when it
finally took the accumulated reserve into earnings, analysts ignored it as a one-time gain.
Progressive then refused to talk to analysts at all. Mr. Lewis felt that analysts' phone calls "seem often to be
an attempt to get inside information." After slamming the door on Wall Street, "nothing bad happened to the
stock price," Mr. Lewis says.
But when Progressive needed to do a secondary stock offering a few years back for a big shareholder (not
Mr. Lewis), investment bankers said Progressive "won't get a good price on the stock unless you agree to talk
to analysts," Mr. Lewis says. So the company started talking, but it still doesn't give any guidance.
Why isn't he that open with analysts? "We provide everything," he bristles, including "an annual, detailed
study of our loss reserves, stuff other people don't show. We withhold nothing."
Nobody knows what next week's earnings will bring -- not even Mr. Lewis. "We don't know what happens until
we close our books," he says. "The wind blows, people run into each other, and we can't tell when they're
going to do it."
For what it's worth, McDonald's Ms. Benacci expects first-quarter earnings of $1.45 a share. The consensus
estimate is $1.49, First Call says. Ms. Benacci's "buy" rating has more to do with long-term expectations than
quarterly earnings.
No matter how many unexpected bumps surface, some institutional holders remain fans. Sequoia Fund, a
mutual fund that owns nearly as much Progressive stock as Mr. Lewis, said in its annual report that the
stock's January massacre hurt performance. But it is sitting tight: "We still have confidence in this unusual
company's longer-term prospects."

Fix the Yen
Fix the Yen
By Wayne Angell
1,125 words
13 April 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
How can Japan restore normal economic growth? Zero interest rates, an income tax rate reduction,
increased deficit spending and restructuring the banking system may not be enough. A decade of falling real
estate and wholesale prices has damaged the domestic store of value credibility of the yen. Holding yen
deposits with near-zero returns still seems preferable to investing in houses and office buildings that fall in
value against an appreciating yen. Thus Japan must use monetary policy to fix the yen, a floating currency
without an anchor.
Continuing to lower interest rates is not likely to be attempted. In Japan's deflationary morass, rates would
have to fall to perhaps negative 1% or 2% in order to lower the domestic savings rate to equal the investment
rate. The decline in interest rates on bank certificates of deposit to 0.08% from 8.1% in 1990 has not
provided sufficient incentive to redirect the flow of income away from saving into spending. Economic
uncertainty has been a spur to saving that easily overcomes the decline in interest rates. At any rate,
negative interest rates -- in effect, a tax on savings -- would not seem to be politically viable.
Monetary policy is much more efficient when its overriding goal is to stabilize the value of the currency
rather than to set an interest rate that stimulates growth, as the Federal Reserve has ably demonstrated. In
1979 Paul Volcker led the Federal Open Markets Committee to abandon interest-rate targeting in favor of a
slower growth rate of reserve bank credit sufficient to stabilize the price level. From 1979 forward, the FOMC
put first priority on ending the scourge of inflation.
Now, 20 years later, the Bank of Japan should abandon interest-rate targeting in favor of growing its balance
sheet at a rate sufficient to stabilize the value of the yen. The yen exchange rate should be fixed against the
dollar. Theoretically the yen could be stabilized while being free to float against the dollar. But practical
difficulties in choosing and implementing another target, such as the price of gold, favor fixing the yen against
the dollar. More important, fixing the yen-dollar exchange rate is the most efficient means of channeling
Japan's excess saving into world capital markets.
Rather than trying to discourage saving, stabilizing the yen exchange rate would mean that abstinence saving
above capital spending would be drawn into the global capital market. Japan could thereby turn its high
saving rate into a comparative advantage. And Japanese households would no longer be penalized by low
interest rates, but instead would be rewarded by the level of interest rates prevailing in world capital
markets.
Would fixing the exchange rate of the yen against the dollar pull yen interest rates up to dollar interest
rates? Only if the people of Japan believed that they were guaranteed that they could bring their interest and
principal returns home to Japan at a fixed exchange rate. It may be helpful to explore the impact of such a
change in interest rates in Japan. In Japan deposit money (M2+CDs) totals 608 trillion yen ($5 trillion at the
current exchange rate of just over 120 yen to the dollar). In the U.S., the comparable deposit money number
(M2) is $4.5 trillion, or $13,000 per capita. If deposit interest rates in Japan moved up to the world dollar
rate, deposits per capita of $40,000 would yield an extra $250 billion of consumer income, an average
increase of $2,000 per capita. This would amount to a five- to tenfold increase in income on investment and
retirement accounts.
A fixed exchange rate system for Japan would bring Japan's savings directly into the global dollar market.
Exchange rate risk would be eliminated not only for savers but also for investors and every business entity in
Japan. Most important, Japan's citizens would know that the value of their currency could no longer rise and
that the price of houses and capital investments could not continue to fall. Deflation would end. Price stability
would return. As households in Japan escape the environment of financial uncertainty, the savings rate would
likely fall. The ensuing increase in income would likely provide Japan with more consumer spending and
eventually an even larger total saving pool.

--- Page 202 ---
Page 202 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A good case can be made for fixing the exchange rate for a minimum of three or five years. An unspecified
period could give the impression that an exchange rate would be chosen to last 25, 50 or 100 years. That
might not be as credible as a pledge to fix rates for five years. A five-year fix would leave uncertain forward
rates past the specified period. Long-term interest rate differentials would respond to a market estimate of the
extent and direction of change. But this uncertainty would be much less than in the current floating rate
system.
What should the exchange rate be? One possibility is to choose the current rate: 120 yen to the dollar. But
this would endorse a price of gold of 33,600 yen per ounce, dangerously close to its recent low price that has
been consistent with continued deflation. A rate of 120 yen to the dollar would not provide the extra liquidity
that might be needed in the adjustment process. Interest rates would probably rise quickly.
Another possibility would be a rate that would bring the price of gold up to 40,000 yen per ounce with the
hope of providing more yen liquidity and a soft cushion for the real estate deflation. That would imply an
exchange rate of about 140 yen to the dollar. A lower yen that ends, once and for all, the commodity deflation
would also help offset the continued rise in unemployment as businesses banks get on with their restructuring
tasks.
The current floating exchange rate system inevitably produces monetary policy confusion. Interest rate
targeting has no price stability anchor, and the current nonanchored floating rate system practically
guarantees a very volatile exchange rate. Indeed, the yen has swung between 108 and 147 per dollar within
the past six months.
Japan needs a certain and transparent commitment to a fixed exchange rate system. Let interest rates float
as determined by Japan's saving preference and by the world dollar rate of return on capital. It is time to end
the long decline in house prices, the long decline in interest rates and the long period of uncertainty for
households and businesses in Japan. The cost of an unanchored float for the yen has been too high. Fix the
yen against the dollar.
---
Mr. Angell, formerly a Federal Reserve governor, is chief economist at Bear Stearns.

South African Exchange's Top OfficialAims to Halt Listing Exodus to London
South African Exchange's Top Official Aims to Halt Listing Exodus to London
By Andi Spicer
Dow Jones Newswires
706 words
12 April 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
No Page Citation
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
JOHANNESBURG, South Africa -- The recent exodus of some of South Africa's largest companies to the
London Stock Exchange has been interpreted by foreign investors as a body blow.
"It's been seen as a vote of no-confidence in the country," said Russell Loubser, executive president of the
Johannesburg Stock Exchange.
After years of economic stagnation under apartheid, the euphoria that followed South Africa's first all-race
elections in 1994 has started to abate. Low economic growth, a series of precipitous falls in the rand's value,
high interest rates and unemployment have hurt confidence.
Mr. Loubser said a number of senior European business leaders approached him at the World Economic
Forum meeting in Davos, Switzerland, in early February to ask about the flight of his exchange's most
prominent companies.
On the corporate scene during the last two years, Mr. Loubser has watched mining concern Billiton PLC and
brewing concern SAB PLC move their primary listings to London from Johannesburg.
Now South Africa's largest company and the jewel in the crown of the JSE, mining and investment behemoth
Anglo American Corp., has joined the migration and will list in London in May. The country's largest insurance
and financial group, South African Mutual Life Assurance Society, better known as Old Mutual, will list in
London in June.
Other major JSE-listed companies are known to be considering a similar move, including Gold Fields Ltd.,
the second-largest gold producer in the country; industrial group Barlow Ltd.; and the two
information-technology groups, Dimension Data Holdings Ltd. and Datatec Ltd. Many others could follow.
"I've no idea what pent-up pressure there is," said Mr. Loubser.
Amid the setbacks, the JSE is fighting back. The bourse is edging closer to sealing a proposed merger with
the South African Bond Exchange and the South African Futures Exchange, which trades in financial, equity
index and commodity futures and options.
To create this super exchange, what is needed is a small change in South African securities law and the
agreement of all the exchanges' members. Both obstacles should be overcome by the end of the year, Mr.
Loubser said.
The merger will slash costs and make the new exchange more competitive in world terms, he said.
The JSE also is relocating to affluent suburb of Sandton from the run-down central business district in
downtown Johannesburg. The new building will be ready by September next year and will have room for all
three exchanges under one roof.
Mr. Loubser has become more strident in his criticism of companies' motives. He dismisses claims that it is
cheaper to raise capital in London than Johannesburg and argues that the necessity of being included in
benchmark equity indexes such as the FTSE for tracker funds to buy companies' stock is simply outmoded
reasoning.
"I'm irritated with the general lack of understanding of all the issues involved, and it can't do us any favors if
the trend continues," he said.

--- Page 204 ---
Page 204 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"The reason to move listing to get into an index is just old thinking. That's going to disappear soon, as we're
now getting sectorial indexes that span the globe with no physical boundaries. The index will come to you,"
he said.
What he does agree with is that the JSE has failed in its settlement facilities. "It works, but slowly, and that
isn't good enough," he said.
In early July, the exchange's new settlement system will come into action and many of these problems should
be corrected, he said. This will provide electronic logging of scrip in a central securities depository, effectively
removing paper from the transaction.
But the flight of companies has already dented confidence both in South Africa and the JSE. Lower liquidity
on the JSE will be an obvious result of the departures.
Mr. Loubser said it isn't an axiom that when a primary listing is shifted elsewhere that this is where the most
activity will take place.
Despite the flight of corporate capital, he said South Africa has a credible government that has provided
stability but not economic growth.

Roundtable---Setting the Tone:A CEO and a professor offer their insights on how to keep the troopsmotivated
Executive Pay (A Special Report)
Roundtable --- Setting the Tone: A CEO and a professor offer their insights on how to keep the troops
motivated
By Joann S. Lublin
3,880 words
8 April 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
R10
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
In T.J. Rodgers's neighborhood, money talks.
The president and chief executive of Cypress Semiconductor Corp. routinely suspends several vice
presidents' paychecks until they complete subordinates' performance reviews. He halted his own paycheck
during 1996 for the same reason. He once hired a Brink's truck to deliver newly minted gold coins as part of a
profit-sharing plan for everyone at the San Jose, Calif., international distributor of semiconductors.
Mr. Rodgers, an outspoken business leader, learned early how to reward others. When he was 11, his
mother paid him $3 upfront to cut the lawn. He bought a watermelon for 50 cents and persuaded
neighborhood children to do his work in exchange for a snack.
The 51-year-old entrepreneur founded Cypress in 1982 and rapidly developed a gruff management style that
earned him a spot in Fortune magazine's list of toughest bosses. He sometimes sends workers memos
stamped with the words, "It's Lonely at the Top. But You Can Eat Better."
The Wall Street Journal asked Mr. Rodgers and labor economist Edward P. Lazear to explore the link
between CEO compensation and retention of key staff. Mr. Lazear, past president of the Society of Labor
Economists, is a professor of human-resources management and economics at Stanford University's
Graduate School of Business in Stanford, Calif. He has written extensively about the effects of rank-and-file
pay on productivity, and about executive pay's impact on corporate performance.
Here are excerpts from their provocative encounter:
THE WALL STREET JOURNAL: Do you think your relatively modest salary (of $344,453 in 1998) hinders or
helps in motivating Cypress employees?
MR. RODGERS: If you look at CEOs in Silicon Valley companies our size, I would bet I rank either last or
second to last. Employees don't look up and see the big guy, particularly in bad years, making a huge amount
of money. That is a source of ill will.
It also helps me when I have a vice president who earns $250,000 a year who thinks he is underpaid. He just
looks at my salary and we have a discussion about value and he no longer feels underpaid. Of course, that
VP would also have a lot of stock as well. Equity is where the big money comes from, and pay is what keeps
you rolling.
WSJ: How big is your Cypress stake?
MR. RODGERS: I own and have options to own three million shares. Our stock is down right now. We have
had a bad semiconductor market. I just bought $3 million of our equity. That stake is just about 3%. I owned
5% on the first-round financing deal. That was diluted down to about 2% by the time of the IPO; it drifted up
over time.
WSJ: Have you always tried to keep your salary at a certain multiple of your lowest employee's pay?
MR. RODGERS: I don't believe in employee top/bottom ratios. That is something a disciple of Karl Marx
invented.
My salary today is 17 times the lowest employee's.

--- Page 206 ---
Page 206 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
By the way, I am not trying to be modest at all. I have made tens of millions of dollars from Cypress. People
understand that I live in a very nice house, which I built. I have a vineyard and a winery surrounding my
house. I have made money from Cypress, and I am not embarrassed about that a bit.
But I do want low pay because it is good for the company. It puts a lid on the salary structure. It sets a tone
for the company. The big guy is not someone who milks the company. The big guy is somebody who is paid a
reasonable salary.
PROF. LAZEAR: When you say low pay, that's not necessarily low compensation. The total compensation
could be quite high.
MR. RODGERS: Exactly. I earned my shares. Very shortly after the IPO, I made $10 million (at least on
paper, as the value of his stock rose). My equity share (as of late February had a market value) somewhere
between $20 million and $30 million, which is not grand on the IPO scale -- although I am not going to sit here
and whine about how I am underpaid with $20 million.
PROF. LAZEAR: What about your second-in-command? How much does he get in stock relative to you?
MR. RODGERS: Typically, the guys just underneath me, the three or four senior vice presidents, bump right
up against me (in base salary and bonus). There are several people within $10,000 to $20,000. The lid is
really, truly a lid.
PROF. LAZEAR: But what about total compensation?
MR. RODGERS: Variable compensation is as much as 80% of your base salary. During the last couple of
years, that has been few and far between. Our bonus plans shut down hard when the company is not doing
well. (Cypress had a net loss of $111.2 million last year, after a $92.4 million restructuring charge.)
Equity is typically two or three times more options per year for me than for the next ones down. To pull
numbers out of the air would be like 250,000 vs. 100,000 options.
PROF. LAZEAR: You are a young guy. You are not worried about succession. But one of the issues that
always comes up about salary structures is how much incentive does the group of vice presidents have to
want to become the president.
MR. RODGERS: The way we set both base salary and stock is we have comprehensive salary surveys. I
look at the other CEOs and I peg myself right near the bottom of the semiconductor industry and companies
comparable to ours in size.
That sets a peg with the top VPs compressed against it. Even the next level down is paid a significant fraction
of it. I do have good retention of vice presidents. They get CEO offers all the time. I have dealt in the last two
years with one, two, three, four, five, six CEO offers against our VPs.
WSJ: Of the 10, six have gotten offers?
MR. RODGERS: There have been two for one VP. I have only lost one. Some offers were for significantly
more money.
WSJ: Why did you only lose one?
MR. RODGERS: A bunch of reasons. Based on those offers, I say, "I want to keep you here." Then I go and
adjust the vice president's stock options to match the outside world.
I never match the higher salary. I say this out loud all the time. "We are entrepreneurs here. If that is what you
are about, then you belong here. If you are looking for big salary, real time right now, regardless of how the
company is performing, you need to go work somewhere else."
WSJ: Do you work for money?
MR. RODGERS: I haven't written a check to pay for anything or deposited my own check or observed my
bank-account balance for a decade. My girlfriend keeps my money. I eat what I want and drive where I want
and buy what I want. I just never ever think about money. The counting aspect of money is an annoyance that
I have always hated.
PROF. LAZEAR: We've got our first minor disagreement here. (The high-level) people you are talking about
probably are not concerned overly with money. But when you go further down in that organization, money
matters a lot down at the bottom. People move in a minute for money.

--- Page 207 ---
Page 207 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
MR. RODGERS: I will agree with you for direct labor and for technicians. But you get all the way down to,
say, a $60,000 engineer, and I believe my generalization holds.
WSJ: They don't work for money?
MR. RODGERS: Where they work is not determined by money as long as they feel they are fairly
compensated. When an engineer quits, sometimes it is for money. The story is always the same. "I have this
problem. I want to buy a house." Real estate is expensive. But basically if they are not being hassled by
money, money is not the primary motivating factor for them coming or going.
WSJ: Isn't it false modesty to talk about your relatively low salary when you own so much stock? How does
that incentivize Cypress people?
MR. RODGERS: They all own stock, too -- an amount of stock which is nontrivial relative to their base salary.
Our Filipino direct-labor operators who earn 85 cents an hour also have some stock. I don't know any
company even one-fifth our size that still maintains equity ownership for all employees. My CFO keeps telling
me, "God, you have to start rethinking this." Maybe someday we will.
WSJ: Are you giving actual shares?
MR. RODGERS: Options.
WSJ: Is it a good idea for Cypress to give every employee stock options?
PROF. LAZEAR: The big problem of giving very low-wage workers stock options is that stock options tend to
be risky. An alternative is to give them nonvested bonds rather than nonvested stock. A bond essentially
guarantees them a particular return. You say, "What we are going to give you is a U.S. T-bill that will vest in
five years. You don't get it for five years, but if you are here in five years, you get it. You get the interest on it."
That takes the risk away, but it also creates a retention incentive.
MR. RODGERS: All of our employees in America understand stock and like it.
I have had three secretaries who have gotten into a house with their capital gains in Cypress shares.
There is an intensity in start-ups trying to go public that you just don't get anywhere else. I am trying to
maintain as much of that intensity and loyalty and company ownership as I can.
WSJ: How do you maintain motivation with a depressed stock price?
MR. RODGERS: There is nothing better than a low stock price when you get an option. If employees believe
the company is going to move forward, they can see a big gain.
WSJ: Do you think a CEO ought to participate in repricing of stock options? (Repricing is lowering the price at
which current options can be exercised -- thus restoring the discount when a stock's share price has plunged.
Cypress has included Mr. Rodgers in three of its seven repricings since 1987.)
MR. RODGERS: I don't think there should be a rule. There are different situations. You have a large company
and the CEO screwed up. The shares go down. He is the chairman and reprices his own shares. And makes
money that the investors don't. If I was an investor, I would bitch.
Or, you have a company newly public. You have a highly technical CEO (whose departure) would be
catastrophic. The board is made up of venture capitalists and large investors. They say, "We would like to
give this person incentive to stick around. It is our money." Who is going to criticize them for spending their
money?
PROF. LAZEAR: There is a way to get around these problems. If you don't reprice and if you are too far out
of the money, those options have no incentive value anymore.
If you know that you are going to be repriced anytime you make a mistake, that doesn't give you the right
incentives not to make a mistake.
But you can essentially charge your executives every time you reprice. "It is going to cost you something to
do that. We are going to take it out of our salary this year. We are going to take some money from you. We
repriced it. We have essentially given you options again." You have the incentives, but you haven't been
rewarded for a mistake. You get the benefit on the upside -- if things go well for the company.
WSJ: Specifically, how would that work?
PROF. LAZEAR: Reduce the salary component or essentially have an escrow account reduced.

--- Page 208 ---
Page 208 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
MR. RODGERS: Suppose you have repriced 100,000 options downward by $2. Then you charge the person
$200,000?
PROF. LAZEAR: $200,000. It is essentially like a margin call.
MR. RODGERS: But you are charging the whole amount of the repricing?
PROF. LAZEAR: Because you have lost the whole amount.
MR. RODGERS: What is the benefit?
PROF. LAZEAR: You are now back in a range where you can be back in the money.
MR. RODGERS: But I just gave you $200,000 for the option to make $200,000 if the stock goes up.
PROF. LAZEAR: We want to punish you. We don't want to let you off the hook because the stock price did
fall. If you reprice, you are letting your executives off the hook.
If you don't reprice, you have rendered those options meaningless. To have it both ways, reprice the options,
but also create a penalty for the executives.
MR. RODGERS: I would refuse to take this new package as being a disadvantage relative to my old
out-of-the money options.
I have 100,000 options at $10. I screw up and the shares go down to $8.
I give you $200,000 and you drop my options to $8. I would rather give you nothing and leave my options at
$10.
PROF. LAZEAR: My plan addresses that problem. I should have made clear initially that you would get more
options upfront -- but not enough to make you whole if things go badly and there is repricing.
Let's say we usually give you 100,000 options a year, but when we reprice, we don't charge you. Now, I am
going to give you 150,000 options a year, but when the price of the stock falls (again), we are going to charge
you for that. That is the penalty that you pay for getting an extra 50,000 options a year. If things go well, now
you are leveraged even further.
WSJ: Would you take more than your usual options, putting some money aside as a penalty if Cypress
reprices?
MR. RODGERS: That is something we would consider. Options have more reality when somebody has a little
bit of skin in the game. Because options work one way, you make money. If not, you don't lose anything. We
have talked about -- we haven't implemented -- requiring people at the VP level to own a certain amount of
Cypress shares.
WSJ: Why don't you do that now?
MR. RODGERS: We just haven't. It is another Big Brother kind of thing where you are forcing people to do
things with their money. I do need to hire people. Therefore, I do need to be competitive.
WSJ: You need to have a competitive bonus plan, too. In recent years, your bonus plans haven't paid off very
much. Where is the motivational value?
MR. RODGERS: The key-employee bonus plan covers the top 100 to 125 people. At the VP level, that pays
as much as 80% of your base pay. That 80% of your base pay is divided into two parts: 40 percentage points
for financial performance, divided equally between revenue and profit {goals}. That is vested over a two-year
period. After a while, that piles up some money that the other company has to fork up to get somebody to go
across. That is the holding power. It is that one that hasn't paid well.
And 40 percentage points for attainment of specific objectives, divided per quarter. The specific objectives
include major new products, getting a plant up and running. The 10 percentage points per quarter are paid
quarterly.
During current times, we are probably getting 25 out of 80 percentage points. Everybody knows it can be a lot
better.
We also have always had some sort of all-employee bonus program paying hundreds to maybe a little over
$1,000 per person per quarter. The current bonus is tied to new-product revenue. An all-for-one, one-for-all

--- Page 209 ---
Page 209 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
bonus is like the old Marine Corps drill where nobody gets fed until the last guy comes in from the run. You
find it creates a lot of peer pressure.
WSJ: How do your bonuses help you keep people when Cypress has had such mixed results lately?
MR. RODGERS: The bonuses don't. That is another argument why we felt that repricing stock options was
essential.
WSJ: Ed, what do you think of Cypress giving every employee a bonus for new-product development?
PROF. LAZEAR: That is a clever idea. If you pay people on the basis of their current output, they tend to
ignore either R&D kinds of activity or even marketing development.
You can say, "We are going to compensate people based on the new products that they develop." But if there
is too big of a kick there, then you get people focusing entirely on new products and not paying enough
attention to the existing products. If there is not a big enough kick, then the reverse happens.
How do you get those weights right?
MR. RODGERS: We have always been weighted, based on our start-up culture, to make the quarter. That
means turn the crank on existing products. We wanted to put more emphasis on new products.
We used to have profit sharing. We moved to new-product royalties because profits are too removed. You
cannot get the average person to connect to the {earnings per share}. You can get them to connect to
products they are working on -- especially if you pay quarterly. It has been dramatically successful.
WSJ: When a company's fortunes sour, should the CEO get fewer options?
PROF. LAZEAR: If you are talking about a situation where a CEO is getting base salary well below the
market and you are giving the guy options, most of the reward is in some form of a variable pay. During these
down times, the CEO isn't making a whole lot of money. You are penalizing the CEO significantly.
WSJ: Some shareholders get apoplectic when the board gives the CEO more options amid bad times.
MR. RODGERS: That whole scenario sounds real sick. I get options for long-term compensation. They don't
matter year by year.
If you don't like my performance and my performance is bad, then fire me. If investors want to go "apoplectic,"
let them invest in something else.
PROF. LAZEAR: It is not like the CEO is getting off the hook because he has been given this new set of
options.
If you are going to keep the CEO, you are going to give him the same set of options that you gave him in the
past.
WSJ: T.J., tell us about the Cypress penalty program under which several VPs saw their paychecks cut off.
MR. RODGERS: There is only one group of people in the program, and that is the 10 vice presidents. It still
catches four to six of them per quarter. Only one offense can cause you to have your paycheck cut off, and
that is not completing performance reviews on employees in your group. Or allowing someone in your group
down lower not to complete a performance review.
They get a warning. Two weeks later, they hit the wall. Which is fair, because they are causing the employee
to miss raises.
WSJ: Does the missed paycheck arrive once the VPs turn in their reviews?
MR. RODGERS: Exactly.
PROF. LAZEAR: Is that much of a penalty for your high-wage guys? I would think these guys are not living
paycheck to paycheck.
MR. RODGERS: That is really part of the point. The grand penalty is typically delaying one paycheck for two
or three days. But it shows we are dead serious about getting reviews done.
The way it used to work here was the VP of HR (human resources) would come in. She says, "Here is the list
of reviews (that haven't been done)." I would go to my staff meeting and say, "G2 d2 it, get the stuff done."

--- Page 210 ---
Page 210 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Two weeks later, there is still a large list of reviews that aren't done and two of the guys didn't do any. You
scream at them. I was doing that for three years and I realized it just didn't work.
I was in my staff meeting. I said, "What the hell do I have to do? Do I have to cut off your pay?" I just stopped
and said, "That is what I am going to do."
Two weeks after that, two got cut off. One was a founder. He said, "Gee, you are carrying this thing a little bit
too far." He was trying to say, "You are going to let me off the hook, aren't you?"
I truly wouldn't ever let him off the hook and said: "I will watch your children leave school, your house get
auctioned off and you lose all your property before I pay you another nickel, until you get your reviews done."
The only person who has ever had a substantial time with their paycheck cut off was me.
WSJ: How long?
MR. RODGERS: A year.
WSJ: Whose reviews did you not do?
MR. RODGERS: The VPs.
WSJ: When?
MR. RODGERS: In '96.
WSJ: You got no paychecks in 1996?
MR. RODGERS: Got real busy. Shut down two plants. The reviews sat on the corner of my desk.
You get done with work at 8:30 at night. You go look at the stack of eight, nine, 10 folders on your desk. It is
still bulls--- paperwork relative to what you do. But it matters to other people.
The VP of HR withholds my money from January through December. She comes in on Dec. 31 and dumps a
check on me for the whole year's pay. I lost an entire year's interest, but got nailed for income tax on it
anyway.
WSJ: Ed, what do you think of this pay-penalty idea as a motivational technique?
PROF. LAZEAR: It is primarily symbolic. The costs aren't significant either way. If you did it to your production
workers, you would really be affecting people's lives.
WSJ: Does it send a powerful message to the rank-and-file employees?
MR. RODGERS: It says your reviews and your bonuses matter. Your boss is going to pay if he doesn't stay
on top of it.
Now, I do my reviews on time.
WSJ: Your bonus has fluctuated in recent years. (In 1998, Mr. Rodgers got a $59,238 bonus, up from $1,151
the year before.) Why haven't you taken a salary cut, too?
MR. RODGERS: I earn what I make.
WSJ: What happens if the board decides to give you a zero raise?
MR. RODGERS: That will never happen. Never.
WSJ: Because you are always going to be worth more?
MR. RODGERS: Right. Because I am.
