Home-Grown Mutual-Fund FirmTries to Shake Dismal Numbers
TEXAS JOURNAL
Home-Grown Mutual-Fund Firm Tries to Shake Dismal Numbers
By Jonathan Weil
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
2,360 words
7 April 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
Texas Journal
T1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
SAN ANTONIO -- In a 500-seat hotel ballroom, where the predominantly elderly audience comes to hear an
educational seminar on stock picking, fund manager Art Bonnel surprises his listeners by ending his
presentation with a sales pitch.
He needs, he tells the sparse crowd, to raise $100 million this year for the Bonnel Growth Fund, which he
manages for San Antonio-based U.S. Global Investors Inc. That's an ambitious doubling of the size of the
fund, and he asks the crowd to consider chipping in. "Don't be surprised to see the Standard & Poor's index
trading at 50 times earnings," he says. "This is a buying opportunity."
Never mind that the S&P 500 is trading at 34 times earnings and that his target would be an unprecedented
valuation for the index. A lot is riding on his fund's reaching its target: U.S. Global Investors, where Mr.
Bonnel has worked under contract as a fund manager since 1994, desperately needs the $400,000 a year in
management fees such an infusion would generate.
U.S. Global, the only publicly traded mutual-fund company based in Texas, isn't living up to the promise of its
ticker symbol, which is "GROW." Its stock funds consistently have posted some of the industry's worst
returns. Its fund expenses as a percentage of assets are ballooning, and revenue is plunging: During the six
months ended Dec. 31, revenue dropped 25% to $4.4 million from year-earlier levels. And after operating
profitably for two years, U.S. Global is suffering losses again. Last year, its stock mutual-fund assets under
management dropped 26% to $349 million. (The company has another $1 billion in money-market accounts,
which account for a small part of revenue.)
Unless U.S. Global's situation turns around quickly, analysts say, the very future of the company is in doubt.
"I don't know that this is triage time, but this present condition cannot last for long," says Michael Lipper,
chairman of New York-based Lipper Inc., which tracks mutual-fund data. "Either they get a market turn in their
favor, they attract more capital, or they sell out. Or else at some point the negative cash flow will impact their
viability."
But U.S. Global officials dismiss warnings that the company is in jeopardy. They blame their past troubles on
bad luck and poor oversight by fund managers -- and say the company is now poised to recover.
The formula, they say: a new focus on fundamentals. The company is devoting 60% of its $120,000
marketing budget to pushing its two most successful funds, including the one managed by Mr. Bonnel -- a
campaign designed to bring a new infusion of management fees. And since October, when Chief Executive
Frank E. Holmes took on additional duties as chief investment officer, the company has required fund
managers to meet strict performance standards for each stock in their portfolios.
"The performance sucks," Mr. Holmes says. "And I can't stand it. I hate losing. This isn't a short quick-fix. It's
a long-term solution."
So far, Mr. Holmes's tenure at the company has been rocky. In 1989, as a 34-year-old executive at a Toronto
brokerage firm, Mr. Holmes paid $2.2 million for a controlling interest in U.S. Global's predecessor, United
Services Advisors Inc.
He found a firm in chaos. During the mid-1980s, investigators with the Securities and Exchange Commission
discovered that a portfolio manager had taken bribes from two Canadian stock promoters to pack his United
Services Prospector Fund, now called the U.S. Global Natural Resources Fund, with worthless mining
shares. The former manager and the promoters ultimately went to prison, and the company's founder was

--- Page 212 ---
Page 212 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
barred from the securities industry after the SEC determined he knew of the problem long before it was
disclosed to the commission. (United Services ultimately reimbursed the fund for its losses, with interest.)
The firm was also in an unpromising line of business. The company's core holdings were a handful of gold
funds, which accounted for about 90% of the company's revenue, but gold's luster as an investment had
dimmed with inflation in check. So if the firm was going to grow, Mr. Holmes believed, it needed a new niche.
His diversification efforts initially proved difficult. Efforts to market new funds to U.S. Global's existing base of
customers -- die-hard gold bugs -- fell flat. And Mr. Holmes says it was tough to persuade five-star money
managers to move to San Antonio. (Before coming aboard, Mr. Bonnel insisted on working out of his
hometown, Reno, Nev.) Still, by 1994, Mr. Holmes had launched two new funds, including a second
traditional equity fund -- the Bonnel fund, investing primarily in medium-cap U.S. stocks -- to supplement the
existing All American Equity fund.
But because of the difficulty in attracting money managers to San Antonio, he decided to focus on overseas
markets, which the gold funds had been involved in for years. Plus, foreign-market funds allow the company
to charge higher management fees than traditional funds because there is less competition and managing the
funds is more work-intensive. U.S. Global's expertise abroad became the focus of marketing campaigns.
Mr. Holmes viewed China as the best growth opportunity. It also was one of 1993's hottest markets, making it
easier to market to investors lured by other China funds' strong earlier returns. "This was a big opportunity,"
Mr. Holmes says.
But the China fund was the first in a string of disappointments for the company.
-- Since the launch in February 1994 of the company's China Region Opportunity Fund, the country has
turned cold. Shares of the fund are down more than 60% since its inception, and its assets at the end of 1998
were $15.6 million, slightly more than half their year-end peak in 1996, thanks to losses and redemptions.
-- The firm's other recent emerging-markets forays have proved equally disastrous. Shares of the Regent
Eastern European Fund -- overseen by a unit of Hong Kong-based Regent Pacific Group Ltd. -- lost 28.2% of
their value during the year ended March 31, and are down 17.4% since the fund's March 1997 inception. Last
year, the fund ranked 128 out of 176 funds in Lipper's emerging-markets group.
-- The company's Global Blue Chip Fund, then on its second portfolio manager, had a 21.8% loss for the year
ended March 31, compared with an average 3.4% return by its peers. In fact, for the year ended March 31,
only three of that fund's peers fared worse, out of 229 total. Moreover, the fund's expenses totaled 7.5% of
assets during its latest fiscal year, compared with the 1.8% median for its peers.
-- While the company's overseas bets came up snake eyes, its other funds didn't provide much of a cushion.
All American Equity beat the S&P 500 during the year ended March 31 with a 17% return, but its $48 million
in assets aren't enough to contribute large management fees. U.S. Global's two gold funds, which also invest
heavily overseas, rank among the worst against their peers and are losing assets rapidly, as are its
natural-resources and real-estate funds. U.S. Global's Gold Shares Fund is down 84.2% since its inception in
the 1970s, according to Lipper, while its 33% one-year loss through March 31 was the third-worst out of 44
gold funds.
For some experts, this track record is cause for concern. "It raises the question of whether they have the
ability to do all these things," says Don Phillips, president of Morningstar Inc., the Chicago firm known for
rating mutual funds.
And Mr. Phillips calls the company's foray into foreign stocks a particularly bad call. "You have your choice of
what kind of funds you launch," he says. "If {Mr. Holmes} did a tech-oriented fund five years ago, he'd
probably be sitting there with dazzling returns."
Mr. Holmes says his company has been working for the past six months to get a grip on the funds' losses.
"We can now easily identify what went wrong and how to turn it around," he says.
As for the lack of a technology fund, he says his firm has considered it, but decided that because the Bonnel
Fund already invests considerably in technology stocks that it wasn't necessary. He also says he believes
that China's equities market appears to have reached a bottom and could come back quickly and vigorously.
(U.S. Global also recommends that investors not keep more than 3% of their portfolio in China and not more
than 5% in gold.)
Mr. Holmes blames the funds' poor performance on a mixture of factors.
The China region fund, he explains, was overweighted toward mainland Chinese stocks. Fund officials saw
more value in those stocks than those based in Hong Kong or Singapore; however, when the Asian crisis hit,

--- Page 213 ---
Page 213 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
mainland stocks took a harder pummeling than offshore ones. Mr. Holmes admits the company didn't move
quickly enough to pull the fund's money out, but says the firm has been working to correct the imbalance
since October.
In the case of the two-year-old Eastern European fund, Mr. Holmes says the poor performance tracks the
region, and contends the ranking -- 128 of 176 -- is misleading because the emerging-markets group tracked
by Lipper includes funds that invest in other regions. (Lipper confirms this.)
Among the gold funds, Mr. Holmes says U.S. Global's Gold Shares Fund was heavily invested in South
African stocks, where steep drops in that country's currency have triggered even steeper falls in stock prices
than in other gold-producing countries. And U.S. Global's World Gold Fund was heavily invested in small-cap
and midsize stocks, which were punished even worse than the industry's troubled large-cap issues, Mr.
Holmes says.
The Natural Resources fund, Mr. Holmes says, was barred by its prospectus from investing more than 25% of
its assets in integrated oil companies. But, of late, those diversified companies have been some of the best
performers in the sector. Mr. Holmes says the company is now in the process of getting shareholder approval
to increase that percentage maximum to 50%.
As for the Global Blue Chip Fund, Mr. Holmes says the fund is now repositioning itself to invest in large-cap
stocks both in the U.S. and internationally. He attributes the high expenses to legal costs associated with a
change of management: Last fall, U.S. Global brought the fund, which had previously been run by a Baltimore
newsletter writer, in house after poor performance. The company soon will be implementing a cap on
expenses of 2.5%.
In addition to bad luck, Mr. Holmes says, U.S. Global's portfolio managers suffered from a lack of discipline.
As chief investment officer, he says he's implementing a more structured system of identifying promising
stocks, under which fund managers have to more specifically identify their investment criteria, demonstrate
that their approach has been historically successful, and then stick to it, monitoring their holdings' progress
constantly.
Before, he says, the portfolio management at times bordered on the haphazard, while the level of monitoring
by prior chief investment officers was scant. And he says that any fund managers whose performances are in
the bottom half of their peer groups will be dismissed.
Mr. Holmes says he expects to take a 12% salary reduction, from about $316,000, in fiscal 1999. Moreover,
Mr. Holmes says if the company's poor performance persists through the rest of the fiscal year, he will not get
any bonus.
"I have a plan," Mr. Holmes says. "If I didn't have a plan, I'd be very worried. I understand money
management and what it takes to drive performance."

World Watch:
International
World Watch:
Compiled by Lily H. Li
1,482 words
2 April 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A8
English

EUROPE/MIDEAST
Mannesmann Unit
Boosts Position
In German Market
Mannesmann Arcor, the telecommunications unit of German engineering and technology conglomerate
Mannesmann, agreed to purchase the fixed-line telephone business of one of its biggest rivals, O.tel.o
Communications, for 1.15 billion euros ($1.23 billion). The takeover combines the two largest competitors to
dominant German carrier Deutsche Telekom and is the first major move to consolidate the German telecoms
market, which has seen the entry of more than 50 national long-distance firms since deregulation in 1998.
With the purchase, Mannesmann Arcor strengthens its position and become the clear leader on the German
market after Deutsche Telekom. Mannesmann Arcor will acquire O.tel.o's customers, its wireline business
with about 2,800 employees, and the trade name. The sale must still be approved by the companies'
supervisory boards and antitrust authorities.
O.tel.o is a joint venture previously owned by German utilities Veba and RWE. The telecoms firm incurred a
loss of more than one billion euros last year. It has been slow in the market in part because of persistent
disagreements between its shareholders over strategy. Veba and RWE had also negotiated with MobilCom,
whose shares gained this week in anticipation of a deal. Veba said in a statement it realized a profit of about
350 million euros from the deal, effective yesterday.
Russia Reserves Down to 3-Year Low
Russia's gold and foreign-exchange reserves have dropped to a three-year low, the Central Bank
announced. By the end of last week, reserves were just $10.9 billion, down from $11.2 billion the previous
week. The drop was caused by the government's selling dollars to support the ruble and using reserves to
pay off some of its international loans, analysts said. The bank spent $2.1 billion of its foreign-exchange
reserves in the first quarter of the year to help the government pay its debts, the bank's chairman said. Almost
all of the country's 18 most important banks, which control 80% of Russia's banking operations and 45% of its
private deposits, need government help, he said.
Portugal Carrier Unveils Detail of Sale
Portuguese flag carrier TAP-Air Portugal said Swiss company SAirGroup will pay 15 billion escudos ($80.5
million) for its 20% stake in the state-owned airline. The parent of Swissair has the option to take a further
10% stake over the next four years. Portugal also confirmed that it would privatize up to 49% of the airline. In
addition to the 30% reserved for Swissair, 10% is set to be sold to employees of the Portuguese carrier.
BT Cuts Call Rates on Mobile Phones
British Telecommunications said it would cut the cost of calls on its service to mobile phones by an average
of more than 25% starting April 30. The fall in prices follows last December's agreement by BT, Vodafone and
Cellnet, the latter owned by BT and Securicor Group, to lower prices in the wake of an antitrust inquiry into
prices from fixed lines to mobile phones. Calls on the BT network to Cellnet, Vodafone, One-2-One, the latter
owned by Cable & Wireless and MediaOne Group, and Orange mobile phones are being reduced.
Total of France Issues New Shares

--- Page 216 ---
Page 216 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
French oil group Total said it has issued 43.3 million new shares to finance the acquisition of a 41% stake in
Belgium's Petrofina. The move follows European Union Commission approval for Total's takeover of the
Belgian company, conditional on the sale of some gasoline storage facilities in northern France. The new
shares raise the number of Total shares outstanding to 288 million.
BRIEFLY:
-- Britain's Inchcape yesterday continued its divestment program with the sale of its Middle East marketing
interests to Cupola Investments, a Dubai-based development capital and operating group, for $116 million.
-- Belgian software-servicing and products company Real Software said it acquired a 53.5% majority stake in
Oasis Systems Consulting for 100 million Belgian francs ($2.67 million). Oasis, operational since 1994, is
based in Singapore.
---
ASIA/PACIFIC
U.S. Firms Sign China Deals
Four U.S. companies signed telecommunications deals with China valued at $141 million in a ceremony
linked to a visit by U.S. Commerce Secretary William Daley, who has urged China to step up the industry's
pace of liberalization. The signings didn't include an anticipated agreement to open up the sector by
permitting overseas investment into CDMA, or code division multiple access, networks, a U.S. standard for
cellular telephones. A unit of Motorola has agreed to sell $80 million in equipment to expand Hunan
province's global system for mobile communications, or GSM, network, a competing European cellular-phone
standard. China's postal bureau bought $41 million in equipment to help automate its operations.
Hewlett-Packard recorded sales of $27 million in the deal, International Business Machines $10 million, and
Compaq Computer $7 million, according to executives.
Japan Institution Modeled After RTC
Japan launched a government-backed institution to recover banks' bad loans, in what could be an important
step toward repairing the country's battered financial system. The Resolution and Collection Corp. is modeled
after the Resolution Trust Corp. of the U.S., which helped clean up the 1980s savings and loan fiasco. The
RCC will buy problem loans from banks and then try to sell the collateral, mostly land, to recover as much
money as possible. Banks generally have been unable to sell the collateral because of a moribund real-estate
market. And while the banks have built up reserves against their expected losses on those loans, most still
hold the collateral, making them vulnerable to further declines in property prices. Japan's land prices fell for
the eighth consecutive year in 1998, dropping an average of 4.6%, compared with 2.4% the previous year.
Japan Bond Issuance to Slow
Japanese companies issued a record amount of domestic corporate bonds in the fiscal year ended March 31,
but the pace is expected to slow considerably in the new business year that began yesterday, market
watchers said. That's because banks have become more willing to lend to companies, reducing their reliance
last year on bond issues to raise funds. Companies in any case are continuing to cut excess capacity rather
than invest in new plants and equipment. A record 11.045 trillion yen in corporate bonds were issued in the
fiscal year that just ended, a jump of about 20% from the previous year. Companies' funding concerns eased
dramatically late last year, thanks to the government's implementation of a plan to guarantee loans to small
and midsize companies.

THE AMERICAS
Citigroup to Buy Stake in Chile Firm
A spokeswoman at the Chilean unit of Citicorp, itself a unit of Citigroup, said the company will buy 16% of
Comunicaciones Capitulo, a Chilean telecommunications company, for an undisclosed sum. She confirmed a
report in yesterday's edition of financial daily Estrategia that Citigroup might buy a stake in the Chilean
company. Estrategia said Comunicaciones Capitulo controls Teleductos, which operates a fiber-optic network
in Santiago, Valparaiso, and Vina del Mara. It also owns 50% of Chile's Telesat Cia. de Telefonos, a local
telephone company in the Santiago metropolitan area. The other half of Telesat is owned by Telex-Chile.
Hanson to Buy Canada Brick Business
Hanson said it will pay $390 million Canadian dollars (U.S.$258.5 million) for Jannock of Canada's brick
business, making the United Kingdom-based group the second-largest player in the North American brick

--- Page 217 ---
Page 217 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
market behind Boral Industries of the U.S. Hanson's finance director, Jonathan Nicholls, said the British group
intended to pursue its growth further in North America. "Our overall strategy is to expand in heavy building
materials," he said, explaining that a boost in U.S. infrastructure spending over the next six years, combined
with the continuing strength of the U.S. economy, meant prospects for the construction sector there were
good. Hanson ranks as the second-largest U.K. brick maker.
Ecuador Reduces Freeze on Deposits
Ecuador President Jamil Mahuad said yesterday he was reducing to six months the freeze on checking
accounts and to seven months deposits on savings accounts. The government previously had declared a
freeze on bank deposits for one year. However, the new decree won't apply for long-term deposits, which will
remain frozen for one year. The president also authorized the release of deposits of nongovernment
organizations, which carry out public works, and of holders who can prove their need to access their funds for
serious health problems.
BRIEFLY:
-- British conglomerate Tomkins said it agreed to acquire ACD Tridon, a Canadian maker of windshield
wipers, clamps and automotive electronic parts, for $36 million, plus assumed debt of $66 million.

The Americas:Canada Gives the Inuits a Homeland, but No Malls Please
The Americas: Canada Gives the Inuits a Homeland, but No Malls Please
By Collin Levey
1,167 words
2 April 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A11
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
In the upper regions of the Northwest Territories of Canada, the caribou are said to outnumber humans by 25
to one. That may change now that federal bureaucrats from Ottawa are moving in with that classic message:
"We're from the government and we're here to help."
Yesterday, Canada redrew its map to give 136,000 square miles of land to the native Inuit as part of the
creation of a new territory called Nunavut. After two decades of legal bickering over specifics, this might seem
an unusually generous approach to a questionable demand for historical justice. But long before it benefits
the Inuit, the arrangement is likely to prove a lifetime employment act for Canadian officials.
The land of Nunavut will be 1.24 million square miles, roughly one-fifth the land mass of Canada, with its
capital city of Iqaluit on Baffin Island. It will retain its connections to Ottawa, but will, in theory at least,
become self-governing by the Inuits who represent some 85% of the area's 23,000 people. With residents
scattered in remote and icy enclaves, the area already is a cameo of distress: three times the rate of heavy
drinking and six times the suicide rate of the rest of Canada; one-third of its residents on welfare; and a per
capita income of $11,000, about 50% lower than the rest of Canada.
"These are some of the reasons why there's been such a push for creating Nunavut in the first place," says
Ken MacRury, Nunavut's new deputy minister for health and social services.
The concept is good as far as you can throw it. All the frosty territory that is to be given to the Inuit is former
Crown land -- which might explain the poverty in the first place -- so there isn't the typical conflagration over
private property rights. Getting the land out of government hands should, in theory, be good for economic
incentives. But not the way this deal is built.
The details of the agreement are likely to undermine much of the potential wealth creation that comes with
private property: The Inuit land in Nunavut will be held communally -- partially in keeping with tribal custom
but also because those are the terms that were set out in the negotiations. That in itself reduces the incentive
for individuals to use the land productively.
Not that the Inuits are the only aboriginal group whose land claim is bungled up with economic disincentives.
Canadian land claims typically restrict the way native groups may exercise the title to their own land. They
often can't sell the land unless they want to sell it back to the Crown; and as Owen Lippert of Canada's Fraser
Institute points out, they can't build anything deemed offensive: no strip malls, no parking lots. In other words,
they are not allowed to do the things that makes owning private property a worthwhile investment.
Another potential source of wealth creation for the Inuit is harvesting Nunavut's base metals and gold. But
again the prospects look dim. Under the agreement, the Inuit have the mining rights to a relatively small
chunk of their total territory -- some 14,000 square miles out of 136,000. As for what remains, the Inuits will
receive royalties on any money that Ottawa makes in mining it -- 50% on the first $2 million and 5% of
anything after that each year.
And because the land will be held communally, the mining companies will have to negotiate with the
aboriginal nation as a whole. As Melvin Smith, the author of "Our Home Or Native Land " and a former
constitutional adviser to the Canadian government puts it, "anyone who wants to mine is going to have to
jump through all sorts of hoops." Tagak Curley, chief negotiator for the Inuit leadership made it clear back in
1991 that the nation will be pleased to deal with the handful of companies interested in the area provided that
they employ a certain number of Inuits and consider other social benefits to the community, like training and
housing. These kinds of restrictions do nothing to recognize the importance of private initiative and have the
potential to deter investment altogether.
Despite all the claims that the primary goal of Nunavut is to help the Inuits, the creation of a large, clunky
territorial government promises to be nothing more than a make-work program for federal bureaucrats. There

--- Page 219 ---
Page 219 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
will be a new territorial court system and myriad departments and agencies, but most of the aboriginals won't
be qualified to fill the fancy new positions. Almost half of Nunavut adults have less than a ninth grade
education. Mr. Smith quips that there might be three lawyers among the whole Inuit population. (In fact, as
recently as 1996, there were none.) So who are all these jobs going to go to? White bureaucrats, he says.
"The Inuit will still be suffering in their ice houses and the bureaucrats will be toasting their toes by the fire."
Despite being the largest land claim in Canadian history, the creation of Nunavut has been met with almost
no resistance, challenge or debate. Unlike the lengthy discussion that surrounded the creation of the
provinces of Alberta and Saskatchewan, when Nunavut came up for a vote in June of 1993, the whole thing
was pushed through in one afternoon.
Nunavut is a case study in interest group liberalism run amok, "proposed by aggressive bureaucrats, prodded
by the most powerful lobby in the country and acceded to by compliant politicians," Mr. Smith explains. Yet it
is being looked to as a model for land claims by other native groups like the Nisga'a in British Columbia. Its
creation sets a series of dangerous precedents for 280 or so land claims that are still pending.
In its well-intentioned, patronizing way, the Supreme Court even set up other Canadian aboriginals for
endless legal battles over the spoils.
In the 1997 decision of Delgamuukw v. British Columbia, Chief Justice Antonio Lamer decreed that "oral
history," including songs and stories, should be considered on "equal footing" with the usual criteria for
proving ownership. As Mr. Lippert says, songs and stories have been undergoing a kind of claim inflation
ever since, as one group's supposed ancestral lands rapidly encroach on another's.
Whatever the motives of this and similar attempts to offer retroactive justice to indigenous people, it always
ends up tangled in interest group politics, social engineering and bigger government. Canada's latest debacle
is perhaps the triumph of hope over experience. But when all the noisemakers and confetti have left the new
territory, the Inuit may not be any better off than they were when they started.
---
Ms. Levey is a member of the Journal's editorial page staff.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Inuit and Nunavut" -- WSJ April, 15, 1999)

EUROPE/AFRICA/MIDEAST
Big French Banks
Reject a Truce
Over BNP's Bids
Despite calls from France's Socialist government for a negotiated superbank deal, it looks as if efforts to
create the world's largest bank will fall into the hands of shareholders sooner rather than later. The chiefs of
Societe Generale and Paribas forcefully rejected the idea of sitting down at the negotiating table to end the
battle over the twin takeover bids for both banks by Banque Nationale de Paris. Instead, the two targeted
banks say shareholders should let their positions be known on the proposed three-way, $37 billion tie-up or
the two banks' own plans to merge -- a proposal that was thrown off track by BNP's unsolicited move. With
the latest development, which also includes a similar urging on the part of the nation's independent central
bank, all this appears headed toward a stock-market battle in which both sides will seek to convince
shareholders that they can offer the better deal.
BNP will argue that its three-way alliance creates a French champion in the race for size, while Societe
Generale and Paribas will say that their $17.2 billion merger deal will offer more value and be easier to
implement. More than a few analysts expect Societe Generale to sweeten the pot by adding a cash
component to its all-share offer for Paribas.
South Africa's Anglo Makes Twin Offer
South Africa's biggest company Anglo American launched a surprise bid to buy out the minority shareholders
in two of its holding companies at home -- this as it prepares to shift its primary listing to London from
Johannesburg. But while the 8.85 billion rand ($1.42 billion) share-swap offer for Anglo American Gold
Investment and Anglo American Investment Trust sent the shares of both concerns soaring, Anglo
American's stock fell sharply. Still, when things settle down, most analysts think the market will view the move
as a good one for the company. That's because the twin buyouts are seen as playing a significant role in
helping Anglo American to overcome lingering concerns that its asset structure is far too complex and
unfocused.
Russia to Investigate Yukos's Issues
With foreign investors wary of putting their money in Russia, that nation's chief market regulator apparently
thinks the time may be right to look into the concerns of the investment community. It announced an inquiry
into Yukos, Russia's second-largest oil company, which barred minority investors from three shareholders'
meetings this month and then pushed through massive share issues diluting their holdings. Meanwhile, two
U.S. congressmen launched their offensive against a new IMF loan for the country. House Majority Leader
Dick Armey of Texas and New Jersey Rep. Jim Saxton, a Republican, demanded the administration oppose
new lending because of suspicions that Russia's central bank channeled past IMF money into secret
offshore accounts.
Sweden, Norway Sign Merger Deal
Telecommunications companies Telia of Sweden and Telenor of Norway finalized an agreement to merge,
saying partial privatization of the group will take place no later than next year. Sweden will hold 60% and the
smaller Norway 40% of the new entity created from the merger of the two big state-owned companies across
European Union lines; Sweden is a member of the 15-nation trade bloc, while Norway is outside the EU. The

--- Page 221 ---

deal calls for the two countries, however, to reduce their stakes to just over a third each at the time of the
initial public offering, evening up the ante.

ASIA/PACIFIC
KAL's Shares Soar on Hopes of Rebound
Korean Air Lines, like so many carriers in Asia, has been dragged down by the economic crisis not only at
home but in the entire region. Now, with South Korea's economy improving and the nation's currency
strengthening, foreign investors are bidding up the airline's shares in the belief that more Koreans will get
back into the habit of traveling. Since the beginning of the year, the stock has climbed nearly 30% on the
trading floor in Seoul as signs of a turnaround are already apparent; last year, the carrier, one of the world's
biggest, swung to a profit of 296.6 billion won ($242.1 million) from a loss of 397.4 billion won a year earlier.
And at least one analyst says he expects passenger demand to jump 10% this year.
Bangkok Unveils Details of Tax Cuts
Thailand unveiled the details of tax cuts and spending programs that total $3.5 billion. The package, which
has the blessing of the International Monetary Fund, calls for Bangkok to borrow $1.45 billion from Japan and
the World Bank to finance investments designed to create jobs and bolster demand for goods and services in
the Southeast Asian nation. It also calls for a cut in the value-added tax to 7% from 10%, an income-tax
reduction for those with more than 50,000 baht ($1,330) of net income and lower energy prices. With all this
in place, Thailand is now forecasting economic growth of 1% for this year -- a figure that would be impressive
given that the economy contracted 8% in 1998.

for foreign investment in Japan, a U.S. government official said.

Brazil's foreign-exchange reserves will receive a substantial boost in coming days with the International

Legislator's Slim AgendaMirrors His Private Interests
TEXAS JOURNAL
Legislator's Slim Agenda Mirrors His Private Interests
By Robert Elder Jr.
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,905 words
31 March 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
Texas Journal
T1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
With just nine bills introduced this session, state Rep. Gary Elkins is one of the least prolific lawmakers in the
Capitol.
He's also one of the most focused: Four of the nine bills sponsored by the third-term Houston Republican are
aimed at benefiting a single industry -- pawnshops. It's an industry in which Rep. Elkins has more than a
passing interest, since he and his family control a trust that owns a Houston pawnshop.
Though it might seem like a conflict of interest for lawmakers to carry legislation that would benefit their
private business interests, Texas law gives its citizen-legislators wide leeway to do just that. State ethics rules
say only that lawmakers can't carry or vote on laws that would benefit their own individual business;
legislation to aid other businesses in the industry or the industry as a whole is fair game. In other words, Rep.
Elkins can sponsor all the pawnshop bills he wants, as long as his pawnshop isn't the sole beneficiary.
Critics say the current loose standards create the appearance of self-dealing by lawmakers in the bills they
pass. "All it takes is one example" to hurt public confidence in the Legislature, says state Rep. Steve Wolens,
speaking generally. The Dallas Democrat has introduced legislation that would require legislators to disclose,
in writing, if they think they may have a conflict of interest on a particular bill, though they would not be barred
from voting on it. Avoiding the perception of a conflict, Rep. Wolens says, is "an integral part of what we do --
[demonstrating] that we are not self-dealing and that we're acting for the public good."
Rep. Elkins, of course, isn't the lone example. About one-half of the 26 bills carried by Rep. Kyle Janek, a
Houston Republican who is a physician, concern doctors or health-care issues. One bill would require HMOs
to pay physicians and health-care providers for services more promptly or face new fines. And earlier this
month, state Rep. Tom Craddick, a Midland Republican, sponsored legislation to grant a tax break to oil-well
producers. Rep. Craddick receives royalty income from some of the operators that would benefit from the l
legislation. (Rep. Janek, the only physician in the 150-member House, says he feels comfortable introducing
health-care bills if they primarily benefit patients. Rep. Craddick says he doesn't have a conflict because it's
practically impossible to tie the effects of a specific tax break to his diverse royalty holdings. Also, he says, he
needs to weigh in on energy bills because his district's economy is so dependent on oil and gas production.)
But critics say Rep. Elkins is an especially troubling case, because his legislative resume is so light, and so
closely tied to his pawnshop interests. What's more, people familiar with his legislative and business activities
say, Rep. Elkins has been working against legislation that would add strict new regulations for another one of
his business interests, "sale-leaseback" stores. These stores pay consumers cash upfront for their household
appliances and lease them back to the customers at steep biweekly or monthly rates.
Rep. Elkins represents "one of the worst examples" of conflict of interest since the state passed wide-ranging
ethics reforms in 1991, says Tom Smith, executive director of Public Citizen's Texas office in Austin, referring
to the lawmaker's involvement in the pawnshop and sale-leaseback bills.
Rep. Elkins denies any suggestion of self-dealing and says he introduced the pawnshop bills only at the
request of industry trade groups. And he says he has no reservations about doing so, because he
understands the business. "Quite frankly, I'm the most knowledgeable person in this House on pawnshop
legislation," he says. "I don't know why [the House] should not have my expertise."
Indeed, under the concept of citizen-legislators, the legislative process is supposed to benefit from a
lawmakers' outside interests. "This is supposed to be the wisdom of our Legislature, that members bring
experience in an issue to legislation," says Ed Shack, an Austin lawyer who advises state officials and
candidates on ethics laws.

--- Page 224 ---
Page 224 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Ethics experts also say it's unreasonable to expect lawmakers to recuse themselves on every bill that might
affect their private interests. They say the ethics law is intentionally flexible because the state has part-time
lawmakers who earn just $600 a month, plus a per diem when they are in session. "It's hard to be specific on
conflicts of interest when you have a working Legislature," says Mr. Shack. "This isn't Washington, D.C.,
where [legislators] are making real money."
Rep. Elkins also defends his relatively light tally of bills. As a conservative Republican, he says, "my agenda
is more to kill bills than it is to pass them." And his status as a relatively junior lawmaker in the minority party
in the House means he doesn't get to take the lead in major legislation. "Only committee chairmen carry
major pieces of legislation," he says.
Rep. Elkins wouldn't provide any more details about the trust or his pawnshop company, saying his Ethics
Commission filings contain all that the law requires him to disclose. Those records give only the name of the
trust, GE & CE LLC.
Open-government advocates say the scant disclosure requirement is another flaw in state ethics rules. State
ethics laws don't require lawmakers to disclose the nature of their business holdings or the location of
real-estate holdings. Nor do they require disclosure of the holdings of trusts. "The whole problem with Texas
law is that it isn't specific enough to discern the kind of activity [Rep. Elkins] is involved in," says Public
Citizen's Mr. Smith, referring to Rep. Elkins's pawnshop interests.
That information is, however, contained in the records of the state Consumer Credit Commission -- not
because Rep. Elkins is a lawmaker, but because his trust owns a pawnshop. Those records show that Rep.
Elkins and his family, through their trust, hold a controlling interest in Texas Diamond & Gold Pawn Inc. of
Houston. According to the records, Rep. Elkins owns 25% of the trust; the balance is held by five other
parties identified only by their initials. Four sets of initials coincide with the initials of Rep. Elkins's four
children. The records show that Rep. Elkins's trust acquired the pawnshop in 1997.
The first of Rep. Elkins's pawnshop bills, introduced one day before the March 12 deadline for filing bills,
would increase the number of pawnbrokers on the Texas Finance Commission, which regulates the industry.
Among the other bills are one to ease zoning restrictions on pawnshops and another that would compensate
pawnbrokers for goods sold to pawnshops without the owner's consent.
He says he doesn't consider his involvement in the legislation an unethical conflict, because the pawnshop is
not his main business. "It's just not a big issue to me," he asserts. "It's not my love."
His main business is Personal Rental Corp., a company in Bellaire that pays customers cash for their
appliances and leases them back; Rep. Elkins says his customers are charged $30 over two weeks for every
$100 they get upfront for their merchandise. Rep. Elkins is also president of two consulting firms that work for
sale-leaseback companies.
Because of his close ties to that business, Rep. Elkins says, he intentionally has refused to be active in
legislation affecting that industry. On Monday, the House Financial Institutions Committee, of which Rep.
Elkins is a member, heard bills that would essentially outlaw sale-leaseback transactions by classifying them
as loans and subjecting them to a set of interest-rate caps. Rep. Elkins skipped part of the hearing when the
bills were discussed.
"It's obviously a tremendous conflict of interest since it affects my business directly," he said the week before
the hearing. "I've stayed out of it all year in terms of lobbying for it or against it. I only talk about it when I'm
approached."
But two of the Dallas lawmakers carrying the legislation -- GOP Sen. John Carona and Democratic Rep. Dale
Tillery -- say Rep. Elkins hasn't stayed clear of the issue.
In fact, Rep. Tillery says, Rep. Elkins returned to the hearing room later Monday night, and the two men had
what Rep. Tillery described as a "heated exchange" over whether regulation of the industry was warranted.
Committee Chairman Kip Averitt, a Waco Republican, confirms Rep. Tillery's account.
Rep. Tillery also notes that Rep. Elkins attended a Jan. 29 meeting with about 25 operators of sale-leaseback
companies at which legislative strategy was discussed with two industry lobbyists, Nancy Fisher of Austin and
Brenda Arnett of Houston, a former executive director of the Texas Department of Commerce. Ms. Fisher
won't discuss any details about the meeting.
Rep. Elkins's attendance "means to me he may be coordinating efforts against this legislation," Rep. Tillery
says.
Rob Schneider, a senior analyst for the Consumers Union office in Austin and a critic of sale-leaseback
practices, says Rep. Elkins has sought him out for several conversations about the legislation. "He was trying

--- Page 225 ---
Page 225 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
to convince me that our position was wrong, that sale-leaseback transactions are not a bad thing and that
they comply with the law," Mr. Schneider says.
Rep. Elkins wouldn't respond to questions about the two lawmakers' allegations, any discussions with Mr.
Schneider or his role at the Jan. 29 meeting.
Incidentally, it was a legal dispute over sale-leasebacks that prompted Rep. Elkins to run for the Legislature.
In 1991, after an investigation by the Credit Commission, the state attorney general sued Rep. Elkins,
alleging that Personal Rental was disguising loans as leases and charging illegally high interest rates. In
1993, a Harris County jury ruled in Mr. Elkins's favor.
Rep. Elkins says he felt that legitimate players in the industry needed better protection from regulatory
interference. "It was this long battle with the attorney general's office that got me into this place to start with,"
he says.
---
Legal Loophole
Rep. Gary Elkins is sponsoring four bills that affect the pawnshop
industry even though he runs several companies involved in that
business. But state ethics law allows a lawmaker to vote on
legislation that affects his or her industry -- as long as the bill
doesn't single out the legislator's company for special treatment.

Big Spender
Bookshelf
LEISURE & ARTS
Big Spender
By Maury Klein
923 words
30 March 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A24
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Almost a year ago, Ron Chernow's biography of John D. Rockefeller demolished one of those "robber baron"
caricatures that cling leechlike to the financiers and industrialists of the late 19th century. In that same vein
Jean Strouse offers "Morgan: American Financier" (Random House, 796 pages, $34.95), a magnificent,
insightful study of the only man who was even more influential and mysterious than the oil baron.
If Rockefeller was a titan, J. Pierpont Morgan was a colossus who not only symbolized his age but in many
ways defined it. Both men were aloof figures who became legends in their own time. Both harbored a passion
that amounted to a second career. For Rockefeller it was philanthropy, for Morgan the collecting of art.
Where Rockefeller took hold of a young industry and completely organized it, Morgan emerged gradually as
the most powerful banker of his era. Ironically, the power he displayed in acting as a surrogate central bank
before the Federal Reserve system was created, especially during the gold crisis of the 1890s and the Panic
of 1907, caused him to be reviled by a public that feared the power more than it appreciated the service.
The Morgan of legend was often known as "Jupiter," a stern, towering figure with a fierce glare and Olympian
powers. The actual man was a shy, inarticulate individual, fastidious of dress and grooming, possessed of a
tender side that one acquaintance called a "feminine streak," who fought all his life against ill health and
chronic bouts of depression.
Born in Hartford, Conn., in 1837, a child of privilege, he grew up in a sophisticated world on both sides of the
Atlantic. The one true love of his life was dying of tuberculosis when they wed; his second marriage in 1865
turned sour early and sent Morgan into a lifelong pattern of affairs with other women who shared his sybaritic
tastes and passion for art. His distaff preferences extended to his children: He doted on his daughters while
neglecting the son who would be his heir.
Morgan was ruled well into middle age by his banker father who, as Ms. Strouse tells us, "sent him away to
school, subjected him to decades of critical scrutiny, and taught him by relentless lecture and example what it
meant to be the right kind of man." Junius Morgan's firm was in London; Pierpont settled in New York in 1857.
He avoided service in the Civil War by buying a substitute, did well at banking and in 1871 joined a friend to
form Drexel, Morgan.
The firm moved to 23 Wall Street in 1873 and steadily improved its stature, shifting its primary business from
government finance in the 1870s to railroads in the 1880s and industrial consolidations in the 1890s. His
father's death in 1890 left Pierpont the senior partner in both firms. Only then did he emerge as the most
powerful banker on Wall Street. By the time of his own death in 1913, he had become the icon of an era that
was about to vanish.
"Jupiter" was far from infallible. Although possessed of a good mind, his success derived more from his ability
to shoulder great responsibilities, his unquestioned integrity and his sheer strength of character. His business
record was surprisingly mixed. Some endeavors, like General Electric and U.S. Steel, turned out well; others,
notably International Mercantile Marine and the New Haven Railroad, failed miserably.
Always a lavish spender, Morgan paid top dollar for acquisitions and never hesitated over the scale of an
enterprise. The two largest corporations of the age, U.S. Steel and Northern Securities, were his creations.
Never a workaholic, he took long vacations abroad yet was notorious for working his partners to death. Nor
was he a keen student of human nature. "I am not a good judge of men," he admitted. "My first choice of a
man is sometimes right; my second choice never is."
Increasingly after 1900 Morgan paid less attention to business and more to the buying of art, on which he
spent half of his income during the last 20 years of his life. For all the jokes made about his emptying Europe

--- Page 227 ---
Page 227 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
and Egypt of its art treasures, his tastes were refined, sophisticated and unconventional. His contribution to
American cultural development rivals that of his business career, and Ms. Strouse gives it equal attention. No
other collector bought so much of so many different kinds of art. An impressive part of that legacy still resides
in the Metropolitan Museum and in the library he built in 1906.
A good biography does not so much progress as accumulate. Where the historian offers portraits within a
certain period, suggesting figures with a fixed character, the biographer provides a sense of the changes,
often profound, that shape and reshape the subject over time. Seeing an action in the broader context of the
subject's whole life gives it a very different slant. This is especially true with Morgan, whose life was in so
many ways larger than life. By this measure Ms. Strouse's biography is a superb one indeed. Rich in detail,
informed by a wealth of new sources, written in a clear style that wears well, it provides the fullest and most
revealing look at this remarkable, complex man that we are likely to get.
---
Mr. Klein is the author of "The Life and Legend of Jay Gould." His biography of E.H. Harriman will be
published next year.

Russia Says IMF Aid Plan Is Back On,But News for Debtholders Is Still Bad
International
Russia Says IMF Aid Plan Is Back On, But News for Debtholders Is Still Bad
By Andrew Higgins and Mark Whitehouse
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
1,232 words
30 March 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A21
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
MOSCOW -- Russia says the International Monetary Fund has agreed to restart a suspended aid program,
but any money won't start flowing until late next month at the earliest and will be far less than that promised
last year.
Even a modest infusion, however, will allow Russia to cover debt repayments to the IMF and unblock further
funding of about $3 billion from the World Bank and Japan. It would also open the way for Russia to
reschedule about $70 billion of Soviet-era debt under the Paris and London clubs of government and
commercial creditors.
Prime Minister Yevgeny Primakov announced the IMF credits after a three-hour meeting yesterday with the
fund's visiting managing director, Michel Camdessus. A joint statement, however, referred only to agreement
on broad economic policy and pledges of "constructive cooperation."
Russian media reports say the IMF will give Russia $4.8 billion in four installments over the next year. Details
of a final deal will be worked out by an IMF mission due in Moscow next week and then presented for
approval to a meeting of the fund's board of directors in late April.
But Russia's apparent progress with the IMF, which some analysts believe was helped by the crisis in
Kosovo, coincided with yet more bad news for holders of treasury bonds on which the government defaulted
last August.
Mr. Primakov, who last week turned back from a trip to Washington in midair to protest the imminent start of
attacks against Yugoslavia, is due to fly to Belgrade today for talks with President Slobodan Milosevic. The
White House called the trip, billed as a peace mission, useful.
Russia yesterday released further details of a controversial proposal to restructure 280 billion rubles ($11.5
billion) in frozen treasurys, further souring the deal for foreigners holding nearly a third of the debt.
In a statement, the Finance Ministry and central bank said foreign bondholders who accept the deal and
choose to repatriate ruble proceeds will pay a premium of as much as 20% to buy dollars at special auctions.
The news comes a week after the central bank said it would provide only $200 million for the auctions, due
to begin in April. Western bankers complain that Russia had offered $550 million for repatriation.
The statement said the central bank might consider holding more auctions if its financial state allows. The
likelihood of only modest funding from the IMF will leave the government with little spare cash as most of it
will go to paying off Russia's debts to the fund itself, which total $3.8 billion between April and year end.
Russia has said it can afford to pay only $9.5 billion of debt obligation this year totaling $17.5 billion.
Neither Mr. Camdessus nor Mr. Primakov specified how much the IMF might provide. Former Prime Minister
Viktor Chernomyrdin said on Sunday after talks with Mr. Camdessus that a loan of $8 billion was under
discussion. But the Interfax news agency, citing people close to the talks, said Russia would get $4.8 billion.
Last July, Russia won an IMF-led package totaling $22.6 billion, $15.1 billion of which was to come from the
IMF with the rest from the World Bank and Japan. The IMF disbursed only $4.8 billion and then suspended
the program after Russia defaulted on its treasury debt, let the ruble slide and installed a new government
stacked with Soviet-era apparatchiks.
Negotiation to break the deadlock has often been acrimonious, with Mr. Primakov ridiculing IMF experts as
"kids" and his chief economic strategist First Deputy Prime Minister Yuri Maslyukov complaining of "indecent"
demands. The IMF meanwhile dismissed Russia's budget as unrealistic.

--- Page 229 ---
Page 229 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But yesterday's joint statement spoke of broad harmony on the budget and "key elements of the economic
program... for 1999 and beyond." Both sides have given some ground. After demanding a budget surplus of
3.5% before interest payments, the IMF and Russia have now agreed on a primary surplus target of 2%.
Russia in turn promises to postpone a value-added tax cut from July until 2000 and step up revenue
collection from gas, electricity and oil producers.
The war in Yugoslavia may also have helped prod the IMF toward a preliminary accord. "Kosovo is a catalyst.
It didn't change the whole picture, but it accelerated the process," says Goohoon Kwon, senior economist at
ABN-Amro in London, "The IMF either had to say forget about a deal or we give you the money."
Russia has condemned the North Atlantic Treaty Organization's airstrikes against Yugoslavia. The IMF's
biggest shareholders are NATO members and are eager to prevent Russia's largely symbolic protests from
escalating into active support for Yugoslavia. The State Department yesterday expressed hope that Mr.
Primakov's planned peace mission to Belgrade would persuade President Slobodan Milosvic to halt the
crackdown in Kosovo.
IMF support for Russia could also help damp an upsurge in anti-Western sentiment. On Sunday, shortly
before Mr. Camdessus met with the government's senior economic policy makers, a gunman in camouflage
fatigues shot at the U.S. Embassy here after trying unsuccessfully to fire a grenade launcher.
Rather than a cure for Russia's ailing economy, however, IMF money is only a short-term salve to stave off
further defaults. Despite an increase in Russia's trade surplus due largely to the ruble's collapse, Russia's
foreign reserves continue to decline. The latest central bank figures show that foreign currency and gold
reserves had slipped to $11.2 billion by March 19 from $11.4 billion a week earlier.
The shortage of foreign cash has led Russia to whittle away an already slender deal on offer to holders of
defaulted treasurys. Bankers involved in the restructuring, already valued at less than five cents per dollar of
face value, said the repatriation premium announced yesterday slightly worsens an already unsavory deal.
"It's just bad faith on top of bad faith," said one foreign banker. "They've already stolen 95 cents on the dollar.
They're just stealing another penny."
The central bank and Finance Ministry statement also confirmed that foreigners will be allowed to invest
so-called restricted rubles into Russian stocks -- but only through authorized banks and only in shares
approved by the government. The list of eligible shares will be released "shortly," and should represent more
than 90% of last year's activity on the country's two leading trading systems, the statement said.
The new details still fell short of the final term sheet that some Western bankers -- representing about 25% of
foreign bondholders -- have demanded before deciding whether or not to accept the deal. "It's a non-event
again," said one banker. The holdouts want to see a full list of eligible ruble investments, as well as published
regulations on the tax regime and other aspects of the deal.
The final deadline to tender frozen securities for the restructuring is April 20. The statement said that 35% of
foreign bondholders had already accepted the deal. Credit Suisse Group's Credit Suisse First Boston, which
represents about 40% of foreign bondholders, has said it will refuse.

The Road to 10,000
REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
The Road to 10,000
773 words
30 March 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A26
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
We still prefer the rock-solid sound of the "Dow Jones Industrial Average," but to many people it must feel like
the Dow Jones Starship, which yesterday transported Michael Dell, Bill Gates, Jack Welch and untold
numbers of shareholding passengers past point 10,000. It's been a long journey. Indeed, at Dow Jones we
have been taking the measure of this passage for more than a century.
However, there is a view about that the market's recent exuberance all began with, say, Jeff Bezos and the
Internet Age companies whose valuations can only be scaled with the help of bottled oxygen. Alas, no. This
journey began, we think, in the early 1980s.
How long ago was that? Well, in the early 1980s the very youngest of our current generation of Internet
entrepreneurs were amusing themselves with a computer called the Commodore 64. Their idea of a digital
game was "Speak `n' Spell." To play video games, they rifled Mom's purse for quarters and went to an
arcade. And the U.S. economy was in the tank, treading water.
Indeed, the still waters of the stock market in the 1970s are clear enough in the accompanying chart. (See
related graphic -- WSJ March 30, 1999) The stock market managed to tread water for about 10 years. While
going nowhere, this exercise no doubt built great aerobic capacity and muscle tone. No telling how far this
fellow might run if he ever got out of the tank.
Then, in 1983, the long run to 10,000 began.
There will be many explanations for this achievement. Ours is going to be primarily political. It begins in 1973,
the year that Richard Nixon delinked the dollar from gold, imposed wage and price controls, and put the
stock market in the tank. Inflation ensued and then engulfed the Carter Presidency. Government was fat, as
anything would be that chowed down billions and billions in tax revenue over 15 years.
President Jimmy Carter may well have sensed it was time for a change, appointing deregulators such as
Alfred Kahn and then most crucially Federal Reserve Chairman Paul Volcker. Good choices, but too late for
Mr. Carter. The American people decided in 1980 to put the former Governor of California, the nation's most
entrepreneurial state, into the White House. His policy, said Ronald Reagan, was to "get government out of
the way." His economic policy consisted of three prongs -- sound money, lower marginal tax rates and
deregulation.
It looks like it worked. The chart depicts a stock market rising steadily from 1983 until now. The famous '87
crash was overcome, the last recession merely a flattening. The last turbocharged spurt started with the
election of a Republican Congress in 1994 and accompanying sea-change in proposals of the Democratic
Administration.
Reaganomics, adapted from classical liberal economics, would have worked anytime we think, but its arrival
in the U.S. in the 1980s was propitious. A technological revolution in biology and electronics was aborning,
and the geniuses driving it were looking mainly for one missing element -- capital. Had this been Europe, the
whole biotech/digital enterprise would have been stillborn. Instead, our financial markets provided them the
capital they needed. Michael Milken's "junk" propelled the likes of TCI and MCI. Venture capitalists funded
Apple, Intel and Microsoft. Spurred by these upstarts, by the takeover threat and by the Japanese, fat Fortune
500 firms concluded that future growth was impossible without "restructuring" their operations to gain
efficiencies.
All well and good, but both the feisty start-ups and the Citicorps and GEs still needed an economy shorn of
the national government's plodding post-War weight if all that intellectual and managerial energy were going
to come to anything. Government of course doesn't like getting out of the way; most politicians, even now,
can't conceive of themselves as handmaidens to the larger economy. But despite hiccups from both the

--- Page 231 ---
Page 231 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
succeeding Bush and Clinton administrations, there has been no truly significant departure from the path laid
out by Mr. Reagan's guiding principles. The Dow's rise the past 15 years has been breathtaking.
We here at Dow Jones are celebrating this historic milestone that bears our name, but that mighty climb to
10,006.78 in Charles Dow's now-ubiquitous index reflects much more than the gains in share prices.
Ultimately, cumulatively, it measures work well done, work by hundreds of thousands of adult men and
women, some retired since the bull market began its run, some just now beginning. Our hats are off to them.

EUROPE/AFRICA
EU's Farm Accord
Falls Far Short
Of Expectations
It seemed as if the time was finally right for the European Union to make the kind of sweeping changes in its
farm program that the global community had been urging for years. But in the end, a million French farmers
and strong agricultural lobbies in Italy and Spain stood in the way of the expected overhaul of the common
agricultural policy -- a scheme that consumes a whopping 42% of the EU's annual budget. While some
concessions were made at the EU summit in Berlin -- including limiting future expenditures rather than letting
them continue to grow -- the 15 member nations fell far short of doing what they had set out to do. For
example, instead of a 20% reduction in cereals support prices, summit participants agreed on 15%; a dairy
price reform scheduled to begin in 2003 was shelved until 2005; and a proposal to cut overall farm supports
by a fixed percentage annually for six years was scrapped altogether -- and that after the European
Commission was talking about the need to cut prices by a third to bring them closer in line with those being
paid to farmers elsewhere in the world.
Still giving in on the reforms could make things more difficult for the EU down the road given that it is moving
toward enlarging its membership to include some East European nations with big farming sectors and
considering too that it will be tougher to defend such policies in the face of moves to liberalize global trade in
agricultural goods.
French Bankers Meet Over BNP Bid
The warring sides in France's giant banking takeover battle met yesterday in the presence of the governor of
the nation's central bank, but it all ended without either side giving ground, according to people familiar with
the situation. After a series of one-on-one meetings between the central bank's governor and the chairmen
of Banque Nationale de Paris, Societe Generale and Paribas, the three sides met jointly without speaking
directly to each other. Nevertheless, it represented a slight thawing of the icy relations because it was the first
time the opposing sides gathered around the same table since BNP launched its double-barreled $37 billion
takeover bid for both Societe Generale and Paribas nearly three weeks ago. The twin bid aims to foil a $17.2
billion merger agreement between Paribas and Societe Generale that was close to completion.
Remarks Suggest ECB Rate Reduction
In what may be another hint that the European Central Bank is ready to cut interest rates, a member of the
ECB's directorate said that slumping economic growth in the euro zone is more disconcerting than the euro's
recent slide. In fact, Otmar Issing implied the new currency's recent weakness has less to do with
fundamentals than the fact that times have been so good in the U.S. The euro fell to $1.0726 in London
trading on Friday, its lowest level since the currency began trading at the beginning of the year. Meanwhile,
more than a few economists are predicting that other noneuro countries -- including Norway, Greece, the U.K.
and Denmark -- will follow Sweden's lead and cut rates amid concerns over the impact of global-economic
turbulence.
IMF Clears Loan Package to Zambia
The International Monetary Fund gave the green light to a three-year loan package totaling $349 million for
Zambia to support the southern African nation's plans to try to boost growth, reduce the rate of inflation and
introduce a wide range of reforms to the struggling economy. But for a recovery of the scope envisaged by

--- Page 233 ---
Page 233 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
the government, analysts concede much will depend on commodity prices, the weather and if Zambia
remains determined to revive the stalled privatization of its state-held mining assets. In particular, the IMF is
emphasizing the need for a swift completion of the sale of Zambia Consolidated Copper Mines, which is in
dire need of fresh capital to get back on its feet.
Big London Project Wins Key Approvals
The plan for an exhibition center in east London's Docklands won key government approvals that will allow
hotel and retail development to move ahead at the site, accessible by the Jubilee Line extension on the
London Underground. The Exhibition Centre for London will be built at the old Royal Victoria Docks and will
vastly increase the capacity of the outmoded space offered by such venues as the exhibition hall at Earls
Court in west London. The new project is scheduled for completion late next year. And though it could herald
the capital's rebirth as a major player in the European exhibitions arena, it will still be much smaller than the
National Exhibition Centre in Birmingham in central England.

ASIA/PACIFIC
U.S., China Accelerate WTO Efforts
The U.S. says it and China are still far apart on agreeing to the terms for Beijing's entry into the World Trade
Organization. But with the two sides eager to sign a deal during Chinese Premier Zhu Rongji's visit to the
U.S. in little over a week, things may begin to happen. U.S. officials in Beijing say they expect U.S. Trade
Representative Charlene Barshefsky to step up talks when she returns to Beijing this week. In addition,
details emerged over the weekend of a new Chinese offer on reforming the banking industry that would
significantly liberalize the nation's closed financial markets. China also pledged to further open its insurance
market, saying foreign insurers would be allowed to sell products in cities that were once entirely off limits.
Court Case May Stall China Investment
The cost of borrowing for companies in China has risen sharply because of the recent collapse of Guangdong
International Trust & Investment Corp., or Gitic. Now analysts say how China's judicial system winds up
handling the group's bankruptcy proceedings in early April will determine if those costs will climb even further.
Gitic was owned and managed by the Guangdong government in southern China, and when Beijing ordered
it closed, it shattered an assumption that foreign banks relied on for years: that Beijing would bail out
government companies, even in the case of defaults. This time around, if the court process appears unfair,
not only will borrowing costs for mainland companies soar even higher, but Beijing itself may find it difficult to
attract new foreign investment.

Brazil's Jobless Rate Moves Lower
Brazil's unemployment rate fell two-tenths of a percentage point to 7.5% last month from a month earlier. The
February figure, however, was slightly higher than the rate of 7.4% a year earlier. Last month's rate was also
the second highest monthly figure for a February since the government began calculating the data in 1982.
Greater Sao Paulo, the most populous region, registered an unemployment rate of 8.4%, down from 10.1%
the previous month. The Rio de Janeiro metropolitan region tallied the lowest rate of 5%, down from 5.7%.
Venezuela Aims to Borrow $5 Billion
Venezuela's finance minister says the country is looking to borrow $5 billion this year to help finance its
budget deficit, which is projected at 9% of the nation's economic output or about $9 billion. The plan would
entail borrowing $3 billion from overseas capital markets, $1 billion from multilateral lending agencies and $1
billion from the domestic market. Meanwhile, lawmakers went ahead and passed a measure that grants the
president broad powers to deal with the nation's economic troubles.

EUROPE
Poland to Study
Allowing Currency
To Float Freely
With its eyes set on eventually moving Poland into the euro zone, a committee of the central bank is set to
begin examining plans to let the nation's currency float freely. Though such a move remains at least a year
away, it marks an important step in the former Communist nation's march toward reforming its economy. The
central bank, by addressing the issue now, is determined to keep the country from slipping into the kind of
problems faced by other transitional economies such as Brazil, Russia and Thailand -- all of which had their
backs to the wall when they floated their currencies. Unlike the others, Poland is working from a position of
relative strength because the zloty has proved stable and strong throughout the global market turmoil. Now it
appears the goal is to move quietly but deliberately away from the current managed float toward eventual
membership in the European exchange-rate mechanism, a precursor to joining the euro zone.
Still as one economist notes, Poland will need to bring its inflation rate closer to Western European levels if it
hopes to pull all this off smoothly. So far Warsaw is doing an admirable job; inflation stood at 5.6% for the 12
months through February, down sharply from 14.2% for the same period a year earlier.
Commerzbank to Raise $2.72 Billion
Commerzbank, one of the biggest players in the German banking industry, plans to raise about 2.5 billion
euros ($2.72 billion) to strengthen its capital base and put itself in a stronger position to make acquisitions as
European banks continue to undergo changes that would have seemed unthinkable only a few years ago.
The bank -- which recently slipped from Germany's third largest to the No. 4 spot after the emergence of
HypoVereinsbank from the merger of two big Bavarian banks rearranged the rankings -- will raise 436 million
euros in newly authorized capital, and a further two billion euros in debt by issuing convertible bonds and
other corporate paper. And with Commerzbank set to put a plan before its stock holders this spring to buy
back as much as 10% of its shares, it could have even more money at its disposal to fortify any future
shopping spree.
Norway Bank Deal Oversubscribed
In a move that suggests the market for state-owned bank shares will remain strong, Norway's sale of 16% of
Christiania Bank og Kreditkasse was nearly three times oversubscribed. With the 2.7 billion kroner ($348.7
million) sale of 90 million shares, the government will reduce its stake in Norway's second-largest commercial
bank to just over a third. The price was set at 30.60 kroner a share and the absence of a discount from the
market price of the shares on the Oslo exchange was seen as a sign of strong demand. Regardless, the sale
couldn't have come at a better time for the nation's leadership; the central bank recently took sharp aim at
the government for wearing the hats of both bank supervisor and shareholder.
EU Crisis May Outlast Berlin Summit
At their summit in Berlin later this week, European Union leaders will try to mend fences and resolve the crisis
sparked by the resignation of European Commission President Jacques Santer following an independent
report that accused the EU's executive body of turning a blind eye to corruption, cronyism and nepotism. But
despite the emergence of Italy's Romano Prodi as an early favorite, it isn't clear that EU member nations are
really ready to put aside their differences and pick the next commission president that soon. Also given the
EU's history of horse-trading, some countries could make their support for an individual candidate conditional
on obtaining a suitable deal on how much they pay into or receive back from the EU's annual budget.

THE AMERICAS
Moody's Says Latin Banks Set for Y2K
The economies of Latin America may have their troubles, but Moody's Investors Service says that many of
the banks it rates in the region appear to be on track to deal with the Y2K computer problem. The rating
agency gave high marks to the effort by the banks, especially those in Brazil, Mexico, Chile and Colombia, to
be fully compliant and avoid the confusion that could kick into place if computers read the changeover to the
year 2000 as 1900. But at least part of the reason the financial institutions are in such good shape in those
countries is that their respective governments have set tight deadlines and even threatened to close the
banks if their computer systems aren't in order to ring in the new century.
Brazil State Suspends Aid to U.S. Firms
The Brazilian state of Rio Grande do Sul cobbled together a $470 million aid package back in 1997 to lure
two of the world's biggest auto makers on to their soil. Now the province in the southern region of Latin
America's biggest economy is suspending more than $250 million of those incentives meant for General
Motors and Ford Motor. The regional government says it can better use the money on badly needed projects
including health care, basic sanitation and even to pay its civil servants. But GM, the world's biggest auto
maker, says it expects the state "to meet all of its contractual obligations" while Ford has requested a meeting
with the state's governor.
Ecuador Bank Forced to Shut Its Doors
Banco del Progreso, Ecuador's second-largest bank, closed its doors yesterday because it very likely
wouldn't have had enough money to meet its obligations. It was the eighth of Ecuador's 39 banks to turn
away depositors in the past four months amid fears the South American nation's financial system is close to
collapse. There were no further details about the future of the bank, but the financial institution's president did
try to calm his customers by assuring them that the bank's deposits would be guaranteed by the state.
Peru Cites Secret Deals to Buy Debt
Peru says that between July 1994 and the end of 1996 it bought back debt owed to the former Soviet Union
valued at $4.6 billion, and that many of the deals were made in secret. The government disclosed that it took
such a route to allow the nation to restructure its debt and return to the good graces of international lenders.
Meanwhile, Peru also said it bought back debt restructured under its Brady Plan or issued new debt that
netted it $3.49 billion.
