View / Iditarod Trail Race:Sled-Race Leader Gets Gourmet Mush
WEEKEND JOURNAL
View / Iditarod Trail Race: Sled-Race Leader Gets Gourmet Mush
By Susan G. Hauser
1,463 words
19 March 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
W15C
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Anvik, Alaska -- After racing 654 miles over five days and nights on the Iditarod Trail, frontrunner Doug
Swingley turned his team of 14 sled dogs onto the frozen Yukon River probably at the same time Chef Alex
Perez put the saffron fumet on to heat. A pan of vichyssoise went on the camp stove's second burner when
the clanging of the church bell signaled that Mr. Swingley's galloping dogs were in sight and about to enter
the village. Richard Leslie, surely the only man on the Yukon in a tuxedo, lighted the candles in the silver
candelabra and carefully stacked 3,500 crisp dollar bills next to the single place setting on the white linen
tablecloth.
The freshly minted bills would be Mr. Swingley's "after-dinner mint," part of the prize awarded by the Regal
Alaskan Hotel of Anchorage to the first musher to reach the Yukon River in the annual Iditarod Trail Sled Dog
Race. But before serving the mint, Mr. Leslie, the hotel's food and beverage director, would place before the
exhausted musher a seven-course gourmet meal, from lobster ravioli as an appetizer to Chocolate Delight as
dessert, with a bottle of fine wine for every course but the sorbet.
Since 1987 the Regal has flown its executive chef and its maitre d', along with about 10 heavily insulated
boxes of food and cookware, not to mention an arrangement of fresh flowers, into Alaska's frozen interior to
prepare and deliver this unique prize. There are numerous other prizes for mushers who are the first to reach
certain checkpoints along the 1,161-mile, Anchorage-to-Nome Iditarod Trail -- a gold watch here, a pile of
gold nuggets there -- but the First Musher to the Yukon River Award is the only edible one.
Last Friday in the town hall at Anvik, an Athabascan Indian village of 82, Mr. Perez and Mr. Leslie had been
preparing for the musher's arrival since early morning. From all reports, the first dogsled was due to cross the
Yukon and pull up to the hall around noon, certainly a civilized hour to enjoy a seven-course meal. In past
years the award has been dished up at all hours of the day or night.
Except for the few people manning the checkpoint and the three volunteer veterinarians waiting to give the
sled dogs their checkups, no curious connoisseurs had yet braved the sunny but cold (five degrees below
zero) weather to see what was cooking. Mr. Perez had set out his cutting board and knives and was tinkering
with the camp stove, which, for a heart-stopping few moments, refused to work. His ingredients, partially
prepared beforehand, sat in a cooler on the front porch. Mr. Leslie, meanwhile, had stuck bottles of
Chardonnay and Fume Blanc in a snowbank next to the front door. "It's the world's largest walk-in
refrigerator," he said, hurrying back to the warmth of the building. Before the musher's arrival, he opened
bottles of Pinot Noir, Merlot, Cabernet Sauvignon and Petite Sirah to allow them to breathe.
The bottles cost $60 to $90 each, but were not at the top of the hotel's price list. "We didn't want to be too
pretentious," explained Mr. Leslie. "This is the Iditarod, after all." Still, he predicted that not a drop would be
drunk -- not by the musher, anyway. In the award's past 12 years, the winner of the Yukon dinner, focused on
keeping a competitive edge over the 50 or so mushers hot on his or her heels, has declined the wine.
However, the meal is always re-awarded at the Anchorage hotel on a later date, when the musher can
actually relax and enjoy it.
Mr. Perez, head chef at the Regal for less than four months, is new to the hotel's tradition of serving gourmet
meals in the middle of nowhere. But with excitement mounting in anticipation of the lead musher's arrival, and
a sudden flood of villagers and journalists filling the hall's large room, he stayed calm as he cooked in a
corner, remarking, "I just do what I do. Some people race dogs, I do food."
The lobster ravioli was ready to be cooked when Mr. Swingley halted his dogs and anchored the sled in the
snow. But it was his dogs' stomachs he tended to first. Using supplies stored at the checkpoint, he spread out
straw for their beds, prepared a hot stew and ladled it into individual bowls. His appetite whetted, he went to
collect his own gourmet meal.

--- Page 239 ---
Page 239 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
There had been some concern that Mr. Swingley, a Montanan who in 1995 was the first Iditarod winner from
the lower 48, would not stop for his dinner. "Last time I took a piece of cheesecake and headed down the
trail," he recalled. The chef was left with an uneaten meal while Mr. Swingley chalked up a record time of nine
days, two hours and 42 minutes.
This time he signaled his willingness to stay by stripping the top of his arctic garb down to his long underwear,
yanking a baseball cap onto his head and plopping down at the table. He spread the purple linen napkin in his
lap and looked expectantly at his waiter. Mr. Leslie, whose tuxedo had been elevated to Alaska Formal by the
addition of a fox-fur hat with a bushy tail, offered the windburned, bewhiskered musher a glass of wine. Mr.
Swingley surveyed the six open bottles and answered, "I'll trade it for a big Coke."
As he chugged his Coke, he heard a description of the first course: ravioli stuffed with lobster, sauteed sweet
peppers, onions and fresh herbs in a saffron fumet. A reporter asked, "Doug, how many times have you had
saffron fumet?" He answered, "I don't even know what saffron fumet is." But he slurped the
white-wine-and-fish-stock sauce up anyway, down to the last drop, then exclaimed, "That's better than my
cooking!"
Mr. Perez beamed at the compliment as he put the roasted garlic-shrimp spread on toast points and ladled up
the vichyssoise, made with Yukon gold potatoes. The next course, served with another bottle of Coke on the
side, was seared Alaskan scallops on a bed of tomato risotto. Mr. Swingley, whose table manners were
impeccable, patted his lips with his napkin, then rubbed his eyes with his fists. This was the first time he had
been able to rest in 11 hours.
Next came a magenta-colored sorbet of Alaskan lingonberries, blueberries and salmonberries. He took a bite,
smiled and said knowingly, "This is to cleanse my palate." The sorbet was followed by a veal chop with a
ragout of root vegetables. Mr. Perez explained to the musher that the high-carbohydrate turnips, parsnips,
carrots, onions and beets were for carbo loading, just like for a marathon.
"Is it going to help you out?" Mr. Perez asked hopefully.
"It's definitely going to help me out," replied Mr. Swingley.
He had no sooner made that claim than his closest competitor, three-time Iditarod winner Martin Buser,
swooped into Anvik, fed his dogs and showed up hungry at the dinner table. "So you saved some dessert for
me," exclaimed Mr. Buser, pointing cheerfully at the stack of dollar bills.
The ever-resourceful Mr. Perez put the water on to boil again for Mr. Buser's ravioli. He had brought enough
food for four mushers, just in case. Mr. Swingley, who had just finished his fifth course, graciously waited for
Mr. Buser, a Swiss-born Alaskan, to catch up. Then the two friendly rivals enjoyed their seasonal greens with
chevre and roasted-red-pepper vinaigrette.
"This is very, very, very good," said Mr. Buser to the very, very, very happy chef.
The two mushers licked every plate clean, especially the Chocolate Delight, circles of chocolate cake dipped
in Grand Marnier orange sauce and layered with chocolate mousse. Mr. Perez painstakingly decorated each
dessert with lines of Kahlua chocolate syrup and cheery circles of raspberry and mango coulis.
Then Mr. Perez turned off the camp stove, Mr. Leslie cleared the plates, and the contented mushers retired
for a nap, part of a mandatory eight-hour rest along the Yukon. Surely visions of fumet and coulis danced
through their heads before once again they hit the Iditarod Trail and steered their sleds for Nome.
---
Departing Anvik one hour and 45 minutes before his rival, Mr. Swingley developed a commanding lead, and
on Wednesday crossed the finish line in Nome for a time of nine days, 14 hours and 31 minutes.

Dollar Firms Against Yen as CommentsBy Japanese Officials Lift U.S. Currency

Dollar Firms Against Yen as Comments By Japanese Officials Lift U.S. Currency
By Michael Casey
Dow Jones Newswires
665 words
18 March 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C18
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- The dollar looked a little healthier against the yen after Japanese officials indicated their
discomfort with the gains the yen has made this month.
Against the euro, the U.S. currency was mostly flat on the day. However, the fact that the European currency
spent much of the 24-hour period above $1.1000 led some to suggest it has regained a tentative upward bias
against the dollar.
Mike Faust, currency analyst and portfolio manager at Bailard Biehl & Kaiser in San Mateo, Calif., said the
market may interpret the recent political shakeups in Europe as positive for the euro in the long term, despite
the uncertainty they create.
The resignation last week of German Finance Minister Oskar Lafontaine, followed by the resignation of the
entire executive board of the European Commission earlier this week, may spell a welcome "end to the
bureaucratic vision of Euroland," Mr. Faust said.
"It has the potential to bring back some of the optimism for the euro that people had last year, although it's
still not clear how durable or long-lasting that will be," he said.
Earlier, at the opening of Tokyo trading, the dollar was rescued from lows near 117.00 yen by Huruhiko
Kuroda, head of the international bureau at Japan's Ministry of Finance, who said recent gains in the yen had
been "a little excessive."
Helped by reports of similar yen remarks from other Japanese officials, the dollar embarked on an unbroken
rally to a high of 119.14 yen near midday in London. At that point, however, profit-takers entered the market
to bring it down to about 118.00 yen in New York.
Many traders expect this late correction to offer dollar buyers a bargain-hunting opportunity. Dollar bulls can
safely assume Japanese officialdom is behind them, these traders say.
Late in New York Wednesday, the dollar was trading at 118.15 yen, up from 117.78 Tuesday in New York.
The euro was trading at $1.0995, up from $1.0993. Sterling was trading at $1.6300, up from $1.6292. In
Tokyo at midday Thursday, the dollar was trading at 118.55 yen, and the euro was trading at $1.1009.
Wednesday, back in New York, sterling recovered from a London low of $1.6245. Some traders said it may
have gotten a boost from a late rally in oil prices. However, others noted that the pound hardly moved against
the euro.
Meanwhile, mixed signals from oil and gold prices caused volatility among selected commodity currencies.
The April crude contract closed on the New York Mercantile Exchange Wednesday above $15 per barrel for
the first time since Oct. 30 after data showed a decline in gasoline and distillate inventories. The
crude-futures rally inspired a late surge in oil-linked currencies like the Mexican peso and the Norwegian
krone.
In late New York trading, the euro was trading at 8.5300 kroner, well below its level of 8.5495 kroner late in
Oslo Wednesday and from 8.5425 kroner late in Europe Tuesday. The euro is the favored benchmark for
Scandinavian currencies.
The peso saw a similar pattern against the dollar. In Mexico City, the U.S. currency ended at 9.6610 pesos,
its lowest level since Aug. 25, and down from 9.6800 pesos Tuesday. The peso initially opened lower against

the dollar as local interest rates softened, but the oil price rise and a local stock-market rally helped it regain

Emerging-Markets Investors Worry About EffectsOn Stocks Should China Devalue Its Currency
World Stock Markets
Emerging-Markets Investors Worry About Effects On Stocks Should China Devalue Its Currency
By Wall Street Journal staff reporters Suzanne McGee in London, and Craig S. Smith in Shanghai
2,267 words
17 March 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Global markets are rejoicing: the Dow Jones Industrial Average is flirting with 10000, investors returning to
Japan in droves have propelled Tokyo's Nikkei index to seven-month highs, and even some long-stagnant
emerging markets have shown signs of reviving.
So why are investors from Hong Kong to Helsinki once again brooding about the possibility of China spoiling
the party?
"At the moment, the market is trying to decide if the rest of the world is going to be able to pick up its pace of
recovery before the U.S. economy finally slows down," says Mark Howdle, European equity strategist at
Salomon Smith Barney in London. "Anything that threatens that scenario is a problem for markets
everywhere. If China were to devalue its currency, that would be a big enough factor to tip the balance toward
another slowdown in the world economy."
At the beginning of the year, analysts had other things to fret over. Topping the list was the prospect and later
the reality of Brazilian deflation. Then came worries that U.S. policy makers might raise interest rates to fend
off inflation. Had that happened, investors would have had to bid farewell to hopes the strong U.S. economy
would extend a helping hand to Asia and Latin America.
But in the past few weeks, fears of higher U.S. interest rates have waned and several struggling Asian
economies have shown signs of new life. In Thailand, the passage of a new bankruptcy law sent Bangkok's
stock market soaring 4.4% Monday. In South Korea, wholesale and retail sales rose 2.8% in January over
year-earlier levels.
With few other reasons to fret, analysts are again free to ponder the risks that China will try to solve its
economic problems through devaluation.
"China is a wild card for markets," says Dale Griffin, senior portfolio manager for AIM Investment
Management in Houston. "The fear factor comes and goes, depending on the news that's come out over the
last 24 hours."
China's central bank governor, Dai Xianglong, last week vehemently denied that policy makers are
pondering devaluation of the yuan. Premier Zhu Rongji again this week repeated his pledge not to devalue.
But over the past two years, emerging-markets investors have learned to expect the unexpected.
"At the very least, just the prospect of something happening on this front means a lot of volatility, especially in
Asia, and a bumpy ride up," says Miren Etcheverry, head of global equities at John Hancock Funds in Boston.
"And there are some new concerns out there."
Another question mark is the yen, says Donald Hanna, market economist at Goldman, Sachs & Co. in Hong
Kong. The Japanese currency has fallen to about 118 yen to the dollar from 114 yen a month ago, hurting
Japan's ability to import from China and other Asian countries. With Chinese exports already showing signs of
stalling -- they fell nearly 11% in January over year-earlier levels -- that's bad news. If the yen falls through
the 140 level, that would be ominous, Mr. Hanna believes.
"Trying to guess when and whether China will devalue is directly linked to their perceptions of when the
downward export trend threatens their assumptions on trade flows and on their reserves," says Tim Love,
emerging-markets strategist at Societe Generale in London.
Of course, China, unlike Thailand or Indonesia, can't be forced into devaluation through a speculative attack
on its currency, because the yuan still isn't fully convertible. And it has some $150 billion of foreign reserves
at its disposal to defend the yuan.

--- Page 243 ---
Page 243 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But domestic pressures remain. Chinese economists and exporters as well as some state factory managers
increasingly view devaluation as a solution to the export problem. Among the most vocal: steelcompany
executives who are struggling to compete with cheaper South Korean steel, and state shipbuilding managers
who can't compete against shipbuilders based in Asian countries and doing business in currencies like the
South Korean won that have already been devalued.
China also is battling internal economic problems. Early this month, Premier Zhu Rongji told the Chinese
legislature that growth will fall to 7% from last year's 7.8%.
"No one knows the extent of bad loans at the banks and state-owned enterprises," says Ayez Ebrahim, chief
investment officer of Indocam Hong Kong, an asset-management firm.
Confidence remains a problem: China's decision in January to declare Guangdong International Trust &
Investment Co. bankrupt, with much of its $4.3 billion of debt owed to foreign investors, has had an unnerving
effect.
But analysts believe Chinese policy makers are aware that devaluation may actually worsen the country's
dilemma by triggering a series of competitive devaluations throughout Asia just as the region's economic
health is improving.
"They're sensible enough to know that that would just knock them right back to square one," says Mr.
Ebrahim. Moreover, because other regional currencies have risen in the past year, the Chinese yuan appears
less overvalued than it did 12 months ago.
Moreover, devaluation now would wipe out the reserves of good will accumulated over the past 18 months as
countries like the U.S. have praised China's forbearance. Some say it might even hamper the country's
efforts to become a full member of the World Trade Organization.
There are also some domestic reasons not to move. While some Chinese exporters would benefit from
devaluation, those importing raw materials or components would be hurt, and the economy would suffer from
higher prices for oil and other commodities. A devaluation -- particularly after such persistent pledges not to
actcould shake confidence domestically at a critical time. Social strains are high, and the last thing the
country's leadership needs is political unrest.
That's why Asian observers "now are largely more comfortable with the belief that China won't devalue," says
Mr. Ebrahim.
But investors elsewhere aren't as sanguine. Ronnie Armist, London-based chief investment officer of
Lombard Odier, says his firm's Hong Kong office "is not as adamant as 12 months ago that devaluation is out
of the question." That is why he is ready to sell a range of Asian investments on any sign of imminent
devaluation.
Meanwhile, markets continue to price in the risk of devaluation. The so-called red chips, shares of Chinese
companies traded on the Hong Kong Stock Exchange, are struggling. Mr. Ebrahim still owns stock in
Huaneng Power and Guangdong Kelon, an electrical appliance manufacturer. Both trade at about five times
earnings and are down 36% and 26%, respectively, from their January highs. AIM Investment Management's
Mr. Griffin, who proclaims himself "a big believer" in Asian growth, clings to shares in some red-chip road
companies, Shenzhen Expressway (down 22% so far this year) and Zhejiang Expressway (down 22%).
But Mr. Griffin is ready to admit the possibility of even a 10% devalution of the yuan remains, and is enough
to further unsettle still-jittery markets. "It's a nervous time," he says.
Other Market Activity
In Asia, several markets passed significant milestones on the way higher. Japan's Nikkei 225 index marched
to the highest level seen in more than seven months, topping the strategic 16000 level, while in Hong Kong,
the Hang Seng index posted a modest gain to finish at an 11-month high. In Australia, the All Ordinaries
benchmark rose for the fifth day running to hit a record 2992.20 points.
In Latin America, markets also continued to climb sharply. But European stock markets ended the day little
changed to only slightly higher, despite political upheaval at the European Commission.
Overall, the Dow Jones World Stock Index rose 1.68 points or 0.80%, to 211.77.
In trading in Asia Wednesday, Tokyo closed the morning session down 0.6%. South Korea was up 0.8% in
late-morning dealings, and Hong Kong had gained 0.2% in early trading.
In TOKYO yesterday, the Nikkei index jumped 293.22 points, or 1.9%, to 16072.82 points, with volume again
topping a billion shares. Traders described the move as driven by liquidity as foreign investors redirect assets

--- Page 244 ---
Page 244 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
into Japanese stocks after a long absence, encouraged by a spate of corporate restructuring
announcements. Japan Airlines said after the market closed Tuesday it would cut its payroll 10% within the
next three years and simultaneously trim debt. Its stock jumped 11% to 356 yen before the formal
announcement. Fuji Bank gained 4% to 655 yen after the bank spelled out details of its plan to unwind cross
shareholdings, cut costs and restructure. Boosting the market further, the Bank of Japan described the
country's economy as appearing "to have stopped deteriorating."
In HONG KONG, the Hang Seng index climbed 0.7% to 10911.25, its highest level since April. But blue-chip
stocks were mixed during the trading day, causing some concern that the rally may be losing momentum.
HSBC Holdings rose 0.4% to HK$238. Hutchison Whampoa climbed 1.2% to HK$63.25 amid continued
speculation it will make a large telecommunications investment. Cheung Kong (Holdings) rose 0.4% to
HK$60.
In SYDNEY, the All Ordinaries index climbed 0.1% to 2992.2 points, but failed to break through the
psychologically important 3000 level. Profit-taking hampered the advance late in the day, with Commonwealth
Bank of Australia bearing the brunt of the selling. Its shares fell 3.3% to A$25.78. Shares of the Australian
Stock Exchange, the company that runs the Sydney market, climbed 4% to A$15.80. Gold stocks slid,
however, in response to a slide in the price of the metal itself. Normandy Mining slipped 4.2% to A$1.37.
In SEOUL, the KOSPI index jumped 1.7% to 601.83, its highest level in nearly eight weeks. The move came
in the wake of Wall Street's recent gains, and signs of stability in the level of the yen against the dollar.
Wall Street's gains, however tentative, also buoyed shares in Manila, which rose 2.1%. But in KUALA
LUMPUR, stocks bucked the trend as Moody's Investors Service retained a negative outlook on the country's
debt rating in its annual credit report on Malaysia. The Kuala Lumpur Composite Index fell 1.3% to 511.90.
In SAO PAULO, the Bovespa index added a further 2.4% advance to Monday's 8.8% gain. The index rose
244 points to end the day at 10658, its highest level since August. The move was attributed to a return to the
market by foreign investors, and to the apparent calm in the foreign-exchange market. The Brazilian real
ended the day at 1.87 to the dollar, compared with 1.855 reals to the dollar late Monday. Telebras receipts
rose 1.4% to 1.90 reals yesterday. But shares of LightPar, an electric energy-asset holding group, were
suspended from trading yesterday after the Brazilian bourse sought a clarification of details surrounding the
company's operations. Monday, shares of LightPar more than doubled amid speculation that the company will
begin generating revenue from a data-transmission business.
In Argentina, the Merval index rose 0.9% to 3.80, while Mexico's IPC index climbed 2.5% to 4890.39.
In Europe, stock markets remained unruffled by the mass departure of European Commission leaders.
In FRANKFURT, the Xetra DAX index rose 1% to 5090.93. Hoechst fell 2.1% to 41.50 euros amid fears that a
planned merger of some of its operations with French company Rhone Poulenc might put downward pressure
on the stock price. Bayer rose 2% to 35.50 euros after the company said it expects net income to rise 7% this
year.
In PARIS, the CAC-40 index remained almost unchanged, up 1.23 points at 4186.35 points. Banque
Nationale de Paris rose 2.6% to 80 euros, Societe Generale climbed 4.3% to 171 euros and Paribas edged
up 0.8% ahead of last night's regulatory approval of BNP's $37 billion bid for the other two banks. French
stock market regulator Conseil des Marches ruled the tender offer admissible.
Renault tumbled 6.5% to 33.10 euros ahead of an anticipated announcement that the French auto maker will
take a stake in Nissan, a fall blamed on selling by U.S. institutions.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 slid 4.9 points to 6201.9. Colt Telecom was the best-performing blue chip,
climbing 6% to 995.5 pence, as buyers returned to the stock following Monday's sell-off in
telecommunications shares.

Business World:RJR Update: Who Was at the Gate?
Business World: RJR Update: Who Was at the Gate?
By Holman W. Jenkins Jr.
1,162 words
17 March 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A27
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
To say that Wall Street is motivated by money is to add little to the sum of human understanding. What was
remarkable about RJR Nabisco was how much effort went into the search for a pot of gold, and how little was
found. Hence the announcement last week dismembering the company 10 years after its famous LBO
inspired the all-time business potboiler, "Barbarians at the Gate."
All the Brighties of Wall Street's Grand Canyon took their turns with the company, before it finally ended up in
the hands of buyout king Henry Kravis and his partners at KKR. The helpful maidens of Shearson, Salomon,
Drexel, Morgan Stanley and Wasserstein Perella kept the luckless prospectors fed and watered with
encouragement at every step. Even Saint Warren of Omaha whispered admiration of a business with fat profit
margins where the customers are addicted and fanatically brand-loyal.
But not even Mr. Buffett explained how the share price could be made to reflect these facts.
Whether or not the cigarette maker was fat and slovenly was never the issue. Wall Street's peanut gallery
hadn't bought the notion that a cigarette company wasn't a terminally risky venture. The peanut gallery knows
whereof from sharks and seeing lawyers and health regulators circling, it refused to award RJR the same
price-earnings multiple as companies selling, say, cat food.
The first attempt to convince them otherwise came when RJ Reynolds moved heavily into food, hoping to
achieve a food multiple instead of a tobacco multiple. The Nabisco merger brought aboard Mr. Johnson, who
found good uses for the gusher of tobacco money (lots of corporate jets, putting his golfing pals on the
payroll), but who also kicked Procter & Gamble's derriere in a cookie war, launched the marketing phenom
known as Joe Camel, and spent $400 million trying to transform the industry's image with a smokeless
cigarette.
Alas, he was a frustrated man: "We get no recognition for the food business. I earn a dollar, and Procter &
Gamble earns a dollar. The market says my dollar is worth $8 and the one at P&G is worth $13."
All along shareholders had disdained the food gambit, practically pleading with him to use the tobacco gusher
to buy them out of their shares instead. They finally got what they wanted, but only because the sharpies on
Wall Street, led in the first place by Mr. Johnson, believed they still knew better. As famously told in the book,
he launched a management buyout offer but was outbid by Mr. Kravis. Mr. Johnson later got the ceremonial
bad press always due previous management in any LBO. But in kicking off the bidding war that led Mr. Kravis
to pay $108 a share for a company that had previously been selling for $70, he did his shareholders a
monumental service.
The 1988 LBO has somehow been accorded a high place in the annals of greed. In reality Mr. Kravis, for one
shining moment, achieved what everyone else had failed to do: valuing tobacco like a normal business. Of
course, he did it with his own money.
The $22 billion debt incurred to buy the company was supposed to soak up the excess cash and provide
discipline, forcing RJR to become more efficient. But Mr. Kravis hadn't counted on another claimant for the
excess cash: the lawyers and politicians. Bill Clinton was eyeing a giant cigarette tax to fund Hillarycare; the
pirates of the plaintiffs' bar were launching a class action on behalf of everyone who had ever smoked; in
darkest Mississippi an attorney general and an asbestos plutocrat were cooking up a theory of how tobacco
could become a major revenue source for state Medicaid spending.
Any money that was freed up by cutting costs or growing the business would land in their pockets, not Mr.
Kravis's.
There was a kind of frantic hopelessness to the LBO drama after this point. Mr. Kravis put Lou Gerstner in
charge, who would dump the smokeless cigarette project, sell the RJR air force, fight a price war with Philip
Morris and upgrade the Winston, Salem and Camel brands. But still the public insisted on seeing the cigarette

--- Page 247 ---
Page 247 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
company as a cigarette company, whose sprawling 40% profit margins were not tempting enough to offset
the political cloud over its shares. As Mr. Gerstner confided to Business Week, "I pride myself on being a
strategist, and I don't like telling a reporter I don't know what to do."
But he did know what to do, immediately decamping to lead the turnaround at IBM, whose computers are not
alleged to cause cancer.
Since then, the RJR story has been a story about a fight over a lifeboat. Whereas the smarty-pants of Wall
Street once had loaded up the company with cookies and foods in hopes of dispelling the tobacco odor from
its share price, now they battle over how to unload the cookies and food so these businesses will no longer
be weighed down by tobacco. This is where Messrs. Icahn and Lebow, two of the market's less-pedigreed
pirates, have lately entered the tale. Result: last week's announcement finally splitting up the conglomerate.
The Japanese government, as majority owner of Japan Tobacco, agreed to take RJR's international cigarette
operations off its hands, and at an honest price. In this country, we do things differently. Based on a wan
hope, the company will spin off domestic tobacco as a freestanding unit and pray the lawyers and their
political bagmen will be satisfied to feast on its remains and leave Nabisco alone. Here, take my right arm but
please leave me the other one.
The author of this surrender is RJR's latest CEO, Steven Goldstone, a lawyer by profession. The business
idealists like Mr. Johnson and Mr. Kravis have headed for the hills. At last tally, about $1.70 per pack is now
being sent to enrich lawyers and subsidize various levels of government, and even Mr. Clinton has talked
about launching a federal lawsuit to claim a bigger share than he's already getting. In the coming weeks, the
Supreme Court may well uphold the FDA's claim to regulate tobacco as a drug.
We're left only with a renewed appreciation of the investing public, which foresaw this denouement even as
the best minds of Wall Street entertained themselves with fantasies about unlocking the "hidden value" of the
cigarette business. In some sense, the broad investing public is also the political culture, and twice before in
this century, powerful prohibitionist movements against cigarettes have come out of the heartland, far from
the sophisticated precincts of Wall Street.
Certainly Wall Street's habitues were late to get the word this time. If anything the book did them an injustice.
They were the suckers at the gate.

Page 248 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A Special Summary and ForecastOf Federal and State TaxDevelopments
Tax Report
A Special Summary and Forecast Of Federal and State Tax Developments
By Karen Hube
927 words
3 March 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
MILLIONAIRES want to use charge cards to pay their taxes, too.
A new IRS program that lets taxpayers charge taxes is unexpectedly attracting some super-wealthy
individuals. At least seven taxpayers have asked to charge between $1 million and $9 million in taxes on
American Express or MasterCard, says U.S. Audiotex, San Ramon, Calif., which is processing credit-card tax
payments. At least 20 taxpayers have asked to charge more than $100,000, the firm says. The large
payments haven't been processed yet.
"People are attracted to the rewards programs" the cards offer, says Robert McKinley, president of
CardWeb.com Inc., Frederick, Md. Even after paying a fee as high as 2.5% to charge taxes ($25,000 per $1
million), in some cases the rewards can pay off: For example, a $4 million charge could rack up enough
points "to buy 40 friends first-class round-trip airline tickets to London," Mr. McKinley says. The tickets' value:
about $400,000.
The biggest payment processed so far is about $30,000, according to Audiotex.
THE IRS WANTS TO KNOW how long it really takes you to prepare your tax forms.
Official estimates, which by law must be printed on each form or its instructions, are "a terrible reflection of
how long it really takes," an IRS spokesman says. For example, according to official IRS estimates, it will take
taxpayers an average of 11 hours and 34 minutes to fill out a form 1040 this year, two hours longer than last
year. That includes 40 minutes just to copy and mail the form. But, an IRS spokesman says, "unless you're
copying it by hand, I don't think it's going to take you that long."
The problem is that the IRS formula assumes each new line added to a form adds to taxpayer time, even if it
simplifies the process. To come up with better estimates, the IRS has launched a multiyear, multimillion-dollar
project to devise a new formula. Under the Paperwork Reduction Act, government agencies must measure
the burden they place on citizens.
SUPPORT SWELLS in Washington for liberalizing retirement-savings rules.
Senate Finance Committee Chairman William V. Roth plans to introduce a bill, possibly this week, that
includes sweeping changes in retirement savings plans that he proposed in January. The bill would eliminate
income restrictions on contributing to tax-deductible IRAs and Roth IRAs, raise the income limits for Roth IRA
conversions to $1 million from $100,000, and increase maximum contributions for IRAs and 401(k), 403(b),
457 and SIMPLE plans. It also would permit investors over age 50 to make 50% higher contributions and
allow after-tax contributions to 401(k) and 403(b) plans, letting earnings grow tax free.
While some initiatives would pinch revenue, "it's a trade-off," says Rande Spiegelman, a manager at KPMG,
San Francisco. "People will more easily take diminished Social Security benefits if they have more chances to
save." Bipartisan support is gathering around many moves to liberalize retirement savings, particularly by
raising the $2,000 IRA contribution limit.
If the limit had been indexed for inflation, it would be about $5,350 today.
TAX REFUNDS continue to flood taxpayer bank accounts at a more rapid pace than last year, the IRS says.
As of Feb. 26, the IRS had mailed $43.5 billion in refunds, up 18.1% from $36.8 billion in the same period last
year. About 25.2 million people have been sent refunds so far, up 2.3% from 24.6 million.
IT COULD BE WORSE: A Chinese vice premier reminded taxpayers last month that tax evasion can result in
the death penalty. "It's to stem widespread tax evasion," says Karen Kole, a U.S. tax professor living in

--- Page 249 ---
Page 249 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Shanghai. So, she adds, when U.S. taxpayers face IRS penalties, "they can say, `Maybe things aren't so bad
after all.'"
AUDIT-PHOBIA: Some taxpayers still worry that filing electronically will give the IRS easier access to their
data and therefore boost chances of being audited. But the IRS says that's a myth: Information on all tax
returns gets computerized, so it makes no difference how it's filed.
NO GOLD STARS were given the IRS in an audit of fiscal 1998 financial statements.
Congressional investigators found the agency is "plagued" with management deficiencies that have resulted
in increased taxpayer burdens, at least $17 million in fraudulent refunds and inadequate security controls for
cash, checks and sensitive data received from taxpayers. While the IRS did a good job collecting $1.8 trillion
in taxes, it "cannot do many of the basic accounting and record-keeping tasks" necessary to properly
administer its $8.1 billion budget, says Gregory D. Kutz, GAO associate director.
To blame are the IRS's old computer systems and chronic weaknesses in basic administration, says Mr. Kutz.
Donna H. Cunninghame, the IRS's chief financial officer, says she is "deeply disappointed that we failed to
meet our obligations" and is committed to making improvements.
Among items recorded in IRS books but not found: a Chevy Blazer and a "$300,000" laser printer.
BRIEFS: A government witness-protection program won't hide you from the IRS, according to IRS guidelines.
. . . Beware of tax form 8606 for Roth IRAs: A New York-area accountant says it's so complex that "if entries
look wrong they're probably right, and if they look right they're probably wrong."

Vancouver Stock Exchange Aims for ComebackBut Must Battle a Poor Image and Performance
World Stock Markets
Vancouver Stock Exchange Aims for Comeback But Must Battle a Poor Image and Performance
By Mark Heinzl
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
2,393 words
1 March 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
VANCOUVER, British Columbia -- The worst-performing North American stock exchange last year is trying to
make a comeback, but it is finding the struggle tough.
While shares on other U.S. and Canadian exchanges have soared in recent years, the Vancouver Stock
Exchange index has sunk to less than half of its level when it was first compiled-in 1982. Last year, the VSE
index tumbled 36%, while the Toronto Stock Exchange 300 index slipped just 3.2%, and the Standard &
Poor's 500-stock index chugged ahead 27%.
Wilting commodity prices have clobbered shares of the hundreds of tiny mineral and oil-exploration
companies that dominate the VSE. And a string of scandals in Canada's mining industry in recent years,
including the Bre-X Minerals Ltd. gold fraud, sideswiped the VSE's efforts to clean up its shady reputation.
"When the mud hit the fan, a lot of it stuck to us," even though Bre-X and the other scandals had nothing to
do with the VSE, complains Brent Wolverton, president of Wolverton Securities Ltd., a small brokerage
concern here. The stock of BreX, whose supposed big gold find in Indonesia was exposed as an elaborate
fraud in early 1997, was listed on other exchanges.
VSE trading volumes in the past couple of years have fallen so low that some local brokers are giving up on
the exchange. "Microsoft trades more in an hour than the entire VSE does in a month," laments one broker.
"I'm trying to wean myself off the VSE." Now, he focuses on selling stocks listed on other, bigger exchanges.
And for institutional investors, the low liquidity is a deterrent; average daily trading value on the VSE last year
was about 15 million Canadian dollars (US$9.95 million), compared with nearly C$2 billion on the Toronto
Stock Exchange.
But the VSE is fighting back on two fronts. First, the exchange has won applause for improving stock-trading
surveillance and fighting fraud. At the same time, it is trying to attract more listings, especially for
high-technology companies, and market itself as a "pre-Nasdaq" exchange for tiny start-ups. In the past year
or so, the exchange's marketing officials have traveled to the U.S., six European cities, Mexico, and South
America to try to drum up interest.
Experts say advances such as the VSE's fully automated trading system, developed earlier this decade to
monitor all stock bids and trades, have helped cut fraud. Also, the VSE in recent years has barred several
"unacceptable" individuals from being directors or employees of any VSE-listed company. "The instances of
significant stock manipulation over the past five years have been minimal," says Gerry Halischuk, an
enforcement official with the British Columbia Securities Commission.
Joseph Groia, a Toronto lawyer and former Ontario Securities Commission prosecutor, adds, "There's no
question that Vancouver has made significant progress in trying to clean up their market." The exchange has
become "a lot more aggressive" in disciplining rulebreakers and keeping unwanted people and companies
from the exchange, he adds.
But the exchange's old reputation has been hard to shake. "For too long a time, (the VSE) earned and
deserved the scam-capital crown," says Adrian du Plessis, a longtime private investigator of VSE stock
schemes and a former British Columbia regulatory official. Senior VSE officials, including President Michael
Johnson, declined to be interviewed for this article.
In previous years, a long list of dubious, heavily promoted ventures and outright frauds made the VSE
infamous. In a high-profile fraud in the 1980s, for instance, two Vancouver stock promoters were convicted of
colluding with a Texas mutual-fund manager to manipulate shares of several VSE-listed mining companies.

--- Page 251 ---
Page 251 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Such incidents have made investors wary, as Michael Pezim discovered on a recent trip to New York. Mr.
Pezim, chief executive officer of MedEra Life Sciences Corp. here, tried to persuade U.S. financiers to invest
in his company, which he is transforming from a mineral-exploration concern to a medical software and
onlineinformation company.
"I went there with a stock under C$5, on the VSE, in the mineral-exploration business, and it was not a warm
reception," Mr. Pezim admits, noting that the potential investors are still deciding.
It is a big change from the VSE's brief heyday before the Bre-X scandal, when everybody in town seemed to
have a hot mineral property to sell, Mr. Pezim says. The executive, a former surgeon, recalls that one
anesthesiologist would even pitch deals over the operating table.
Besides trying to overcome its past, the VSE is looking to its 150 or so small high-tech start-ups for success
stories. Cathy Munn, president of a 20-employee Vancouver-based e-mail management concern called
Electric Mail Co., says she listed her company on the VSE early last year partly because of its "active
surveillance department." Electric Mail's sales last year were just C$1 million.
Though Electric Mail lost money last year and expects losses to continue, Ms. Munn is disappointed that the
listing hasn't produced even a touch of Internet fever. Indeed, the company's stock has fallen to 70 Canadian
cents recently from the initial public offering price of C$1.25 early last year. "Vis-a-vis what's happening to the
Internet stocks in the U.S., it's a little frustrating," Ms. Munn sighs.
Despite its efforts, the VSE has failed to attract the high-tech and foreign companies it wants. The number of
nonresource companies on the exchange actually fell to 444, or 32% of total listings, last year from 501, or
34%, in 1996. Foreign-company listing dropped to 345 from 377.
The VSE notes that 20 technology companies have "graduated" to larger exchanges since 1996. But few of
those graduates are well known. Silent Witness Enterprises Ltd., a British Columbia maker of
video-surveillance equipment, was one of the few solid performers on the VSE last year. The company
switched to a Toronto Stock Exchange listing last summer and is seeking a Nasdaq Stock Market listing as
well this year. Novadigm Inc., a Mahwah, N.J., management software concern, switched to Nasdaq from the
VSE in 1996.
Despite the problems, brokers, stock promoters and entrepreneurs who toil in this picturesque city, situated
between snow-capped mountains and the sea, keep living on hope. At the Jolly Taxpayer, a mining-industry
hangout, graying geologists sipping midday beers eagerly discuss mineral properties that face tremendous
odds against ever being mined.
Publisher George Cross says his mining-industry newsletter here has been publishing for 50 years because
"there's always someone" willing to take a flier on a hot mining stock. After all, there are occasional flurries of
excitement on the exchange; shares of Vancouver-based Argentina Gold Corp., for example, shot up from
pennies to above C$5 last year as the company reported on a gold find in South America. The company is
hoping to attract another takeover offer, after shareholders recently rejected a C$5-a-share offer from Toronto
gold miner Barrick Gold Corp.
But Mr. Cross has doubts about the VSE's growth strategy. Noting that larger exchanges offer higher profiles,
Mr. Cross asks, "Why would you come to Vancouver?"
Friday's Market Activity
In global markets Friday, most markets continued to trade in a narrow range as investors remain uneasy
about the direction of U.S. bond and stock markets.
The exceptions were in some Asian markets, as Hong Kong's Hang Seng index rallied 2.1% to 9858.49, and
Korean stocks showed a dramatic recovery after a recent sell-off. Seoul's Kospi index surged 4.2% to 520.06
as local interest rates fell and fears of labor unrest abated.
But in Europe, unease remains about rising bond yields in the U.S. The release of some corporate earnings
also weighed on investors. At the end of the day, Frankfurt's Xetra DAX index fell 14.59 points, or 0.3%, to
4903.96. Shares of Dresdner Bank tumbled 2.9% to 31.70 euros a share as investors responded to poor
earnings and higher-than-expected risk provisions, as well as subsequent analysts' downgrades of the stock.
In Paris, the CAC-40 index slid 1.4%. London's FTSE index seemed more resilient, with cash flow from
individual investors into tax-sheltered retirement plans helping to offset selling by some institutions. The FTSE
ended the day down 31.40 points, or 0.5%, at 6175.10.
Overall, the Dow Jones World Index fell 0.92 point, or 0.46%, to 200.54.

The G-7's Missed Opportunity
The G-7's Missed Opportunity
By David Malpass
1,226 words
23 February 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A22
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Last Saturday, the finance ministers and central-bank chieftains of the Group of Seven industrialized
countries met in Bonn and agreed to do practically nothing about the world economy. The U.S. pressured
Europe and Japan to embark on "demand-led" growth, which is the antithesis of the notion that efficient
production is the key to wealth creation. There was no clear statement on exchange-rate stability, suggesting
that the current chaos in currency markets will continue, with wild swings in the yen, a very strong U.S. dollar
pushing down world commodity prices, and periodic collapses in developing countries. Little mention was
made of the economic slowdown underway in most industrialized countries, or of the economic destruction
taking place throughout the developing world.
The failure of the G-7 summit to reach any meaningful consensus is all the more distressing given that many
of the answers to the global financial crisis were within reach. Japan, which at the G-7's urging has spent the
past eight years fighting a monetary-policy problem with government spending and currency manipulations,
could restart its economy overnight by using aggressive monetary stimulus to bring deflation to a halt.
Improving Japan's economic outlook would create a healthy demand for yen, allow the yen to stabilize at a
pro-growth level, and provide a vital stimulus to export-dependent nations. But the current "try all avenues"
advice from the U.S. is too subtle, and it is likely that all Japan has done in recent days is talk the yen weaker
and bond yields lower, without taking real monetary steps to stop the deflation.
The G-7 could also have provided valuable advice to Russia by urging it to legalize the use of foreign
currencies within its borders. For years, the G-7 and the International Monetary Fund have abetted a
monetary policy for Russia that forced the ruble to lose 99.99% of its value while leaving intact the Russian
government's monopoly on money. Putting a stop to this policy would have reversed Russia's slide into
criminality and allowed its barter economy to come out of the dark. But nothing was done.
Having heaped billions of dollars upon the IMF, the G-7 could have instructed it to rethink its latest fiasco in
Brazil, where it has concentrated on fiscal austerity during a currency crisis, the same mistake made in
Thailand and Russia. The IMF should focus single-mindedly on strengthening the Brazilian currency to
minimize the damage from the January devaluation. This could be done by focusing on the quantity of Brazil's
currency outstanding so that supply matches demand. This approach would quickly lower Brazil's interest
rates, allowing the economy to restart and the government's finances to improve.
More broadly, the G-7 ministers could have used the summit to announce a preference for stable currency
values. Such a preference is best pursued neither through exchange-rate laissez faire nor negotiated
currency zones, but rather by having each country unilaterally commit itself to a stable future value for its
currency. This would help avert a repeat of the inflation of the 1970s, which began when the U.S. came off
the gold standard and the value of world currencies plummeted by 90%. Similarly, Japan's deflation in the
1990s was prompted by a doubling of the value of the yen.
The current G-7 system almost guarantees that the value of money will fluctuate so severely in the future as
to become unusable. In the shorter run, the system has wreaked massive damage on the poor countries of
the world. South America has fallen into another deep recession, burdened by IMF policies which favored tax
collection and high interest rates over currency stability and growth. Mexico's per-capita income is well
below 1994 levels despite robust U.S. growth. Southeast Asia was encouraged to leverage itself with
borrowed dollars channeled through unregulated local banks. As if on cue, the Southeast Asian nations saw
the dollar revalued sharply in early 1997 while the IMF pulled the rug out from under them by advocating
devaluation. The Muslim world has seen Indonesia and Pakistan destroyed by IMF malfeasance and the
oil-rich states whip-sawed by the changing value of the U.S. dollar. The G-7 has not yet renounced or
modified this sucker-punch approach to the developing world.
As a result, the global economy is polarizing in a way that completely undermines the so-called Washington
consensus. This is the hodgepodge of austerity-minded, anti-growth ideas that form the G-7's approach to
economic development. Countries are supposed to "float" -- meaning sink -- their exchange rates to improve

--- Page 255 ---
Page 255 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
the competitiveness of their exports. In the guise of structural adjustment, poor countries are bribed with the
off-budget IMF and World Bank dollars to install value-added taxes no matter how impoverished the
consumer. Country performance is gauged by the 1960s standard of balance-of-payments accounting, a
standard that is completely unworkable now that capital flows swing day-by-day.
The kicker in the Washington consensus is that countries that change their systems to match U.S. economic
principles are supposed to be rewarded with capital inflows. This concept has failed miserably because the
countries enjoy neither our Constitution nor our currency. The G-7 should declare dead the Washington
consensus.
The success of any G-7 vision of the world's financial future depends on guaranteeing the value of money.
Currency commitments can take many forms -- monetary union, currency boards, price-rule monetary policies
in which the quantity of money is controlled to maintain its value, or the use of a stable foreign currency. Any
of these approaches would help turn back the tide of Islamic fundamentalism in Turkey, joblessness in South
Africa and lawlessness in Colombia.
As part of its vision, the G-7 could invite developing countries to join the "convergence" trend familiar to
Europeans. When nations commit their futures to stable money, as Europe did by agreeing to the Maastricht
Treaty in 1992, their cost of capital falls quickly and their economies expand. In Europe, bond yields and the
cost of capital "converged" down to Germany's low rates through a massive rally in the continent's financial
markets. Why not apply this concept elsewhere?
Thus, much could have been accomplished at the G-7 gathering. Yet even as they were faced with Brazil's
collapse, Russia's chaos, Japan's lost decade, and looming stagnation in Europe, the G-7 finance ministers
and central bankers chose to accentuate the positive: sustained, non-inflationary growth in the U.S., the
euro's successful implementation, and the happy prospect of quickly spending the IMF's huge new horde of
cash.
The G-7 should not wait for a perfect solution to the global currency crisis. The current system is seriously
broken, and dooms the world's poor to inflation, stagnant living standards, and increasing government
control. At Bonn, the finance ministers had a chance to use their vast power to create a vision for the world's
financial future, solidly founded upon private-sector economic growth, currency stability, smaller government,
and rising incomes. Sadly -- and predictably -- they wasted it.
---
Mr. Malpass is chief international economist at Bear Stearns.

Low Foreign-Currency ReservesThreaten Russian Foreign Debt
International
Low Foreign-Currency Reserves Threaten Russian Foreign Debt
By Mark Whitehouse
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
401 words
23 February 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A19
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
MOSCOW -- Russia's foreign-currency reserves are running too low to guarantee further payment of the
country's foreign debts, a top Central Bank official said.
"Our abilities are running out -- we cannot risk our own solvency," the Interfax news agency quoted Central
Bank Deputy Chairman Oleg Mozhaiskov as saying. The bank declined yesterday to comment further.
Since the government devalued the ruble in mid-August, the cash-strapped Finance Ministry has shifted the
burden of paying foreign debts to the Central Bank. As a result, the bank's foreign-currency and gold
reserves have shrunk by about $1 billion this year, ending the second week of February at $11.3 billion.
Meanwhile, government tax receipts aren't picking up the slack: Roland Nash, an economist at the MFK
Renaissance investment bank in Moscow, said cash tax revenue in January was less than in the same period
of 1998, despite 90% inflation.
"The revenue situation is so dire that the Finance Ministry has had no choice but to go to the Central Bank,"
Mr. Nash said. "If that money is not forthcoming, then it's difficult to see how they're going to meet
foreign-debt obligations."
Russia has effectively defaulted on much of its Soviet-era debt, including $20 billion to the London Club of
commercial creditors and $40 billion to the Paris Club of government creditors. But it has so far remained
current on post-Soviet debts, such as Eurobonds and loans from the International Monetary Fund. Russia
must pay about $300 million to the IMF each month except in July, when the payment jumps to more than $1
billion. In March, it must also make $177 million in interest payments on Eurobonds.
In an effort to build up its reserves, the Central Bank has demanded that exporters repatriate 75% of their
hard-currency revenues. But reserves keep falling despite a trade surplus that recently reached $3 billion a
month, a combination that suggests massive capital flight. In one common scheme, banks use fake import
contracts to send hard currency abroad.
"Capital flight...is one of our weak points," Mr. Mozhaiskov said. "The Central Bank is trying different ways to
stop it, but our people's inventiveness has no limits, and each new regulation gives birth to a new invention."

Home Monitors HelpBattle Hypertension,But Are They Reliable?
HEALTH JOURNAL
Home Monitors Help Battle Hypertension, But Are They Reliable?
By Marilyn Chase
884 words
22 February 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
HOME BLOOD-PRESSURE monitors are used by millions of Americans trying to control their hypertension.
But after shelling out $80 to $95, many find the answer isn't at their fingertips.
Nobody was more deflated by this discovery than Jerry Wang of San Jose, Calif. After purchasing a fingertip
blood-pressure monitor for his parents as a New Year's gift, he found it gave erratic readings.
"My parents told me it doesn't work," says Mr. Wang, who happens to be the director of engineering and
quality assurance for the monitor's maker, A&D Medical of Milpitas, Calif., a unit of Tokyo-based A&D Co. Ltd.
"To this day I have it in my office," he adds. "I never did get my money back."
Grumbles from patients and experts alike haven't been lost on companies. Mr. Wang is now advocating that
A&D phase out the fingertip model. Another monitor manufacturer, Omron Healthcare Inc. of Vernon Hills, Ill.,
a unit of Tokyo-based Omron Corp., already discontinued manufacturing its fingertip models. But caveat
emptor: These out-of-favor units are still on retail shelves.
Failure of fingertip monitors to combine convenience with precision is just one of the challenges faced by this
country's 50 million people with hypertension -- a silent killer.
High blood pressure is defined as a reading of 140/90 or more -- the numbers reflecting the pressure exerted
inside vessels by the beating heart and by the heart at rest between beats. Unchecked, hypertension raises
the risk of heart attack, stroke and kidney damage.
DRUGS, DIET, exercise and even meditation may help control it. But many people endure a period of trial
and error before finding the right mix of medication and lifestyle change.
Home monitoring makes patients partners with their physicians, encouraging them to stick to their treatment
plan, and tracking their response to medication to tailor doses. It reduces doctor visits, potentially trimming
costs. Self-monitoring also separates constant from "white coat" hypertension -- where anxiety makes the
mercury skyrocket at the doctor's office.
But the readings must be accurate. The gold standard in professional blood-pressure measurement is still
the inflatable arm cuff, which compresses the brachial artery while a doctor listens to returning circulation and
notes the pressure on a mercury column.
Years ago, patients doing home monitoring had to pump up the cuff manually and listen with a stethoscope
while noting pressure on a dial -- a lot of juggling. Next came semiautomatic devices with hand-pumped cuffs
and digital readouts. Now, devices have cuffs that slip on easily, push-button inflation, digital readouts and
memory. Prices range from $20 to more than $100.
Experts have their preferences. "I don't like the [finger] or the wrist monitors," says Hector Santiesteban of the
University of California at San Francisco-Stanford Health Care. "We recommend that they use the arm cuff
with automatic inflation and digital readout."
Choose a device that is validated according to American Association for the Advancement of Medical
Instrumentation, or the British Hypertension Society. Calibrate your monitor against your doctor's professional
sphygmomanome ter. "A lot of devices that come out of the box are inaccurate," says Sheldon Sheps,
hypertension authority at the Mayo Clinic.
"Change batteries every month, whether or not it reads `low battery,'" suggests William White of the
University of Connecticut. Old batteries "can give crazy readings."

--- Page 258 ---
Page 258 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Even when the machine works perfectly, getting accurate, stable blood-pressure readings isn't easy. Coffee,
cigarettes, exercise, stress or a full bladder can raise pressure. A big meal can depress it.
At best, blood-pressure checks give a snapshot of the tides of the cardiovascular system. "Your Heart beats
160,000 times in 24 hours, says Mayo's Dr. Sheps. "And you're looking at two beats." The ambulatory
monitors allow for dozens of readings over a 24-hour period.
EXCITING UPGRADES in electronics are due soon, predicts Thomas Pickering of New York Presbyterian
Hospital. Direct transmission of blood-pressure readings from home to doctor's office via computer or
telephone may eliminate the fudge factor among patients tempted to edit out high readings.
One company, LifeLink Monitoring Inc. of Bearsville, N.Y., plans to introduce in May a product linking
monitors to phones to provide direct data transmission.
All this technology in consumers' hands, however, is transferring some cases of white-coat hypertension to
the home. Bernice Kleinman of West Hartford, Conn., says the mere act of hooking up her digital monitor
sends her pressure soaring. "I'm too nervous to use it," she says.
Adds Catherine Allen of Oak Park, Ill., "Every now and then, you say `Nuts!'"
For others, a home monitor helps them reinforce healthful habits and even reduce dependency on
antihypertensive drugs. So it was for William Lee, former chief financial officer of the New York brokerage firm
of Mitchum Jones & Templeton. Mr. Lee records his pressure at home twice a day. With exercise, he's
trimmed his blood pressure and weight. Heartened, his doctor first lowered and then eliminated his verapamil
dose for a trial period.
"Now," says the 53-year-old Queens, N.Y., resident, "I'm in training for the Long Island Marathon."

--- Page 259 ---
Page 259 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
India Seeks to Cool Country's Gold Fever---Imports of Precious MetalSoar, Causing Trade GapTo Become Big Worry
International
India Seeks to Cool Country's Gold Fever --- Imports of Precious Metal Soar, Causing Trade Gap To
Become Big Worry
By Jonathan Karp
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
797 words
22 February 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
BOMBAY, India -- India's economy is suffering from a bout of gold fever.
Soaring gold imports are at the heart of a worrying trade deficit. India's bill for foreign gold in the current
fiscal year is forecast to more than double, to about $6 billion, making the precious metal the country's
second-biggest import, after oil.
The government, which liberalized the gold trade in 1997, is now scrambling to tame Indians' passion for a
commodity that it feels this poor country can't afford, at least not while exports are falling. New Delhi's
strategy is two-fold: raise customs duty to curb imports, and put India's vast hoard of privately owned gold to
work for the economy through gold-linked bank deposits, bonds and mutual funds. Neither plan is certain to
succeed.
From rich to poor, Indians have long treasured gold as security against inflation and hard times. In cities,
gold sales rise when stock and property markets are down. Rural Indians who don't have access to banks --
or don't trust them -- keep their savings in gold. After bumper harvests, farmers barter crops for gold jewelry.
In a country of nearly one billion people, all this adds up to a cache of gold estimated at 11,000 metric tons (a
metric ton equals 2,204.62 pounds) and worth more than $100 billion.
Figures released last week confirm India as the world's biggest gold consumer. The Geneva-based World
Gold Council said Indian demand rose to 815 metric tons in 1998, up nearly 11% from 736.7 metric tons the
year before. Meanwhile, global demand shrank by 11%. The vast majority of gold in India is imported.
The skyrocketing import figures alarmed policymakers, who were already concerned about India's
deteriorating trade balance and anemic tax revenue because of slowing economic growth. Some private
economists say the country's trade deficit could reach a high 5% of economic output in the fiscal year ending
March 31. In an attempt to fix these problems, Finance Minister Yashwant Sinha raised the import duty on
gold by 60% in January. Economists and gold-industry officials say the move is likely to backfire: Instead of
slowing gold imports, it will drive them underground, back to smugglers who used to dominate the trade.
"There is a demand in this country that nobody can deny," says Derrick Machado, a World Gold Council
official in Bombay.
He questions whether the financial burden of gold imports has increased as dramatically as statistics
suggest. The surge has come since October 1997, when the government threw open gold imports to
authorized banks and trading agencies. The change legalized what had been a heavy illicit, and untaxed, flow
paid for with foreign currency at high premiums to gold's international price. Even legal gold remittances by
overseas Indians were financed by black-market deals in India.
Still, with trade concerns mounting, India's Finance Ministry and central bank are seeking innovative reforms
to make gold a more productive import. The ideas, which banks hope to launch in a few months, aim to draw
Indians' stockpile into the banking system through gold-linked savings and investment plans. Monetizing
gold would create a new financial asset, and by lending and selling that asset locally, banks could relieve
goldimport pressure.
"We don't expect it to start with a bang. The Indian mindset is still to hoard gold," says Khizir Ahmed,
executive director of the Reserve Bank of India, the central bank. "We have to change this psychology."
India's previous efforts to mobilize private gold -- in return for bonds -- flopped. The last bond issue, which
offered 2% annual interest, garnered just 41 metric tons. This time, bankers say, the initiative is much
broader. Banks recently started giving gold loans to Indian jewelry exporters.

--- Page 260 ---
Page 260 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Beyond psychological hurdles, there are commercial concerns. Indians favor 22-karat gold, but what they
buy is usually less pure. India needs to establish credible assaying and refining facilities. Even then,
depositors might be hesitant. "My jeweler will buy gold back from me as if it's 22 karats," even if it isn't that
pure, says a government official, who asked not to be identified. "Why should I give it to a bank at 17-karat
prices?"
India's cash-strapped government believes gold savings will be its secret weapon in financing economic
development. Prime Minister Atal Bihari Vajpayee said as much last November, when he dismissed
skepticism that India could afford his proposed $7 billion highway network. Within days, though, the
government scaled down the ambitious project.

EUROPE/MIDEAST
Electrolux Revamp
Spurs Big Surge
In Bottom Line
It was a long time coming, but the sweeping changes going on at Sweden's Electrolux, the world's biggest
appliance maker, are beginning to pay off. The group saw its bottom line more than quadruple last year to
5.93 billion kronor ($753.1 million) -- a result that surprised more than a few analysts, who apparently had
underestimated the progress being made in the broad restructuring undertaken by the maker of a host of
household goods from vacuum cleaners to refrigerators. The upbeat news propelled a surge in the group's
shares in Stockholm, but whether that rally can continue is another matter. Since the company first
announced its revamp nearly two years ago -- including the shedding of 11,000 jobs and the closing of
numerous plants -- the stock has climbed more than 60% in value after languishing for years.
Still with the restructuring set to be completed by this summer, market watchers anticipate that earnings --
and along with it sales, which rose 4% to 117.52 billion kronor on strong gains in the U.S. market -- will
continue to move higher this year. While Brazil could be an irritant for the company, Electrolux says it's
making preparations to head off any problems from a further weakening in demand there.
Turk Telekom Readies for Privatization
Turkey's national phone monopoly could see its shares listed later this year in London and New York after it
launches an initial public offering of 14% of the company. But a few things have to happen first; the
government needs to go through a crucial national election in April; Parliament has to establish an
independent regulatory authority for the telecom sector and Turk Telekom needs to open an offer for the sale
of 20% of the monopoly to a strategic investor. If the red tape can be cleared, the deal should attract a lot of
interest. At least three overseas phone operators with Turkish partners are competing to be chosen as the
all-important strategic investor. They include SBC Communications of the U.S., Telefonica of Spain and
Telecom Italia.
About 8% of French Taxes Paid in Euros
In line with changes taking place all over Europe, France announced that nearly 8% of the revenue it received
last month in tax collections from businesses nationwide were paid in Europe's new common currency, the
euro. That figure is high considering that the new form of payment only went into effect on Jan. 1 and that
French shop and factory owners have been doing business in francs for generations. The fact that so many
businesses are embracing the euro early on may indicate that the currency could be in for a smooth transition
when it replaces the franc at the retail level early in the next century.
EU Raids Banks in Probe of Euro Fees
Meanwhile, saying it hoped to catch them by surprise, European Union investigators raided more than half a
dozen banks in Germany, France, Spain and Italy in a probe into whether the industry is adhering to set fees
for converting currencies in the euro zone. The institutions searched were among some of Europe's biggest,
including Germany's Deutsche Bank and Dresdner Bank; France's Societe Generale and Caisse Nationale
de Credit Agricole; Banco Bilbao Vizcaya and Argentaria Corp. Bancaria de Espana in Spain; and Italy's
Banca Commerciale Italiana and Cariplo. The inquiry follows complaints from consumers that banks are
charging far too much to change francs, marks and other currencies into euros, though several of the banks
say that isn't the case and none have been charged with any wrongdoing.

--- Page 262 ---
Page 262 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
BRIEFLY:
-- U.K. bank Barclays said its operating profit fell nearly 20% in 1998, but a promise to keep costs steady this
year and the imminent arrival of a new chief executive were enough to prompt a rally in its shares.
Meanwhile, pretax profit was up 12% to GBP 1.92 billion ($3.14 billion), in line with expectations.
-- Swedish pharmaceutical group Astra's earnings rose 15% last year to 16.44 billion kronor on a 27% jump in
sales. But that takes into account a big gain from two deals with Schering-Plough of the U.S.
-- The European Commission approved the acquisition by German media giant Bertelsmann of privately held
scientific publisher Springer Verlag for an estimated one billion marks ($577.4 million).
-- Citing a likely slowdown, Swedish auto maker Volvo says it will lay off 650 workers in its truck division.
-- Dutch cable and telecom group United Pan-Europe Communications says its initial public offering brought
in 1.29 billion euros ($1.46 billion).
---
ASIA/PACIFIC
Korea's Jobless Rate Likely to Climb
South Korea's jobless rate is already at the highest level since the government began tallying such figures
back in 1982. Now Seoul is predicting that the closely watched number will climb a full percentage point to
9% in late February or March as the country continues to deal with its worst economic crisis in years. But
there's hope on the horizon; with a likely rebound in the overall economy said to be just around the corner,
officials say they expect the unemployment problem to ease when the construction industry begins hiring
more workers in the spring. Just over a year ago, South Korea's jobless rate stood at 3.1%; since then many
companies have gone bankrupt while others have laid off workers.
Honda to Open Sales Outlets in China
Japan's Honda Motor plans to open 100 automobile dealerships throughout China over the next couple of
years, yet another sign of the improving business relations between the two countries. The auto maker --
which has been relying heavily on sales outside of Japan for years, particularly in the U.S. market -- says it
will open the first couple of dozen outlets as early as next month. That's when it's set to begin sales of the
Accord passenger car model assembled at its plant in Guangzhou, the capital of Guangdong province. That
factory is expected to eventually produce 30,000 vehicles a year.
Australia Study Lifts Hope of Rate Cut
Australia's central bank cleared the way for a possible cut in interest rates later this year by slashing its
inflation forecast. In its quarterly economic outlook, the central bank said inflation will reach an annual rate
of 2% by the end of this year, up from 1.6% at the end of 1998 but barely touching the bottom of the central
bank's target range of 2% to 3%. Analysts expect the bank to lower interest rates once it has evidence of a
significant slowing in economic growth, though they concede that signs of a slowdown haven't yet emerged.
BRIEFLY:
-- A sharp fall in imports caused the Philippines to post its lowest trade deficit in 26 years, with the gap
narrowing to a mere $164 million in 1998 from $10.7 billion in 1997. For December alone, a trade surplus was
booked for the seventh consecutive month, totaling $461 million after a deficit of $562 million a year earlier.

THE AMERICAS
Mexico Mulls Splitting Airline Concern
Mexico is pondering the idea of dividing its local airline holding company Cintra into two. If it goes ahead with
such a move, it would end a long disagreement in the corridors of power regarding the advantages and
disadvantages of having a holding company that controls the vast majority of the nation's major airline routes.
Right now, Cintra is the holding company for Mexico's two biggest airlines, Aerovias de Mexico, or
AeroMexico, and Mexicana de Aviacion. It also has a controlling stake in AeroPeru in association with Delta
Air Lines of the U.S. Antitrust authorities, fired up by competition concerns, have been looking for some time
for an opportunity to break up Cintra. The company was created in 1996 as both AeroMexico and Mexicana
were on the brink of bankruptcy.

--- Page 263 ---
Page 263 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mexico Set to Upgrade Its Highways
Mexico says it will invest more than 13.8 billion pesos ($1.39 billion) to modernize its transporation
infrastructure. The government says most of the money will go to the federal highway network and for the
improvement of rural roads. Officials also plan to develop railroad projects for the Mexico City metropolitan
area and will invest 2.5 billion pesos in the country's ports, of which more than 25% will be from private
sources. Meanwhile, the government says it's analyzing two possible sites for a new international airport to
serve Mexico City. One option is Texcoco, northeast of the capital, and the other is in Tizayuca, north of the
capital in Hidalgo state.
BRIEFLY:
-- Canada's Royal Oak Mines, hurt by weak metal prices and debt, filed for protection from its creditors. Royal
Oak, which operates a copper and gold mine in British Columbia, late last year said it deferred interest
payments on its debt, which stands at $335 million. The company said the latest move will allow Royal Oak to
continue operating while restructuring its debt.
-- Mexico's industrial production jumped 6.6% last year, while output was up 3.9% in December from a year
earlier.
