Should Investors Buy or Sell for Y2K?
Your Money Matters
Should Investors Buy or Sell for Y2K?
By Ruth Simon
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,145 words
16 February 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Don't panic.
That is about the only thing on which financial advisers seem to agree when asked how investors should
prepare their portfolios for the year 2000.
"I certainly wouldn't go into a doomsday investment mode," cautions Edward Yardeni, chief economist with
Deutsche Bank Securities, who nevertheless is known for having predicted major business disruptions in
2000. "Anyone who sells everything now because they become obsessed with the year 2000 may wind up
giving quite a windfall" to the Internal Revenue Service in the form of taxes on capital gains.
Beyond that, figuring out what year-2000-related computer headaches will mean for financial markets -- and
what investors should do -- is a bit like trying to pick the next Microsoft: There are plenty of people with strong
opinions, but their answers are all over the place.
Some predict that airline and healthcare stocks will suffer as computer glitches cause scheduling and
payment problems. Others see a drop in computer sales. There are forecasts of declines in emerging
markets and even a global recession because of computer failures. Then there are those who believe
large-capitalization U.S. stocks will benefit from turmoil in other markets.
No one will know the real answer until sometime in January. But financial advisers say that now is the time for
investors to decide how big a problem they think the year 2000 will be and whether they should adjust their
portfolios accordingly.
The year-2000, or Y2K, problem is expected to occur when older computers programmed for dates with two
digits malfunction or shut down. Computers that read "99" as 1999, might read the year 2000 as 1900, for
example, which could wreak havoc in computer-dependent businesses.
Mr. Yardeni says investors who think year-2000 troubles could spark a global recession may want to boost
their holdings of government bonds. "If the year 2000 does become an investment issue later this year, there
could be another flight to quality Treasury bonds and Treasury bills," he says. "It could set up a stockmarket
sell-off like last summer, or worse."
Recession or not, investors may want to rejigger their portfolios if they don't have the stomach to weather a
possible year-2000 market correction. "If you think you'll lose sleep, panic and be scared...you need to make
an interim adjustment," says Harold Evensky, a financial planner in Coral Gables, Fla. He suggests that jittery
investors shift 15% to 20% of their total portfolio from stocks to bonds and then pick a specific time when they
will go back into the market.
But not everyone thinks that bonds are the place to be. Peter Canelo, U.S. investment strategist for Morgan
Stanley Dean Witter, expects the federal government to boost spending and money supply to keep year-2000
ills from battering the economy. "I don't want to own a bond in a strong economy with an expansionary
monetary policy," says Mr. Canelo. "That's how you lose a lot of money in bonds."
Mr. Canelo and Mr. Evensky agree that municipal bonds, particularly lower-rated ones, could be a trouble
spot because some local governments don't seem to be doing much to prepare for the possible year-2000
problem.
Those who believe year-2000 fears could be bullish for some sectors of the stock market reason that big U.S.
companies -- like those in the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index -- are viewed as leaders in year-2000
preparedness. They could become a haven as anxious foreign and U.S. investors reduce their exposure to
foreign markets and small-company shares.

--- Page 279 ---
Page 279 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"If you want to reduce risk and still be in equities, the S&P 500 is where you want to be," says Thomas
Galvin, chief investment officer at Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette.
The cost of year-2000 fixes isn't likely to put much of a dent in corporate profits, adds Mr. Canelo, whose firm
recently analyzed year-2000 disclosures in quarterly filings by 523 large and midsize firms. Total year-2000
spending in the U.S. is likely to total between $78 billion and $89 billion, or one-third to one-half many
previous estimates, he says. Most big companies should be year-2000 compliant by the middle of this year,
he adds.
Worries about year-2000 glitches could discourage investment in "Asian and Latin American economies or
emergingmarket economies in general," Mr. Galvin says. "People already worried about the state of these
economies are starting to wonder whether distribution systems are year-2000 compliant. That adds a...level
of risk that investors may be unwilling to accept."
The computer industry is drawing close attention. The hot question is, "Will users, in the second half of this
year, freeze up their buying of new computer hardware and software and, even to a limited extent, slow up on
initiating new computer-services projects?" says Merrill Lynch computer-services analyst Stephen McClellan.
He says this uncertainty could benefit computer-services companies that have continuing service
arrangements with client companies.
Though he figures that personal-computer sales will remain extremely strong, Mr. Yardeni says year-2000
problems could lead investors to switch out of many highflying technology stocks and into more-defensive
sectors, such as food, alcohol, drugs, tobacco, energy and gold.
Some see an upside in such a shift. "People may be selling because they think it's the end of the world, but it
may be a great opportunity," says Mr. Canelo. He thinks technology orders will be "unusually strong" next
year as companies begin to tackle previously sidelined projects.
Investors worried about specific companies should take a look at the year-2000 disclosures that public
companies must make in their quarterly filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission.
Thomas Madden, chief investment officer-U.S. equities/high yield for Federated Investors, says a "yellow
light" should go off if a company has spent only a small portion of the funds budgeted for year-2000 fixes or
hasn't clearly articulated how it will address the issue. Others say a big jump in year-2000 spending from one
quarter to the next could also signal trouble.
Some advisers say the best thing investors can do is to stick with
their current investment strategy. "I'm not sure, given the level of the
stock market, that you do anything different" no matter what your
year-2000 scenario is, says Art Micheletti, chief investment strategist
for Bailard, Biehl & Kaiser. "If the year 2000 is a problem, it takes
everybody," he adds. "There won't be a place to hide."
Journal Link: For an online discussion about the year-2000 problem, see
The Wall Street Journal Interactive Edition at
http://wsj.com

CORPORATE PERFORMANCE 1998 REVIEW: 4TH QUARTER---Corporate Earnings Climb 3%,As Consumer Demand HelpsU.S. Economy Stay on Track
CORPORATE PERFORMANCE 1998 REVIEW: 4TH QUARTER --- Corporate Earnings Climb 3%, As
Consumer Demand Helps U.S. Economy Stay on Track
By Tristan Mabry
Staff Reporter
7,058 words
16 February 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C13
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Net income at companies that compose the Dow Jones Global-U.S. stock index rose 3% to $77.71 billion in
the fourth quarter, as the seemingly insatiable appetite of the American consumer supported domestic
demand, while insulating many sectors from the chilling effects of the global financial crisis.
The gain represented a sharp improvement from the third quarter, when net income declined 5%. (The
third-quarter figure was revised from a preliminary estimate of a 3% gain.) The fourth-quarter gain was
boosted by DuPont's $2.4 billion gain on the sale of Conoco common stock and a $1.3 billion gain from an
accounting change at Lucent Technologies.
Net from continuing operations, a more narrow measure of profit that excludes one-time accounting entries,
fell 2% for the three months ended Dec. 31, compared with a 1% gain a year earlier.
Despite a difficult quarter for the financial-services industry and an energy sector deflated by an oversupply of
oil, corporate profits overall were lifted higher by a surge in technology spending and long lines at the
checkout counter.
The profit report covers 658 of the 756 companies that make up the Dow Jones Global-U.S. stock index, a
group of publicly traded companies representing about 80% of the stock-market capitalization for each sector
of the nation's economy. A revised tally of net income for the index will be compiled by the end of March, after
the release of all available earnings reports.
Meanwhile, First Call, which tracks operating earnings for the same group of companies, said operating
earnings rose 4.6% in the fourth quarter from the year-earlier period.
Although The Wall Street Journal's net-income results and First Call's operating-earnings figures are much
closer than in some previous periods, the continuing debate over which income measure offers a better guide
to the bottom line -- net income or operating earnings -- has never been louder.
Net income includes write-offs and other one-time charges or gains. Operating earnings, however, exclude
one-time events and extraordinary items in an attempt to isolate day-to-day corporate performance. Wall
Street analysts generally focus on operating earnings. But in recent months, government regulators have
raised the possibility that investors, by focusing mainly on operating earnings, are being misled about the
health and condition of firms that take frequent write-offs.
"There is no standard definition" that companies have to follow when calculating operating earnings, said
Bear Stearns accounting analyst Janet Pegg. "That's one of the big problems to start off with." By focusing on
net income, she said, at least "you get a bottom line," although "both can be manipulated very easily."
While industry analysts may be more qualified to determine what should or shouldn't affect a company's
earnings, the swelling ranks of the investing public are increasingly vocal in demanding transparency.
"Shareholders are expecting companies to provide the best numbers available," said Patrick McGurn, vice
president of Institutional Shareholder Services, "and what they would like to see is more profound and reliable
reporting."
Last month, the Securities and Exchange Commission notified 150 publicly traded companies that it may
review certain charges recorded last year, including asset write-downs and restructuring costs, for potential
violations of SEC accounting rules.

--- Page 281 ---
Page 281 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Nevertheless, advocates of both net income and operating earnings were presented with robust results in the
fourth quarter that helped allay continuing difficulties in some sectors aggravated by sinking commodity
prices.
While some observers have labeled the long-term slump in commodity prices a paradigm of the new,
low-inflation economy, more-cautious analysts believe prices don't have much more to fall, and may even
edge higher later this year.
"I do think the slide in commodity prices is cyclical," said Ian Shepherdson, chief U.S. economist at High
Frequency Economics. "While it's being driven largely by Asia, I think prices have bottomed out."
If true, that is welcome news to the basic-materials sector, which has been hammered by falling demand
overseas and disappointing prices at home.
The steady corrosion of profits at the nation's chemical companies continued in the fourth quarter, despite the
big gains posted by DuPont from the sale of its Conoco oil unit. Specialty-chemical makers enjoyed no such
windfall, and the group's earnings fell 80%.
Unlike chemical companies, the country's steel industry believes it can isolate the cause of its painful losses:
its longtime nemesis, imports. Singling out Japan, Russia and Brazil, steelmakers have turned to Washington
for help, raising hopes that even the threat of trade action could help stem the tide of imported steel. As a
group, steelmakers' earnings fell 72%. Aluminum makers fared better, with earnings falling 35%, but are less
optimistic about demand for the metal this year.
Among the edible commodities, supply and demand rendered an ironic outcome, with efficient production
forcing profits into the ground.
A bountiful growing season brought no cause for celebration to the heartland as oversupply depressed
already meager profits. As the nation's farmers wait for prices to rise, the companies that supply growers with
agricultural equipment were left with idle machines, although some were able to compensate by tapping a
strong market for construction equipment.
For both builders and farmers, the low cost of fueling their pickups offered at least some consolation.
Cheap gasoline was just one of the effects of a global market flooded with crude: Oil companies suffered from
oversupply and an unseasonably warm winter. Major oil companies saw their profits melt 91%, while the
energy sector ended the year with a loss of more than $555 million.
Planes, trains and trucks also picked up savings from lower fuel costs, but the railroads failed to improve their
performance, ending the fourth quarter with a 26% drop in net income, due in part to the logistical expenses
of dividing Conrail between CSX and Norfolk Southern. Trucking companies, however, delivered a 20% jump
in net income for the quarter, while truck makers and other transport-equipment suppliers posted a net gain of
8%.
More-demanding freight schedules also lifted business for air-freight couriers, up 20%, as domestic traffic
offset static conditions abroad.
The skies over America were less kind to passenger airlines, especially those flying overseas. As a group,
the airlines watched fourth-quarter profits fall 58%, pulled lower by cheaper fares and fewer passengers
traveling across both the Pacific and the Atlantic, as well as South American routes.
Auto makers enjoyed a surge in customer traffic, but not quite enough to outpace dragging markets in Asia
and Latin America. Nonetheless, depressed steel and rubber prices lowered the cost of making cars.
Large retailers, the second largest group among businesses in the cyclical consumer sector after the auto
makers, rang up sharply higher sales, elevated by a stellar holiday shopping season. Parking lots at Wal-Mart
Stores were especially crowded; the discount chain posted a 27% jump in fourth-quarter net income. As a
group, the bottom line for broadline retailers rose 12%.
Other standouts in the cyclical crowd include the busy home builders, up 67% for the quarter, and furnishing
and appliance makers, up 24%.
Household products, from toothpaste to Tupperware, helped earnings in the noncyclical consumer sector, but
not enough to boost net income for the quarter. Makers of some famous American exports -- Coca-Cola,
PepsiCo and RJR Nabisco Holdings -- had disappointing performances. Tobacco companies enjoyed strong
domestic sales but the group ended with a loss. Beverage makers faced severe price competition, and drug
companies reported strong sales but unimpressive earnings.
Overall, net income for the noncyclical consumer sector dropped 2%.

--- Page 282 ---
Page 282 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
While consumer spending still accounts for about two-thirds of the U.S. economy, commercial customers
were the driving force behind the big success of the technology sector. IBM, Intel, Microsoft and Lucent
Technologies finished the year with a flourish, helping to drive the technology sector up 64%. A number of
factors are credited with raising expenditures on computers, software and communications equipment,
including network upgrades in anticipation of the year-2000 bug and the Internet.
The World Wide Web has undeniably sparked a gold rush, but just as in the original California frenzy of
1849, it is the merchants that serve the community created around the boom that are actually making money.
While Internet companies are hot, most right now are lucky to turn a profit. Meanwhile, net income at
network-computer maker Sun Microsystems rose 75% to $261.1 million, while sales of the eye-catching iMac
personal computer tripled profits at Apple Computer.
Telecommunications companies also picked up new business from the surge of Internet traffic. Telephone
companies registered a 37% jump in net income, due partly to data-transmission services as well as new
wireless customers. The strong showing by telecoms, in conjunction with bright earnings for electric utilities
and natural-gas providers, lifted net income for the entire utilities sector by 96%.
On Wall Street, however, the tumult of markets overseas forced underwriters to sit on their hands as they
waited for the dust to settle; the group saw their profits drop 21%. Yet among the financial-services industry, it
was property-casualty insurers who were squeezed most severely, incurring a 46% fall in net income, due
mainly to intense competition.
Among industry groups, air-freight couriers reported strong improvements in domestic profit and diminished
impact from global economic problems. Airborne Freight's earnings improved sharply, as domestic volume
and revenue per package grew and operating costs fell. Earnings at FedEx parent FDX increased as the
company began benefiting from the superior margins of units in Caliber System, which it acquired in 1997.
The air couriers continued to trim their sails overseas, delaying most expansions in international markets and
focusing on cutting costs. Looking forward, the carriers expect domestic pricing to stay healthy; Federal
Express and United Parcel Service announced across-the-board price increases for the current quarter.
The nation's home builders posted profits that exceeded analysts' expectations, thanks to a mild fall and
winter combined with eager home buyers, a strong economy and easily available mortgage funds. MDC
Holdings, Pulte and Lennar saw net income more than double from strong sales and wider profit margins.
MDC Holdings said its profit growth was due to record home closings and substantial increases in home
gross-profit margins. Centex and Kaufman & Broad Home also saw big profit increases, as new-home sales
continued at a quick pace.
Makers of manufactured homes also fared well. Clayton Homes said the number of homes it sold rose 14% in
its fiscal second quarter. But Oakwood Homes saw its fiscal first-quarter net income drop, partly due to
reduced financial-services income.
Computer makers mostly enjoyed a strong quarter, as corporate America ramped up its purchases of
powerful server computers, PCs, storage systems and related services. Most beat analysts' expectations
despite the lingering effects of the economic crisis in Asia. Sun Microsystems was one of the biggest
beneficiaries, thanks to the Internet boom, which has fueled strong demand for its computers that host large
networks. PC maker Gateway reported record profit as unit PC sales jumped 35%, despite a 14% drop in the
average sales price of its PCs.
International Business Machines proved something of a counter to the trend, reporting that computer sales
fell 2% in the quarter, despite a 43% rise in PC unit shipments, as customers postponed server purchases in
advance of expected new products. Still, booming sales of software and services, which contributed 60% of
Big Blue's profit, more than offset the weak performance in hardware.
Chemical companies continued to disappoint, capping a year in which earnings slid, global demand
weakened and prices sank. If there was a bright spot in the quarter, it was that many companies predicted
they would hit bottom and that market conditions shouldn't get much worse. Analysts weren't so sure,
cautioning that a recession in 1999 could push basic-chemical prices to new lows.
Specialty-chemical companies like Rohm & Haas and Eastman also posted steep declines, disappointing
investors who expected these companies' more-selective markets to perform better in a downturn. In the
basic-chemicals sector, Dow Chemical saw earnings fall 59%, including $185 million in one-time charges,
while Union Carbide posted a 48% decline, despite a $43 million unusual gain from a lawsuit settlement.
DuPont presented the most complex numbers, due to its initial public offering of 30% of oil unit Conoco in the
quarter.
BASIC MATERIALS

U.S. steelmakers, continuing to be hurt by record levels of imported steel, watched their shipments drop and
their pricing power erode. At the same time, strikes caused a big inventory buildup at General Motors, Big
Steel's biggest customer. Although the fourth quarter wasn't as bad as analysts had feared, some of the
major steel companies slipped into the red, and three smaller ones faced bankruptcy-law proceedings.
Shipments dropped more than 20% at some companies, and several mills idled operations and laid off
workers to compensate. In recent weeks, steelmakers have announced price increases that should bring the
industry some relief by the second quarter.
U.S. aluminum makers faced a weak commodity market and a series of restructuring and strike-related costs,
dragging down profits. Things could get worse, as London Metal Exchange prices are expected to drop to an
average of 55 cents a pound from an average of 62 cents a pound in 1998.
Continuing a two-year slump caused mostly by sagging metals prices, most gold- and copper-mining
companies reported weak results. The continuing economic crisis in Asia kept downward pressure on copper
prices by reducing demand as inventories of the metal rose steadily through the second half of 1998. Average
copper prices for the quarter fell 20%, compared with a year earlier, on the Comex division of the New York
Mercantile Exchange, to 70 cents a pound. The result: more cost-cutting efforts. Asarco said it would close its
El Paso, Texas, smelter for three years and recently announced a series of staff cuts and other belt-tightening
moves.
Meanwhile, Comex gold prices fell steadily through the quarter, ending at $288.10 an ounce, far below the
year-earlier quarter's average of $306.07 an ounce. Gold companies, too, have sought to cut production at
their highest-cost operations. The coming year promises to be even tougher. Despite some predictions that
the Asian economic crisis will ease this year, most analysts don't expect a rebound in Comex copper prices in
the near future, and industry observers aren't much more bullish about gold.
In the forest-products sector, the Asian economic crisis continued to affect all lumber segments, but analysts
say there are signs of a pickup in the container-board industry, which should help spur a turnaround.
Companies reported few surprises, and some saw earnings slightly above expectations. Weyerhaeuser,
whose major Asian market is Japan, reported weakened earnings in its logs, lumber, paper, pulp and
newsprint markets. Other companies, like Georgia-Pacific, have been buying back their own stock with cash
generated by a strong balance sheet, among other factors.
Least affected by Asian economic problems were panels and plywood, because of the strong domestic
housing market. But analysts fear foreign imports of writing paper are expected to increase because of
expanding overseas capacity.

A glut of crude oil, coupled with a warm winter, continued to hurt the oil patch. Many of the major oil
companies posted
losses, reflecting large asset write-downs and restructuring charges. Without one-time items, Exxon emerged
with the fewest battle scars, though its profit still fell 39%. Unocal, Mobil, Phillips and Texaco all reported
losses, and Atlantic Richfield suffered the most, taking $864 million in charges.

--- Page 284 ---
Page 284 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The coming year looks bleak for the sector, as commodity prices remain at record lows. With firms having
less money to spend on exploration and production, analysts see a production decline and more
consolidation like the BP-Amoco merger and Exxon's agreement to buy Mobil.
Thanks to diversification, many big natural-gas and pipeline companies fared a bit better than the integrated
oils. Sonat's net income slid due to warm temperatures, which decreased throughput in its pipeline division.
Enron increased its net income slightly, because of growth in its natural-gas commodity and electricity-trading
business. Energy and communications company Williams posted a loss, recording more than $100 million in
special charges.
If low commodity prices and thin margins persist, the coming year could pose plenty of challenges for this
segment of the energy sector. Many companies have already begun cost-reduction programs, while others
plan to sell noncore assets.
INDUSTRIAL
Net Income % Change
(in billions) From Year Ago
4th Qtr. '98 $3.74 -28%

Major railroads reported mixed results. Burlington Northern Santa Fe continued to make progress digesting
its big rail merger and showed gains in some types of shipments. But at CSX and Norfolk Southern, earnings
fell on higher expenses related to their planned carve-up of Conrail operations. Shipments of export coal were
sharply lower for both Norfolk and CSX.
Union Pacific continued to recover from its yearlong service problems, which began after it took over
Southern Pacific Rail. In an effort to handle more freight and prevent traffic snarls, Union Pacific hired more
train crews and built additional track. Excluding a charge of $285 million, reflecting a goodwill write-down at
its trucking unit, earnings in the quarter would have totaled $96 million. Analysts expect the pattern of mixed
results to continue in the current quarter.
Truck companies posted solid gains on strong freight growth, better rates and lower fuel prices. Among
truckload carriers, J.B. Hunt said it benefited from improved productivity and safety results. Werner
Enterprises added 276 trucks to its fleet, and Swift Transportation, helped by enhanced driver recruitment
and retention, saw its fleet grow nearly 17%.
Less-than-truckload carriers, which haul shipments for more than one customer in each trailer, were helped
by industrywide rate increases. However, Roadway noted some diversion of freight to nonunion competitors,
which the carrier tried to offset with cost-containment moves and an emphasis on faster-delivery, higher-yield
services. As a group, trucking companies are expected to post profit gains in the first quarter, with growth in
freight and rates continuing, analysts say.
Truck makers saw strong sales growth and boosted production as they tried to whittle away at big order
backlogs for highway rigs. Paccar, which has spent the past two years upgrading its manufacturing facilities,
said it delivered a record 93,800 trucks in 1998, up 24% from a year earlier. The breakneck pace also spurred
demand for engines: Navistar shipped 67,500 midrange diesel engines last year, up 28%.
Demand for trucks is strong partly because more cargo is being shipped over the highway these days. With
railroad companies facing congestion and the increased acceptance of "just-in-time" inventory among
manufacturers, trucks are being seen by some as more reliable modes of transportation. Still, truck makers
say their boom cycle may be winding down. Order backlogs are falling, and order cancellations, a key
indicator of falling sales, have risen in recent months.
Turmoil in the farm economy continued to hammer away at sales of agricultural equipment, as low commodity
prices, record harvests and weakening exports hurt farmers' ability to buy new tractors. Case, which posted a
loss, said farm-machine sales fell 19%. Case and Deere said they will continue to lower production and
offered poor forecasts for 1999.
The picture is somewhat brighter on the construction-equipment side, as housing starts remain strong and the
new federal highway bill spurs construction on the nation's roads and bridges. But Caterpillar said pricing

--- Page 285 ---
Page 285 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
power is slipping as Case and Deere offer incentives on construction equipment to make up for lower
farm-machine sales.
CONSUMER, CYCLICAL
Net Income % Change
(in billions) From Year Ago
4th Qtr. '98 $11.46 + 3%
3rd Qtr. '98 14.72 + 35
2nd Qtr. '98 39.60 +199
1 Qtr. '98 29.70 +132
General Motors, Ford Motor and DaimlerChrysler prospered thanks to strong demand in the U.S. and Europe,
which largely offset disappointments in Latin America and Asia. But most industry forecasters see a mild
slowdown in 1999, though U.S. car and light-truck sales show no sign of easing. Industry economists worry
that American consumers have been outspending their incomes for so long that some retrenchment is
inevitable.
Given the industry's overcapacity, any drop in overall demand could trigger new rounds of costly discounting
and production slowdowns. With No. 1 auto maker GM determined to regain market share after last year's
damaging U.S. strike, competition in many segments, particularly large pickup trucks, will probably become
more intense, pressuring profit margins. The industry's cost crunch is expected to drive further consolidation
among both auto makers and suppliers. Industry executives see moves such as Ford's to buy Volvo's
passenger-car business, as well as Goodyear Tire & Rubber's expanded alliance with Japan's Sumitomo
Rubber Industries, as opening-round moves with more deals to come.
Though many merchants have yet to report earnings, analysts say retailers rang up even better numbers than
expected for the fourth quarter, which for many ended at the close of January. Wall Street was expecting
retailers to post gains of about 10% to 12%, but strong sales just after Christmas and into January led some
analysts to raise their expectations. Discounters such as Wal-Mart and Costco are expected to lead the pack,
and specialty retailers such as Gap and AnnTaylor also should post strong numbers, analysts say. Midline
department stores were the weak area, particularly Sears and J.C. Penney, both of which issued earnings
warnings.
Most stores are entering the fiscal first quarter with healthy, low inventory levels following strong sales during
the holidays, analysts say. One exception: Dillard's has an enormous amount of inventory left over from
autumn and early winter, which could hurt it in the current quarter.
Hotel profits generally defied Wall Street's low expectations. Hilton Hotels, Marriott International and
Starwood Hotels & Resorts all posted better-than-expected earnings, sending their stocks up in recent weeks.
Wall Street was expecting that slightly lower hotel-room occupancy rates would translate into lower profits.
Instead, room prices rose enough to overshadow declines in occupancy.
The results have led many on Wall Street and in the hotel industry to increase their forecasts for the current
quarter and year. Bjorn Hanson, a consultant with PricewaterhouseCoopers in New York, says he believes
that room prices will rise 3.9% this year, causing him to predict very strong profits for the industry, particularly
for upscale hotels.
Among restaurants, McDonald's earnings were reduced by a $98.6 million charge for a new cooking system.
The chain's U.S. restaurants posted an 11% increase in operating income, while those in Latin America and
the Asia/Pacific region experienced continued soft sales. Darden Restaurants posted strong sales gains at
stores open at least a year at both its Red Lobster and Olive Garden chains. At Cracker Barrel, same-store
sales for the fiscal first quarter slid 1.6%. The company took steps to sweeten restaurant managers' pay to
reduce turnover and began testing a menu intended to improve its price-value perception with customers.
CKE Restaurants, which operates the Hardee's, Carl's Jr. and Taco Bueno chains, said all three achieved
improved results. Brinker International, despite what it called "difficult commodity prices" on poultry and dairy
products, posted higher earnings on strong sales gains at its Chili's and Romano's Macaroni Grill businesses.
Profits for casino operators continued to be disappointing, but many gambling stocks rebounded slightly after
a year of lows that left investors expecting the worst. MGM Grand reported a 36% drop in earnings but still
beat Wall Street's estimates, sending its stock up.

--- Page 286 ---
Page 286 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The number of visitors to Las Vegas rose slightly, fueling some optimism about future profits. But some
casino companies say that margins continue to be squeezed by an oversupply of hotel rooms in the city. And
with an additional 6,700 rooms to be built in Las Vegas by the end of April, many analysts remain cautious
about profits. Outside of Las Vegas, gambling markets improved steadily, drawing more investors to
companies with business in these so-called secondary markets.
For toy makers, sales of some traditional playthings slumped while electronic products grew, leading to sales
and earnings declines for Mattel and gains for Hasbro. No. 1 Mattel blamed its 70% drop in net and 4.3%
sales decline partly on the failure of Toys "R" Us and other retailers to reorder after Thanksgiving as they
pared back inventories for the Christmas season. Mattel also warned that its mainstay, Barbie sales, declined
14% last year and will grow slowly this year.
Hasbro, benefiting from the electronically enhanced Furby, one of the season's few hit toys, exceeded
analysts' expectations for the quarter. This year, Hasbro expects earnings growth to top its 15% annual
target, in part because it has the toy-licensing rights to the new "Star Wars" movie, due out in May.
Big cable operators are expected to report healthy gains in cash flow, a key indicator of cable-company
health, pushed along by strong demand for their rapidly expanding cable-TV offerings. Though most of the
companies won't report fourth-quarter numbers until March, analysts generally expect to see big expenditures
for upgrades to cable-TV systems, which are being outfitted to handle a range of new digital fare. That
includes interactive video, Internet hookups and telephone.
Domestic operators, for the most part, have steered clear of problems in the global economic markets. With
the exception of MediaOne Group, most cable-TV companies don't have operations abroad.
The nation's major publishers posted mixed results in a tumultuous advertising environment. Gannett's net
income rose, buoyed by stronger advertising at USA Today. The New York Times and Meredith also posted
stronger ad revenue. Meredith saw record revenue growth in its publishing group, which includes the
magazines Ladies' Home Journal and Better Homes and Gardens. Reader's Digest's bottom line grew, in part
from cost cuts and the sale of art, but it warned that business would be volatile in 1999. Meanwhile, McGraw
Hill's net rose, led by growth in its financial-services division.
The Washington Post reported stronger advertising growth in its newspaper division but posted lower net
income because of a gain a year earlier. Dow Jones posted a slim loss, stemming in part from a drop in
print-publishing revenue as ad linage at The Wall Street Journal slipped. The company's results also included
several one-time costs, including technology write-downs.
Airlines were buffeted by powerful headwinds in the quarter, resulting in lower earnings for Continental
Airlines, AMR's American Airlines, UAL's United Airlines and US Airways Group. Northwest Airlines, still
trying to recover from a late-summer strike, reported a loss of $181 million. On top of weak markets in Asia
and Latin America, airlines suffered softness in pricing on trans-Atlantic routes, and some even reported
lower unit revenues in domestic markets. Delta Air Lines and America West Airlines bucked the trend with
small earnings gains, while Southwest Airlines and Alaska Airlines recorded double-digit increases.
Although analysts say a weaker-revenue trend appears to be continuing into 1999, the industry was bolstered
a bit when a fare increase stuck. The boost, 2% on business fares and 4% on leisure ticket prices, was the
first across-the-board fare increase in 16 months.

Continued restructuring and a glut in the hog market depressed some food companies' earnings last quarter.
Agribusiness concerns such as Tyson Foods and Hormel Foods reported that poor pork results diminished
companywide profit gains. ConAgra, long plagued by problems in its pork and fresh-meats business, started
to see a turnaround, however. Restructuring charges at Campbell Soup, Nabisco and Kellogg continued to
hurt earnings. Sara Lee reported a higher-than-expected pretax charge of $76 million to cover costs related to
a meat recall.

--- Page 287 ---
Page 287 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Restructuring alone won't solve food companies' problems. Analysts are concerned that the core business of
some companies may not be strong enough to sustain long-term growth. At best, the sector's prospects are
expected to improve in the second half of this year.
Major soft-drink companies posted dismal earnings, with economic turmoil hurting sales in international
markets and price discounting squeezing profitability in the U.S. Coca-Cola, already battered by weak foreign
currencies, witnessed the full-blown effect of a sharp slowdown in several markets, including Brazil, Russia
and South Africa. PepsiCo's net income was lifted by a gain of $294 million, or 19 cents a share, but about 17
cents of that gain was offset by several restructuring charges.
While Pepsi said it anticipates a "flattish" first quarter, operating profit at its domestic beverage and snack
divisions should increase for the quarter and the full year. Analysts are pessimistic about Coke's prospects for
the first half of the year and are hoping that a possible turnaround in Japan and Germany will lift profits.
Health insurers and managed-care companies, beginning to emerge from a three-year industry slump,
enjoyed modest profit gains. Aetna beat analysts' estimates on an operating basis, but Wall Street was
concerned that the strong performance of its health-care unit, Aetna U.S. Healthcare, was fueled more by
higher investment income than by the strength of its core business.
The managed-care industry has suffered from a protracted price war to gain market share as well as soaring
costs for prescription drugs and new medical technology. This year, companies should see better profits as
they reap the rewards of such recent steps as raising premiums for many employers by 7% or more,
revamping pharmacy benefits to pass on more costs to consumers for pricey, brand-name drugs, and exiting
from unprofitable Medicare markets. Meanwhile, hospitals have been hurt by the continuing impact of
cutbacks in Medicare reimbursements enacted in 1997.
Drug-company profits declined 8%, mainly because of large one-time charges taken by several companies.
Excluding the charges, earnings rose significantly at most companies, due to robust sales of relatively new
drugs for depression, allergies, cholesterol and cancer. Johnson & Johnson's net income declined
significantly because of an expected $610 million charge for a long-term restructuring of its international
manufacturing capabilities. But Wall Street liked the company's performance, as sales were stronger than in
previous quarters, and earnings rose 10% when the charge was excluded. The strongest-performing drug
company was Warner-Lambert, whose net income soared on sizzling sales increases for its
cholesterol-lowering drug Lipitor.
Analysts expect most drug companies to continue to record relatively strong profit and sales gains for the
next few quarters, until a series of patent expirations starts cutting into sales in 2000 and 2001. Other than
the expirations, the main threat to the industry is the possibility of a Medicare drug benefit.
As they have in quarters past, tobacco-settlement charges substantially affected cigarette makers' earnings.
But the companies benefited from strong performances from their U.S. tobacco units.
Market leader Philip Morris said its profit fell 79%, largely because of $2.4 billion in pretax charges taken last
year, mostly for tobacco-litigation settlements. Excluding these charges as well as various one-time items
taken in 1997, the company's earnings rose 9% to $1.8 billion on a 6% gain in revenue. The company's U.S.
cigarette business remained strong despite a barrage of regulatory and legal attacks. Excluding one-time
items, U.S. tobacco operating income in the quarter rose 15% to $1.4 billion on higher cigarette pricing.
The trends were similar at RJR Nabisco Holdings, although its tobacco unit is struggling with a portfolio of
aging cigarette brands in the U.S. and economic crises abroad. The tobacco and food marketer's long-ailing
U.S. tobacco business posted a 25% increase in operating income to $445 million, on a 15% gain in sales.

In the semiconductor industry, Intel reported that a strong Christmas computer trade helped boost sales of its
microprocessors despite fears that the Asian financial crisis could hurt demand. The company expects
first-quarter sales to be down sequentially, following a seasonal cycle in the post-Christmas quarter.

--- Page 288 ---
Page 288 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Not every computer-chip maker enjoyed the same success. Advanced Micro Devices turned a profit, but
continuing price pressures at the low end of the market led the company to expect an operating loss in the
first quarter. Analog-chip makers showed some profit gains but also suffered from the overall chip downturn
that saw industry revenues fall 10% in 1998.
Semiconductor-equipment suppliers like Applied Materials have been hurt more by the downturn, since
massive overcapacity means few chip makers are willing to splurge on new equipment. But Applied and other
suppliers of chip-making equipment are hoping for a turnaround this year, lagging behind chip-maker
recoveries. Most chip makers agree with forecasts that the industry will put its worst year in a decade behind
it in 1999. Dataquest recently forecast that the chip market would grow 14.6% this year.
In a related realm, while it may be a different story later in the year, the year-2000 computer problem proved
a boon to most software companies. Many big corporate software buyers are preparing to "lock down"
computer systems in preparation for the century rollover in December. As a result, they did a lot of their
spending in the fourth quarter, knowing they will be leaving their systems alone for the rest of the year.
That proved to be good news for companies like Microsoft, which reported a 75% increase in profit from the
prior year, along with a 38% boost in sales. The results were far above Wall Street's expectations.
PeopleSoft, however, reported weak earnings and announced it was cutting 6% of its staff. The company is
facing slowing sales because of increased competition in the market for software to run a company's
business functions. Industry officials warn that the rest of the year might not be as good as the last quarter,
and thus are being careful with their expansion plans.
Leading aerospace companies largely matched already scaled-back expectations for the quarter, although
several reported slimmer profit margins and weaker revenues than a year earlier. Continuing fallout from
Asia's economic woes depressed results for various segments of the industry, with some companies,
particularly Northrop, predicting the pain could get worse in coming quarters.
While Boeing, the largest company, modestly beat analysts' expectations, it relied largely on robust results
from its military-aircraft unit. Reporting only $63 million in operating profit from commercial-aircraft operations,
Boeing said it is aggressively pursuing new cost reductions and efficiency improvements.
Biotechnology companies generally posted stronger profits, as a spate of new products have been selling
briskly for some of the larger concerns. Amgen was the standout, powered by a 35% leap in sales of its
Epogen anemia drug, in part because of more-generous Medicare-reimbursement rules. Biogen posted
record results for the quarter, as sales of its Avonex treatment for multiple sclerosis rose by 76%, in part due
to strong sales in Europe.
Monsanto suffered from increased research-and-development spending and large restructuring charges. The
company is expecting another rough year in 1999, but analysts are focusing on prospects for its new
Celebrex arthritis-pain drug.
Analysts are looking for continued strong growth early this year from those biotech companies with successful
products already on the market.

For the banking industry, the fourth quarter largely mirrored the third: Big banks with a lot of foreign exposure
continued to feel the sting of the global financial crisis, while many regional banks posted solid earnings
growth on the back of the healthy U.S. economy. Chase Manhattan was one exception among big banks,
with a 31% rise in profit. BankAmerica, created by last fall's merger of the old BankAmerica and NationsBank,
saw net income drop partly because it continues to be weighed down by its relationship with investment firm
D.E. Shaw. Citigroup's earnings tumbled after restructuring charges related to the merger of Citicorp and
Travelers Group.
Analysts cautioned that, though there are indications that the financial-market turmoil is easing, some banks
show signs that interest-rate spreads are narrowing, which could put a brake on future profitability.

--- Page 289 ---
Page 289 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In the insurance industry, grueling competition among property-casualty insurers continued to take its toll,
with many companies reporting lower profits. Those companies most reliant on standard
commercial-insurance products, such as liability and executive protection, took the biggest hit, as competition
made it nearly impossible to raise prices. CNA Financial, a Loews unit, took a $220 million charge for
unanticipated claims costs, among other charges.
Life insurers such as Hartford Life and American General, which continue to make inroads into highly popular
retirement annuity products, turned in strong operating-profit growth, even as sales from more-traditional
life-insurance products proved stagnant.
Wall Street firms continued to take it on the chin, as many saw their earnings hurt in the aftermath of last
year's market turmoil. The biggest losers were firms, such as Merrill Lynch and Lehman Brothers Holdings,
that have large institutional businesses, meaning they make money by underwriting and trading stocks and
bonds. Nearly every major securities firm that plays in this arena reported lower earnings. Analysts attribute
the decline to a dearth of underwriting business and a drop in proprietary-trading revenues as firms hit hard
by the global financial crisis tried to clean up their balance sheets.
But Legg Mason and Charles Schwab both reported higher earnings. The firms specialize in "retail"
brokerage services, acting mostly as conduits for small investors, who continue to pour money into stocks
and mutual funds.
UTILITIES

Most of the big telephone companies posted earnings in line with analysts' expectations thanks to strong
demand for data services. But Sprint posted lower-than-expected earnings in its core telephone business as
the company continued to spend heavily to build a data-oriented telecommunications network.
Sprint, AT&T and Bell Atlantic all reported strong growth in wireless services because of heavy promotion of
flat-rate pricing plans. The currency crisis in Brazil is expected to hurt at least one big telephone company:
BellSouth. It operates wireless phone services in that nation and said it expects to take a one-time charge in
the first quarter related to the devaluation of the real.
Despite generally lower U.S. revenues as a result of warmer-than-usual weather, many large electric utilities
reported solid profits, largely on the strength of unregulated businesses, including energy services, and
foreign investments.
Duke Energy's strong earnings growth primarily reflected gains at the unit that builds and acquires power
plants and pipelines, as well as its trading and marketing arm. Other utilities posted losses on investments in
Latin American power projects but benefited from increased electric use by domestic customers they still
serve as monopolies. Southern took a $200 million charge on its projects in Argentina and Chile, while
reporting increased electric sales back home.
In this quarter, industry profits will depend on a number of regulatory rulings, as well as the outcome of
competition for various generating assets for sale around the country that will help utilities boost their
unregulated businesses.

World Watch
Compiled by Paul J. Deveney
1,564 words

The Wall Street Journal

ASIA/PACIFIC
Indonesia Unveils
Plan to Shut Down
More of Its Banks
With the International Monetary Fund and others breathing down its neck, Indonesia says it's getting ready to
shut down more of its troubled banks by the end of this month. The only catch is it didn't say how many
financial institutions will be forced to close their doors or which ones will be targeted. Some in the industry
were quick to note that the announcement by Indonesia's president, B.J. Habibie, could trigger a run on
banks by depositors fearing that their institutions may be on the list. But others say they doubt that would
happen this time around given that since the last run on the nation's banks -- in November 1997 -- Jakarta
has instituted a policy of guaranteeing bank deposits.
Indonesia's banking system has been having a tough time keeping its head above water amid the economic
crisis in the region -- the sector has been particularly hard hit by high interest rates and a big drop in the
value of the currency. In addition, the government has been accused of not being open enough about the way
it makes decisions when it comes to recapitalizing troubled banks. But the governor of the country's central
bank says lawmakers are expected to approve a state aid package soon and that this time the aid will be
transparent.
China Factory Data Raise Questions
China has been spending money like crazy the last few months on big infrastructure projects. So officials took
particular delight in the latest figures on industrial production, which soared 17.9% in January from a year
earlier. But analysts weren't as enthusiastic, saying that they want more proof that things are improving
before pinning too much hope on the industrial sector's ability to fuel economic growth this year. While they
concede that factory output has indeed been steadily climbing since last fall, they say the huge leap last
month may have had as much to do with the fact that there were more working days in January this year than
last because of the timing of the Lunar New Year holiday. Also, they say the idea of soaring output may not
be all it's cracked up to be, given that retail sales growth has slowed and prices were down 2.8% last month
from a year earlier.
Hong Kong Firms' Generosity Is Cited
With Hong Kong in recession, more and more companies in the territory have been forced to lay off workers.
But a survey by the accounting firm KPMG shows the companies may have heart after all. The study found
that more than two-thirds of those surveyed offered a bonus in addition to the government-mandated
minimum severance pay of between one and three months salary. While this may be attributable solely to
corporate generosity, it may also have something to do with the fact that once placid workers in the former
British colony are speaking up for themselves in the face of downsizing. Late last year, Hong Kong
Telecommunications was forced to back down from a plan to slash its payroll amid unusually strong protests
from the rank-and-file.
Asia Sees PC Sales Picture Improving
It looks as if the downward trend in sales of personal computers in Asia may be finally reversing itself.
Spurred by a 27% increase in such shipments to China, the number of PCs sold in the region, excluding
Japan, jumped 7% in the fourth quarter of last year to 2.9 million units. What has some in the industry elated
is that that figure, from International Data, is the highest quarterly total ever recorded for the region. In fact,

--- Page 292 ---
Page 292 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
the news prompted predictions that unit sales -- which have been sagging for most of last year -- will hit a
record for all of 1999 as well. One analyst said China, India and Australia are expected to lead the pack this
year, although concerns remain about China's economy and the stability of its currency.
BRIEFLY:
-- San Miguel, the big Philippine food and beverage group, saw its earnings soar to 24.4 billion pesos ($626
million) last year from 2.96 billion pesos a year earlier amid some big asset sales. But stripping out those
one-time gains, its profit still managed to climb 21%, a figure broadly in line with analysts' expectations.
-- Bangkok Bank, one of Thailand's biggest financial institutions, approved a plan to raise 40 billion baht
($1.08 billion) from the sale of mutual-fund units backed by issues of preferred shares and debentures.

EUROPE
Sweden's Investor AB Names New CEO
Some things never seem to change. Investor AB, the main holding company of Sweden's powerful
Wallenberg family, annointed 42-year-old Marcus Wallenberg as its new chief executive officer. It's a
symbolic move that marks continuity of the powers that be. The changing of the guard follows a tumultuous
year for the holding company, in which it experienced defiance from unexpected quarters and saw economic
turmoil hurt some of the big industrial companies in its portfolio. Moreover, the company, which saw its
earnings fall 4.9% last year, saw its once-undisputed authority challenged by one of its former disciples,
Volvo President and Chief Executive Leif Johansson, who attempted "a hostile takeover" of
Wallenberg-controlled truck maker Scania.
U.K.'s Rover Slashes Some Auto Prices
BMW's British unit Rover says it's cutting sticker prices on selected models by an average of 9.5%, effective
immediately. Usually such sharp discounts are seen by those in the industry as an effort to move cars off of
dealers' lots before the new models hit showrooms next month. But there may be more to all this for the
unprofitable unit of Germany's Bayerische Motoren Werke. Given Rover's serious difficulties in fighting back
competition from its rivals, some say the deep cut in prices could also be a desperate attempt to defend the
market share of the once proud British marque, which now lags behind even French auto makers Peugeot
and Renault in the U.K. market.
GratisTel's Strategy Includes U.S.
Many of the world's biggest telecom operators will start offering free telephone services later this year for
those callers willing to put up with the once unthinkable -- having their calls interrupted by commercials. The
concept was pioneered by GratisTel International of Sweden, and it's catching on. Since its inception early
last year, some 100,000 subscribers have signed up in Sweden and about half that number in Norway. Now
the company is extending its reach across the oceans; it says it will announce license deals early next week
to launch free phone service with large operators in the U.S., Japan and Britain.
BRIEFLY:
-- British industrial materials concern Cookson plans to cut 700 jobs amid the world-wide slowdown in the
electronics industry.
-- French computer maker Cie. des Machines Bull's earnings tumbled 97% last year to three million euros
($3.4 million) amid its restructuring. Meanwhile, it said it hired an investment bank to find a strategic partner to
buy the French government's 17.4% stake.
-- Citing the possibility of a conflict of interest, the head of MFK Renaissance financial group, Boris Jordan,
says he'll quit as chairman of Russian oil firm Sidanko. One of MFK Renaissance's funds is a creditor of
Sidanko, which could face bankruptcy proceedings.
-- The French insurer Axa saw revenue rise 1.9% last year to 371.5 billion francs ($64.17 billion), despite
strong growth in financial services.
-- Germany's Commerzbank will likely seek approval for a share buyback at its general meeting next month,
individuals say.

Brazil State Files Suit Over Debt Terms
The Brazilian state of Rio Grande do Sul filed a suit in Federal Supreme Court against the debt-renegotiation
contract signed by the state's previous leadership with the federal government in 1997. The state is one of
seven that have drawn up a joint proposal to renegotiate the terms of such deals, which saw the federal
government assume 108 billion reals ($57.4 million) in obligations from 26 of the country's 27 states. Earlier
this week, Brazil's president canceled meetings with the rebel governors that were to center on the debt
renegotiation.
Brazil Cracks Down on Phone Firms
Following its first assessment of post-privatization telecommunications services, Brazil regulators said they
will apply sanctions to several providers for failing to meet performance targets. They said eight of the 34
carriers in Brazil will be required to indemnify customers for failing to install new phone lines purchased as
part of an expansion program initiated when the companies were still under government control. The
government sold Telecomunicacoes Brasileiras, or Telebras, last July. The terms of the sale specified that
the new owners would have to comply with service commitments made by the local carrier before the
privatization. Each of the companies receiving sanctions will have to provide affected subscribers with service
perks, including free minutes on calling cards.
BRIEFLY:
-- Canada's Royal Oak Mines won a last-minute reprieve from the government of British Columbia, which
allowed the company's Kemess South copper and gold mine to continue operating while it resolves a
problem with its refuse dam.

Finance Stocks Give Up Advances; Yahoo!, CNET Fall in Carnage
By Robert O'Brien
Dow Jones Newswires
1,168 words
10 February 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Amid yesterday's tech-stock carnage, other stock sectors tried to rally, but it was rough going.
Financial stocks, for example, did make an effort to rally intraday, which could have lent the broader market
some support, but the group, with a few exceptions, couldn't sustain its advance, ending lower.
BankAmerica got up more than two points, but closed 1/4 point lower at 59 3/4. Chase Manhattan closed 1
1/8 lower at 72. Citigroup fell 1 7/8 to 50 5/8. J.P. Morgan did end higher, up 1 1/4 at 100 1/2, but that marked
a decline of nearly two points from its best levels of the session.
Market watchers said the weakness in the financial sector represented a lack of confidence in the group's
ability to increase profits. Earnings momentum already looms as a problem, and the rise in interest rates just
puts more pressure on that situation. The weakness in global economies raises worries that asset quality
could deteriorate.
Brokerage-company stocks were hurt, as well, with Donaldson Lufkin & Jenrette off 2 9/16 to 48 3/4, Morgan
Stanley Dean Witter declining 2 13/16 to 85 3/4, and Charles Schwab falling 2 5/8 to 62 7/8.
Internet-brokerage operations caught the worst of both worlds, with weakness in technology and banking.
Ameritrade Holdings (Nasdaq) declined 14 15/16 to 65 1/16 and E*Trade Group (Nasdaq) lost 5 9/16 to 40
3/16.
There was a broad-based setback for blue-chip stocks, as the Dow Jones Industrial Average surrendered
158.08, or 1.70%, to close at 9133.03. It marked the second-worst one-day loss by the industrial average in
this young year, behind a 228.63-point setback on Jan. 14, but didn't rank among the Dow's 20 worst point
losses in history, let alone rank near the top of any percentage-based lists.
Technology stocks suffered one of their worst lashings of the past six months as investors in that sector,
already wary after a week's worth of volatility, found they couldn't digest bad news about a major Internet
stock.
Lycos fell 33 to 94 1/4 on the Nasdaq Stock Market after the company, which operates Internet sites, agreed
to a takeover by USA Networks. The disappointment apparently stemmed from what investors saw as a
relatively modest acquisition premium in the price of the takeover, which suggested subsequent deals for
Internet concerns won't be done at dizzying prices.
Those worries scorched values in the group. Big names like Yahoo! (Nasdaq), which lost 17 7/8 to 140 3/4,
and America Online, which fell 11 1/16 to 147 15/16, had another volatile day. Those moves were relatively
modest, given the volatility associated with Internet stocks.
But some other Internet names had more drastic moves. CNET (Nasdaq), for example, lost 25 3/4 to end at
98 1/2.
"The profit-taking in Internet stocks is certainly taking place," said Richard Cripps, vice president of research
at Legg Mason. "The question is, where is that money going to go?"
Intel lost 6 11/16 to 125 5/16 on Nasdaq. Other bellwether technology stocks that fell by six points or more on
Nasdaq included Dell Computer, which lost 6 1/4 to 97 13/16; Sun Microsystems, which declined 6 7/8 to 97
5/8 ; and Cisco Systems, which dropped 6 to 95 15/16.
Microsoft (Nasdaq) got off relatively easy: Its loss amounted to 5 3/16, dropping it to 160 1/16.

Dollar Slips Against the Yen but Gains on Euro
Foreign Exchange
Dollar Slips Against the Yen but Gains on Euro
By Stephanie Hoo
Dow Jones Newswires
659 words
10 February 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C21
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- The dollar fell against the yen and rose against the euro, making small corrections of
Monday's trends but no sharp moves.
Action centered on the euro, which slumped yesterday against the dollar and the yen, partially reversing the
big gains it made Monday.
"The ongoing bias is still to sell euros on the rallies," said Susan Stearns, director of foreign exchange at the
Bank of Montreal in New York. "Take a look at the structural picture. We're still talking about double-digit
unemployment in Euroland."
Just as the euro's rally against the yen Monday took the dollar higher against the Japanese currency, traders
said the euro's fall yesterday against the yen pulled the dollar lower against it too.
The dollar nonetheless held the bulk of its recent gains against the yen, leaving the U.S. currency poised to
go higher, traders said.
But to some degree, the trade of dollar against yen may be getting caught by the confusing debate in Japan
about how to bring down yields on long-term government bonds.
"This very public discourse in Japan has the market trying to assess what each and every possibility may
bring," said Ms. Stearns. He expects the dollar will be flat against the yen ahead of the Bank of Japan
meeting Friday.
Late in New York, the dollar was trading at 114.17 yen, down from 114.57 late Monday. The euro was trading
at $1.1305, down from $1.1327. Sterling was trading at $1.6375, down from $1.6400. About mid-day
Wednesday in Tokyo, the dollar was trading at 114.15 yen, while the euro was trading at $1.1306.
European Central Bank president Wim Duisenberg yesterday reiterated his argument for keeping euro-zone
interest rates steady, saying that structural reform, not a rate cut, is needed to spur growth.
But the euro remains pressured by rate-cut speculation, though some analysts have amended their forecasts,
predicting a cut in the first half of 1999, though not necessarily in the first quarter.
The euro's outlook is clouded by "the fact that it's a new currency, the fact that there's constant revaluing of
existing bonds, the fact that there are no accurate risk-versus-reward levels and no accurate read on what the
economies are doing," said Dan Bernzweig, a trader at Bank Leumi in New York.
Sterling edged down against the dollar and was virtually flat against the euro yesterday, trading in narrow
ranges ahead of the Bank of England's quarterly inflation report today. The euro was quoted late yesterday
at 0.6904, compared with 0.6907 late Monday.
The Brazilian real came close to breaking the two-reals-to-the-dollar level yesterday, showing the pressure
isn't yet off. The real closed flat at 1.91 reals to the dollar after a volatile session.
Financial wrangling between the federal government and individual states is seen weighing on the real. "And
there's still nothing definitive on the IMF (International Monetary Fund) deal," said Bank of Montreal's Ms.
Stearns.
The real had traded as low as 2.44 reals to the dollar on Jan. 29.

--- Page 297 ---
Page 297 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Canadian dollar weakened slightly on profit-taking, after staging strong rallies last week. The U.S. dollar
was quoted late yesterday at C$1.4953, up from C$1.4913 late Monday.
Other so-called commodities-based currencies also weakened, tracking the slide in gold prices.
Late yesterday, the Australian dollar was trading at US$0.6459, down from US$0.6508 late Monday.
The South African rand weakened to 6.1100 rand late yesterday in New York, from 5.9705 rand late Monday.
The rand was additionally pressured by the South African Reserve Bank's estimate that the nation's
current-account "showed substantial deficits" in the fourth quarter.

Dollar, After Rising in Early Trading,Loses Ground Against the Yen and Euro

Dollar, After Rising in Early Trading, Loses Ground Against the Yen and Euro
By Marianne Sullivan
Dow Jones Newswires
646 words
5 February 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C20
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Despite beginning the day on a bullish note, the dollar gave up its gains against the yen and
the euro in timid markets unable to decide on which way economic developments in Europe and Japan were
moving.
The U.S. currency reversed direction throughout the day, first moving higher with the fall in Japanese
government-bonds yields. Meeting selling pressure at the 114 yen level, it then headed lower, setting off a
chain reaction of selling that resulted in a decline of more than two yen, to 111.65 yen. The slide was
countered by rising fears that the Bank of Japan would intervene to stop the yen's appreciation, traders said.
Nor was volatility absent in the dollar's relationship with the euro. The euro sank to new lows yesterday,
hitting $1.1248 after the Bank of England's interest-rate cut seemed to signal that rate cuts were also on the
way for the euro-zone countries.
But again the dollar couldn't sustain its gains and moved lower after the European Central Bank announced
it was holding interest rates steady.
Meanwhile, commodity-based currencies and sterling put in a strong performance. The Australian dollar hit its
highest level since May 2 after the Canadian dollar broke above the key C$1.500 level, reaching a six-month
high on the back of rising gold and silver prices.
Late in New York, the dollar was at 112.13 yen, down from 112.84 yen late Wednesday in New York. The
euro was trading at $1.1336, up from $1.1304 Wednesday. Sterling was trading at $1.6451, up from $1.6353.
In Tokyo at midday Friday, the dollar was trading at 112.15 yen, and the euro was trading at $1.1352.
Sterling was the currency that again surprised economists, rallying despite the Bank of England's decision to
cut its securities repurchase rate to 5 1/2 % from 6%, a quarter-point more than traders had expected.
While the pound initially fell a half cent, the currency made a turnaround as optimism grew that the cut would
help the British economy and, in turn, help the pound.
"The pound has rallied consistently after eases," said Henry Willmore, senior economist at Barclays Capital in
New York. "It rallies because of the view that the rate ease will help contain the slowdown and avoid a
recession."
Since October, the Bank of England has cut repurchase rates every month, to bring it to its current level from
7 1/2 %. The reductions come amid attempts to promote growth as the British economy slows.
Economists, however, said sterling should still be able to benefit from the interest-rate differential advantage
that the United Kingdom still has over the euro.
"The {Bank of England's} cut highlighted the fact that there will be more European cuts on the way," said
Thomas Benfer, foreign exchange adviser at Bank of Montreal in New York. "It's a symbol of what is to come
and supported the dollar earlier in the morning."
Commodity currencies powered higher as gold rose to $289.45 per troy ounce, following silver's rally to
$5.765 an ounce.
The rally in commodities was the trigger which helped the Canadian currency through the key C$1.500
technical barrier, Mr. Benfer said. Stop-loss selling of U.S. dollars created a snowball effect which led the
Canadian dollar even higher, peaking at $1.4900, its highest level since July 21.

The rally in the Australian dollar also was helped by the yen's rise, as well as by Australian Prime Minister
John Howard's affirmation that his government expects growth to slow to 3 1/4% in the fiscal year ending
June 30.

Deutsche Bank Discloses InvolvementIn the Financing of Nazi Death Camp
International
Deutsche Bank Discloses Involvement In the Financing of Nazi Death Camp
By Christopher Rhoads
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
706 words
5 February 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
FRANKFURT -- Deutsche Bank AG disclosed yesterday that it helped finance the construction of Auschwitz,
the Nazi death camp, a revelation that raised questions in at least one state over regulatory approval of its
acquisition of Bankers Trust Corp.
The bank's historian said the bank provided financing for several firms that were the main builders of the
infamous Polish camp, where more than 1.5 million prisoners, mostly Jews, were killed during World War II.
Previously, the bank had portrayed itself as less involved with the Nazi regime. Its chairman was said to have
had contact with the German resistance, and it was taken over by Nazi elements later than others.
But the latest revelations are a blow. "This is a cause of deep consternation for us," said Rolf Breuer,
chairman of Deutsche Bank, in a memo to staff. "I should like to emphasize once again, on the bank's behalf,
that we deeply regret the misery and injustice suffered and that we acknowledge the bank's ethical and moral
responsibility."
The bank conceded previously that it had dealt in gold looted from victims of the Nazi regime, but stressed
there was no proof bank officials at the time were aware of the gold's source. Those allegations are the basis
of an $18 billion suit against the bank and other German lenders.
The disclosures could delay the approval from U.S. state and federal regulators of the bank's proposed $10.1
billion acquisition of Bankers Trust, a deal announced in November, analysts said.
Alan Hevesi, the New York City comptroller, has raised objections about the acquisition, asking regulators to
delay approving the deal until Deutsche Bank satisfies concerns about its role during the war. In response to
yesterday's development, his office reiterated that "federal and state officials should take no action on the
proposed merger between Deutsche Bank and Bankers Trust until these issues are fully resolved."
Mr. Hevesi doesn't have any formal regulatory approval power. Approval is required from the Federal
Reserve and the New York state Banking Commission.
Mr. Breuer, chairman of Deutsche Bank, said earlier this week that a delay on the regulatory front could
undermine the "economic reasoning" of the deal. He pointed out that if approval isn't obtained by April or
May, as planned, it could be delayed another six months due to the so-called millennium bug issue. Mr.
Breuer said he was under the impression regulators are reluctant to approve deals that would affect
back-office operations too close to the beginning of 2000. The Fed has said it would consider the ability of
financial institutions to handle the Year 2000 computer bug as part of the regulatory-approval process for
mergers or acquisitions, but says it has set no deadline.
The disclosures resulted from the recent work of an independent historical commission set up by the bank in
1997 to look into its role during the Nazi period.
The group discovered from recently collated documents that the bank's branch in Katowice, Poland extended
credit on one occasion in 1942, to a construction company called W. Riedel & Sohn, Eisenbeton-und
Hochbau. A copy of the contract, which the bank displayed, detailed the costs of building bridges, railways,
ditches and other transport-related work at the Auschwitz camp. The research also showed that in 1943, the
branch extended monthly credit to I.G. Farben's Auschwitz factory. I.G. Farben developed the gas used to kill
prisoners.
Among other findings, the research showed that the Gestapo, the Nazi secret police, held accounts with
Deutsche Bank branches in Berlin and Hamburg that contained proceeds from the sales of property seized
from Jews.

--- Page 301 ---
Page 301 of 305 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Manfred Pohl, the director of the Historical Institute of Deutsche Bank, said the evidence indicates that in all
likelihood the managers of the Katowice branch were aware of where the bank's money was going, though
there is less evidence that the senior managers in Berlin were aware of the purpose of their financing.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Death Camps Were Not `Polish'" -- WSJ Feb. 22, 1999)
